Misconception 240: Misunderstands how to calculate the lower bound in a division, believes it is found by dividing both upper bounds
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands the relationship between upper bounds, lower bounds, and division, specifically believes that the lower bound of a quotient can be calculated by dividing both upper bounds, showing a lack of understanding that the lower bound is determined by dividing the lower bound of the dividend by the upper bound of the divisor.

Misconception 241: Thinks co-interior angles sum to 360 degrees
Expanded misconception:
Thinks co-interior angles formed by a transversal with a pair of parallel lines sum to 360 degrees, rather than understanding that co-interior angles are supplementary (add up to 180 degrees).

Misconception 242: Thinks the hundred thousands column is the ten thousands column
Expanded misconception:
Confuses place values within five-digit or larger numbers, specifically misidentifying the hundred thousands column as the ten thousands column, leading to computation errors or misinterpretation of numerical values.

Misconception 243: Identifies a common factor but not the highest common factor
Expanded misconception:
Fail to identify the greatest common divisor (GCD), instead settling for a common factor as the solution when simplifying fractions or finding equivalent ratios.

Misconception 244: Believes the solution of mx + c = 0 is x = m
Expanded misconception:
Ignores the need to isolate the variable in linear equations, specifically when dividing by coefficients, incorrectly assuming the solution to mx + c = 0 is solely x = m, rather than x = -c/m.

Misconception 245: Does not realise that to maintain balance in an equation they must add or subtract the same amount from both sides
Expanded misconception:
Does not realise that to maintain balance in an equation, they must apply inverse operations or the same operation (i.e., adding or subtracting, multiplying or dividing) with the same value or expression to both sides of the equation, rather than changing one side independently.

Misconception 246: Does not think a kilogram is a unit of mass
Expanded misconception:
Does not think a kilogram is a unit of mass, often confusing it with other units of measurement such as volume or weight and may incorrectly apply it to quantities of liquids or area.

Misconception 247: Found the percentage but added it to 100%
Expanded misconception:
Failed to apply percentage changes correctly by adding the percentage to the original total (100% or baseline), rather than treating it as a proportion of the original total, and adjusting the calculation accordingly.

Misconception 248: Believes a pie chart makes 360% not 360 degrees
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets or confuses percentage and degree measurements, particularly in circular figures such as pie charts, leading to incorrect calculations or understanding of proportions.

Misconception 249: Does not understand a denominator represents equal parts
Expanded misconception:
"Does not understand that a denominator represents equal parts in a fraction, often leading to incorrect simplification, scaling, or comparison of fractions."

Misconception 250: Believes a fraction cannot have a numerator of 0
Expanded misconception:
Believes that a fraction cannot have a numerator of 0, and therefore may incorrectly apply rules for undefined or invalid mathematical expressions, or may be overly restrictive when simplifying or manipulating fractions.

Misconception 251: Includes a data point with the value, when asked to find the number of data points below a value
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between values being less than or less than/equal to a given value when determining the number of data points, resulting in incorrect counts due to inclusion or exclusion errors.

Misconception 252: Does not think 'other' is an acceptable category for data
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand or recognize the concept of an 'other' or 'miscellaneous' category, and instead attempts to force data into predefined categories that may not be suitable or relevant, indicating a lack of flexibility or awareness of data classification principles.

Misconception 253: Does not recognise that all lengths on a cube are equal
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognise or apply the geometric property that all lengths (e.g., edges or sides) on a cube or square prism are equal.

Misconception 254: Believes all triangle numbers are odd
Expanded misconception:
Believes all triangle numbers, or the sum of consecutive integers starting from 1, are odd and applies this incorrect generalization to pattern recognition and arithmetic problems involving specific sequences or series.

Misconception 255: Does not know the meaning of the word regular 
Expanded misconception:
Does not know the meaning of specified terms or concepts within a mathematical context (e.g. the meaning of the word "regular" in reference to shapes or polyhedra).

Misconception 256: Does not reverse the order of operations when solving an equation
Expanded misconception:
Failure to adhere to the order of operations or BEDMAS/BODMAS (Brackets/Braces, Exponents/Orders, Division, Multiplication, Addition, and Subtraction) when solving an equation, resulting in incorrect application of operations and/or failure to first solve within brackets or parentheses.

Misconception 257: Uses numerator as whole part of a fraction
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets division or fraction problems by using the numerator as the whole, failing to account for the denominator, and neglecting to perform the correct division operation.

Misconception 258: When finding a missing number in a factor tree, multiplies by the factor given rather than dividing.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply division operation to find the missing number in a factor tree, instead uses multiplication with the given factors, possibly due to a misunderstanding of the relationship between multiplication and division in this context.

Misconception 259: Thinks that when drawing the elevation, the bottom layer does not need to be included 
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that specific features, such as the base, bottom layer, or foundation, are irrelevant or unnecessary when constructing a visual representation of an object or shape, such as an elevation or cross-section, resulting in an incomplete representation.

Misconception 260: Thinks cubing a number means adding 3 to that number
Expanded misconception:
Confuses exponentiation with arithmetic addition, mistaken operation applied to bases of exponents, specifically believing that cubing a number involves adding 3 to that number rather than raising it to the power of 3.

Misconception 261: When multiplying just writes the numbers next to each other
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly apply the multiplication operation or concept, instead simply writing the numbers next to each other or incorrectly adding the numbers together, indicating a lack of understanding of the multiplication process or incorrect recall of multiplication facts.

Misconception 262: Believes the intersection in a prime factor venn diagram does not contribute to the size of the number represented by a circle 
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to recognize the intersection in a prime factor Venn diagram as a shared factor, and/or does not understand its multiplicative contribution to the overall product represented by each circle, leading to incorrect calculations or failure to take the common factor into account."

Misconception 263: Does not understand direction of North East, North West, South East and South West cardinal points
Expanded misconception:
Has difficulty understanding or applying direction based on the cardinal points (North, South, East, West) and their intermediate directions (North East, North West, South East, South West) in various spatial contexts, including geometry, geography, and navigation.

Misconception 264: Subtracted rather than divided by a scale factor
Expanded misconception:
Failed to recognize the inverse relationship between multiplication and division, resulting in subtracting rather than dividing by a scale factor or multiplier when adapting or scaling quantities, resulting in an incorrect proportional relationship, possibly due to a misunderstanding of unit conversion or ratio equivalence.

Misconception 265: Does not realise a quadratic must be in the form ax^2+bx+c=0 to be factorised
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand that a quadratic expression must be in the standard form ax^2+bx+c=0 to be factorised or solved using factoring methods, which requires the equation to be set equal to zero before attempting to factor.

Misconception 266: Identifies a letter as a common factor but doesn't identify a number as a common factor
Expanded misconception:
Overemphasizes the role of variables in determining common factors, and has difficulty recognizing numerical values as greatest/common factors, such as when a numerical constant is the smallest shared factor among terms.

Misconception 267: Writes the variable before the coefficient in an algebraic term
Expanded misconception:
Fails to follow conventional algebraic notation by incorrectly ordering or representing coefficients and variables in terms.

Misconception 268: Believes lines of reflection must be vertical or horizontal
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that lines of reflection can be at any angle, or incorrectly assumes that the line of reflection must always be parallel or perpendicular to a specific axis, shape, or object.

Misconception 269: When finding the distance between 2 points, believes the distance is the coordinate of the furthest point from origin
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for both the horizontal and vertical displacements between two points when calculating the distance, mistakenly relying on the coordinate of the furthest point from the origin or taking only a single coordinate difference, rather than applying the distance formula that finds the shortest path between the two points using a combination of changes in both the x and y coordinates.

Misconception 270: Thinks the term polygons only applies to shapes with more than 3 sides
Expanded misconception:
Confuses or misinterprets geometric terminology by overgeneralizing or misapplying basic properties, specifically, thinks the term 'polygons' or related terms only applies to shapes with more than 3 sides or higher order characteristics rather than any two-dimensional closed shape with at least three sides.

Misconception 271: Believes triangle numbers are formed differently to the correct way
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the formation or calculation of triangle numbers, such as mistakenly adding consecutive integers from 1 without accounting for the correct cumulative sum, misunderstanding the relationship between triangle numbers and the sequence of natural numbers, or applying an incorrect formula or pattern to generate triangle numbers.

Misconception 272: When finding a missing side of a parallelogram given the area completes an extra step of dividing by 2
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to apply the area formula of a parallelogram correctly by unnecessarily dividing the area by 2 when finding a missing side, assuming the area must be halved, rather than using the formula A = base * height and solving for the unknown side."

Misconception 273: Thinks 1l = 1 cubic centimetre
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that 1 liter is equal to 1 cubic centimeter, failing to recognize that 1 liter is equal to 1000 cubic centimeters (or milliliters) and that the conversion is necessary when solving problems involving volume units.

Misconception 274: Confuses angle of a sector with the radius
Expanded misconception:
"Confuses angle of a sector with the central angle, radius, circumference, or other geometric properties, particularly in contexts involving proportions, measure calculations, or spatial reasoning related to circles and sectors."

Misconception 275: When finding a point on a line, assumes the point is always the midpoint
Expanded misconception:
When finding a point on a line, assumes the point is always the midpoint, or fails to correctly apply midpoint formula or proportional reasoning when determining a specific point's location on a line, often leading to incorrect identification of a point's coordinates or proportion of the line segment.

Misconception 276: Adds rather than multiplies when scaling up in direct proportion problems
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply proportional reasoning in scaling-up problems, instead incorrectly using addition to calculate new values rather than multiplication, leading to linear rather than proportional increase.

Misconception 277: Thinks pressure is force per unit volume
Expanded misconception:
"Does not distinguish between pressure and stress, or incorrectly applies the pressure formula, often resulting in a confusion between pressure (force per unit area) and force per unit volume."

Misconception 278: Believes a power of 2 has no impact on its value
Expanded misconception:
Ignores or underestimates the effect of exponentiation, specifically believing that a power of 2 (or other exponents) does not significantly change the original number's value, leading to incorrect calculations or estimates when performing operations involving powers of 2.

Misconception 279: Mixes up square rooting and cube rooting
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct inverse operation of squaring and cubing, often confusing the procedures for finding square roots and cube roots, including but not limited to selecting the incorrect root type or misinterpreting the degrees of exponents when determining roots.

Misconception 280: Describes lines when asked to describe angles
Expanded misconception:
Describes lines or line segments as the answer when asked to describe or identify angles, possibly due to confusion between the role of lines in forming angles and the angles themselves, or a lack of understanding that angles are distinct geometric entities measured by the amount of rotation between the lines that form them.

Misconception 281: Thinks that if two lines do not intersect on a diagram then they will never intersect and are therefore parallel
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider that lines may intersect outside the visible section of a diagram, such as at a point beyond the portion shown or in a part of the diagram not depicted, mistakenly concluding that non-intersecting lines in the given representation are parallel.

Misconception 282: Does not understand that a point on the line of reflection will stay the same when reflected 
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand that a point on the line of reflection will remain unchanged, and often applies reflection rules incorrectly to points on the line, expecting them to move or change coordinates after a reflection.

Misconception 283: Believes reflection in y = -x switches x and y coordinates
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognize or apply the effect of the negative sign in the equation y = -x, specifically expecting a simple swapping of the x and y coordinates instead of reflecting the point across the line y = -x, resulting in a corresponding change in the sign of the new coordinate that is opposite in sign to the original.

Misconception 284: Believes the gradient of a speed-time graph is the speed
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the gradient of a speed-time graph with the speed itself, instead of recognizing it as the acceleration.

Misconception 285: Lines up the digits incorrectly when doing an addition involving two numbers with a different amount of digits
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the place value alignment or properly adjust the positioning of the digits when performing addition with numbers of varying digit lengths, often resulting in the incorrect placement or handling of the place values, digits, or resulting sums.

Misconception 286: when subtracting a fraction from an integer, just adds their absolute value and places a negative sign in front
Expanded misconception:
When subtracting a fraction from an integer, incorrectly handles the operation by either: adding their absolute values and placing a negative sign in front, ignoring the integer and only considering the sign of the fraction, or only subtracting the whole number part of the fraction while ignoring the fractional part.

Misconception 287: Thinks y = ? is a vertical line through ?
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that the equation y = [constant value] represents a vertical line through a point identified by the constant value on the y-axis, failing to consider that the actual line extends infinitely in both the positive and negative x-directions.

Misconception 288: Does not break down all factors in a factor tree to primes
Expanded misconception:
Fails to factor or fully factor expressions, often stopping at composite factors or failing to break down all factors in a factor tree to primes, which can lead to incomplete or incorrect simplification, cancellation, or solution.

Misconception 289: Writes the numerator and the denominator the wrong way round in a probability
Expanded misconception:
Writes the numerator and the denominator the wrong way round in a probability, often due to a misunderstanding of the specific event/outcome being measured and the total possible outcomes, or incorrectly copying the fraction from the problem context or previous calculation.

Misconception 290: Thinks a decreasing sequence cannot be quadratic
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that quadratic functions can exhibit decreasing sequences, and instead associates decreasing sequences only with linear or exponential functions with negative growth rates.

Misconception 291: Thinks a negative y value in a column vector means to move up rather than down
Expanded misconception:
Thinks the sign of a vector component in a specific direction (e.g., y-axis) determines the opposite direction of intended movement (e.g., misconstruing a negative y value to move up or a positive y value to move down, or similarly for x-axis, z-axis, etc., in higher dimensions), rather than correctly moving in the direction indicated by the sign in a coordinate system.

Misconception 292: Converts fraction to percentage by putting % sign after the denominator
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply proper conversion rules when converting between fractions and percentages, often by merely appending a percentage sign or incorrectly rearranging the fraction.

Misconception 293: Does not understand that similar shapes sides must all have the same scale factor
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand that similar shapes must have proportional sides, and all corresponding sides must have the same scale factor, often assuming that a partial proportionality or selective scaling is sufficient for similarity.

Misconception 294: Thinks that only the sides need to be equal for a polygon to be regular
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that only the side lengths need to be equal for a polygon to be regular, neglecting the equal angle measure requirement in a regular polygon.

Misconception 295: Does not know the word and/or does not know the function of a compass
Expanded misconception:
Does not know the definition of/relation between the concept (word) and/or lacks understanding of the tool's function (compass) or operation, leading to difficulty applying the concept or tool in mathematical problems.

Misconception 296: Ordered fractions by comparing denominators rather than using equivalent fractions.
Expanded misconception:
Ordered fractions incorrectly by comparing denominators rather than using equivalent fractions, such as incorrectly applying ascending/descending order rules due to misunderstanding of fraction equivalency or omission of necessary steps to obtain a common denominator or comparator.

Misconception 297: Forgets you need a compass to draw arcs
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or apply the use of required specialized tools, such as compasses or protractors, when performing specific geometric constructions or calculations, resulting in incomplete or inaccurate solutions.

Misconception 298: Confuses a constant with a variable
Expanded misconception:
"Confuses a constant with a variable, neglecting to apply mathematical operations correctly to the constant or incorrectly treating a numerical value as a variable that can take on different values."

Misconception 299: When solving time problems, finds the difference in the hours and the difference in the minutes but then assumes the minutes will be added on to the number of hours
Expanded misconception:
When solving time problems, incorrectly combines the hour and minute components by finding the difference in the hours and the difference in the minutes, then incorrectly adds the difference in minutes as a separate unit to the hours, rather than converting the minutes to hours or considering them as a separate quantity.

Misconception 300: Believes a direct proportion graph does not have to start at the origin
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that a direct proportion graph must pass through the origin, resulting in the misconception that a line representing direct proportion can have a non-zero y-intercept or start at any point other than (0,0).

Misconception 301: Thinks you can multiply by a decimal to find a multiple of a number
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that multiplication and division operations are interchangeable, or mistakenly uses multiplication by a decimal or fraction to find a multiple or part of a number, rather than adding or subtracting.

Misconception 302: Assumed each part of a fraction diagram is worth 10%
Expanded misconception:
Assumes equal parts of a fraction diagram or shape are always divided into a convenient number of equal parts, such as 10 equal parts, without considering the actual number of parts or percentage value each part represents.

Misconception 303: Believes that when eliminating a variable, if the signs of the terms with matching coefficients are the same, we do the operation inverse to the sign (- if positive, + if negative)
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly applies the concept of eliminating a variable by inverting the operation of like terms, specifically confusing the effect of the sign on the operation, mistakenly using addition when the same-sign terms with matching coefficients require subtraction to eliminate the variable, or vice versa.

Misconception 304: Believes knowing the perimeter of squares does not give you enough information to find the perimeter of combines shapes using those squares
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply known geometric properties and relationships, such as the perimeter of a square, to more complex shapes composed of those squares in various arrangements, often assuming that the properties of individual components cannot be generalized or combined to solve larger problems.

Misconception 305: Does not know the formula for area of a trapezium
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand or recall the formula for the area of a trapezium, or incorrectly applies other area formulas (e.g., triangle, rectangle, or parallelogram) to trapezium problems.

Misconception 306: Added rather than multiplied by a scale factor
Expanded misconception:
Added a quantity rather than scaling or multiplying it by a required factor, unit conversion rate, or percentage, often failing to distinguish between addition and multiplication in scaling contexts.

Misconception 307: When using Pythagoras to find the height of an isosceles triangle, uses the whole base instead of half
Expanded misconception:
When using Pythagoras to find the height of an isosceles triangle, incorrectly applies the theorem by using the full base length instead of half the base length in calculations, failing to account for the triangle being split into two right-angled triangles.

Misconception 308: Believes that dividing by 100 gives 10%
Expanded misconception:
"Confuses percentage calculations, incorrectly assuming that dividing by 100 indicates 'of the whole', resulting in incorrect identification of percentages, ratios or proportions in various mathematical contexts."

Misconception 309: Believes angles on a straight line add to 90 degrees
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to recognize that angles on a straight line add up to 180 degrees, not 90 degrees, and incorrectly applies this understanding when solving angle-related problems, even in contexts where angles form a straight line but not necessarily a right angle."

Misconception 310: Guesses when something is "twice as high/much", rather than multiplying by 2
Expanded misconception:
Guesses or exhibits limited understanding of proportional relationships and multiplication factors, resulting in incorrect application of comparative language such as "double," "twice as high/much," or other multiplier descriptions, leading to an inaccurate quantitative response.

Misconception 311: Believes gradient is the sum of y the coordinates divided by the sum of the x co-ordinates 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct formula of slope (gradient) calculation, incorrectly assuming that it is the sum of y-coordinates divided by the sum of x-coordinates, rather than the change in y divided by the change in x between two points.

Misconception 312: In ratio question where the difference between the sides is given, uses that as the total for one side instead
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between part and whole quantities in proportion or ratio problems, leading to errors such as using the difference between two quantities as the total amount of one quantity instead of understanding its proportional relationship.

Misconception 313: Believes edges of a 3D shape do not include edges of base
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider or account for edges of the base when calculating or analyzing the total edges of a three-dimensional shape, often resulting in an incorrect count by only considering the lateral edges.

Misconception 314: When calculating the power of a negative number, thinks they can ignore the sign when multiplying and then put a negative sign back on at the end, regardless of the power 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the rules of exponents for negative bases consistently, attempting to simplify by either ignoring the negative sign while evaluating the product and incorrectly adding the sign back based on their own rules or not fully considering the properties of even and odd exponents when determining the sign of the result.

Misconception 315: Has not realised that the answer may be changed by the insertion of brackets
Expanded misconception:
Ignores or misapplies the order of operations, specifically failing to recognize the impact of introducing or omitting brackets in mathematical expressions, which can alter the correct order of calculation and thus change the final answer.

Misconception 316: Mixes up primary and secondary data types
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to accurately distinguish and apply primary data types (direct measurement or observation) versus secondary data types (derived from primary data through calculations, transformations, or inferences), potentially leading to incorrect interpretation or manipulation of data."

Misconception 317: When multiplying fractions, adds the numerators and multiplies the denominators
Expanded misconception:
When multiplying fractions, incorrectly applies the rule for adding fractions by adding the numerators and multiplying the denominators, instead of multiplying the numerators and multiplying the denominators.

Misconception 318: Assumes that converting a decimal percentage to a fraction, they can just convert the decimal to a fraction, instead of writing the decimal over 100.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that converting a decimal percentage to a fraction requires expressing the decimal over 100, instead simply converting the decimal part to a fraction, neglecting the original percentage context.

Misconception 319: When factorising, thinks the smallest coefficient will be the numerical factor, regardless of whether it divides into all terms
Expanded misconception:
When factorising expressions, believes that the numerical factor must be the smallest coefficient present, rather than identifying and selecting the greatest common factor that divides all terms, resulting in incorrect factorization.

Misconception 320: When factorising into double brackets, finds the two values which sum to give the constant rather than the coefficient of x
Expanded misconception:
When factorising quadratic expressions into double brackets, incorrectly identifies the two values that sum to give the constant term rather than using those that sum to give the coefficient of the linear term to form the middle term.

Misconception 321: Believes class width multiplied by frequency gives frequency density
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the relationship between class width, frequency, and frequency density, mistakenly believing that frequency density can be found by simply multiplying the class width by the frequency, rather than dividing the frequency by the class width.

Misconception 322: Believes that rounding must change the number
Expanded misconception:
Believes that rounding a number always results in a change to the original value, regardless of the value and the desired level of precision, and does not account for instances where rounding leaves the number unchanged.

Misconception 323: When measuring a line segment with a ruler, assumes the line starts at 0
Expanded misconception:
When measuring a line segment with a ruler, assumes the line starts at 0 or an incorrect unit starting point, rather than aligning it with one of the units marked on the ruler or considering the starting point on the line segment itself.

Misconception 324: Believes the two variables have to add to the same value in inverse proportion
Expanded misconception:
Believes the two variables in an inverse proportion relationship have to add or multiply to the same constant value, or incorrectly assumes a direct relationship between inverse proportion variables, resulting in incorrect calculations involving variable relationships and equation manipulation.

Misconception 325: Does not check the labelling of the axes 
Expanded misconception:
Does not check or understand the labelling of the axes, potentially leading to incorrect interpretation or application of data, including misidentifying the scales, units, or variables represented on each axis.

Misconception 326: When converting fractions to ratios, puts the other side of the ratio as the total of the numerator and denominator
Expanded misconception:
When converting fractions to ratios, incorrectly assumes that the other side of the ratio must be the sum of the numerator and denominator, rather than being the denominator itself or another value in a proportional relationship.

Misconception 327: Mixes up total and part in ratio
Expanded misconception:
"Does not distinguish between parts of a ratio and the whole, or incorrectly applies part-to-whole or part-to-part ratio relationships when solving problems."

Misconception 328: Performs addition ahead of multiplication
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the order of operations (PEMDAS/BODMAS), specifically performs addition and/or subtraction ahead of multiplication and/or division, disregarding the conventional mathematical priority order.

Misconception 329: Does not understand a percentage is out of 100
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand that a percentage represents a proportion out of a hundred, and thus may incorrectly interpret or calculate percentages in relation to the total or parts of a whole, often treating percentages as isolated values or misapplying proportions in calculations.

Misconception 330: When working with inverse and direct proportion, forgets to apply the root or index to find k, but remembers when doing the second calculation
Expanded misconception:
Forgets to apply or incorrectly applies the required operation or exponents (such as roots or indices) when finding the constant of proportionality (k) in problems involving inverse or direct proportion, but correctly applies these operations during subsequent calculations.

Misconception 331: Does not understand the term factor
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the mathematical concept of factor, specifically how a whole number can be expressed as a product of its factors, or fails to identify the correct number of factors, or incorrectly assumes that a factor is always prime.

Misconception 332: Confuses reciprocal and quadratic graphs
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize key differences between reciprocal and quadratic graphs, specifically confusing parabolic shapes with hyperbolas or vice versa, and/or misidentifying axis of symmetry, asymptotes, or key features, such as vertex, x-intercepts, or turning points.

Misconception 333: Not considered the y value when reading coordinates
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly interpret coordinate points by not considering the corresponding y-value or misidentifying the y-value when reading coordinates from the coordinate plane, often resulting in incorrect identification of x or y coordinates.

Misconception 334: When solving an equation with x in the denominator, thinks the inverse operation is to make it the numerator on the other side
Expanded misconception:
When solving an equation with a variable in the denominator, incorrectly applies inverse operations and inappropriately moves a variable from the denominator to the numerator on the other side, rather than multiplying both sides by the denominator or applying a suitable algebraic technique.

Misconception 335: Believes 30% is equivalent to 1/3
Expanded misconception:
Believes certain percentages (typically those ending in 0 or 5) are equivalent to simple fractions (e.g., 30% equals 1/3, 25% equals 1/4, or 50% equals 1/2) without converting or calculating accurately.

Misconception 336: Does not understand that having values after the decimal point makes a number larger than a number with the same units but no decimal places
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand that having values after the decimal point represents a fractional part, and therefore, may incorrectly assume that a number with decimal places is equal to or smaller than a number with the same units but no decimal places, when in fact the number with decimal places may be larger.

Misconception 337: Believes the solution of mx + c = a is x =a
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for the coefficient or constant term in simple linear equations, assuming a direct relationship of equality between the input and output variable, incorrectly concluding that x must be equal to a, without considering the effects of m or c in the equation mx + c = a.

Misconception 338: Believes only straight lines count as edges on 3D shapes
Expanded misconception:
Believes only straight lines or visible segments count as edges on complex or partially occluded 3D shapes, failing to account for hidden or implied edges.

Misconception 339: When dividing, ignores any remainders
Expanded misconception:
When dividing, ignores or incorrectly handles any remainders, assuming the integer result is the final solution or incorrectly rounding down to the nearest whole number.

Misconception 340: Does not understand that when multiplying both sides of an equation by an amount every term must be multiplied by the same amount
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the distributive property of multiplication over addition when applying operations to both sides of an equation, failing to multiply every term by the same amount and often neglecting to multiply constants or terms with variables correctly.

Misconception 341: Believes the second term in a 2 term quadratic equation is always a coefficient and the variable
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that the second term in a two-term quadratic equation can be a constant, and incorrectly assumes the presence of a variable, often mistakenly treating the coefficient of the first term as part of the second term or incorrectly handling negative coefficients.

Misconception 342: Uses the whole base rather than half the base when forming a right angled triangle from an isosceles triangle
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the Sliding Square or Half-Base property correctly when forming a right-angled triangle from an isosceles triangle, resulting in an incorrect side length, altitude, or other dimension.

Misconception 343: Estimates the frequency shown on a pie chart rather than calculating
Expanded misconception:
Estimates the frequency shown on a circle or pie chart rather than accurately calculating the data using the given information, proportions, or percentages.

Misconception 344: Does not understand that a probability of 0 represents something that is impossible
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand that a probability of 0 represents an impossible event and a probability of 1 represents a certain event, and may have difficulty interpreting probabilities at these extremes.

Misconception 345: Has treated this like an addition problem and added the ones column.
Expanded misconception:
Has inappropriately applied a basic addition strategy to a multi-step problem, possibly ignoring or not fully understanding the concept of operations precedence and order of operations.

Misconception 346: Does not understand how to multiply algebraic terms
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand how to multiply algebraic terms, fails to apply the distributive property of multiplication over addition for non-numeric coefficients, or neglects to combine like terms during algebraic manipulations.

Misconception 347: When solving an equation with a fraction, thinks you don't need to multiply every term on both sides when multiplying by the denominator 
Expanded misconception:
When solving an equation involving fractions, fails to recognize the need to distribute the multiplication by the denominator to every term on both sides of the equation, often neglecting to multiply the constants or other terms, resulting in incorrect simplification.

Misconception 348: Writes times in the afternoon as if 10 is midday on a 24 hour clock
Expanded misconception:
Misconstrues time representation by incorrectly assuming that 12-hour or 24-hour clock systems start at a different reference point, such as counting 10 as midday or mistaking morning hours for afternoon hours, leading to incorrect conversion or ordering of time values on both analog and digital clocks.

Misconception 349: When adding surds, just multiplies the numbers under the surd rather than first trying to simplifying to find like surds
Expanded misconception:
Fails to simplify surds to like terms by multiplying the numbers under the radical sign without rationalizing the denominator or combining like terms, resulting in incorrect combination of unlike surds or incorrect expansion of surd expressions.

Misconception 350: Forgets to carry the tens when doing column multiplication
Expanded misconception:
Forgets to apply place value for partial products or intermediate results when using multi-digit numbers, such as carrying or regrouping, often resulting in errors when performing multi-digit addition, subtraction, or multiplication operations that involve multiple place values.

Misconception 351: Thinks a chord does not have to start and end at the circumference of the circle.
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that a chord does not have to start and end at the circumference of the circle, perhaps believing that it is sufficient for the chord to be within the circle or to intersect the circle at only one point, or that the endpoints of the chord do not have to be points on the circle's circumference itself, but rather anywhere along the circle or on an intersecting line.

Misconception 352: Believes the fx column for calculating mean is value - frequency
Expanded misconception:
"Misunderstands or misapplies column calculations, specifically believing the formula for calculating mean from a frequency table is the product of 'values' and 'frequency' rather than sum-of-products of 'values' and 'frequencies'."

Misconception 353: Does not recognise when one part of a fraction is the negative of the other
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the concept of opposites in fractions and decimals, which would simplify or cancel out terms, resulting in an incorrect solution, such as adding or subtracting the components as if they were separate numbers rather than complementary parts.

Misconception 354: Believes class width represents frequency in a histogram
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the relationship between class width, frequency, and area in a histogram, leading to incorrect interpretations where they mistakenly believe class width directly represents the number of occurrences (frequency) rather than the number of occurrences being represented by the height or area of the bar.

Misconception 355: Thinks the interior angle of a regular polygon is 10n
Expanded misconception:
Thinks the interior angle of a regular polygon is 10n (typically indicating a misunderstanding of the relationship between the number of sides 'n' in a polygon and the correct formula for the interior angle, which is usually 180(n-2)/n, resulting in an overly simplistic calculation).

Misconception 356: Finds the sum of the missing angles but not the angle being asked
Expanded misconception:
Finds the sum of the missing angles or related angles in a geometric figure, but fails to correctly identify or calculate the specific angle being asked for in the problem, often due to a misunderstanding of the question or a lack of attention to the context provided.

Misconception 357: Does not understand that in the ratio 1:n the total number of parts would be 1+n 
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand that in a ratio a:b, the total number of parts is often required for proportion calculations and should be calculated as a+b or does not recognize that the total number of parts in a ratio is essential to make accurate comparisons, convert ratios to fractions, or find equivalent ratios.

Misconception 358: Believes digits of a number are read/written from right to left
Expanded misconception:
Believes the place value or digits of a number are read and/or written in reverse order, either from right to left, incorrectly interpreting or manipulating digits within a number, such as reversing the tens and units place, or misinterpreting the order of digits in multi-digit numbers.

Misconception 359: Confuses random and stratified sampling methods
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the randomness criterion in random sampling with the stratification criterion in stratified sampling, leading to an inability to distinguish between the two sampling methods, resulting in incorrect application of sampling techniques to real-world data collection scenarios.

Misconception 360: Gives frequency when asked for relative frequency
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between frequency and relative frequency, and provides the actual count of occurrences instead of a proportion of the total when asked for relative frequency.

Misconception 361: Believes that repeated prime factors are only counted once
Expanded misconception:
Believes that repeated prime factors are only counted once, neglecting to raise each prime factor to its respective power when simplifying expressions, solving equations, or performing calculations involving prime factorization.

Misconception 362: Believes f(x) - a translates the function right a units
Expanded misconception:
Believes that subtraction of a constant within a function's input translates the function in the wrong direction, i.e., mistakenly thinking f(x) - a translates the function right a units instead of left a units.

Misconception 363: Only applies a division to one of multiple terms in a numerator when simplifying an algebraic fraction
Expanded misconception:
Only applies operation to one of multiple terms in a numerator when simplifying an algebraic fraction, failing to apply distributive property or order of operations to all terms.

Misconception 364: Thinks they just remove the decimal point when converting a decimal to a percentage
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the concept of multiplying by 100 when converting a decimal to a percentage, thinking it is sufficient to simply remove the decimal point without scaling the value appropriately.

Misconception 365: Thinks that we cannot calculate the range if values are repeated
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that repeated values can be taken into account or excluded when calculating a range or uses an incorrect method for dealing with repeated values when determining the minimum or maximum in order to calculate the range.

Misconception 366: Does not know the meaning of the identity symbol
Expanded misconception:
Does not properly apply or interpret the identity property (eg. identity symbol) of addition (0) or multiplication (1), or confuses it with other arithmetic properties.

Misconception 367: Does not know that co-interior angles must be in parallel lines
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand that co-interior angles are only formed when a transversal intersects two or more parallel lines are supplementary, and therefore incorrectly applies angle properties or assumes angle relationships in other geometric configurations.

Misconception 368: Thinks a kite has one pair of parallel sides
Expanded misconception:
Misconceives a kite as a general quadrilateral with one pair of parallel sides rather than a specific quadrilateral with two pairs of adjacent sides that are equal in length and two pairs of sides where one pair is longer than the other pair.

Misconception 369: Forgets to add starting value when calculating new coordinates
Expanded misconception:
Ignores initial or starting value in calculations or transformations, often in problems involving coordinates, measurements, or multi-step calculations with carryover values.

Misconception 370: When solving simultaneous equations, believes the terms must have the same sign in order to be eliminated.
Expanded misconception:
When solving simultaneous equations, believes that terms of the same variable must have the same coefficient and/or the same sign in order to be eliminated, regardless of the actual operations required for elimination, such as adding, subtracting, or multiplying the equations by appropriate constants.

Misconception 371: When dividing fractions, divides by the reciprocal, instead of multiplies
Expanded misconception:
Does not differentiate between dividing and multiplying fractions, consistently applying the reciprocal in mixed arithmetic operations, i.e., inverts division of fractions to multiplication by the reciprocal, but may also incorrectly inverts multiplication.

Misconception 372: In ratio question where the difference between the sides is given, uses that as the total
Expanded misconception:
In ratio or proportion questions where the difference between the two parts or sides of a ratio is given, incorrectly uses the difference as the total amount or as a basis for one part of the ratio, rather than calculating the actual parts or total amount.

Misconception 373: Believes you can add or subtract from inside brackets without expanding when solving an equation
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or misapplies the order of operations, specifically neglecting to expand expressions within parentheses or brackets before performing arithmetic operations outside the brackets, instead treating contents inside the brackets as static values that can be directly added to or subtracted from.

Misconception 374: Thinks a hexagon has 8 sides.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize fundamental properties of basic geometric shapes, specifically incorrectly counting or recalling the number of sides of a hexagon.

Misconception 375: Believes you can only have one mode
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to recognize that a data set can have multiple modes, and incorrectly assumes that there is always only one mode, leading to errors in identifying and working with multimodal distributions."

Misconception 376: Unable to equate a division and multiplication to a single division
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize division as the inverse operation of multiplication, making incorrect conversions and resulting in a wrong order of operations, which consequently leads to calculation errors.

Misconception 377: When asked for the mean of a list of data, gives the mode
Expanded misconception:
When asked for a measure of central tendency (mean, median, mode), incorrectly identifies or relies on the mode (most frequently occurring value) instead of the correct statistical measure.

Misconception 378: When finding the difference between two points on a time series graph, only uses one of the points. 
Expanded misconception:
When finding the difference between two points on a time series graph, only uses one of the points, neglecting to account for the change over the specified time period, and instead may use the value at one point as the answer.

Misconception 379: Finds the y intercept when asked to read the solution to a quadratic equation from a graph
Expanded misconception:
Finds the y-intercept (or incorrectly identifies the y-intercept as the solution) when intended to read and identify the x-intercepts as the solutions to a quadratic equation from a graph.

Misconception 380: Does not understand the first significant value is the first non-zero digit number
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the concept of significant figures, specifically that the first significant value in a number is typically the first non-zero digit, potentially leading to incorrect rounding, truncation, or representation of numbers in calculations.

Misconception 381: When dividing a fraction by an integer, divides both the numerator and denominator by the integer
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the division rule for fractions, specifically when dividing a fraction by an integer, mistakenly dividing both the numerator and denominator by the integer instead of inverting the integer and multiplying.

Misconception 382: Does not know how to combine different symbols in Roman numerals
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the additive and subtractive principles of Roman numerals, and/or may not know how to combine different symbols in Roman numerals by correctly applying the rules for combining letters of different values, such as I, V, X, L, C, D, and M, in various combinations.

Misconception 383: confuses cubic and reciprocal graphs
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize key differences between the graphs of cubic and reciprocal functions, including variations in shape, asymptotes, x-intercepts, and end behavior, or mistakenly applies properties or characteristics of reciprocal functions to cubic functions or vice versa.

Misconception 384: When solving simultaneous equations graphically, they think the solutions are the x-intercepts
Expanded misconception:
"When solving simultaneous equations graphically, they mistakenly think the solutions are the individual x-intercepts of each equation rather than their intersection points, demonstrating a lack of understanding of the simultaneous nature of the equations."

Misconception 385: Thinks the reciprocal of n, 1/n = 0.n
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to understand the concept of reciprocals and may incorrectly equate the reciprocal of a number (1/n) with its decimal representation (e.g., 1/n = 0.n), demonstrating a lack of understanding of the differences between fractions, decimals, and reciprocals."

Misconception 386: Believes that an exterior angle can have a value of 360 degrees
Expanded misconception:
Believes that an exterior angle can have a value of 360 degrees or greater, not realizing that an exterior angle of a polygon is always less than 360 degrees.

Misconception 387: Thinks there are 100 seconds in a minute
Expanded misconception:
Thinks there are 100 seconds in a minute, fails to understand time units, and may incorrectly convert between time units, such as seconds to minutes or minutes to hours, leading to miscalculations in time-based problems.

Misconception 388: When placing a negative number on a number line student assumes numbers with a greater absolute value are placed to the right.
Expanded misconception:
When placing a negative number on a number line, the student assumes numbers with a greater absolute value are placed to the right and struggles to comprehend that actually, the number line moves in the opposite direction from positive to negative, where numbers with greater absolute values indicate a greater distance from zero in the negative direction.

Misconception 389: Combines variables with constants when writing a formula from a given situation
Expanded misconception:
Fails to maintain separation between variables and constants, inappropriately combining, adding, or substituting them when translating a word problem or situation into a mathematical formula or equation.

Misconception 390: Believes you find the mode by calculating the mean
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between measures of central tendency, specifically believes you find the mode by calculating the mean, confusing the concept of mode as the most frequently occurring value in a dataset with the concept of mean as the average value, often resulting in incorrect calculations or misunderstandings of descriptive statistics.

Misconception 391: Mixes up the value of two terms when substituting
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly substitute values or variables by incorrectly swapping or mixing up the numerical or algebraic values of terms, often resulting in a solution that contains one or more incorrect variable or constant values.

Misconception 392: Believes you divide 1 by the number of values, rather than the total, when finding the mean
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the concept of total sum in calculating the mean and incorrectly believes the divisor should be the number of values being averaged, rather than the total sum of values being divided by the quantity of values.

Misconception 393: Believes adding coordinates of endpoint and midpoint of a line will give the other endpoint
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the midpoint formula correctly, believing that adding the coordinates of the endpoint and midpoint of a line will give the coordinates of the other endpoint, instead of understanding that the midpoint coordinates must be doubled and both doubled coordinates then subtracted from the known endpoint coordinates to find the missing endpoint.

Misconception 394: Divides instead of multiplies when multiplying by a decimal
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply multiplication behavior when the multiplier is a decimal or fraction, instead performing the inverse operation by dividing rather than properly adjusting the calculation or converting to an equivalent fraction or whole number.

Misconception 395: Thinks there are 10,000kg in a tonne
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between metric tonnes and US or Imperial tons, specifically thinking a metric tonne equals 10,000 kg instead of 1,000 kg, and/or confusing metric tonnes with US/Imperial tons (approximately 2,000 pounds or 907 kg).

Misconception 396: Does not recognise the height of a trapezium as the perpendicular length between the parallel sides
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between base and height of a trapezium, incorrectly calculates the area by not recognising the height as the perpendicular distance between the parallel sides, which may lead to using another dimension of the shape or its orientation as the height.

Misconception 397: When calculating the range does not reorder the data to find the largest number minus the smallest number
Expanded misconception:
Ignores the necessity of ordering data from smallest to largest (or vice versa) to accurately identify the minimum and maximum values before calculating the range.

Misconception 398: Does not know that we can use the conservation of perimeter to find missing lengths
Expanded misconception:
Does not know or apply the concept that in certain geometric transformations or rearrangements, such as changes in the shape of a polygon, the total perimeter remains constant, and fails to use this principle (conservation of perimeter) to solve problems or find missing lengths by correctly setting up and solving equations based on the equality of perimeters.

Misconception 399: Confuses place value when rounding to estimate
Expanded misconception:
Fails to accurately apply place value understanding when rounding numbers, leading to incorrect estimates or calculations, and possibly displaying a lack of comprehension of the relationship between digits in a number and their corresponding place value.

Misconception 400: Thinks multiplying a variable by a number is the same as raising that variable to the power of that number
Expanded misconception:
Thinks multiplying a variable by a number is the same as raising that variable to the power of that number, implying a lack of understanding of the fundamental difference between multiplication and exponentiation in algebraic expressions.

Misconception 401: When given the equation of a straight line, believes the first term always gives the gradient, even when the first term is actually c
Expanded misconception:
When given the equation of a straight line, believes the coefficient of the variable (usually 'x') always gives the gradient, regardless of the form of the equation (e.g., slope-intercept form y = mx + c, general form ax + by = c), and fails to consider that the equation may be in a non-standard form or rearranged to prioritize other information (e.g., x-intercept or y-intercept), or that c may be the first term when the equation is not in slope-intercept form.

Misconception 402: Does not know the definition of a cone
Expanded misconception:
Does not know or misapplies the definition of a cone, including its properties (vertex, axis, base), differences from other three-dimensional figures (e.g., cylinder, sphere), and calculations related to its characteristics (e.g., surface area, volume), indicating limited understanding of geometric concepts.

Misconception 403: When rationalising the denominator, confuses the numerator with the denominator
Expanded misconception:
"When rationalising the denominator to get rid of a radical in the denominator, mistakenly applies the conjugate or rationalisation process to the numerator instead of, or in addition to, the denominator."

Misconception 404: Uses cos when sin is required
Expanded misconception:
Confuses trigonometric ratios, particularly in problems involving right triangles, using the wrong trigonometric function (e.g., cosine instead of sine or tangent) due to misunderstanding the relationship between the sides and angles of a right triangle.

Misconception 405: Believes the only way to find the interior angle of a regular polygon is to subtract the exterior angle from 180.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply or understands the limitations of a general formula for finding the interior angle of a regular polygon, specifically the concept that the interior angle can also be determined by the number of sides using the formula (n-2) * 180 / n, and believes that the only method for finding an interior angle is to subtract the exterior angle from 180.

Misconception 406: Believes class width multipled by frequency gives the area of the bar in a histogram
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that the height of a bar in a histogram, which represents the frequency or frequency density of a given class interval, multiplied by the class width gives the area, and instead believes that multiplying class width by the actual frequency (without considering frequency density) gives the area of the bar, leading to incorrect interpretation of histogram representations.

Misconception 407: Does not equate y=-x with x=-y
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the equivalence of negative linear equations in different forms, specifically confusing the absolute value of the slope and the direction of the y-intercept when comparing equations of the form y = -x with x = -y, often leading to the incorrect assumption that they are not inverse operations or have different graphical representations.

Misconception 408: When multiplying fractions, multiplies both the numerator and denominator
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly apply the multiplication rule when multiplying fractions, resulting in either multiplying both the numerator and denominator or performing an incorrect operation, such as adding or subtracting the numerators and denominators instead of multiplying them, or multiplying only one part of the fraction and incorrectly incorporating the other part.

Misconception 409: Identifies first decimal place value when asked for first significant figure
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consistently apply rules for determining significant figures, specifically identifying the first non-zero digit in a value (or the first digit after a decimal point if the value is less than 1), and mistakenly focuses solely on identifying the first decimal place value.

Misconception 410: Believes the size of an angle can be given as a word rather than a number
Expanded misconception:
Confuses descriptive terms (e.g. 'right', 'acute', 'obtuse', or 'straight') with numerical angle measurements, suggesting that verbal or qualitative descriptions of angles are sufficient or equivalent to quantitative measures.

Misconception 411: When identifying the center of rotation, chooses a point with the wrong y-value
Expanded misconception:
When identifying the center of rotation in a geometric transformation, chooses a point with the wrong y-value, possibly due to misunderstanding the relationship between the center of rotation and the axis of rotation or incorrectly applying the transformation's effects on specific coordinates.

Misconception 412: Assumes that an enlargement is always from the smaller object to the bigger one
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider that an enlargement or enlargement scale factor can be applied in either direction, incorrectly assuming that an enlargement always starts from a smaller object and results in a larger one, and neglecting the possibility that an enlargement can be in the opposite direction with a scale factor less than one, resulting in a smaller image.

Misconception 413: Believes that n/n = n, instead of 1
Expanded misconception:
Believes that any number divided by itself equals the number itself, rather than understanding the identity property where any non-zero number divided by itself equals one.

Misconception 414: Divides by the order of the root
Expanded misconception:
Divides by the order of the root instead of using it as an exponent or incorrectly applies properties of radicals, or in some cases uses the root's order as a factor in an arithmetic operation.

Misconception 415: Incorrectly identifies angles as vertically opposite
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly identifies angles as vertically opposite, such as assuming that angles formed by intersecting lines are equal, without considering the actual relationship between the angles or the fact that vertically opposite angles are formed by two intersecting lines and have the same measure, leading to incorrect conclusions in geometric problems involving angle relationships.

Misconception 416: Believes the intersection of two complements can be found using the complement of the intersection
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets set operations, specifically believing that finding the intersection of two sets' complements is logically equivalent to finding the complement of the intersection of the original sets, often stemming from a misunderstanding of De Morgan's laws or failure to distinguish between set complements and intersections.

Misconception 417: When reading values from graphs, writes down the wrong units
Expanded misconception:
When reading values from graphs, writes down the wrong units or incorrectly interprets the axis labels, failing to consider scale, label, or unit markings, which may result in misattributing units of measurement, losing scale context, or ignoring axis titles.

Misconception 418: Believes that when factorising a quadratic, they can choose any two numbers that add to give the coefficient of x
Expanded misconception:
Believes that when factorising a quadratic of the form ax^2 + bx + c, they can choose any two numbers that add to give the coefficient of x (b) without considering whether those numbers multiply to give the constant term (c).

Misconception 419: When converting a fraction to a decimal, divides the denominator by the numerator
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply correct conversion process by inverting the fraction and performing division, specifically by consistently dividing the denominator by the numerator instead of the numerator by the denominator.

Misconception 420: When finding bearings, finds the back bearing to what the question is actually asking
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly calculates bearings by finding the back bearing to the direction the question is actually asking, instead of performing the required reciprocal operation to obtain the correct bearing.

Misconception 421: Mixes up odd and even numbers
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or apply properties of odd and even numbers, such as incorrectly identifying the parity of a number resulting from arithmetic operations, ignoring the concept that an even number times an odd or even number yields an even product, or mistakenly assuming that an odd or even result can be obtained through certain operations.

Misconception 422: Thinks x = y is an axis
Expanded misconception:
Thinks x = y, or similar equations, represent an axis in the coordinate plane, rather than a line with multiple points that have an equal x-coordinate value or an equal y-coordinate value.

Misconception 423: Does not know the meaning of equilateral
Expanded misconception:
Does not consistently understand the concept of "equilateral" in geometry, failing to recognize or apply the property that all sides of an equilateral shape are equal in length.

Misconception 424: Adds 3 decimal places rather than moving 3 places
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly shift decimal places when applying the rules of significant figures, instead often simply adding the number of decimal places moved, resulting in incorrect placement or calculation.

Misconception 425: Assumes the third column of a grouped frequency table is always cumulative frequency
Expanded misconception:
Assumes the third column of a grouped frequency table is always cumulative frequency, rather than class or relative frequency.

Misconception 426: Believes corresponding angles sum to 90 degrees
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly applies properties of complementary angles to non-complementary angles, specifically believing that corresponding angles formed by a transversal intersecting two lines always sum to 90 degrees rather than being equal in measure.

Misconception 427: Answers as if there are 100 minutes in an hour when writing minutes as decimals
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct conversion ratio between units of time, such as assuming 100 minutes in an hour instead of 60, leading to errors when converting time from minutes to decimals or fractions.

Misconception 428: When a point is on an axis, gives both coordinates as the value it's at on the axis
Expanded misconception:
When a point lies on an axis, incorrectly gives both coordinates as the value where it intersects the axis, failing to recognize that the other coordinate should be zero.

Misconception 429: When multiplying a surd by an integer, believes the value under the surd can be swapped with the integer
Expanded misconception:
Believes the operation is commutative when a surd is multiplied by an integer, resulting in incorrectly rearranging or swapping the values, and often misunderstands the concept of distributive property in this context, and/or fails to recognize the need to multiply the integer by the value under the surd instead.

Misconception 430: When finding bearings, finds the bearing going in the reverse direction to the one stated
Expanded misconception:
When finding bearings and directions, consistently confuses or fails to account for the reciprocal nature of directions along a line, resulting in the incorrect selection of a bearing that is the reverse direction to the one stated, often by adding or subtracting 180 degrees.

Misconception 431: Finds interior angle rather than given angle
Expanded misconception:
Finds interior angle rather than the specific given angle being asked about, such as mistakenly solving for an angle within a triangle rather than the exterior angle or angle being directly referred to in the question.

Misconception 432: Believes the equation of a straight line is y=-mx-c
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the correct form of the equation of a straight line in slope-intercept form, incorrectly assuming it to be y = -mx - c, rather than y = mx + c, which may indicate a misunderstanding of the role of the slope and the y-intercept.

Misconception 433: Believes a negative power results in a negative answer
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that a negative exponent in an expression indicates the reciprocal of the base raised to the positive exponent, leading to the misconception that the result of a negative power is always negative.

Misconception 434: Believes only angles on parallel lines can be called "corresponding"
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the concept of corresponding angles to non-parallel lines intersected by a transversal, or incorrectly restricts corresponding angle relationships to only parallel lines with a transversal, neglecting the idea that corresponding angles can exist in any multiple angle pairs that have equal measurements due to being in the same relative position but have lines intersecting in some way.

Misconception 435: Finds a scale factor but forgets to multiply the original value in proportion questions
Expanded misconception:
Finds a scale factor or ratio but fails to apply it correctly by not multiplying or dividing the original value in proportion, scaling, or equivalent ratio problems.

Misconception 436: Does not understand when like terms can be collected
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand when like terms can be collected, failing to apply distributive property or combining coefficients of the same variable correctly, resulting in incorrect simplification of algebraic expressions.

Misconception 437: Does not recognise that probabilities of all possible outcomes will sum to 1
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that the sum of probabilities of all possible outcomes in a given scenario must be equal to 1, indicating that there are no other possible outcomes not considered, and misinterprets the probability calculation as a result.

Misconception 438: Believes cubing a number means you multiply it by 3
Expanded misconception:
Believes cubing a number means you multiply it by 3, rather than raising it to the power of 3, demonstrating a misunderstanding of exponentiation or power operations in mathematics.

Misconception 439: Believes gradient = change in y multiplied by change in x
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that gradient is actually the rate of change of a quantity, often represented as the ratio of the change in y (dy) to the change in x (dx), and incorrectly applies a reversed or distorted version of this concept by reversing the ratio or using wrong operations, such as believing gradient equals change in y multiplied by change in x instead of divided by the change in x.

Misconception 440: Thinks n=0 for the first term in a sequence
Expanded misconception:
Thinks the index of a sequence starts from 0 instead of 1, resulting in an off-by-one error when identifying the first term or applying a given term number to calculate or describe the sequence.

Misconception 441: Does not recognise a unit of volume
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognise or apply a unit of volume to a specific context, often confusing it with units of length or mass, or failing to consider unit conversions when solving problems involving measurement or calculation of volume.

Misconception 442: Confuses cumulative frequency and frequency density
Expanded misconception:
Confuses cumulative frequency (the total or running count of values or observations up to a certain point) and frequency density (the number of values or observations per unit of measurement or range), resulting in misunderstandings of data distribution and interpretation of graphical representations.

Misconception 443: Thinks there are 12 hours in 1 day
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands units of time or fails to account for the total number of units in a given time period, specifically confusing or incorrectly applying the concept of hours in a day, possibly assuming a 12-hour clock cycle represents a full 24-hour day.

Misconception 444: Confuses the value outside of a Venn diagram as the total number of entries in the diagram
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between the value outside of a Venn diagram, which often represents the total number of items outside the category (or categories), and the total number of entries within the entire diagram, resulting in incorrect calculations or inferences.

Misconception 445: Counts squares instead of the correct scale when plotting coordinates
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the scale and units when plotting or interpreting coordinates on a grid, resulting in counting individual grid squares rather than using the proportionate units of the coordinate system.

Misconception 446: Repeats the digits twice when squaring a number
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply exponent rules and instead repeats or merely modifies the digits of the base, such as incorrectly doubling or changing the digits when squaring a number rather than computing the actual product of the number multiplied by itself.

Misconception 447: Believes dividing by 0.1 is the same as dividing by 10
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands the effect of dividing by a decimal value, often treating division by a tenth (0.1) or hundredth (0.01) as equivalent to dividing by a whole number ten or hundred, respectively, thus failing to consider the shift in the decimal point.

Misconception 448: Confuses adjacent and opposite sides
Expanded misconception:
Confuses adjacent and opposite sides in geometric shapes, such as polygons and quadrilaterals, or in trigonometric relationships, where they incorrectly apply properties or formulas related to adjacent sides to opposite sides, or vice versa, often leading to calculation errors or incorrect conclusions.

Misconception 449: Does not realise we can scale the perimeter as a whole, rather than finding individual side lengths
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand or apply the concept of proportional scaling to a shape's perimeter when one dimension is multiplied or divided by a factor, often resulting in incorrect calculations of the new perimeter by failing to multiply all side lengths by the scale factor.

Misconception 450: Confuses the terms faces and vertices
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the terms faces and vertices in a three-dimensional shape or geometry context, incorrectly counting or identifying one for the other, such as substituting the number of faces for vertices or vice versa in calculations or descriptions.

Misconception 451: Does not recognise parallel lines angle rules applied inside a shape
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply angle properties of parallel lines intersecting a transversal, including consecutive interior angles, alternate interior angles, corresponding angles, and alternate exterior angles, when solving problems involving angles inside various shapes.

Misconception 452: When a variable in an expression has a power, thinks the power applies to the whole expression
Expanded misconception:
"Incorrectly generalizes exponentiation to the entire expression, failing to understand that the exponent applies only to the specific variable or base being raised to that power."

Misconception 453: Believes qualitative data can be continuous
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between qualitative and quantitative data types, incorrectly assuming that categorical or ordinal variables can represent continuous values or displays a lack of understanding that qualitative data cannot be measured or ranked on a numerical scale, often leading to misplaced application of numerical operations or metrics.

Misconception 454: Thinks measures of area must end in squared
Expanded misconception:
Thinks measures of area must end in squared and fails to recognize that the unit of area can be in different forms (e.g., square units of feet, meters, centimeters) and that 'squared' doesn't necessarily refer to the literal unit suffix.

Misconception 455: Subtracts 1 rather than 2 from the number of sides when finding the sum of interior angles
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly apply the formula for the sum of interior angles of a polygon by incorrectly adjusting the number of sides, typically subtracting 1 rather than 2.

Misconception 456: Believes you can divide the change by 100 to find the percentage change 
Expanded misconception:
Applies incorrect percentage change calculation by dividing by 100 instead of the original value, failing to recognize that percentage change is a comparison to the original amount.

Misconception 457: Does not know how to calculate volume
Expanded misconception:
Does not know how to calculate volume properly, such as not using the correct formula (e.g., length * width * height) or not applying the correct units (e.g., converting between units of length or not cubic units), which can be due to a lack of understanding of the concept of volume, incorrect application of formulas, or failure to perform calculations correctly.

Misconception 458: When finding an input of a function machine thinks you apply the operations given rather than the inverse operation.
Expanded misconception:
When finding an input of a function, mistakenly applies the operation in the forward direction instead of using the inverse operation, such as adding instead of subtracting, multiplying instead of dividing, or performing an exponentiation instead of taking a root.

Misconception 459: confuses circumference and area
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to differentiate between circumference and area of a circle, often applying formulas or concepts applicable to one to calculate or solve problems related to the other."

Misconception 460: Believes the graph of x squared + c has the turning point of (-c, 0)
Expanded misconception:
