Fails to recognize that the vertex of a parabola described by the equation x^2 + c is at (0, c) rather than (-c, 0), indicating a misunderstanding of the relationship between coefficients and the graph's orientation, often mistakenly placing the vertex at the x-intercept or based on incorrect transformation assumptions.

Misconception 461: When finding the median for an odd numbered data set, find the midpoint of two values rather than giving the middle value
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that when finding the median for an odd-numbered data set, the median is the actual middle value itself and does not require averaging, often mistakenly applying the method for finding the median of an even-numbered data set.

Misconception 462: Thinks that they can combine functions in a function machine before applying them to the input
Expanded misconception:
Believes that multiple functions or operations can be combined and performed in any order, without regard to proper order of operations or the concept of nesting, and that this approach will yield the correct output value, thereby demonstrating a lack of understanding of hierarchical or step-by-step sequential function applications.

Misconception 463: Reads the complement of what is required from a pie chart
Expanded misconception:
Reads the complement of what is required from a pie chart or misinterprets the direction or focus of a question presented with a pie chart, such as mistakenly calculating the percentage of the part not directly asked.

Misconception 464: Believes that you are unable to calculate the sum of the interior angles of an irregular polygon given the number of sides
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply or recognize the validity of the formula for calculating the sum of interior angles of polygons, (n-2) * 180 degrees, where 'n' is the number of sides, particularly in the context of irregular polygons, indicating a lack of understanding of polygon properties or difficulty in transferring knowledge to non-standard cases.

Misconception 465: Does not know the diagonals of a square bisect at right angles
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to recognize and apply geometric properties of squares, specifically that the diagonals intersect at right angles and bisect each other, often resulting in miscalculations involving squares and right-angled triangles formed by diagonal splits."

Misconception 466: When calculating with numbers in standard form, just applies the operation to the number and the power separately
Expanded misconception:
When calculating with numbers in standard form, applies the operation to the number and the power separately without considering the correct order of operations and the rules for combining and manipulating exponents, resulting in incorrect calculations and misunderstandings of exponential notation and its properties.

Misconception 467: Treats zero as a ten when subtracting
Expanded misconception:
Fails to fully understand place value concepts and treats zero as a non-significant placeholder or incorrectly adjusts regrouping when subtracting, often leading to an incorrect calculation or incomplete regrouping process.

Misconception 468: Believes you find the frequency for a pie chart sector by calculating its angle divided by the total frequency
Expanded misconception:
"Believes that you find the frequency for a pie chart sector by directly calculating its angle divided by the total frequency, rather than the proportion of the angle divided by 360 degrees, or the proportion of the area to find the corresponding data point or value."

Misconception 469: Believes the position of a sequence cannot be an integer
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that a sequence can be defined by integer positions, often due to a lack of understanding of the one-to-one correspondence between sequence terms and their integer indices.

Misconception 470: Cannot reflect shape when line of symmetry is diagonal 
Expanded misconception:
Struggles to identify and apply line of symmetry, particularly when it is diagonal or not aligned with the axes, and may have difficulties visualizing or reflecting shapes across non-horizontal or non-vertical axes.

Misconception 471: Believes that the solution to ax + b = 0 is x = -a/b
Expanded misconception:
Believes that the solution to ax + b = 0 is x = -a/b, and incorrectly applies this rule to equations with a different sign, incorrectly dividing or failing to consider coefficients of x other than 'a', or incorrectly generalizing it to quadratic equations or more complex expressions, and failing to recognize that this formula only applies to linear equations where a, b, and x are real numbers.

Misconception 472: Multiplies the value as if the original recipe was for 1 person
Expanded misconception:
"Ignores the change in the number of people the recipe is intended for, and multiplies or divides ingredients incorrectly when scaling up or down a recipe, treating the original quantities as if they were for a single serving."

Misconception 473: Underestimates the impact of the size of images in a misleading statistical diagram
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for the effect of unequal image sizes or scaling in misleading statistical diagrams, leading to incorrect interpretations of the data being represented.

Misconception 474: Thinks 1l = 100 cubic centimetres
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the volume of one liter with the volume of one milliliter or does not correctly apply the relationship between one liter (L) and cubic centimeters (cm³) or milliliters (mL), where 1L is equal to 1000 cubic centimeters (cm³) or milliliters (mL), not 100 cubic centimeters (cm³) or milliliters (mL).

Misconception 475: Believes the total of a part whole model is always 1 (or equivalent) instead of the given total
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the explicitly stated total or the context-specific size of the part-whole model, incorrectly assuming a default total or denominator (e.g., assuming 1 whole for fractions or 12 months for yearly quantities), leading to miscalculations or inaccurate representations of proportional relationships.

Misconception 476: Thinks Angle-Angle-Angle triangles are congruent
Expanded misconception:
Thinks Angle-Angle-Angle triangles are congruent, rather than recognizing that Angle-Angle similarity criterion and Angle-Side-Angle or Side-Angle-Side theorems are required to prove triangle congruence.

Misconception 477: Believes when multiplying fractions you cross multiply top left and bottom right for numerator, and the other diagonal for denominator
Expanded misconception:
Applies incorrect pattern of cross-multiplication in fraction multiplication, resulting in the numerator as the product of the top-left and bottom-right values, while mistakenly using the product of the other diagonal for the denominator.

Misconception 478: Does not know what a square number is
Expanded misconception:
"Lacks understanding of perfect squares or their properties, or has difficulty identifying numbers that are the result of multiplying an integer by itself."

Misconception 479: Thinks a divide and a negative sign next to each other makes a plus
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that a negative sign and a division sign do not cancel each other out, incorrectly interpreting division by a negative number as addition.

Misconception 480: Believes that angles that look like they are a right-angle are always 90 degrees
Expanded misconception:
Believes that angles that look like they are a right-angle or appear to form a 90-degree angle due to visual appearance, incomplete drawings, or proportions are always exactly 90 degrees, without verifying the angle measures or other relevant characteristics.

Misconception 481: Thinks the inverse of subtraction is multiplication
Expanded misconception:
Thinks the inverse of subtraction is multiplication, rather than addition, often resulting in incorrect handling of negative numbers, errors in equivalent expressions, or incorrect application of inverse operations in algebraic manipulation.

Misconception 482: When simplifying surds, does not understand that the simplified root is what is left after dividing out the largest square number
Expanded misconception:
Fails to simplify surds by not identifying and dividing out the largest perfect square factor, leading to an incorrect simplification of the root.

Misconception 483: Thinks that the square root of an expression square roots each term in the expression, rather than square rooting the whole expression
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that the square root of an expression square roots each term in the expression individually and independently, rather than applying the square root operation to the entire expression as a whole, often resulting in incorrect separation of terms or distribution of the square root operation.

Misconception 484: Mistakes cube root for cubing the number
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between inverse operations involving exponents, specifically mistaking the process of taking the cube root of a number (reversing the operation of cubing) for cubing the number itself or vice versa.

Misconception 485: Believes 1 is not a factor of a number
Expanded misconception:
Believes that the concept of factors only applies to composite numbers and that 1 is not a valid factor of any number, failing to recognize that 1 is a universal factor that divides all numbers without leaving a remainder.

Misconception 486: Does not square the conversion factor when converting squared units
Expanded misconception:
Ignores or inaccurately applies exponent rules when converting between units, particularly with squared or higher-order quantities.

Misconception 487: Believes a composite function multiplies the functions together
Expanded misconception:
Believes a composite function multiplies the functions together, rather than applying the inner function first and using the result as the input to the outer function, indicating a lack of understanding of function composition order of operations.

Misconception 488: Does not correctly apply the distributive property of multiplication
Expanded misconception:
Applies multiplication operation incorrectly, failing to correctly distribute a single term across the terms of an expression or not recognizing when the distributive property of multiplication is applicable.

Misconception 489: Believes speed = time/distance
Expanded misconception:
Misapplies formula by assuming speed is calculated as time divided by distance (when in fact speed is typically calculated as distance divided by time), often resulting from conflating formula components or misunderstanding unit relationships.

Misconception 490: Believes that when constructing triangles, all sides should be measured from the same point
Expanded misconception:
Believes that when constructing triangles or geometric shapes, all sides or measurements should be measured from the same reference point or that lengths or angles should be applied in an absolute or uniform manner, without considering the unique properties or relationships inherent to the specific shape or figures involved.

Misconception 491: Does not know how to calculate the mean
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct calculation for mean by not summing all values or not dividing by the total number of values, often incorrectly using addition or division operations, or incorrectly ordering the steps involved in calculating the mean.

Misconception 492: Believes the graph of x squared + c has the turning point of (c, 0)
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that a quadratic equation of the form x^2 + c represents a vertical translation of the graph of x^2, and incorrectly identifies the turning point as (c, 0) instead of (0, c), revealing a lack of understanding of vertex form and its relation to transformations.

Misconception 493: Thinks you add the number of wholes to the numerator when converting a mixed number to an improper fraction.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct conversion process when converting mixed numbers to improper fractions, specifically incorrectly adding the denominator to the numerator or adding the number of wholes to the numerator, rather than multiplying the denominator by the whole number and then adding the numerator.

Misconception 494: When comparing data, only compares one category of data rather than comparing the totals
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the entirety or total value of data when making comparisons, instead only focusing on a single category, subset, or attribute, leading to incomplete or inaccurate conclusions.

Misconception 495: Thinks converting an integer to a fraction means both the numerator and denominator are equal
Expanded misconception:
Thinks converting an integer to a fraction means both the numerator and denominator are equal, and/or does not understand that when converting an integer to a fraction the denominator can vary (e.g., 4 equals 4/1, 4/2 is incorrect, but 4/2 = 2) resulting in incorrect equivalent ratios.

Misconception 496: When subtracting decimals with a different number of decimals, subtracts one digit from more than one column
Expanded misconception:
When subtracting decimals with a different number of decimals, fails to align corresponding digits correctly and incorrectly subtracts or misaligns digits, subtracting one digit from more than one column or incorrectly placing the decimal point in the result.

Misconception 497: Used the digits of the decimal as the denominator
Expanded misconception:
Misinterpreted decimals by using the digits of the decimal as the denominator, rather than as a fractional representation, likely due to a lack of understanding of place value or improper handling of decimal operations.

Misconception 498: Does not understand that in a ratio 1:n that one part of the ratio would be n times bigger than the one part
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand that in a ratio 1:n, one part of the ratio is n times larger than the other, and as a result, frequently misinterprets the proportional relationship and the relative size of the respective quantities.

Misconception 499: Does not realise ratio in worded problem can be simplified
Expanded misconception:
Fails to simplify ratios or proportions in worded problems, resulting in an incorrect interpretation or calculation of quantities, proportions, or relationships.

Misconception 500: Finds the shaded section rather than the specified section
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets the figure or diagram by focusing on an unintended or irrelevant area, such as finding the area or perimeter of a shaded region or a specific section rather than the one specified in the question.

Misconception 501: Uses tan when cos is required
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the trigonometric function needed for a given calculation or incorrectly substitutes one basic trigonometric function for another when they have distinct domains or ranges, such as using tangent when cosine is required, or sine when the secant function is needed.

Misconception 502: Believes multiplying a number by its square root gives the square root
Expanded misconception:
Inappropriately applies the concept of square roots when multiplying a number by its square root, mistakenly believing the result will yield the square root rather than the square of the number, often due to misunderstandings of inverse operations and the properties of radical numbers.

Misconception 503: Ignores partial symbols on pictograms
Expanded misconception:
Fails to interpret and apply relevant information from diagrams, charts, or pictograms by neglecting or misinterpreting specific components, labels, or partial symbols, leading to incorrect conclusions or calculations.

Misconception 504: Given the mean and the amount of numbers, adds to find the total
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct formula for finding a total when given the mean, often incorrectly adding the given mean to the number of values or using an incorrect formula to find the total (e.g., total = mean x (number of values - 1) instead of total = mean x number of values), thus demonstrating a lack of understanding of the relationship between mean, total, and the quantity of numbers.

Misconception 505: Believes finding the product of two numbers gives their lowest common multiple
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between the product of two numbers and the greatest or lowest common multiple, mistakenly applying properties of multiplication to find common multiples, or alternatively, incorrectly applying algorithms for finding the lowest common multiple to multiplication problems.

Misconception 506: Believes that multiplying variables is the same as adding them together
Expanded misconception:
Believes that multiplying variables is the same as adding them together, often by incorrectly applying the commutative property of addition to multiplication or ignoring the difference in operations symbols when variables or factors are involved.

Misconception 507: Believes the total frequency is the overall total in a grouped table
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to account for grouped categories and overcounts; mistakenly treats the frequency of a specific category or total of a subgroup as the overall total, rather than summing frequencies across all categories or subgroups in the table."

Misconception 508: Thinks there are 1000 seconds in an hour
Expanded misconception:
Thinks there is a certain number of seconds, minutes, or hours in the given time interval or conversion, often miscounting or miscalculating the association between time units (e.g., incorrectly thinking years have 13 or 14 months), or not accounting for conversion factors between these units.

Misconception 509: Believes speed is distance multiplied by time
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or apply the fundamental relationship between speed (rate of movement), distance, and time, specifically, incorrectly believing that speed is equivalent to distance multiplied by time instead of being aware that speed is actually calculated as distance divided by time.

Misconception 510: Believes that a positive x squared coefficient in a quadratic equation results in an n shaped graph
Expanded misconception:
Believes that the sign of the quadratic coefficient (x² term) determines the direction of the parabola's opening, but fails to consider other factors, such as the direction of the y-intercept and vertex, incorrectly assuming that a positive coefficient always results in an 'n' or downwards-oriented shape, rather than the characteristic 'U' or upwards-oriented shape.

Misconception 511: Ignores the negative sign when thinking about the value of a term
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the effect of the negative sign when evaluating expressions, which can lead to incorrect ordering of values or computation of absolute values instead of correctly incorporating the operation associated with the negative sign.

Misconception 512: When asked to solve simultaneous equations, believes they can just find values that work in one equation
Expanded misconception:
When asked to solve simultaneous equations, believes they can just find values that satisfy one equation and does not check or ensure these values also satisfy the other equation, resulting in a failure to find the solution that satisfies both equations simultaneously.

Misconception 513: Believes they need to multiply the numbers together in a worded HCF question 
Expanded misconception:
Confuses greatest common factors (HCF) with multiplication or least common multiples (LCM), leading to incorrect interpretation of mathematical operations in word problems involving shared quantities or partial amounts.

Misconception 514: Believes 0.5absin(c) requires only three sides (potentially confused with cosine rule)
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly applies sine rule, mistakenly believing it only requires three sides, resulting in confusion with the cosine rule, failure to consider the ambiguous case, and overlooking the necessity of two angles and one side or two sides and the included angle.

Misconception 515: Does not know a rectangle is a type of parallelogram 
Expanded misconception:
Does not know or apply the hierarchical relationships between shapes, specifically that certain shapes are special cases or subsets of others (e.g. square is a rectangle and a rhombus, rectangle is a parallelogram and a trapezoid).

Misconception 516: Thinks the inverse of multiplication is addition
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the inverse relationship between multiplication and division, mistakenly assuming that the inverse operation of multiplication is addition instead of division, and may apply this incorrect association when solving equations or problems involving inverse operations.

Misconception 517: Assumes triangles are isosceles if they look like they are isosceles
Expanded misconception:
Assumes geometrical figures to be symmetrical or of specific shapes (e.g., isosceles, equilateral) solely based on visual appearance, without considering the relevance of given measurements, angles, or specific properties.

Misconception 518: Thinks that to decrease by a percentage you multiply by 1+ the decimal equivalent to that percentage
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly apply the mathematical operations involved in percentage calculations, specifically thinking that to decrease a value by a certain percentage, one multiplies by 1 plus the decimal equivalent of the percentage, rather than 1 minus the decimal equivalent, resulting in the opposite effect of an increase instead of a decrease.

Misconception 519: When asked for factors of an algebraic expression, thinks a term will be a factor
Expanded misconception:
When asked for factors of an algebraic expression, mistakenly believes a term in the expression will be a factor rather than identifying combinations of terms that multiply to form the given expression.

Misconception 520: Thinks the inverse of addition is multiplication
Expanded misconception:
Thinks the inverse operation of addition is multiplication instead of subtraction, often leading to errors when solving equations or balancing expressions.

Misconception 521: When asked to triple, writes the digit out three times
Expanded misconception:
Multiplies or increases a number by incorrectly repeating the digits or the entire number a certain number of times rather than performing the correct multiplication operation.

Misconception 522: Does not count from the centre of enlargement to find the image
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply centre of enlargement concept by neglecting to count accurately from the centre of enlargement to identify the correct image location and size in transformation problems, leading to an incorrect determination of an enlargement's outcome.

Misconception 523: Believes you cannot have a negative gradient when plotting a linear sequence
Expanded misconception:
Has difficulty recognizing that linear sequences can have both positive and negative gradients, and consistently misinterprets negative gradients as impossible or indicative of non-linear relationships.

Misconception 524: Does not understand square root
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply or misunderstands the concept of square root, including not recognizing when to take the square root of a value, incorrectly taking the square root of a non-perfect square, or mistakenly treating square root as a simple arithmetic operation such as addition or multiplication.

Misconception 525: Does not understand that a surd is a rational number if the number under the root is a square number
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that a surd can be simplified to a rational number if the radicand (the number under the root) is a perfect square, or commonly referred to as a square number.

Misconception 526: Thinks there are 10000mm in a km
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or incorrectly recalls common metric conversion factors, specifically thinking there are 10000mm in a kilometer instead of 1,000,000mm or 1,000 meters.

Misconception 527: Does know know equilateral means the same as regular 
Expanded misconception:
Does not know the difference between equilateral and regular shapes, specifically that 'equilateral' refers to all sides being equal in length, and 'regular' refers to both equal side lengths and equal angles.

Misconception 528: Does not think that a variable with no coefficient has a coefficient of 1.
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognize that variables, terms, or expressions without an explicit coefficient, exponent, or operator have a default or implied value, such as assuming an missing coefficient is 0 instead of 1, ignoring the implied exponent of 1, or omitting operators and their associated order of operations.

Misconception 529: Believes cumulative frequency is the product rather than sum of frequencies
Expanded misconception:
Confuses arithmetic operations in frequency calculations, such as believing cumulative frequency is the product of frequencies rather than the sum, which may be due to a lack of understanding of the difference between additive and multiplicative relationships in data analysis.

Misconception 530: Confuses the formulas for the circumference of a full circle and the perimeter of a sector
Expanded misconception:
Inappropriately substitutes or confuses the formulas and properties of circumference of a full circle (2πr or πd), the perimeter (or arc length) of a sector, and the perimeter of a sector including the chord (or the total length around a sector), often failing to recognize key differences and applying the incorrect formula or calculation when solving problems related to circular shapes.

Misconception 531: Thinks when finding a percentage you multiply by the percentage being asked
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that when finding a percentage of a value, one multiplies the original value by the percentage being asked (e.g., calculating 25% of 200 as 200 * 25, rather than converting the percentage to a decimal by dividing by 100 first).

Misconception 532: Confuses alternate and opposite angles
Expanded misconception:
Confuses alternate interior angles and opposite angles in geometric properties, specifically fails to recognize the equality of alternate interior angles between parallel lines, or mistakenly attributes properties of opposite angles to alternate angles.

Misconception 533: Does not consider the variable when evaluating an expression that requires substitution.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to substitute variables with their corresponding values or evaluates the expression as if the variable's value is unknown or holds a different value, often leading to incorrect simplification or computation of algebraic or numerical expressions.

Misconception 534: Does not understand what an interior angle is
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand what an interior angle is; incorrectly identifies the interior angle of a shape or polygon, confuses it with the exterior angle, or assumes it is equivalent to the angle supplementary to the exterior angle.

Misconception 535: Believes a missing length on a cuboid can be found by doing volume divided by a different length
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that in order to find a missing length of a cuboid, the volume must be divided by the correct product of the other two lengths (usually area of the base) rather than another individual length, typically resulting in an incorrect algebraic manipulation.

Misconception 536: Ignores coefficients when forming an expression from a question
Expanded misconception:
Fails to incorporate coefficients or constants from the problem statement when translating the verbal description into a mathematical expression, resulting in an incomplete or inaccurate representation of the problem.

Misconception 537: Believes you do not need to give the direction when describing a rotation
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the direction of rotation or assumes a default direction when it is not specified, often resulting in the incorrect application of rotational formulas or misconstruction of rotational transformations.

Misconception 538: Does not perform calculations in the numerator of a fraction before division by the denominator
Expanded misconception:
Does not perform calculations in the numerator or denominator of a fraction before performing division, often treating the fraction as individual numbers and not as a single value that requires simplification.

Misconception 539: Does not understand the value of zeros as placeholders
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or apply the concept of place value, including the importance of zeros as placeholders, in performing calculations or operations that involve multi-digit numbers.

Misconception 540: Believes the discriminant must be a perfect square for an equation to have real solutions
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that a quadratic equation has real solutions if and only if the discriminant (b² - 4ac) is greater than or equal to zero, incorrectly believing that the discriminant must be a perfect square, and may incorrectly conclude that an equation has complex or no solutions when the discriminant is a non-perfect square but non-negative number.

Misconception 541: When asked to find a missing number in a factor tree, finds any factor of the given number rather than the pair of the factor already given.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to identify a unique factor pair when a known factor is provided in a missing factor scenario, instead selecting any possible factor of the given number regardless of the existing factor, resulting in an incorrect solution.

Misconception 542: Believes constructing a perpendicular gives an angle of 60 degrees
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that constructing a perpendicular is a step in creating a particular angle or shape, but mistakenly believes it inherently results in an angle of 60 degrees, regardless of the specific geometric property or construction method being used.

Misconception 543: Thinks that when drawing a plan view, you only include the top layer of the shape
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider depth and obstructions when creating a 2D plan view, incorrectly assuming that only the top layer or visible features of the 3D shape need to be represented.

Misconception 544: When dividing a decimal by a power of 10 adds zeroes to start of the number ignoring the placement of the initial decimal point
Expanded misconception:
Fails to adjust the decimal point when dividing or multiplying by powers of 10, often resulting in adding or removing decimal places incorrectly and misinterpreting the placement of the initial decimal point, leading to incorrect placement of the decimal point in the quotient.

Misconception 545: When given the perimeter of a regular polygon, multiplies instead of divides to find each side length
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply correct order of operations when reversing formulas to find unknown values, specifically multiplying instead of dividing when given a total value that results from multiple equal parts, such as perimeter in the case of regular polygons.

Misconception 546: After multiplying to get a common denominator, simplifies back to the original fractions
Expanded misconception:
After multiplying to get a common denominator in multi-step fraction calculations, neglects to simplify the resulting fraction and either returns an answer with a common denominator or simplifies back to the original fractions.

Misconception 547: Factorises difference of two squares with the same sign in both brackets
Expanded misconception:
Consistently misapplies the difference of squares algebraic identity by incorrectly using identical signs for both terms within the brackets, rather than considering one positive and one negative term when factorizing expressions of the form a^2 - b^2 or a^2 + b^2.

Misconception 548: Believes that a fractional index means you need to find the reciprocal
Expanded misconception:
Believes that a fractional index means you need to find the reciprocal, rather than taking a root, and may also incorrectly apply reciprocal rules when dealing with negative and fractional exponents.

Misconception 549: Incorrectly converted cm to m
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly applied unit conversion between centimeters (cm) and meters (m), possibly by using an erroneous conversion factor (e.g., dividing by 10 instead of dividing by 100, or vice versa) or misunderstanding the decimal placement shift.

Misconception 550: When representing an inequality on a numberline, assumes the filled and unfilled circles have the opposite meaning.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly interpret or represent the endpoint of inequalities on a number line, often confusing open and closed circles (or brackets and parentheses) to indicate whether a value is included or excluded.

Misconception 551: Believes a rectangle has four equal sides
Expanded misconception:
Believes a rectangle has four equal sides, possibly confusing it with a square, rhombus, or equilateral quadrilateral, and displaying a limited understanding of geometric properties and vocabulary.

Misconception 552: Thinks the units for speed should not contain a measure of time
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to recognize that speed is a rate of change that inherently includes a time component and should be measured using units that represent a ratio of distance to time (e.g., meters per second, kilometers per hour), often incorrectly assuming that speed can be expressed solely in terms of distance units."

Misconception 553: Confuses an equation with an identity
Expanded misconception:
Confuses an equation that is only true under specific conditions with an identity that is true for all values of the variable, or incorrectly assumes that an equation that is only valid for certain cases is universally applicable.

Misconception 554: Finds area of one face when asked for volume
Expanded misconception:
Finds the area of a single face or cross-section of a three-dimensional shape, rather than calculating the total volume of the entire shape as required by the problem, typically due to a lack of understanding of volume formulas or misinterpretation of the problem's requirements.

Misconception 555: Only counts visible edges
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider or incorrectly applies geometric or spatial principles, specifically only counts visible edges or lines, and neglects to account for edges or lines that may be implied, hidden, or unseen.

Misconception 556: When describing a translation, counts the corners of squares (including the starting one) rather than the number of jumps between squares.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply correct translation or movement calculation by either counting corners of squares (including the starting one) or misunderstanding the number of jumps or units between squares, leading to inaccurate representation of movement.

Misconception 557: Assumes a fact without considering enough examples
Expanded misconception:
Assumes a fact or rule without considering a sufficient number of examples or exceptions, leading to an overgeneralization or application of the concept in an incorrect context.

Misconception 558: When given a term to term sequence with multiple steps, applies one step per term
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to consistently apply each step of a multi-step process across an entire sequence, instead incorrectly applying one step per term, resulting in partial and incorrect sequence calculations."

Misconception 559: Thinks depth is marked on the cross section of a prism
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets the representation of dimensions on diagrams or cross-sections of 3D objects, specifically thinking that the depth of a prism or the height of a solid figure is marked on the cross-section view, when in fact it is usually provided separately or indicated by other visual cues or notations on the diagram.

Misconception 560: Confuses class width and upper bound of the class
Expanded misconception:
Confuses class width and upper bound of the class, and/or uses class width to define the lower/upper bounds of the class, or uses upper bound to define the boundaries of the class, failing to take into account that the upper bound is the first value that starts the next class.

Misconception 561: Misinterprets the order of division in worded problems
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets the order of operations, especially division, in algebraic expressions or worded problems by performing operations from left to right, neglecting or misidentifying the order of operations as dictated by parentheses, exponents, multiplication and division, and addition and subtraction.

Misconception 562: When drawing a histogram, believes you plot cumulative frequency on the vertical axis
Expanded misconception:
When interpreting and constructing histograms, incorrectly assumes that the vertical axis represents cumulative frequency rather than the frequency or count of data points within each category or interval.

Misconception 563: Counts the squares rather than the diagonals when reflecting in y=x or y=-x 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for the relationship between coordinates when reflecting across lines of symmetry, specifically counting the squares rather than the diagonals when reflecting in y=x or y=-x, resulting in incorrect placement of the reflected image.

Misconception 564: Does not understand set notation for complement (eg. A')
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or apply set notation for complement, intersections, and unions, misinterpreting notations such as A' (complement of set A), A ∩ B (intersection of sets A and B), and A ∪ B (union of sets A and B), which can lead to incorrect computation, algebraic manipulation, or logical deduction involving set operations.

Misconception 565: Divides rather than multiplies 
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to apply the correct operation (i.e., mistakenly divides instead of multiplies) when presented with a multiplication symbol (*) or incorrectly interprets a division word problem as a multiplication problem."

Misconception 566: Thinks the inverse of subtraction is division
Expanded misconception:
Confuses inverse operations: believes the inverse of subtraction is division instead of addition, possibly applying incorrect inverse relationships in equations involving multiple operations or operations with negative numbers.

Misconception 567: When doing operations with decimals, ignores significant zeros after the decimal point
Expanded misconception:
When performing arithmetic operations with decimals, fails to consider the place value of zeros following the decimal point, leading to incorrect rounding, trailing zeros, or misplaced decimal places.

Misconception 568: When solving a problem that requires an inverse operation (e.g. missing number problems), does subtraction when division is required
Expanded misconception:
When solving a problem that involves an inverse operation, mistakenly applies an incorrect inverse operation or operation order, such as performing subtraction instead of division, or performing multiplication instead of addition, often revealing an inadequate understanding of mathematical inverse relationships.

Misconception 569: Believes the solutions to an equation have opposite signs from its x intercepts
Expanded misconception:
Believes the solutions to an equation have opposite signs from its x-intercepts, often due to incorrectly applying or misunderstanding the relationship between roots, zeros, or x-intercepts, and the corresponding sign of the solution, potentially stemming from a lack of understanding of function behavior, graph analysis, or algebraic manipulations.

Misconception 570: Does not understand that all angles and sides must be equal for regular shapes
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand that all corresponding angles and sides must be equal for regular polygon shapes, such as squares, equilateral triangles, and regular quadrilaterals, and applies this property incorrectly or incompletely.

Misconception 571: Believes a smaller number of shaded parts represents a smaller fraction
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the total number of parts in a fraction, incorrectly believing a smaller number of shaded parts represents a smaller fraction, and often overlooking differences in the denominators or the context in which the fractions are being compared.

Misconception 572: Writes repeated letters instead of using index form of a variable
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or apply the concept of index notation, instead treating variables with exponents or repeated applications as separate entities, or incorrectly writing repeated letters rather than using the more concise and conventional exponential or index form.

Misconception 573: Thinks numbers are divisible by all multiples of their factors
Expanded misconception:
Ignores or misunderstands the fundamental concept of divisibility, assuming that if a number is divisible by a factor, then it is also divisible by all multiples of that factor, without considering the necessity of checking the divisibility rules for each specific multiple.

Misconception 574: Thinks there are 100mm in a km
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or apply the correct unit conversion factor between millimeters and kilometers, specifically, mistakenly believes that there are 100 millimeters in a kilometer instead of 1 kilometer being equal to 1,000,000 millimeters, leading to incorrect conversion calculations.

Misconception 575: Thinks that when you put an input into a function machine you apply the inverse operation rather than the operation given.
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that when you put an input into a function machine, you apply the inverse operation rather than the operation given, often by mistaking the inverse relationship between operations (e.g., adding instead of subtracting, multiplying instead of dividing, or using the reciprocal of a coefficient), leading to incorrect output predictions.

Misconception 576: Cannot identify a line of symmetry in a simple shape
Expanded misconception:
Cannot correctly identify or recognize the line or lines of symmetry in simple geometric shapes (e.g., squares, triangles, rectangles) and consistently demonstrates this misconception across various orientations and configurations.

Misconception 577: Does not realise multiplying a variable by itself results in a power of that variable
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand, apply, or generalize the concept of exponentiation, failing to realise that multiplying a variable by itself results in a power of that variable, such as x*x being equivalent to x^2.

Misconception 578: Does not recognise when a surd can be simplified
Expanded misconception:
Fails to identify and simplify surds, resulting in an unsimplified or incorrect answer, often by treating irrational numbers as constants, not recognizing perfect square factors, or not applying rules for simplifying square roots.

Misconception 579: Thinks you cannot multiply by a decimal to get equivalent fractions
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that multiplying a number by a decimal equivalent to 1 (e.g., 0.5 is equivalent to 1/2) can be used to obtain an equivalent fraction or decimal representation, such as in conversions between fractions, decimals, and percentages.

Misconception 580: Does not understand use of the counters to represent algebra
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the concept of using counters or variables to represent unknown or algebraic expressions in equations, and may confuse them with numerical values that need to be directly calculated or replaced with a number, often resulting in a loss of equality or incorrect equation manipulation.

Misconception 581: Writes times in the afternoon as if they are morning on a 24 hour clock
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the 24-hour clock notation rules, specifically omitting the hour shift from AM to PM, resulting in writing times in the afternoon as if they are morning times.

Misconception 582: Confuses the dependent and independent variables on the axes of a line graph
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets the roles of variables on the axes of a line graph, such as the x-axis and y-axis, incorrectly assigning the independent variable to the y-axis and the dependent variable to the x-axis.

Misconception 583: Ignores negative sign when expanding bracket
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply correct operations when working with expressions containing negative signs within brackets or parentheses, such as incorrectly distributing a negative sign when expanding or simplifying expressions.

Misconception 584: Underestimates the area of shapes when counting squares when some squares are neither wholes nor halves
Expanded misconception:
Underestimates the area of composite or irregular shapes when counting visible units (squares, etc.) by failing to accurately account for partial units or fractions of a unit, such as quarters, thirds, or other fractional components.

Misconception 585: Believes lines can be parallel by having the same y intercept OR gradient
Expanded misconception:
Believes that two lines can be parallel if they have either the same y-intercept and/or a completely unrelated attribute (e.g., same gradient), but not necessarily both the same gradient and no common points, indicating a misunderstanding of the necessary conditions for parallel lines.

Misconception 586: Mixes up square numbers and prime numbers
Expanded misconception:
Mixes up square numbers (numbers obtained by squaring an integer, e.g., 4, 9, 16) and prime numbers (numbers greater than 1 that have no positive divisors other than 1 and themselves, e.g., 2, 3, 5), often as a result of confusion between the two concepts, an inability to distinguish between unique factors of prime numbers versus repeat factors of square numbers, or forgetting the definitions and properties of each type of number.

Misconception 587: Thinks that when adding decimals with a different number of decimal places, you should line up the digits to the right 
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that when adding decimals with a different number of decimal places, always lines up the digits to the right (i.e., ignores correct placement of the decimal point, failing to align by the smallest unit of decimal places).

Misconception 588: Thinks 10l = 1 cubic metre
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands the conversion between units of volume or capacity, specifically equating one unit of volume (e.g., litre) to an equivalent unit in a different scale or system (e.g., cubic metres), demonstrating a lack of understanding of the conversion factors and relationships between units.

Misconception 589: Believes extreme values can affect the median
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that the median is resistant to extreme values and outliers, and instead believes that even a single extreme value can greatly impact or even change the position of the median in a dataset.

Misconception 590: Has used slant height and base to find area of a triangle rather than perpendicular height and base
Expanded misconception:
Misapplies the area formula for triangles by using incorrect dimensions, such as relying on slant height and base instead of perpendicular height and base, or mixing up different geometric properties such as perimeter, and failing to adjust calculations accordingly.

Misconception 591: Does not believe the length of a diagonal line can be worked out based on horizontal and vertical measurements
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or apply the Pythagorean theorem in calculating lengths and distances in 2-dimensional spaces, often due to a lack of understanding that the length of a diagonal line can be determined using horizontal and vertical measurements.

Misconception 592: Thinks a fractional scale factor changes direction as well as size
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that scaling by a fraction affects not only the magnitude, but also reverses the direction or orientation of an object, shape, or value.

Misconception 593: Thinks multiplying by a variable is the same as raising to the power of that variable
Expanded misconception:
Thinks multiplying by a variable is the same as raising to the power of that variable or vice versa, such as interpreting 'xa' as a*x instead of x raised to the power of a.

Misconception 594: Believes y=-f(x) is a reflection in the y-axis
Expanded misconception:
Believes y=-f(x) is a reflection in the y-axis, rather than understanding it as a reflection across the x-axis, leading to an incorrect interpretation of function transformations.

Misconception 595: Believes the adjacent side is where the opposite side is in a right angled triangle
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the lengths and positions of the adjacent and opposite sides in relation to a specified angle in a right-angled triangle, possibly due to a misunderstanding of trigonometric ratios or a failure to properly identify the corresponding sides when applying trigonometric functions.

Misconception 596: Chosen the longest value rather than the greatest
Expanded misconception:
Chosen the longest numerical value (i.e., the one with the most digits) rather than the greatest numerical value, disregarding the actual magnitude of the numbers when comparing or ordering them.

Misconception 597: Thinks that to decrease by a percentage you just multiply by the decimal equivalent to that percentage
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that to decrease by a percentage, you just multiply the original value by the decimal equivalent of that percentage, rather than recognizing that the decrease should be calculated as a fraction of the original value (i.e., by converting the percentage to a decimal, then multiplying by the original value, and finally subtracting the resulting decrease from the original value).

Misconception 598: When subtracting a surd from an integer, just divides the integer by the number under the surd
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the correct procedure for subtracting a surd from an integer and incorrectly interprets the subtraction operation by performing a division step using the integer and the number under the surd, often resulting in an incorrect rational number answer.

Misconception 599: Does not understand that an obtuse angle is between 90 and 180 degrees
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the properties and measurement of angles, specifically failing to recognize that an obtuse angle is greater than 90 degrees but less than 180 degrees, and mistakenly classifying or reasoning with it as an acute angle, a right angle, or a straight angle.

Misconception 600: Thinks a horizontal line on a distance time graph means a distance of 0
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets a horizontal line on a distance-time graph, incorrectly assuming it represents a distance of 0 rather than maintaining a constant distance or lack of movement over time.

Misconception 601: Believes a less steep gradient corresponds to a faster speed
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets the relationship between slope or gradient and rate of change, believing a less steep gradient indicates a faster speed or greater rate of increase, when in fact it represents a slower speed or smaller rate of increase.

Misconception 602: Thinks multiplying two or more negative numbers together always gives a positive answer, regardless of how many negative numbers there are in the sum
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that the sign of the product of two or more negative numbers is determined solely by an odd-or-even rule for negative numbers being multiplied together, failing to apply the associative property of multiplication that dictates the resulting sign will always be positive, regardless of the actual number of negative numbers being multiplied.

Misconception 603: Believes you can make assumptions about size of pie chart sectors without enough information
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that angle proportions, not just visual inspection, are required to accurately compare or make assumptions about the relative size of pie chart sectors, and may make incorrect inferences without sufficient information or lack of precise proportion data.

Misconception 604: Gives probability as an integer instead of a fraction
Expanded misconception:
Fails to express probability as a simplified fraction or proportion, such as giving an integer answer, or uses an incorrect scaling (e.g., giving 0.3 instead of 3/10), due to a lack of understanding of probability representation.

Misconception 605: Believes the sin graph relates to the tan function
Expanded misconception:
Misconceives the trigonometric relationship between sine and tangent functions, possibly due to confusing the characteristics or overlaps between the graphs of sine and tangent, or misapplying trigonometric identities involving sine and tangent functions.

Misconception 606: Does not understand how to simplify fractions
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand how to simplify fractions, or maintain the equivalence of fractions and mixed numbers, often leading to incorrect calculations involving division, or failing to reduce or convert a fraction into its simplest form.

Misconception 607: Thinks the area of a sector is theta/360 x r x pi (forgetting the radius is squared)
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct formula for the area of a circle sector by not squaring the radius, resulting in an incorrect formula of (theta/360) x r x pi instead of (theta/360) x pi x r^2.

Misconception 608: Believes an upward line has a negative gradient
Expanded misconception:
Misconstrues the relationship between the direction of a line and its gradient, believing that lines with an upward direction or a positive slope have a negative gradient, and struggling to correctly apply the concept of gradient in various mathematical contexts, including coordinate geometry and linear equations.

Misconception 609: when dividing a fraction by an integer, multiplies the numerator by the integer
Expanded misconception:
When dividing a fraction by an integer, multiplies the numerator by the integer instead of multiplying the denominator by the integer, demonstrating a lack of understanding of the order of operations and fraction division rules.

Misconception 610: Does not recognise how to write a list of prime factors from a factor tree
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognise how to properly construct or interpret a factor tree to derive a list of prime factors in simple or complex numbers, or fails to identify the fundamental prime factorisation of a given number.

Misconception 611: When two digits sum to 10 or more during addition of decimals, does not carry across decimal point to preceding digit.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply regrouping or carrying rules when summing digits or performing arithmetic operations with decimals, leading to incorrect handling of carries across place values or the decimal point.

Misconception 612: Believes that when a shape is enlarged, the area rather than the scale factor is squared
Expanded misconception:
Believes that when a shape is enlarged, the area rather than the scale factor is squared, incorrectly applying the formula for area scaling by squaring the new length or width instead of the scale factor, leading to incorrect area calculations.

Misconception 613: Thinks 0.3 = 1/3
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the difference between a decimal and a fraction, specifically incorrectly converting a repeating decimal (0.3 is not equal to 1/3, but rather a representation of 3/10 or 0.333 recurring, which is approximately 1/3 but not exactly) in calculations or comparisons.

Misconception 614: When solving simultaneous equations, thinks you only transform the term that is being eliminated, rather than the whole equation.
Expanded misconception:
When solving simultaneous equations, incorrectly assumes that operations needed to create opposite coefficients for a particular variable only transform the terms containing that variable, rather than applying those operations to the entire equation.

Misconception 615: Adds rather than subtracts when using Pythagoras to find a shorter side
Expanded misconception:
Adds rather than subtracts when finding a shorter side in Pythagorean calculations, often incorrectly applying the formula as a + b + c rather than a² + b² = c² or incorrectly rearranging the formula to solve for a shorter side, such as a = b² + c² rather than a = √(c² - b²).

Misconception 616: Does not understand that 2n represents an even number
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand that 2n represents an even number, and may incorrectly apply concepts or properties exclusive to even numbers, or struggle with operations or transformations involving 2n.

Misconception 617: Does not apply the inverse function to every term in an expression
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consistently apply the inverse operation to each term within an expression, specifically neglecting to change the signs, distribute, or find the reciprocal when undoing operations such as addition, subtraction, multiplication, or division.

Misconception 618: Does not recall rectangle side length property
Expanded misconception:
Does not recall or apply the property that opposite sides of a rectangle are equal in length.

Misconception 619: Divided by 10 rather than 100
Expanded misconception:
Carries out division operations with the wrong place value order, specifically dividing by a power of 10 one place to the left of the correct value, often resulting in an answer that is off by a factor of 10.

Misconception 620: Gives the vertex instead of the 3-letter angle notation
Expanded misconception:
"Gives the vertex instead of the corresponding 3-letter angle notation (e.g., provides point coordinates or labels in place of angle labels alpha, beta, or gamma)"

Misconception 621: Believes the negative of a positive squared (where there are no brackets) gives a positive answer
Expanded misconception:
Ignores the order of operations and assumes that a negative sign outside a squared variable without parentheses implies the square of the negative of the variable, rather than the negative of the square of the variable.

Misconception 622: Thinks the gradient of a line is found from 2 points by finding the length of that line between those points
Expanded misconception:
Thinks the gradient (slope) of a line is found from 2 points by finding the horizontal or vertical length of that line between those points, rather than finding the ratio of vertical change to horizontal change.

Misconception 623: Assumes the denominator is divided by the numerator
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly applies division, in that it assumes the denominator is divided by the numerator in a fraction rather than the numerator being divided by the denominator, thus reversing or misinterpreting the division operation.

Misconception 624: In ratio question where one side of ratio is given, treats that as the total
Expanded misconception:
In ratio questions where one side of the ratio is given, treats that as the total or the whole quantity, rather than considering it as a part of the total amount, thus ignoring the proportional relationship between the two quantities in the ratio.

Misconception 625: Confuses corresponding and vertically opposite angles
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between corresponding angles, alternate angles, and vertically opposite angles, often incorrectly assuming that the properties of corresponding angles apply to vertically opposite angles or vice versa.

Misconception 626: Thinks the difference between a positive number and a negative number is equal to the positive number
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand absolute value and its implications, instead, mistakenly thinking the difference between a positive number and a negative number is equal to the positive number, and neglecting to account for the change in sign.

Misconception 627: Does not realise similar has a specific mathematical meaning
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or apply the precise definition of mathematical terms like "similar" (or "identical", "equal", "equivalent", or "congruent"), treating them as general concepts rather than specific mathematical concepts with precise meanings.

Misconception 628: When factorising, finds a factor that goes into only the first term of the expression
Expanded misconception:
"When factorising, finds a factor that goes into only one term of the expression, rather than considering a common factor for the entire expression, often leading to incomplete or incorrect factorisation."

Misconception 629: Thinks there are 1000mm in a centimetre
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands the metric conversion between millimeters and centimeters, specifically believing there are 1000mm in 1cm rather than 10mm.

Misconception 630: Does not know parallel lines have equal gradients 
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand that parallel lines have equal gradients and/or does not apply the concept of equal gradients correctly when solving problems involving parallel lines.

Misconception 631: When calculating the mean from a list of data, adds up the numbers but does not divide
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to apply the final division step when calculating the mean, resulting in the sum of the data points being mistakenly identified as the mean."

Misconception 632: Confuses range and interquartile range
Expanded misconception:
Confuses range (the difference between the maximum and minimum values in a dataset) and interquartile range (the difference between the third and first quartiles, measuring the spread of the middle 50% of the data), possibly by incorrectly assuming they measure similar or identical aspects of data spread or variability.

Misconception 633: Does not understand how to subtract algebraic fractions
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply correct rules for subtracting algebraic fractions, specifically mismanaging common denominators or the signs of the fractions, resulting in errors such as subtracting numerators and denominators separately or incorrectly combining like terms.

Misconception 634: Does not know the term numerator
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the concept of the numerator in fractions, or is unable to identify or apply the numerator in a mathematical operation or real-world context, such as multiplying, dividing, or comparing fractions.

Misconception 635: Believes you cannot find the angle size in a pie chart when given its percentage value
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that a pie chart is a visual representation of proportional data and mistakenly assumes that angle sizes or arc measures cannot be calculated or inferred from given percentage values or other corresponding data points.

Misconception 636: Mixes up constant and coefficient in a worded substitution problem
Expanded misconception:
Fails to differentiate between constants and coefficients in worded problems, often incorrectly substituting constant values for coefficients or vice versa, due to a lack of understanding of the distinct roles constants and coefficients play in algebraic expressions and equations.

Misconception 637: Does not recognise the corresponding sides in similar shapes or enlargements
Expanded misconception:
Does not apply proportional reasoning when identifying corresponding sides in similar shapes, or fails to correctly identify corresponding sides after an enlargement or scaling.

Misconception 638: Believes multiplication is not commutative
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that multiplication is commutative, mistakenly thinking that the order of the factors will change the product, and therefore incorrectly applies multiplication operations in a specific order, rather than any order.

Misconception 639: Believes unrelated acute/obtuse angles in a diagram are supplementary
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands angle relationships in a geometric diagram, mistakenly assuming that any two acute or obtuse angles detected are supplementary or form a straight line, regardless of position, orientation, or other relevant variables.

Misconception 640: Does not find the angle being asked
Expanded misconception:
Does not correctly identify or calculate the angle being asked in a geometric figure, or incorrectly interprets the angle measure obtained, possibly due to miscalculation, misunderstanding of angle types, or incorrect application of geometric properties and theorems.

Misconception 641: Does not add on the constant in a worded substitution problem
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for constant terms or addends in mathematical expressions and operations, particularly in worded substitution problems or equations, often resulting in incorrect calculations or solutions.

Misconception 642: Thinks x = ? is a horizontal line through ? 
Expanded misconception:
Thinks a variable equation is an isolated independent event where y-axis or a specific value should have one corresponding answer, and fails to understand that x = ? is actually a vertical line through the graph of the relationship between x and y, typically in a linear equation.

Misconception 643: Think 1km = 1 mile
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to accurately convert between units of measurement, specifically mistaking metric system units (e.g., kilometres, grams, litres) for their Imperial counterparts, often treating them as equivalent, and neglecting to apply necessary conversion factors."

Misconception 644: May have estimated when using ratios with geometry
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to accurately apply ratio properties in geometric contexts, instead relying on estimation methods that do not account for proportional relationships or scaling factors."

Misconception 645: Believes enlargement by scale factor -1 exchanges the x and y coordinates
Expanded misconception:
Believes enlargement by a scale factor of -1 only reflects the shape about the origin and exchanges the x and y coordinates, but may not adequately account for the resulting quadrant change or properly apply this transformation in different contexts or coordinate planes.

Misconception 646: Confuses scalene and right angle triangles
Expanded misconception:
Confuses scalene and right-angled triangles, possibly due to a lack of understanding in classifying triangles based on their angles and side lengths, resulting in incorrect identification and calculation of properties such as angles, side lengths, and trigonometric ratios.

Misconception 647: Thinks co-interior angles sum to 90 degrees
Expanded misconception:
Thinks co-interior angles formed by a transversal and parallel lines sum to 90 degrees, rather than 180 degrees, and mistakenly applies this incorrect property to solve problems involving properties of parallel lines and transversals.

Misconception 648: Finds volume when asked for surface area
Expanded misconception:
Confuses concepts of perimeter, area, surface area, and volume of various geometric shapes, and incorrectly solves for a different measurement when asked for a specific geometric property.

Misconception 649: Beileves you can use the full length when finding an area of part of a composite shape, when part of the length is needed
Expanded misconception:
Fails to identify the relevant dimensions of a composite shape and incorrectly uses the full length or width, instead of the required portion, when finding the area of a part of the shape.

Misconception 650: Believes mean is total divided by number of groups
Expanded misconception:
Believes that the mean of a set of data is calculated by dividing the total sum of all values by the number of groups or categories, rather than by the total number of individual data points.

Misconception 651: Forgets you need a protractor to measure angles
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or utilize the necessary tools (such as protractors, scales, or calculators) to accurately measure or solve problems, especially those involving geometric shapes, angles, or mathematical operations.

Misconception 652: Mixes up the numerator and denominator when writing a division as a fraction
Expanded misconception:
The misconception is: 
Failure to properly invert or switch the positions of the dividend and divisor, thus either mixing up the numerator and denominator when writing a division as a fraction, inverting the fraction, or simply switching the dividend and divisor, resulting in a reciprocal of the correct fraction.

Misconception 653: Does not have when calculating the area of a triangle
Expanded misconception:
Does not apply the formula for calculating the area of a triangle when required, such as failing to multiply the base by the height and divide by 2, or incorrectly applying the formula.

Misconception 654: Believes an interior angle in a regular polygon can be found using 180(n+2)/n
Expanded misconception:
Believes that the formula for an interior angle of a regular polygon is 180(n+2)/n, possibly due to incorrect application of the formula for the sum of interior angles, rather than the correct formula (180(n-2))/n or 180 - (360/n).

Misconception 655: Assumes that a list is given in size order
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for potential variability or irregularity in sequential information, assuming instead that a list or sequence is always presented in a specific order, such as numerical size order, alphabetical order, or chronological order, and applying this unwarranted assumption in calculations or comparisons.

Misconception 656: Reverses the order of operations but does not apply the inverse operations when finding the input of a function machine
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly apply inverse operations when working with function machines, instead merely reversing the order of operations or using incorrect inverse operations, demonstrating a limited understanding of the relationships between operations and their inverses.

Misconception 657: Has completed only one of the two operations.
Expanded misconception:
Has completed only one of the two operations and failed to follow through with the remaining operation, possibly due to lack of priority order understanding, incomplete application of order of operations, or insufficient recognition of multiple operations within the problem.

Misconception 658: Thinks they double the numerator to turn a fraction into a percentage
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct procedure to convert fractions to percentages, specifically thinking that multiplying the numerator by a certain factor (e.g. doubling) is equivalent to finding the percentage representation, rather than multiplying the fraction by 100.

Misconception 659: Does not know how to write a fraction - just given an integer answer
Expanded misconception:
Fails to simplify or correctly calculate a fraction, often giving the numerator, denominator, or a part of the calculation result as a whole number answer, indicating a lack of understanding of fraction representation or arithmetic operations.

Misconception 660: Thinks that the perimeter on a square grid is the number of squares inside that are touching the perimeter of the shape
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that the perimeter of a shape, including those on a square grid or composed of discrete units, is equal to the number of squares or units along the perimeter or that are touching the perimeter, rather than the total distance or length around the outer boundary of the shape.

Misconception 661: Confuses an expression with a term
Expanded misconception:
Confuses an expression with a term, such as incorrectly treating a phrase involving multiple operations or parentheses as a single entity, neglecting the order of operations, or misunderstanding the role of parentheses in evaluating expressions.

Misconception 662: Believes that a number divided by itself equals the original number
Expanded misconception:
The misconception: Fails to recognize that division by the same number (identity property of division, a ÷ a = 1) is different from a general misconception that division results in the dividend; rather, it should be understood that any number (except zero) divided by itself equals 1, not the dividend itself.

Misconception 663: Forgotten placeholders when rounding to significant figures
Expanded misconception:
Fail to maintain placeholder digits when rounding to significant figures, resulting in an incorrect number of significant figures in the final answer.

Misconception 664: Believes -a is always smaller than a, ignoring the possibility that a is negative
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the sign and magnitude of a variable or numerical value when comparing quantities, often resulting in incorrect assumptions that a negative value is always less than its positive counterpart, without considering that the variable 'a' itself could be a negative number, thereby making '-a' larger.

Misconception 665: Believes that the gradient of a horizontal line is undefined
Expanded misconception:
Believes that the gradient of a horizontal line is undefined, possibly thinking that it follows the same rule as vertical lines, rather than understanding that the gradient of a horizontal line is actually zero.

Misconception 666: Thinks grams and tonnes are the same
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the relationship between units of mass, specifically not recognizing that tonnes represent a much larger quantity than grams, often leading to incorrect equivalences and scale conversions between the two units.

Misconception 667: Only considers elements in one set when asked to find the intersection of multiple sets
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply set intersection operation correctly by only considering elements in one set, or a subset of sets, or prioritizing the first set mentioned, when determining the common elements among multiple sets.

Misconception 668: Writes four symbols after rather than one before in Roman numerals
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly apply the additive and subtractive principles in Roman numerals, specifically by adding repeated numerals without considering the rules that a numeral placed before another of greater value can indicate subtraction, and a numeral repeated three or fewer times in succession indicates addition, resulting in incorrectly placing repeated numerals after a larger value rather than a single numeral before a larger one.

Misconception 669: Has used slant height and base to find area of a parallelogram rather than perpendicular height and base
Expanded misconception:
Has miscalculated the area of a parallelogram by using incorrect height, specifically using slant height instead of the perpendicular height.

Misconception 670: Counts the starting number when counting on
Expanded misconception:
Counts the starting number when counting on, resulting in adding an extra instance of the starting number, due to a failure to recognize that the starting number is already included in the initial count.

Misconception 671: When reading decimals on a number line, assumes each dash is 0.1
Expanded misconception:
When reading and comparing decimals on a number line, assumes each equally spaced dash or interval represents a uniform unit of 0.1, failing to consider the possibility of different scales or unequal intervals.

Misconception 672: When asked for a specific term in a sequence gives the next term
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly identify and extract a specific term in a sequence, often mistakenly provides the next term or an adjacent term instead.

Misconception 673: Finds total instead of difference when asked 'How many more'
Expanded misconception:
Finds total instead of difference when comparing quantities or calculating differences, often incorrectly adding or equating the values in a comparison instead of determining the magnitude of the difference.

Misconception 674: Thinks the inverse of addition is division
Expanded misconception:
Thinks the inverse of addition is division, rather than understanding that addition and subtraction are inverse operations, and that the inverse operation of adding a number is subtracting the same number.

Misconception 675: When interpolating using a line of best fit, reads off the value for the closest point to the line rather than the value given by the line of best fit 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly apply interpolation techniques, incorrectly reading or estimating values from a line of best fit by selecting a nearby data point or making an inaccurate visual approximation, rather than accurately determining the corresponding value along the line of best fit.

Misconception 676: Does not recognise that an equation will stay balanced if all terms are multiplied/divided by the same amount
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the concept of equivalent ratios and proportional relationships, specifically that operations performed on both sides of an equation must maintain equality, and ignores the fundamental property of equality that an equation remains balanced if all terms are multiplied or divided by the same non-zero value.

Misconception 677: When asked to subtract a fraction from a unit, removes the unit value from the numerator.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the unit or the whole as part of a mixed number or a decimal when performing operations involving fractions and mixed numbers, often leading to incorrect handling of the whole and mistakenly removing or misinterpreting the unit value in subtraction.

Misconception 678: Believes you cannot find the mode from a pie chart
Expanded misconception:
"Believes the mode, or most frequently occurring value, can only be determined from a data set or specific frequency information and not from graphical representations such as pie charts or bar charts that display categorical data, without considering the underlying frequency or category information."

Misconception 679: Gives a fraction when asked for a decimal
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply correct conversion from fraction to decimal, providing the original fraction instead of performing the required calculation to obtain a decimal answer.

Misconception 680: Does not know the term transversal
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand or recognize the concept of a transversal line in geometry, including its properties, the relationships it establishes between intersecting lines or planes, and the theorems associated with it.

Misconception 681: When drawing a cumulative frequency diagram, thinks you plot cumulative frequency on the horizontal axis
Expanded misconception:
When drawing a cumulative frequency diagram or graph, incorrectly plots the cumulative frequency on the horizontal axis instead of the vertical axis, and the upper or other boundary of the class interval on the vertical axis instead of the horizontal axis, indicating a misunderstanding of the correct representation of cumulative frequency data.

Misconception 682: Thinks millimetres and centimetres are the same
Expanded misconception:
