Fails to distinguish between millimetres and centimetres in measurement units, often incorrectly assuming that they are equivalent or interchangable.

Misconception 683: When factorising, believes they can choose different factors for each term
Expanded misconception:
When factorising, incorrectly believes that different multiplier pairs or factor combinations can be chosen for each term, disregarding their common factors or failing to recognize the need for consistency in factoring across the entire expression.

Misconception 684: Does not find a common denominator when comparing fractions
Expanded misconception:
Fails to find or correctly apply the common denominator when adding, subtracting, comparing, or ordering fractions, often ignoring the different denominators or using incorrect methods for equivalent fractions.

Misconception 685: Does not adjust the hour for “to” times
Expanded misconception:
Does not correctly adjust for half-hour or hour increments when converting between times on the hour and "to" times (e.g., 45 minutes past the hour to minutes before the next hour), resulting in an incorrect conversion or calculation involving time intervals.

Misconception 686: Does not know units of area should be squared
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand that units of area must be squared, resulting in incorrect unit conversions or calculations involving area, such as perimeter being confused with area or area formulas being applied without adjusting for the power of the units (e.g., length squared).

Misconception 687: Confused mixed numbers with improper fractions
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognize or consistently apply the distinction between mixed numbers and improper fractions, often treating them as equivalent or interchangeable due to a lack of understanding of their definitions or operations involving each.

Misconception 688: Confuses the factorised form of repeated roots and the difference of 2 squares
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between the factorised form of expressions with repeated roots (e.g., a^2 - 2ab + b^2 = (a-b)^2) and the difference of squares (e.g., a^2 - b^2 = (a-b)(a+b)), often confusing the correct factorisation pattern or applying the wrong formula.

Misconception 689: When multiplying algebraic terms written in the same base, adds the base variables and multiplies the powers.
Expanded misconception:
When multiplying algebraic terms written in the same base, incorrectly applies the rules for multiplying numbers by adding the base variables and either adding or multiplying the powers, rather than recognizing each term as a whole and multiplying each part, resulting in an incorrect exponents or an incorrect base.

Misconception 690: Struggles to visualise nets of 3D shapes
Expanded misconception:
Has difficulty in visualizing and understanding the properties of the faces and edges of 3D shapes when unfolded into a net, including correctly orienting adjacent sides and identifying the overall structure required to form the original 3D shape.

Misconception 691: To find the range adds the biggest and smallest number rather than subtract
Expanded misconception:
Fails to follow the correct order of operations or numerical calculations when finding the range, incorrect method often involves adding the largest and smallest number instead of subtracting the smallest number from the largest.

Misconception 692: Only uses two terms of a sequence to work out a term-to-term rule
Expanded misconception:
Overly relies on linear or straightforward patterns, often misgeneralizing a rule by only using two or an insufficient number of terms of a sequence to work out a term-to-term relationship.

Misconception 693: Does not divide to get a unitary amount before scaling up
Expanded misconception:
Fails to convert a fraction or decimal to a unitary amount (whole number, single unit, or ratio of one) before applying operations such as scaling or comparison to whole numbers or other quantities.

Misconception 694: Confuses cubic and quadratic graphs
Expanded misconception:
Fails to accurately distinguish between key characteristics of cubic and quadratic graphs, such as axis of symmetry, points of inflection, degrees of symmetry, and the number of local maxima or minima.

Misconception 695: Has found the area of a triangle rather than an area of a parallelogram
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between formulas for finding the area of different quadrilaterals, specifically applying the formula for the area of a triangle to problems requiring the area of a parallelogram.

Misconception 696: In the middle of the total column or row of a two way table, adds the other totals (including overall total), rather than finding the difference
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands the concept of subtraction when faced with subtraction operations involving multiple totals in a two-way table setup, often mistakenly adding adjacent totals (including overall totals) rather than identifying the correct difference between the given quantities.

Misconception 697: Arithmetic error when multiplying by 7
Expanded misconception:
Carries out incorrect arithmetic operations when multiplying by 7, such as incorrectly recalling the multiplication table, misapplying the rules of multiplication with regrouping, or failing to accurately perform mental calculations involving 7.

Misconception 698: Believes each number on a clock face represents 1 minute
Expanded misconception:
Believes each number on a clock face represents 1 minute, failing to recognize that the numbers 1-12 represent hours, with the clock hands or smaller divisions used to indicate minutes and seconds.

Misconception 699: When “borrowing” during a subtraction problem, does not subtract one from the preceding digit
Expanded misconception:
When performing multi-digit subtraction involving "borrowing" (regrouping), fails to follow correct regrouping procedure by not subtracting one from the preceding digit in the minuend when borrowing from a tens, hundreds, or other place value, or incorrectly reconstituting the number after borrowing.

Misconception 700: When calculating missing side length from perimeter, gives the total missing length instead of dividing for one side.
Expanded misconception:
When calculating a missing side length from the perimeter of a shape, fails to account for the symmetric or non-symmetric nature of the shape and incorrectly distributes or fails to distribute the remaining total length among the equal or equivalent sides, providing the total missing length instead of the length of a single side, or divides the total missing length by an incorrect number of sides due to misunderstanding of the shape's geometry or properties.

Misconception 701: Does not know the meaning of vertically opposite
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand or correctly apply the concept of vertically opposite angles, including the property that vertically opposite angles are equal in measure.

Misconception 702: When changing a number to standard form omits zero's of significance 
Expanded misconception:
When changing a number to standard form, omits, adds, or misplaces zeros of significance, or fails to understand the relationship between the number of zeros and the power of 10.

Misconception 703: When adding or subtracting surds, just finds the largest square that goes into each number under the surd and adds or subtracts those rather than using the largest square to simplify the surd
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly apply the distributive property or simplify surds by: adding or subtracting the numbers under the square root sign without properly combining or simplifying radical expressions; or finding the largest perfect square factor that divides into each radicand and then inappropriately combining the resulting expressions.

Misconception 704: Thinks there are 10cm in a metre
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands the conversion factor between units, specifically confuses the number of centimeters in a standard unit (e.g., mistakenly believes 1 meter equals 10 centimeters instead of 100 centimeters).

Misconception 705: When describing the relationship shown on a scatter graphs, gets the effect on the dependent variable backwards
Expanded misconception:
When describing the relationship shown on a scatter graph, incorrectly interprets the direction of causation, effect size, or sign of the relationship, such as reversing the association between the variables (e.g., a positive relationship is incorrectly described as negative or vice versa) or incorrectly stating the impact of an increase or decrease in the independent variable on the dependent variable.

Misconception 706: Carries out operations from right to left regardless of priority order
Expanded misconception:
Carries out operations from left to right regardless of operation priority (i.e. not following the order of operations such as parentheses, exponents, multiplication and division, and addition and subtraction, as dictated by the standard mathematical order of operations).

Misconception 707: Believes that fractions with the same denominator are equal
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand equivalent ratios and relative magnitude, believing that fractions with the same denominator are equal, regardless of the numerator, or neglects to compare numerators when comparing fractions with the same denominator.

Misconception 708: Believes switching the x and y variables in a linear equation gives a perpendicular line
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly assumes that interchanging the x and y variables in a linear equation results in a perpendicular line, demonstrating a lack of understanding of the relationship between the slope of the original line and the line's perpendicular counterpart, and failing to recognize that a 90-degree rotation in the coordinate plane is achieved by taking the negative reciprocal of the slope.

Misconception 709: Adds instead of subtracts when using an area model for multiplication
Expanded misconception:
Adds numbers representing the total areas of the parts instead of subtracting the excess areas to find the intended product when using an area model for multiplication.

Misconception 710: Confuses factors and multiples
Expanded misconception:
Confuses factors and multiples, often incorrectly identifying factors as multiples or vice versa, when solving multiplication and division problems.

Misconception 711: Believes you write the carried value in the number rather than adding it to the next place value column
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply regrouping correctly in multi-digit arithmetic operations, incorrectly representing the carried or borrowed value by either writing it within the original digits or neglecting to add/subtract it from the next place value column.

Misconception 712: Believes you can not take away a larger number from a smaller one
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply or understand the concept of negative numbers or results from subtraction problems, and/or believes that the order of numbers in a subtraction problem determines the validity of the operation, specifically holding onto the misconception that you cannot subtract a larger number from a smaller one.

Misconception 713: When asked for a specific term in a sequence gives a different term, which is not the next
Expanded misconception:
When asked for a specific term in a sequence gives a different term, which is not the correct next term in the sequence and may be an earlier or later term, often due to a lack of understanding of the underlying pattern or formula that defines the sequence.

Misconception 714: Uses the other part instead of total frequency as denominator for relative frequency
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the denominator in relative frequency ratios, incorrectly using parts of the data, such as the number of occurrences of another category or the number of non-occurring cases, instead of the total frequency as the denominator.

Misconception 715: Removes the common denominator when making equivalent algebraic fractions
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to account for the common denominator or incorrectly simplifies when converting between equivalent algebraic fractions, resulting in incorrect cancellation, addition, subtraction, multiplication, or division of fractions."

Misconception 716: Rounds a decimal instead of using a fraction in division
Expanded misconception:
Fails to maintain the exact result of division by converting a division expression to a decimal and using the resulting approximation in subsequent calculations, rather than working with exact fractional values.

Misconception 717: Believes the amount given is for the whole rather than a part
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or account for units or quantities that are given as a part of a whole, and mistakenly applies the value as a complete or entire quantity rather than a portion or fractional amount.

Misconception 718: Uses sin when tan is required
Expanded misconception:
Fails to differentiate between trigonometric ratios, specifically using sine instead of tangent (or vice versa) when solving problems involving right-angled triangles, resulting in incorrect application of trigonometric identities.

Misconception 719: Does not recognise feet (ft) as a unit of length
Expanded misconception:
Does not correctly convert between, or consistently apply, units of length, such as feet (ft), inches (in), yards, and others commonly used in measurement problems, leading to calculation or comparison errors.

Misconception 720: Believes that 'less than' includes the number 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that inequalities like "less than" do not include the boundary value, often interpreting it as inclusive instead of exclusive.

Misconception 721: Does not know what the symbol for equal lengths means
Expanded misconception:
Does not know what the symbol for equal lengths (or equivalent ratios) means, often misinterpreting it as an operation or procedural step rather than a statement of equivalence, and thus incorrectly solving equations or proportions, or incorrectly comparing quantities.

Misconception 722: Thinks the fraction button finds the reciprocal
Expanded misconception:
Misconceives the function or operation being performed or applied, specifically thinking that dividing by a number or using the division or fraction button finds its reciprocal, finds one minus the number, or performs another incorrect operation not equivalent to the intended division.

Misconception 723: Forgets you need a ruler to measure lengths
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or consider the need for a standard unit of measurement, measurement tools, or scales when solving problems involving lengths, distances, or quantities that require precision.

Misconception 724: Thinks the denominator in a combined probablity will be the same as the denominator in the probability of the first event.
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly assumes that the denominator of the combined probability will remain the same as the denominator of one event's probability, failing to account for changes in sample space when events are combined, such as when one or both events have probabilities expressed as fractions.

Misconception 725: Does not know that angles on a straight line sum to 180 degrees
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the properties of a straight line, specifically that the sum of angles on a straight line is 180 degrees and may apply incorrect angle addition principles or fail to recognize this basic geometric property in various problem contexts.

Misconception 726: Believes they can reverse a percentage increase or decrease by dividing by the decimal multiplier of the original change
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly reverse a percentage increase or decrease by incorrectly applying the inverse calculation, such as dividing by or multiplying by the decimal multiplier of the original change instead of using the correct formula or percentage change reversal method.

Misconception 727: Gets their constants and coefficients the wrong way around when writing a formula from a given situation
Expanded misconception:
Gets their constants and coefficients the wrong way around when writing a formula from a given situation, often leading to incorrect representation of the relationship between variables, and ultimately resulting in algebraic errors such as incorrectly positioning numbers or variables when translating word problems into mathematical equations.

Misconception 728: Confuses least and most
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the concepts of least and most, frequently resulting in the selection of an answer that exhibits an opposite extreme or an incorrect ranking, such as choosing the maximum value when the minimum is required or vice versa.

Misconception 729: Thinks the sum of 2 numbers, each squared first, is the same as adding the two numbers and then squaring the total
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the order of operations when applying exponentiation: mistakenly applies the product or sum of the bases after applying an operation separately to each base, instead of applying the operations in the correct order – exponents before addition or multiplication – often resulting in incorrect application of operational rules.

Misconception 730: Ends up £1 out when dealing with change in money
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly calculate total costs or changes when dealing with monetary transactions, often resulting in errors of £1 due to misapplication of basic arithmetic operations or neglecting to account for individual components or increments within a transaction.

Misconception 731: Believes that the third vertex of a triangle will share coordinates with both of the other vertices
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly assumes that two or more vertices of a geometric shape, specifically a triangle, share the same coordinates or overlap, resulting in a degenerate or non-unique shape, or fails to recognize that a triangle's vertices must be distinct and non-collinear points in space.

Misconception 732: Finds the mean instead of the median
Expanded misconception:
Finds the mean instead of the median, often due to misunderstanding or overgeneralizing the concept of 'average', or failing to recognize the distinction between the two measures of central tendency.

Misconception 733: When dividing an integer by a fraction, thinks they can just multiply instead.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply inverse operations correctly, specifically when dividing by a fraction, incorrectly applies the multiplication operation instead of taking the reciprocal of the fraction and then multiplying.

Misconception 734: Confuses square rooting and halving the number
Expanded misconception:
Confuses square rooting and halving the number, failing to apply correct mathematical operations and/or properties of exponents, resulting in treating square root (√) as equivalent to dividing by 2.

Misconception 735: In ratio where total is given, treats that as one part without dividing
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the total given in a ratio problem as the sum of parts, often neglecting to divide by the total parts to find the value of one part.

Misconception 736: When subtracting past zero, adds the absolute values and then writes the answer with a negative sign
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that subtracting a larger number from a smaller number requires a change in operation to addition of absolute values and a change in the sign of the result, resulting in a negative answer.

Misconception 737: Thinks there are 30 minutes in a hour
Expanded misconception:
Thinks there are 30 minutes in an hour and applies incorrect conversions between time units, such as considering 1 hour and 30 minutes as 1 hour, or misjudging time intervals.

Misconception 738: Believes there are 100cl in a millilitre
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the concept of unit conversion within the metric system, specifically the correct relationship between milliliters and centiliters or liters, often mistakenly equating one milliliter to 100 centiliters.

Misconception 739: Rounds to wrong degree of accuracy (decimal places rather than significant figures)
Expanded misconception:
Rounds numerical values to incorrect degrees of precision, incorrectly applying either significant figures or decimal places when performing calculations, particularly in the context of repeated arithmetic operations or conversions between units.

Misconception 740: Does not recognise that for a shape to be reflected it will be "flipped"
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognise that for a shape to be reflected, it must be flipped across a line of reflection, resulting in a mirror image, and may instead believe that a rotated or translated version, or one that remains unchanged, represents the reflected shape.

Misconception 741: Includes too many possibilites when given an 'at least' question in probability
Expanded misconception:
Includes too many or incorrect possibilities when interpreting 'at least', 'at most', 'exactly' phrases in probability questions, failing to account for the specific conditions or boundary values that should be included or excluded in the solution.

Misconception 742: Believes solving an inequality gives a single value as a solution
Expanded misconception:
Believes solving an inequality gives a single value as a solution, rather than understanding that inequalities typically have a range or set of values that satisfy the inequality, including the potentially infinite solutions that can result from inequalities that are continuous or open-ended.

Misconception 743: When calculating the area of a rectilinear compound shape, calculates the are of the surrounding rectangle
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for intersecting or overlapping regions when calculating the area of a rectilinear compound shape, instead calculating the area of the surrounding rectangle and not subtracting the areas of the irrelevant regions.

Misconception 744: When dividing a fraction by an integer, thinks they can just multiply instead
Expanded misconception:
When dividing a fraction by an integer or another fraction, fails to change the operation or invert the divisor, and instead incorrectly simplifies the division to multiplication by not inverting the divisor or not changing the operation due to a lack of understanding of the division properties of fractions or a misunderstanding of the concept of division.

Misconception 745: Confuses midpoint and distance between two points
Expanded misconception:
Confuses midpoint and distance between two points, specifically calculating the midpoint between two coordinates or incorrectly finding the distance between two points as half the distance or incorrectly finding the midpoint as the average or sum of the two points' x-coordinates and/or y-coordinates, respectively, rather than using the formula for midpoint or distance.

Misconception 746: Finds surface area when asked for volume
Expanded misconception:
Confuses or incorrectly applies formulas for different geometric measurements, such as finding surface area when asked for volume or vice versa.

Misconception 747: Believes scales on graphs must match on both axes
Expanded misconception:
Consistently misinterprets the relationship between scale units, tick marks, or axis labels on graphs, and mistakenly assumes that corresponding scales or units of measurement on both the x-axis and y-axis must be identical or proportional in order to correctly read, analyze, or interpret graphical data.

Misconception 748: When finding the negative power of a negative power, assumes they can ignore one of the negatives
Expanded misconception:
When finding the negative power of a negative power, assumes the property a^(-m)^(-n) can be simplified to a^m or a^n by ignoring one of the negatives rather than correctly applying the rule a^(-m)^(-n) = a^(m*n).

Misconception 749: When asked to subtract a fraction from a unit, removes the unit value from the denominator
Expanded misconception:
Fails to maintain the unit value when performing subtraction involving fractions, incorrectly assuming the unit is solely represented by the whole number and removing the unit when subtracting a fraction.

Misconception 750: When using the capture-recapture method, confuses the order of the calculation to find best estimate of population
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct sequence of calculations or incorrectly interprets the role of recaptured individuals in the capture-recapture method, leading to incorrect estimation of population size, often by confusing the mark-release period with the recapture period or misinterpreting the proportion of marked to total individuals recaptured.

Misconception 751: Believes you add the terms together when multiplying like terms.
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to apply exponent rule or incorrectly applies addition of exponents when multiplying like terms, mistakenly treating multiplication as addition, resulting in incorrect application of exponent rules."

Misconception 752: Thinks that when dividing numbers in standard form you subtract the number and divide the power
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that when dividing numbers in standard form, mistakenly applies the rule for multiplication, resulting in incorrect handling of the exponents by either subtracting instead of changing the sign, or confusing the base and exponent when performing division, thus leading to incorrect simplification of the quotient.

Misconception 753: Believes when you plot a linear sequence it doesn't give a straight line
Expanded misconception:
Believes that when plotting a linear sequence, the resulting graph can be non-linear or curved, possibly due to misunderstanding the characteristics of a linear relationship, such as a constant rate of change or proportional growth, and may not recognize that a linear sequence, by definition, should produce a straight line when graphed.

Misconception 754: Does not recognise that an integer multiplied by a surd can be simplified
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the rule for simplifying expressions involving the multiplication of integers and surds, or incorrectly assumes that integers and surds cannot be simplified together when multiplied, resulting in an oversimplified or incorrect final answer.

Misconception 755: Does not know which letters can be subtracted in Roman Numerals
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the rules of subtracting certain numerals in Roman numerals, specifically not recognizing that only I, X, and C can be subtracted from V, L, and D respectively.

Misconception 756: Believes unit fractions with a difference of 1 in the denominator have no other fractions between them
Expanded misconception:
Believes that unit fractions (1/n) with consecutive denominators (i.e., a difference of 1 in the denominator) are immediately adjacent on the number line and have no other fractions between them.

Misconception 757: Adds rather than multiplies when asked for a product
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct operation for a mathematical expression involving multiplication, instead inappropriately applying addition when a product is required, resulting in an incorrect calculation due to the application of an operation for the same order values of two or more multipliers as those for the sum of its addends.

Misconception 758: Believes an outlier is a value that does not fit the same characteristics as the other values
Expanded misconception:
Believes an outlier is a value that does not fit the same characteristics as the other values due to lack of understanding of statistical definitions of outliers, incorrectly assuming outlying data points are determined by subjective interpretation rather than being numerical values that fall far away from the mean or median, often failing to consider measures such as z-scores, interquartile range, or density.

Misconception 759: Confuses a formula with an expression
Expanded misconception:
Confuses a formula with a numerical or algebraic expression, failing to recognize and apply the correct procedure for evaluating the expression or mistakenly substituting values into the formula.

Misconception 760: Converts a fraction to a decimal by using the denominator after the decimal point
Expanded misconception:
Converts a fraction to a decimal by incorrectly applying the denominator after the decimal point or in a manner that fails to take into account the true value of the denominator as a divisor, often by simply placing it as a trailing digit or mistakenly adding/subtracting it as a whole number, demonstrating a lack of understanding of the relationship between fractions and decimals.

Misconception 761: Subtracts instead of adds when finding a midpoint
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly apply arithmetic operations when solving problems, specifically substituting subtraction for addition or vice versa, particularly when calculating averages or finding midpoints, leading to incorrect calculations of mean values or intermediate points.

Misconception 762: Chooses an example when asked for a counter-example
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between examples and counter-examples, selecting an instance that confirms or illustrates a concept instead of identifying an instance that contradicts or disproves it.

Misconception 763: Believes 'inclusive' does not include the end value
Expanded misconception:
Believes 'inclusive' does not include the end value, such as in range notation, and tends to exclude the upper or lower bound in calculations involving intervals or sets.

Misconception 764: Confuses octagon and pentagon
Expanded misconception:
Confuses polygons with similar numbers of sides, specifically octagons (8 sides) and pentagons (5 sides), possibly due to difficulty in distinguishing between close numbers or not fully understanding the definitions of different types of polygons.

Misconception 765: Thinks there are 10kg in a tonne
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to accurately recall/apply standard unit conversions between metric units, in particular, incorrectly recalls or interprets the definition of a tonne (1000kg), potentially substituting a nearby or familiar quantity (e.g., 'thinks there are 10kg in a tonne'), leading to calculation errors."

Misconception 766: Confuses the graphs of  direct and inverse proportion
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between the characteristics of direct and inverse proportion graphs, often incorrectly assuming a negatively sloping line is indicative of a direct proportion or incorrectly assuming that a straight line indicates an inverse proportion when, in fact, an inverse proportion is represented by a hyperbola or curve, and vice versa.

Misconception 767: Thinks there are 10 minutes in an hour
Expanded misconception:
Miscalculates time-related quantities due to a misunderstanding of time conversion factors, such as incorrectly assuming 10 minutes in an hour instead of 60 minutes.

Misconception 768: Finds the circumference of the whole circle instead of arc length 
Expanded misconception:
Ignores the central angle and incorrectly assumes the problem is asking for the circumference of the whole circle, failing to account for the proportion of the circle that the arc represents.

Misconception 769: Confuses the direction of vectors when adding or subtracting
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the direction of vectors when adding or subtracting, often resulting in incorrect interpretation of vector components, failure to consider the head-to-tail rule for vector addition, or failure to flip the direction of the second vector for subtraction.

Misconception 770: Believes class width represents area of the bar in a histogram
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that class width in a histogram represents the range of values in a class interval, not the area of the bar, and incorrectly interprets the visual representation of data, potentially leading to misunderstandings of frequency distribution and density.

Misconception 771: Believes that the solutions to x<b or x>b also satisfy ax<b or ax>b
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for the impact of coefficient 'a' on the inequality sign when multiplying or dividing both sides of an inequality by 'a', assuming that the direction of the inequality remains unchanged regardless of the value and sign of 'a'.

Misconception 772: Rotates instead of reflects
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between rotational and reflection transformations, incorrectly applying rotational transformations to problems where reflection is required.

Misconception 773: Does not understand to find each interior angle of a regular polygon you divide the sum by the number of sides
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply polygon interior angle properties by not dividing the total interior angle sum by the number of sides, leading to incorrect interior angle calculations for regular polygons.

Misconception 774: When subtracting mixed numbers, does not realise that a negative fractional answer needs to be taken away from their integer answer
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply proper procedure when subtracting mixed numbers, resulting in incorrect handling of negative fractional remainders, such as not incorporating the negative fraction into the final integer answer or not converting it to a proper numerical form.

Misconception 775: When adding variables with powers, thinks they can add the powers
Expanded misconception:
When adding variables with powers, incorrectly applies the rule for multiplying variables with the same base by adding the powers, rather than simply adding the variables as separate terms.

Misconception 776: Does not know how to compare size of angles on a grid without a protractor
Expanded misconception:
"Does not know how to compare the size of angles on a grid without a protractor, overlooking the use of grid units, angle relationships, and/or properties of intersecting lines to determine angle measures or relationships."

Misconception 777: When describing the relationship between the parts in a ratio using a fraction, gets the numerator and denominator the opposite way around
Expanded misconception:
Fails to accurately represent the relationship between components in a ratio or proportion by inverting the numerator and denominator of the fraction, such as incorrectly stating a part-to-whole relationship, a part-to-part relationship, or a whole-to-part relationship.

Misconception 778: Struggles to visualise the new position of an object after transformations
Expanded misconception:
Struggles to accurately visualise and determine the new position, orientation, or dimensions of an object after transformations, including rotations, reflections, translations, and scale changes, leading to incorrect predictions and calculations of the object's final state.

Misconception 779: When multiplying multiples of ten and the answer requires an extra digit, leaves off that extra digit
Expanded misconception:
Fails to accurately multiply numbers with tens when the product requires an additional digit, often resulting in incomplete or truncated answers.

Misconception 780: Believes the constant only gives the y-intercept for linear graphs
Expanded misconception:
Believes the constant or coefficient of linear terms only gives the y-intercept in linear equations, neglecting its role in other types of functions, equations, or contexts.

Misconception 781: When reading decimals on a number line, assumes each dash is 0.2
Expanded misconception:
Misconception: Fails to understand equal intervals on a number line when reading or plotting decimals, assuming each dash or unit mark represents a fixed interval (e.g., 0.1, 0.2, etc.) without considering the scale or units used.

Misconception 782: Mixes up quantitative and qualitative data types
Expanded misconception:
Confuses or inappropriately combines different data types, such as mixing quantitative values (numerical) and qualitative values (categorical or descriptive), and/or incorrectly applying mathematical operations or statistical measures to qualitative data and/or misinterpreting results.

Misconception 783: Thinks the sign of solutions in a quadratic equation is always the same as the constant term
Expanded misconception:
Thinks the sign of solutions in a quadratic equation is always the same as the constant term, neglecting the impact of coefficients of the quadratic and linear terms on the overall solution, and ignoring the possibility of a positive constant term with two negative roots or a negative constant term with two positive roots.

Misconception 784: Thinks there are 100g in a kilogram
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognize the metric system's decimal-based prefixes (e.g., kilo- as 1000, not 100), and applies this misconception to conversions between units with such prefixes.

Misconception 785: Confuses co-interior and alternate angles
Expanded misconception:
Confuses properties and relationships of co-interior angles with those of alternate interior angles when two lines intersect, particularly in identifying and applying properties of parallel lines and transversals.

Misconception 786: Believes they write the digits next to each other instead of multiplying, when multiplying a vector by an integer.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply scalar multiplication rules when multiplying a vector by an integer, instead incorrectly treating it as merely placing the integer next to the vector, resulting in an incorrectly formatted mathematical expression.

Misconception 787: Does not scale up a ratio to find an equivalent value
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply proportional reasoning and scaling, resulting in an inability to find equivalent values or adjust ratios when the original ratio's parts are multiplied or divided, often leading to incorrect calculations or misunderstanding of proportional relationships.

Misconception 788: Believes all linear graphs show direct proportion
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that linear graphs can represent various relationships, including direct variation, inverse variation, or constant differences, and incorrectly assumes all linear graphs show direct proportion.

Misconception 789: Does not understand the naming structure of polygons
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the relationship between the number of sides and the naming structure of polygons, often resulting in incorrect application of polygon names such as triangles, quadrilaterals, pentagons, and other regular or irregular polygons based on their side count.

Misconception 790: Thinks the shape of the graph does not affect the area calculation
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that the shape, position, or orientation of a geometric figure has no impact on the calculation of its area, perimeter, or other properties, and may thereby incorrectly apply formulas or algorithms.

Misconception 791: In a worded problem, does not complete all calculations
Expanded misconception:
Fails to complete all necessary calculations in a multi-step problem, often skipping intermediate steps, omitting certain operations, or not fully evaluating the expression.

Misconception 792: Expands when asked to simplify
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the order of operations or simplification techniques when asked to simplify, resulting in overly complex or factually incorrect answers, also expands or changes an expression unnecessarily, especially when performing arithmetic operations with one or more variables.

Misconception 793: Forgets to subtract sum of angles we know from 180 to find missing angle in a triangle
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the angle sum property of a triangle, specifically neglecting to subtract the sum of known angles from 180 degrees to find the missing angle, often due to misunderstanding of triangle geometry principles or calculation errors.

Misconception 794: Rounds up rather than to one significant figure
Expanded misconception:
Rounds to a whole number or to a specified number of significant figures incorrectly, which can involve rounding up or down inappropriately, or applying rounding rules in the wrong context, resulting in loss of precision or inaccurate answers.

Misconception 795: Ignores the negative signs when adding positive and negative numbers
Expanded misconception:
Ignores the negative signs when working with operations involving negative numbers, neglecting the concept of preserving the sign of the result based on the operation being performed and the signs of the numbers involved.

Misconception 796: Draws both angles at the same end of the line when constructing a triangle
Expanded misconception:
Fails to respect angle-side correspondence and incorrectly places one or both given angles at the same vertex when constructing a triangle, indicating an incomplete understanding of geometric constraints and procedures.

Misconception 797: Reflects horizontally or vertically instead of across a diagonal line of symmetry
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider proper line of symmetry, incorrectly assuming it to be a horizontal, vertical, or incorrect diagonal line rather than the actual line dividing the figure into congruent parts.

Misconception 798: Believes all numbers in a prime factor tree are included in the list of prime factors
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between prime numbers and their multiples, and includes all exponents and intermediate products in the prime factorization process, believing all numbers present in the prime factor tree or decomposition are part of the prime factors.

Misconception 799: Only considers elements in one set when asked to find the union of multiple sets
Expanded misconception:
Only considers elements in one set, disregards shared or unique elements in other sets, when asked to find the union of multiple sets, resulting in an incomplete or inaccurate representation of the combined set.

Misconception 800: Thinks you just add a % sign to the numerator to make a fraction into a percentage
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that to convert a fraction to a percentage, the numerator must be divided by the denominator, and then the result is multiplied by 100 to obtain the percentage.

Misconception 801: Forgets to give the centre of rotation
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider or accurately identify the centre of rotation when applying rotational transformations or symmetry concepts, leading to incorrect positioning or orientation of shapes.

Misconception 802: Thinks that when drawing the elevation, the top layer does not need to be included 
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that when drawing the elevation of a 3D object from a given perspective, the top layer or surface is not required to be included or accounted for, despite its significance in accurately representing the object's overall structure and visual appearance.

Misconception 803: Believes an axis is scaled correctly if the marked values increase in a linear pattern (ignores the distance from the origin to the first value) 
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly assumes a linear scale if the labeled units on an axis are in equal intervals, disregarding whether the scale starts at the origin (0) and does not account for the starting value or the units of measurement.

Misconception 804: Believes percentages cannot be converted into fractions
Expanded misconception:
Believes percentages cannot be directly converted into fractions or decimals, or vice versa, and may struggle with equivalent ratio concepts.

Misconception 805: Believes a rotation and a reflection can be combined by applying the rotation to the mirror line
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that the composition of transformations, specifically rotations and reflections, requires correct sequencing and applying the operations in the correct order, rather than simply combining the transformations in a way that seems intuitive (e.g. applying a rotation to the mirror line instead of applying it to the original object).

Misconception 806: Thinks the number in the ratio is the total
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between the component parts of a ratio and the total, often resulting in incorrect calculations or misunderstandings of proportional relationships.

Misconception 807: Believes all quadratic equations have a solution of 0
Expanded misconception:
Believes all quadratic equations, regardless of coefficient values and constant terms, have a solution of 0, indicating a failure to understand that only certain quadratic equations have a root of 0 and that the presence of a constant term should be evaluated in order to determine the existence of this solution.

Misconception 808: Thinks they double rather than halve when finding the area of a triangle
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct formula when finding the area of a triangle, specifically incorrectly doubling the product of base and height instead of halving it in calculations involving formula A = 1/2 * base * height.

Misconception 809: Uses cos when tan is required
Expanded misconception:
Confuses or incorrectly applies trigonometric ratios, specifically using cosine when the problem requires tangent, or vice versa, often due to misunderstanding or misidentifying the relationship between the angles and side lengths of a right triangle.

Misconception 810: Believes the number outside the circles in a Venn represents the intersection
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets the basic principles of Venn diagrams and set theory, specifically believing that the number or value outside the circles or overlapping regions represents the intersection of sets when, in fact, it typically represents elements that are part of one set but not its intersection with other sets.

Misconception 811: Believes they can multiply/divide through by a negative number without changing the direction of the inequality
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly apply the rule of multiplying or dividing both sides of an inequality by a negative number, resulting in a potential change in the direction of the inequality sign due to an error in maintaining or switching the direction of the inequality when performing operations with negative coefficients.

Misconception 812: When dividing numbers with the same base correctly subtracts the powers but divides the base numbers.
Expanded misconception:
When dividing numbers with the same base, fails to apply the quotient rule of exponents, correctly subtracting the powers but incorrectly divides or manipulates the base numbers, rather than keeping them the same.

Misconception 813: Believes parallel is the term used to describe two lines at right angles
Expanded misconception:
Believes that the terms "parallel" and "perpendicular" (or other description of lines at specific angles) are interchangeable or confused, specifically attributing the properties or visual characteristics of perpendicular lines (at right angles) to the concept of parallel lines (lines that never intersect and maintain a constant distance apart).

Misconception 814: Confuses circumference and radius
Expanded misconception:
Confuses circumference and radius of a circle, often using the value of one in place of the other or incorrectly applying formulas that relate the two, such as using the radius when the question refers to the circumference or vice versa, and may not account for the correct relationship (circumference = 2πr) in calculations.

Misconception 815: Counts in the wrong direction on a number line
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for direction of change or misinterprets left and right sequences on a number line, resulting in incorrect ordering or subtraction outcomes.

Misconception 816: Only counts visible faces
Expanded misconception:
Only counts visible, exterior faces, or two-dimensional surfaces of an object, neglecting to account for non-visible faces or three-dimensional properties, and failing to apply this principle consistently across similar problems.

Misconception 817: Believes adding numbers together gives the highest common factor
Expanded misconception:
Operates under the mistaken assumption that addition directly results in a fundamental mathematical relationship, such as the greatest common factor (GCF), least common multiple (LCM), or lowest/highest common denominator, rather than performing the actual operation required by the problem.

Misconception 818: Believes the median is the category with the median frequency
Expanded misconception:
Believes the median is the category with the median frequency, and therefore incorrectly identifies the median as the mode or the midpoint of a specific category bin, rather than calculating the median as the middle value of the dataset when it is ordered from least to greatest.

Misconception 819: When drawing a cumulative frequency diagram, thinks you plot the upper bound of the class intervals on the vertical axis
Expanded misconception:
When drawing a cumulative frequency diagram, thinks you plot the upper bound of the class intervals on the vertical axis, rather than always plotting against the upper class boundary on the horizontal axis, resulting in incorrect representation of the data distribution and associated statistics.

Misconception 820: Does not know that you can leave a fraction in improper form
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand that a fraction can be left in improper form, failing to simplify or incorrectly assuming that fractions must always be in proper or mixed form.

Misconception 821: Mistakes rotations for translations
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between motion due to rotation and translation, incorrectly applying one transformation type to a problem when another is required, such as misidentifying the movement in a shape or neglecting to consider the rotational aspect of an object's or figure's motion.

Misconception 822: When rounding, believes that if the number, after the required degree of accuracy, is a 5 or below you round down
Expanded misconception:
When rounding, believes that if the number, after the required degree of accuracy, is a 5 or less, does not follow conventional rounding rules that round up when the digit after the required accuracy is 5 or greater or round down when the digit is 4 or less, instead consistently selecting the lower value regardless of subsequent digits.

Misconception 823: Assumes they multiply by the numerator of fractional index
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct exponentiation rules by incorrectly assuming that a fractional index or exponent is addressed by simply multiplying by the numerator of the fraction, rather than applying the appropriate rules for fractional exponents.

Misconception 824: Believes rounding to 1 decimal pleace means rounding to the nearest integer, regardless of the value of the number
Expanded misconception:
Believes rounding to a specific decimal place or significant figure sometimes results in rounding to the nearest integer or unit, rather than considering the actual value of the number and applying correct rounding rules.

Misconception 825: thinks when writing a mixed number fraction as an improper fraction, you need to multiply the denominator by the whole 
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that when writing a mixed number as an improper fraction, one needs to multiply the denominator by the whole number part only, but does not properly incorporate the whole number part into the fraction by multiplying it by the denominator and then adding the numerator.

Misconception 826: Confuses intersection and universal set
Expanded misconception:
Confuses intersection and universal set, specifically failing to recognize that intersection refers to the elements common to two or more sets, and instead mistakenly treats it as the entire set or the universal set that includes all possible elements in the problem space, or vice versa.

Misconception 827: Believes when frequencies multiply by a scale factor in a table the mode also multiplies by that scale factor
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that applying a scale factor (multiplication) to numerical data in a table only affects the actual values, but not necessarily measures of central tendency or positional data, such as the mode, median, or percentiles, which may remain unchanged or undergo different transformations.

Misconception 828: Adds when calculating area instead of multiplying
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply appropriate mathematical operation for area calculation, adding instead of multiplying lengths and widths.

Misconception 829: Estimates angle rather than calculating
Expanded misconception:
Fails to perform exact calculations, instead relying on estimation strategies such as visual approximation or rough mental math, particularly in situations involving geometric transformations or angle measurements.

Misconception 830: Gives an example of a factor instead of counting how many total factors a number has
Expanded misconception:
Gives an example of a factor instead of counting the total number of factors of a given number, or confuses the concept of factorization with the count of factors, often providing one or a partial set of factors instead of the complete count.

Misconception 831: Thinks subtraction calculates how many in 
Expanded misconception:
Thinks subtraction calculates how many items in total are in both groups being compared, rather than finding the difference between them.

Misconception 832: Believes that a fraction with equal numerator and denominator cancels to 0
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that any fraction with equal numerator and denominator simplifies to 1, not 0, often leading to incorrect calculation of products or quotients involving such fractions.

Misconception 833: When calculating perimeter, multiplies instead of adds
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the additive nature of perimeter calculations, leading to incorrect multiplication of lengths instead of adding them together.

Misconception 834: When asked for the next term in the sequence adds one to the last term given, despite the sequence not going up in ones.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or apply the underlying pattern or rule of a sequence, instead relying on a simplistic or obvious pattern, such as adding one to the last term given, despite the sequence not following a straightforward arithmetic progression.

Misconception 835: Confuses a formula with an equation
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the distinction between a formula and an equation, incorrectly applying or substituting values in the former, or misunderstanding the solution or variable relationship in the latter.

Misconception 836: Believes the mean is the middle number
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets the concept of mean (average) as the middle number, rather than the sum of values divided by the total number of values, often resulting in confusion with the median, or incorrectly assumes that the sequence or ordering of numbers affects the calculation of the mean.

Misconception 837: Believes that rounding parts of a fraction in different directions means the answer will be the same. 
Expanded misconception:
"Believes that rounding or approximating parts of a mathematical expression, either fractions or numbers, in different directions or using incorrect rounding rules, will inevitably lead to the same result, failing to account for the accumulation of rounding errors and the effects on the final answer."

Misconception 838: When factorising a quadratic without a non variable term, tries to double bracket factorise
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply flexible factorisation techniques and tends to rely on a specific method, such as attempted double bracket factorisation, even when a quadratic expression lacks a constant term or is not suitable for that method.

Misconception 839: When dividing by a non unit fraction, thinks you multiply by the reciprocal of the corresponding unit fraction rather than the fraction given. 
Expanded misconception:
When dividing by a non-unit fraction (a fraction with a numerator other than 1), mistakenly applies the reciprocal rule by only multiplying by the reciprocal of the corresponding unit fraction (i.e., with a numerator of 1) rather than by the actual reciprocal of the given fraction.

Misconception 840: Does not understand the place value of answers from a Napier's bones calculation
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly convert results from periodic tables or non-decimal/integer arithmetic tools like Napier's bones into standard decimal or integer form, indicating a lack of understanding of place value in relation to calculation outputs.

Misconception 841: Does not subtract 2 from the number of sides when finding the sum of interior angles
Expanded misconception:
Does not apply the correct adjustment for the subtraction of the number of sides when using the formula (n-2)*180 degrees to find the sum of interior angles of a polygon.

Misconception 842: Confuses the calculations for HCF and LCM
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the calculations for Highest Common Factor (HCF) and Least Common Multiple (LCM), often stemming from a flawed understanding of the underlying mathematical concepts and properties of factors and multiples, leading to incorrect application of formulas or algorithms, or mistakenly interchanging the roles of factors and multiples in a given problem.

Misconception 843: Does not see fraction diagrams as equivalent if the shading is in a different pattern
Expanded misconception:
Does not see fraction diagrams as equivalent if the shading or partitioning is in a different pattern, or if the same fraction is represented with a different number of equal-sized parts or using different visual representations, indicating a lack of understanding of equivalent fractions.

Misconception 844: Thinks there are 100mm in a metre
Expanded misconception:
Failure to correctly recall or apply unit conversions, specifically thinking there are 100 millimeters in a meter, rather than 1,000, often evidenced by calculation errors involving metric unit conversions, such as converting between millimeters, centimeters, meters, and other metric units.

Misconception 845: Believes they can find the nth term based on the first term only
Expanded misconception:
Believes they can find the nth term of a sequence based solely on the first term, neglecting the underlying pattern or relationship that governs the sequence's progression.

Misconception 846: Thinks there are 1000ml in a centilitre
Expanded misconception:
Confuses or overestimates Litre-based measurement unit prefixes, incorrectly applying thousand-fold conversion factors, as in thinking 1 centilitre contains 1000ml, or 1 kilolitre contains 1000 times 1000ml.

Misconception 847: Thinks they can convert a 2 digit percentage to a fraction by writing one number over the other.
Expanded misconception:
Thinks they can convert a multi-digit percentage to a fraction by directly writing the numerical value of the percentage over 100, rather than first converting it to a decimal and then simplifying, often resulting in an incorrect fraction with the percentage value as the numerator and 100 as the denominator.

Misconception 848: Believes when simplifying a fraction you can divide the numerator and denominator by different values
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply equivalent ratio principle when simplifying fractions, specifically by dividing the numerator and denominator by different values, failing to maintain the original ratio, which may be due to a lack of understanding of the concept of equivalent fractions or difficulty with procedural fluency in applying simplification rules.

Misconception 849: Thinks the nth term is when the value of the term is n
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the term number with the value of the term, incorrectly assuming that the nth term refers to the case when the value of the term equals n, rather than indicating the term's position in the sequence.

Misconception 850: Thinks there are 10mm in a metre
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recall or applies incorrect information about the metric system, specifically believes that there are 10 millimeters in a meter instead of 1000.

Misconception 851: Thinks a negative x value in a column vector means to move right rather than left 
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets the sign of a component in a vector or coordinate, resulting in incorrect direction of operation or movement, such as moving left when the component is negative and moving right when it is positive, or failing to account for the sign in determination of magnitude or resulting movement.

Misconception 852: Thinks there are 100 mg in a gram
Expanded misconception:
Thinks there are 100 mg in a gram, demonstrating a lack of understanding of unit conversions within the metric system, specifically confusing the number of milligrams in a gram (1000 mg) with the number of centimeters in a meter or other unit conversions.

Misconception 853: Does not know how to check tessellation
Expanded misconception:
Does not know how to check tessellations or does not understand that a tessellation requires a repeating pattern of shapes with no overlaps or gaps, indicating a lack of ability to analyze geometric patterns and determine whether the fit together without any errors.

Misconception 854: Divided by 100 rather than multiplied by 100
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to correctly convert between units involving 100, often mistakenly choosing to divide by 100 when the problem requires multiplication by 100, or vice versa."

Misconception 855: Believes integers can have decimal places
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between number types, specifically believes that integers can have decimal places, or incorrectly operates on integers as if they were decimals, such as within calculations involving division or decimal representations.

Misconception 856: When asked to shade a fraction of a shape, gives the total number of parts rather than the number to be shaded.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to differentiate between the number of total parts in a shape and the number of parts that meet the specific condition or criteria, leading to incorrect identification or shading of portions in fractions, proportions, or ratios.

Misconception 857: When the unknown (e.g. x) in an equation is a denominator, gives the value of 1/x rather than x as the answer
Expanded misconception:
Failure to properly handle algebraic expressions when the variable (e.g. x) is in the denominator of a fraction, leading to incorrect solutions such as neglecting to invert the constant or coefficient when multiplying by the reciprocal of the fraction, or simply giving the reciprocal of the constant or coefficient as the answer instead of solving for the variable.

Misconception 858: Believes splitting a multiplication using the distributive property is the same as splitting it into factors
Expanded misconception:
Overlooks the necessity of an additional step or operation when applying the distributive property to split a multiplication, mistakenly treating it as simply splitting into factors without considering the implications of this property on the overall equation.

Misconception 859: Believes they can factorise a difference of two squares by placing the constant in both brackets without square rooting
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly applies the formula for factorising a difference of two squares by either not taking the square root of the constant term or by placing the constant term within both brackets without the square root necessary for correct factorisation.

Misconception 860: Mixes up clockwise and anticlockwise
Expanded misconception:
"Confuses the properties and effects of rotational directions, frequently mixing up clockwise and anticlockwise, and/or failing to consider the implications of such directions on quantities, measurements, or problem outcomes."

Misconception 861: Reads a power as a normal digit
Expanded misconception:
Fails to interpret powers or exponents correctly, instead treating them as a single number that can be read and operated on as a normal digit, potentially misunderstanding its position in the order of operations.

Misconception 862: Does not understand equivalent ratios
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand proportionality in various contexts including equivalent ratios, may not recognize that different ratios can represent the same relationship or proportion, and/or has difficulty identifying and creating equivalent ratios in different forms or representations.

Misconception 863: Believes inequalities can only include integers
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or apply inequalities correctly to various types of numbers, including fractions, decimals, and algebraic expressions.

Misconception 864: Reads 0 on protractor as size of angle when angle is not in usual orientation
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the direction and orientation of angles when measuring with a protractor, mistakenly reading the zero point as the size of the angle when the angle is not aligned with the protractor's baseline or is oriented in an unusual position, neglecting to adjust for and account for the actual starting point of the angle.

Misconception 865: Does not recognise a unit of mass
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between units of measurement, specifically not recognising when a unit of mass (such as grams or kilograms) is required in a calculation, possibly substituting it with a unit of length or volume.

Misconception 866: Believes that the y intercept is where y = 0
Expanded misconception:
Ignores or misunderstands the concept of x and y intercepts, specifically believing that the y-intercept is where y equals zero, rather than where the graph of an equation crosses the y-axis, where x equals zero.

Misconception 867: When finding the area of a parallelogram does not understand which lengths to use
Expanded misconception:
When finding the area of a parallelogram using the formula A = base × height, consistently incorrectly identifies the base and/or height by: using the lengths of the sides incorrectly, using the lengths of the diagonals, or misinterpreting the relationship between the base and height.

Misconception 868: Believes a number raised to a power will always give a positive answer
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the significance of negative bases and even exponents resulting in positive numbers, and negative bases and odd exponents, or zero exponents, resulting in negative or unchanged numbers respectively, when considering how a number raised to a power will yield a result.

Misconception 869: Believes multiplying together prime factors that are not shared gives the highest common factor
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the highest common factor (HCF) concept and mistakenly believes that multiplying together prime factors not shared by the numbers will give the highest common factor, rather than identifying the product of common prime factors raised to the smallest power they appear in either number.

Misconception 870: Only counts visible vertices
Expanded misconception:
Only counts visible vertices in a 2D or 3D geometric shape, failing to consider any potential hidden or implied vertices that are part of the overall shape structure.

Misconception 871: Does not include brackets when attempting to multiply an expression
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for the order of operations (BODMAS/BIDMAS) by neglecting to evaluate expressions within brackets first or improperly handling operations that involve parentheses in expressions.

Misconception 872: Thinks angles can only be measured when one line is horizontal
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply angle measurement principles consistently, believing that valid angle measurements require a horizontal or vertical reference line, and/or prioritizes one line's orientation over the other when measuring angles between intersecting lines or those forming a specific geometric shape.

Misconception 873: Believes that we cannot calculate the mean of negative numbers
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that mathematical operations, including calculating means, can be applied to negative numbers, leading to incorrect assumptions about the limitations of numerical data, resulting in incorrect attempts to avoid or incorrectly handle negative values in calculations.

Misconception 874: Mistakes the place value column of hundred thousands with millions
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between the place value column of hundreds of thousands (10^5) and millions (10^6), resulting in incorrect placement and subsequent calculations or comparisons, often due to a misunderstanding of numerical scales or values exceeding one million.

Misconception 875: Believes vertically opposite angles sum to 360 degrees
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that vertically opposite angles are equal, and instead incorrectly applies the concept of supplementary angles or sum of angles around a point by believing that vertically opposite angles sum to 180 degrees or 360 degrees.

Misconception 876: Believes squaring a negative number just changes the sign
Expanded misconception:
Misapplies or lacks understanding of the rule that a negative number squared is positive, resulting in incorrect sign change when performing operations involving the square of negative numbers, and may overlook the fact that two negative signs cancel each other out when multiplied.

Misconception 877: Does not convert measurements to have the same units before calculating area or volume
Expanded misconception:
Fails to convert measurements or terms to the same units before performing calculations, operations, or comparisons, often resulting in incorrect units in the solution, particularly in situations involving area, volume, or rate calculations that require unit consistency.

Misconception 878: Counts on by 1, when asked to count forward/backwards in a step other than 1
Expanded misconception:
Inappropriately applies counting patterns by consistently counting on or counting back by ones (instead of applying the required step or interval as specified in the problem), resulting in incorrect calculations or sequences.

Misconception 879: Thinks you apply one inverse operation to one term and the other inverse operation to the other term
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consistently apply inverse operations to an entire equation or expression in a correct manner, for example, by mistakenly applying one inverse operation to one term and the other inverse operation to another term, rather than consistently applying both inverse operations to each term or in the correct sequence, resulting in incorrect equation manipulation and solution.

Misconception 880: Thinks a higher average means more consistent data
Expanded misconception:
Confuses a higher average with data consistency, failing to differentiate between the central tendency of a dataset and its variability, and incorrectly assuming that a higher average implies less spread or variability in the data.

Misconception 881: adds all given dimensions when calculating area
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the formula for calculating area correctly by adding all given dimensions instead of multiplying the relevant ones, possibly due to a lack of understanding of the relationship between units or the concept of area as a two-dimensional measurement.

Misconception 882: Believes 0 has no square root
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that every non-negative number, including 0, has a square root, and may have difficulty applying this concept when solving equations involving squares and square roots, often resulting in errors when dealing with algebraic expressions containing these concepts.

Misconception 883: Believes mirror line must lie on a grid line, not in between grid lines
Expanded misconception:
Believes geometric transformations, such as lines of reflection or mirror lines, must coincide with or lie directly on grid lines, rather than existing between or fractional distances from the grid lines, limiting understanding of transformation flexibility.

Misconception 884: When asked for more, find the total
Expanded misconception:
"When asked for more than a single operation or calculation within a problem, tends to only solve for the initial or individual operation and fails to combine the results or perform subsequent calculations."

Misconception 885: Thinks they can simplify algebraic fractions by cancelling unlike variables
Expanded misconception:
Thinks they can simplify algebraic fractions by cancelling unlike variables or terms without a common factor, ignoring the need for common factors in the numerator and denominator to cancel terms correctly.

Misconception 886: Believes that prime numbers cannot be even
Expanded misconception:
Believes that prime numbers cannot be even and may also be unaware of the definition of prime numbers as numbers greater than 1 with exactly two distinct factors, often 1 and the number itself; mistakes multiples of 2 as composites and single-digit even numbers as prime, except for specifically taught cases like 2.

Misconception 887: When using interpolation to estimate frequency, assumes half the frequency of the class containing the value will be above/below it, even if it is not the midpoint
Expanded misconception:
When using interpolation to estimate frequency, assumes half the frequency of the class containing the value will be above/below it, even if it is not the midpoint, failing to account for the actual position of the value within the class interval.

Misconception 888: Does not understand the relationship between a ratio and fraction
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize a ratio as a type of fraction, or incorrectly interprets a fraction as a non- equivalent ratio, often resulting in errors when comparing, converting, or operating on fractions, ratios, and proportions.

Misconception 889: Believes when you plot a sequence, the line always passes through (0, 0)
Expanded misconception:
Believes that a linear sequence or function always starts at the origin (0,0) and generally lacks understanding of the concept of the y-intercept.

Misconception 890: Thinks they had to give the new amount rather than the total percentage that the original has been increased by.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that percentage increase or percentage change questions typically require the calculation of the difference amount as a percentage of the original amount, mistakenly providing the new total amount instead of the percentage increase.

Misconception 891: Incorrectly cancels what they believe is a factor in algebraic fractions
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly cancels out terms in algebraic fractions, often mistakenly assuming that common factors in the numerator and denominator can be directly cancelled, without considering non-reversible operations, missing distributive property application, or non-identical algebraic expressions.

Misconception 892: Believes each vertical line on a box plot represents 1 piece of data
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the concept of grouped data in box plots, believing each vertical line or point on the box plot corresponds to individual data points rather than representing the minimum, maximum, median, or quartiles.

Misconception 893: Believes when changing an improper fraction to a mixed number you divide the numerator by 10, regardless of the denominator
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly applies a division shortcut by consistently dividing the numerator by 10 when converting an improper fraction to a mixed number, rather than dividing the numerator by the actual denominator.

Misconception 894: Believes expanding a squared expression means writing it as a set of double brackets
Expanded misconception:
Believes expanding a squared, or more generally exponentiated expression, means writing it as a set of repeated brackets or multiplication symbols rather than applying the distributive or the binomial expansion method, such as expanding (a+b)^2 as ((a+b)(a+b)) instead of (a^2 + 2ab + b^2).

Misconception 895: Believes a line of reflection must be touching the original shape
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that a line of reflection (or line of symmetry) is a line that the original shape can be folded over to create a mirror image that perfectly matches the original shape, and does not recognize that a line of reflection can be either a line that intersects the original shape, is tangent to the original shape, or does not touch the original shape at all.

Misconception 896: When adding fractions with identical numerators, leaves the numerator and adds the denominators
Expanded misconception:
When adding fractions with identical numerators, incorrectly applies the procedure for multiplying fractions by adding the denominators instead of finding a common denominator, incorrectly assuming that the same operation for equal numerators applies across different arithmetic operations.

Misconception 897: Does not recognise an example of the alternate segment theorem
Expanded misconception:
Fails to identify and apply the alternate segment theorem when an exterior angle of a triangle is successfully split by a line, leading to incorrect application of angle properties in triangles.

Misconception 898: Thinks x = 0 is the x axis
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the difference between the x-axis and the equation x = 0, incorrectly assuming that any equation of the form x = a represents a horizontal line or axis, rather than a vertical line or a specific position on the x-axis.

Misconception 899: Does not recognise parallel lines angle rules
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply properties or theorems of parallel lines, such as corresponding angles, alternate interior angles, alternate exterior angles, or interior angles on the same side of a transversal, to solve problems or identify relationships.

Misconception 900: Believes the constant gives the y-intercept even when a line is not written in the form y = mx + c
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or apply the concept that the y-intercept constant in the slope-intercept form y = mx + b only refers to the point where the line crosses the y-axis, mistakenly assuming the constant represents the y-intercept in other forms of linear equations, such as standard form or those with coefficients.

Misconception 901: Finds unit price but doesn't multiply up for number in question
Expanded misconception:
Finds the unit price correctly but fails to scale up or multiply by the quantity required in the problem, leading to an answer that represents the cost or value per unit rather than the total required in the question.

Misconception 902: Thinks 1 squared is 2
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that odd numbers always result in even numbers when squared, or thinks squaring always increases the original number by 1.

Misconception 903: Has increased by the value of the first value, rather than the difference
Expanded misconception:
