Fails to correctly calculate the difference or change between two values, resulting in an answer that is the initial value increased by the absolute value or the wrong interpretation of the change, rather than its actual difference.

Misconception 904: Has taken the percentage from the original 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to adjust percentage calculations when working with changes to an original value, incorrectly taking the percentage from the original value instead of the current or updated value.

Misconception 905: Thinks the movement of the top number in a column vector is vertical, and the bottom number is horizontal rather than the other way round
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets the concept of column vectors and coordinate systems, mistakenly perceiving the top value in a column vector as representing horizontal movement or positioning, rather than vertical movement or positioning, and the bottom value as representing vertical movement or positioning, rather than horizontal movement or positioning.

Misconception 906: Does not know that sample size affects reliability
Expanded misconception:
"Does not account for the impact of sample size on statistical reliability, failing to recognize the relationship between larger sample sizes and increased precision in results, or mistakenly assuming that sample size does not influence the validity or generalizability of conclusions drawn from data."

Misconception 907: Does not find a common denominator when adding/subtracting fractions
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply equivalent ratio principles and neglects to find a common denominator or common multiple when adding, subtracting, comparing, or simplifying fractions, often resulting in incorrect or incomplete calculations.

Misconception 908: When adding or subtracting surds, believes the numbers under the root and the numbers in front should both be added or subtracted
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply rules for algebraic manipulation of surds, specifically believing that when adding or subtracting surds with the same or different radicands, the numbers under the root and the coefficients (numbers in front) should both be added or subtracted directly, rather than only combining like terms with the same radicand.

Misconception 909: Mixes up squaring with dividing by 2
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between exponentiation (e.g., squaring) and division, demonstrating a misconception that squaring a number is equivalent to dividing that number by 2.

Misconception 910: Cannot identify the correct side lengths to use when asked to find the area of a face
Expanded misconception:
Cannot identify the correct side lengths to use when asked to find the area of a specific geometric face (e.g., face of a 3D solid), likely due to difficulty in visualizing and distinguishing between different dimensions or incorrectly assuming proportionality between various lengths.

Misconception 911: Only multiplies the numerical terms when expanding a bracket
Expanded misconception:
Only multiplies the numerical terms when expanding a bracket, possibly indicating difficulty with the distributive property or failure to apply multiplication across both numerical and variable terms within the expression.

Misconception 912: Thinks adding the same value to each side makes shapes similar
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the concept of similarity in geometry and mistakenly believes that adding the same value to corresponding side lengths or proportions makes shapes similar, rather than preserving their original ratio or scale factor.

Misconception 913: Believes the numerator stays the same when calculating a second pick of the same type without replacement on a tree diagram
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for the change in sample space when determining probabilities of dependent events, often assuming that the numerator remains constant across consecutive events without replacement, which can lead to incorrect calculations of probability when working with tree diagrams or scenarios involving dependent samples.

Misconception 914: Finds 10% of an amount rather than the percentage being asked
Expanded misconception:
Finds a common, round percentage or fraction of an amount (e.g., 10%, 25%, or 50%) rather than the specific percentage being asked.

Misconception 915: Does not recognise metres as a unit of length
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognise the correct unit of measurement for a particular quantity, in this case, metres as a unit of length, and may apply incorrect unit conversions or confuse metre with other units.

Misconception 916: Thinks the fraction bar means multiply rather than divide
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to recognize or apply the correct division operation when encountering the fraction bar, mistakenly interpreting it as a multiplication or fails to reverse the operation, resulting in incorrect calculations or misplaced numbers in the calculation process."

Misconception 917: Believes every direct or inverse proportion question uses the formula p=kq
Expanded misconception:
May inappropriately apply direct or inverse proportion formulas, such as p=kq, to all proportion problems without considering the variables' relationship and context, rather than recognizing that this formula is only applicable when two quantities are directly or inversely proportional and other proportion relationships may require different formulas or approaches.

Misconception 918: Confuses x and y axes
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly distinguish between the x-axis (horizontal axis) and y-axis (vertical axis), leading to incorrect interpretations and applications in graphical and coordinate-based problem-solving situations.

Misconception 919: Believes the gradient of a speed-time graph is the distance
Expanded misconception:
"Believes the gradient of a speed-time graph represents the distance instead of the acceleration, demonstrating a lack of understanding of the relationship between the gradient of a line on a graph and the physical quantity it represents."

Misconception 920: Believes that the cumulative frequency of an interval of grouped data can be used as the cumulative frequency for any value in that interval
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly assumes that the cumulative frequency for an interval of grouped data applies to all values within that interval, failing to recognize that it only represents the total count up to the start of that interval and not the entire range.

Misconception 921: Reads the wrong scale on the protractor when measuring acute angles
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly uses the angle measurement scales on a protractor by either reading from the outer scale instead of the inner scale when measuring acute angles, or vice versa, or failing to align the protractor's base with one of the angle's sides and the vertex on the center of the protractor before reading the angle measurement, resulting in an incorrect calculation.

Misconception 922: Believes we can only take the square root of a perfect square
Expanded misconception:
Believes we can only take the square root of a perfect square and may struggle with applying square root operations to non-perfect squares, possibly resulting in incorrect simplification or evaluation of expressions involving radicals.

Misconception 923: Thinks index notation represents repeated addition rather than repeated multiplication
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that exponentiation or index notation represents repeated addition rather than repeated multiplication, leading to misunderstanding of rules of indices, powers and roots, and the order of operations.

Misconception 924: Believes to find the proportion of a pie chart sector you do frequency/sum of other frequencies rather than frequency/overall total
Expanded misconception:
Fails to normalize proportions or percentages in data visualization by incorrectly dividing a value by its complement or a partial sum rather than the total, particularly in contexts involving charts, such as pie charts, histograms, or when calculating probabilities or fractions.

Misconception 925: Believes the gradients of perpendicular lines are reciprocals of the same sign
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that the product of the slopes of perpendicular lines should be -1, and mistakenly believes that the gradients of perpendicular lines are reciprocals of the same sign, rather than reciprocals of opposite signs.

Misconception 926: Believes the number of sides gives the order of rotational symmetry
Expanded misconception:
Believes the number of sides of a polygon gives the order of rotational symmetry, rather than understanding that rotational symmetry is determined by the number of times a shape looks the same as it is rotated 360 degrees.

Misconception 927: Assumes that opposite angles in a quadrilateral sum to 180 degrees
Expanded misconception:
Assumes that opposite angles in any quadrilateral sum to 180 degrees, failing to recognize that this property is unique to certain types of quadrilaterals such as parallelograms, rectangles, squares, and rhombi, or misunderstanding the difference between various quadrilateral types.

Misconception 928: Divides when converting to a larger unit
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly apply conversion between units, incorrectly dividing when converting from a smaller unit to a larger unit, often demonstrating a lack of understanding of unit conversion principles and the reciprocal relationship between them.

Misconception 929: When using the capture-recapture method of sampling, believes you add the number marked first to the number caught second and add that to the number marked second to get the best estimate of population size
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the principles of the capture-recapture method correctly, specifically misinterpreting the formula by incorrectly adding the total number marked in the first sample to the total number recaptured in the second sample, rather than using the proportion of marked individuals in the second sample to estimate the population size.

Misconception 930: When solving simultaneous equations thinks that identical terms in given equations can be eliminated by either adding or subtracting.
Expanded misconception:
When solving simultaneous equations, overly relies on simplification methods and incorrectly assumes that identical terms, regardless of their coefficients or factors, can be eliminated by simply adding or subtracting the equations, neglecting the need to perform necessary algebraic manipulations such as multiplying or dividing by coefficients to align coefficients of variables.

Misconception 931: Does not understand where to place inputs/ operations/ outputs when drawing a function machine
Expanded misconception:
Does not accurately relate inputs, operations, and outputs when working with functions, including difficulties with correctly sequencing inputs and outputs, misunderstanding the role of variables, or incorrectly applying operation order within function machines, flowcharts, or equations.

Misconception 932: Adds instead of multiplies when calculating total frequency
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the multiplication principle when determining total frequencies, probabilities, or combinations in scenarios where events or objects are being grouped or selected, instead mistakenly adding the frequencies or counts.

Misconception 933: Does not know the meaning of corresponding
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the correspondence or relationship between numerical values or quantities and their mathematical representations or vice versa.

Misconception 934: Thinks the subtraction sign means divide
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets the subtraction sign as division sign, and/or has difficulty understanding the opposite operation implications in expressions, resulting in replacing subtraction with division or not considering changes in magnitude due to the subtraction operation.

Misconception 935: Fails to use the properties of simple shapes to work out missing lengths
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply or recognize properties such as symmetry, complementarity, and supplementarity, or has not used the relationships between lengths and angles within or between similar and/or congruent shapes.

Misconception 936: Thinks the first term in a sequence is the term before the sequence begins (0th term)
Expanded misconception:
Thinks the first term in a sequence is the term before the sequence begins (0th term) and misunderstands the correspondence between term number and its position in the sequence.

Misconception 937: When adding or subtracting surds, simplifies the surds correctly but puts the digits in the answer the wrong way round
Expanded misconception:
Inaccurately applies the commutative property of addition when combining like terms or surds, resulting in a reversed or incorrect placement of digits within the answer.

Misconception 938: Thinks 1 square metre = 100,000 cubic centimetres
Expanded misconception:
"Does not understand the difference between area and volume units, assuming a direct conversion is possible or incorrectly equating one square unit to a large quantity of cubic units."

Misconception 939: Describes position to term rule using term to term rule
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets the positional relationship between terms to be an example of term-to-term relationships, rather than adhering to the term-to-term processing on one side (typically left or right) and then processing across, thereby failing to consider alternation or sequential processing in position-to-term type relationships.

Misconception 940: When writing a function as a function machine confuses the input and the output.
Expanded misconception:
When writing a function as a function machine, confuses the input and the output, typically resulting in incorrectly swapping the domains and ranges, inverting the function, or improperly mapping input values to output values.

Misconception 941: Does not recognise that subtracting a larger number from a smaller number will give a negative answer
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the concept of negative numbers or relative size in subtraction, and may automatically apply the standard subtraction algorithm regardless of the numerical values or their relationship (i.e., subtracting a larger number from a smaller number).

Misconception 942: Thinks you need to include the total as well as the individual parts when asked to write a ratio
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands ratio concept, incorrectly including or combining the total with individual parts when representing ratios, which can lead to duplicate or exaggerated component values.

Misconception 943: Divides by (n-2) rather than n when finding an interior angle
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to apply the correct divisor when finding an interior angle of a polygon, specifically dividing by (n-2) instead of n, where n is the number of sides."

Misconception 944: When dividing involving a power of 10, gives an answer 10 times bigger than it should be
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly simplify fractions or perform division when a denominator is a power of 10, consistently resulting in an answer that is one or more powers of 10 (e.g., 10 times, 100 times) larger than expected.

Misconception 945: Does not think a number can be a multiple of itself
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the concept of a multiple as any product of a whole number, including the number itself, often leading to errors in identifying multiples, factors, or divisibility rules.

Misconception 946: Believes a proportion of a whole is whole/part not part/whole
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct ratio or proportion formula (part/whole), instead incorrectly using the whole/part ratio, leading to incorrect calculations in various mathematical contexts, such as fractions, decimals, percentages, and ratios.

Misconception 947: Makes the number negative when asked to find the reciprocal
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between taking the reciprocal and changing the sign of a number, often incorrectly making the number negative when asked to find the reciprocal.

Misconception 948: Believes squaring is the same as adding two
Expanded misconception:
Believes squaring (e.g., 2^2) is the same as adding two (e.g., 2+2), implying a lack of understanding of exponentiation and its difference from addition.

Misconception 949: Confuses midpoint and gradient between two points
Expanded misconception:
Confuses midpoint and gradient between two points, specifically interpreting the gradient between two points as the average y-coordinate or position of the midpoint and/or incorrectly applying midpoint formula to calculate the gradient or vice versa.

Misconception 950: Thinks there are 5 days in a week
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly recalls or applies basic time or calendar facts, assuming for example, there are 5 days in a week, 3 weeks in a month, or a year is less than or greater than 365 days.

Misconception 951: Only recognises the positive form of a quadratic graph
Expanded misconception:
Only recognises or applies the positive form of a quadratic graph, failing to consider or account for the possibility of the parabola opening downwards or other transformations.

Misconception 952: Adds the index to the number
Expanded misconception:
Adds or ignores the exponent/index in an expression, or fails to apply the correct mathematical operation to exponents/indices, such as multiplying or applying the product rule rather than applying the power rule, when dealing with expressions involving exponents or indices.

Misconception 953: Believes you cannot find the modal category from a pie chart unless given the total frequency
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that the modal category in a pie chart can be determined by identifying the largest section or slice, regardless of the actual numerical frequencies, and instead believes that the total frequency or absolute values of the categories are always required.

Misconception 954: Confuses adding 3 with cubing, in algebra
Expanded misconception:
Confuses exponentiation with different arithmetic operations, such as mistaking raising a number to the power of 3 with adding, subtracting or multiplying by 3 in algebra and numerical expressions.

Misconception 955: When multiplying, treats digits in other columns as if they are units
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for place value when multiplying, treating digits in other columns as either units or applying multiplication improperly across different place values.

Misconception 956: Thinks that fractions involving a fraction as the denominator can just be read as mixed numbers
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that fractions or expressions involving a fraction as the denominator can be simplified merely by converting the fraction in the denominator to a mixed number, failing to actually perform the required division or applying incorrect division rules.

Misconception 957: Believes the mean must be an integer
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that the arithmetic mean can be a decimal or a fraction, and incorrectly assumes the answer must be a whole number or rounds the result to the nearest integer, especially in situations involving non-integer or non-whole number calculations.

Misconception 958: Misunderstands how to calculate the upper bound in subtraction
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly calculate the upper bound, such as, consistently subtracting the larger number from the smaller number, forgetting to adjust the upper bound after regrouping, incorrectly identifying the upper bound, or neglecting to reposition the upper bound after resizing the original quantities in subtraction problems.

Misconception 959: Believes it is impossible to subtract a bigger number from a smaller number
Expanded misconception:
Believes it is impossible to subtract a bigger number from a smaller number, or thinks that subtraction only works when the first number is larger than or equal to the second number, potentially due to a lack of understanding about negative numbers or the concept of a "difference" in subtraction.

Misconception 960: Divides by the index
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for the operation being performed by the exponent or the power (often mistakenly simplifying or ignoring the operation), or incorrectly assumes an exponent is simply an index that needs to be divided by, rather than an instruction indicating how many times to multiply the base.

Misconception 961: Does not spot the difference between a gradual change of gradient and 2 distinct line segments with different constant gradients
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between a continuous function representing a gradual change in gradient and multiple discrete line segments representing distinct constant gradients or piecewise functions.

Misconception 962: Thinks 1 cubic metre = 1000 cubic centimetres
Expanded misconception:
Confuses cubic metre unit conversions, specifically incorrectly recalling that 1 cubic metre equals 1000 cubic centimetres (instead of 1,000,000 cubic centimetres) due to misunderstanding unit exponentiation or linear thinking when converting between volume units.

Misconception 963: Thinks there are 50 minutes in 1 hour
Expanded misconception:
"Misconceives time conversions by incorrectly assuming non-standard time unit equivalencies, such as believing there are 50 minutes in 1 hour, instead of 60 minutes, leading to errors in calculations involving time conversions and unit conversions."

Misconception 964: When dividing involving a power of 10, gives an answer 10 times smaller than it should be
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly handle division when the divisor is a power of 10, typically resulting in an answer that is 10 times smaller than it should be, often due to a misunderstanding of the relationship between powers of 10 and the corresponding place value shifts in division.

Misconception 965: Adds the numerators when working out a combined probability
Expanded misconception:
Adds the numerators when working out a combined probability without considering the need for a common denominator or correct application of probability rules.

Misconception 966: Gives an answer of 0 when there are no solutions to an equation.
Expanded misconception:
Gives an answer of 0, or ignores certain variables/terms, when there are no real solutions, or no finite number of solutions, to an equation, failing to recognize that some equations may have no solution, infinite solutions, or complex solutions.

Misconception 967: When solving simultaneous equations graphically, only gives one of two solutions
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or account for all solutions when solving simultaneous equations, including when lines intersect at a single point (correctly identifying only one solution) or when lines are coincident (not identifying any solutions) or when trying to solve inconsistent equations (resulting in no solution), instead relying solely on graph visualization to provide only one solution.

Misconception 968: Confuses powers and multiples
Expanded misconception:
Confuses powers and multiples, such as mistakenly interpreting 2^3 as 2*3, or misapplying exponent rules by treating powers as repeated multiplication of a different number (e.g., 3^2 as 3*3*2), or improperly handling multiple as single values, (e.g., two twelves as twelve twenty-four) in calculation.

Misconception 969: Believes you can subtract from inside brackets without expanding when solving an equation
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly expand or distribute when working with expressions inside brackets or parentheses, often attempting to perform operations within the brackets as if they were isolated, rather than following the order of operations.

Misconception 970: In inverse proportion, believes k is the total of the two variables 
Expanded misconception:
In inverse proportion (relationships where y = k/x), believes that the constant of proportionality (k) is the sum or the product of the variables themselves, rather than a separate constant that the relationship is scaled by.

Misconception 971: Believes that adding a negative will increase the value of the original number
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct rules of arithmetic when dealing with addition and subtraction involving negative numbers; mistakenly believes that adding a negative number increases the value of the original number rather than decreasing it.

Misconception 972: Does not realise that if the horizontal and vertical measurements of a diagonal line segment switch it remains the same size
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the concept of similar triangles or the properties of right-angled triangles, specifically failing to realise that if the horizontal (x) and vertical (y) measurements of a diagonal line segment or the coordinates of the points defining it are switched, it remains the same size, having the same length and same diagonal width, as the resulting shape or line segment will be congruent.

Misconception 973: Finds area of whole circle rather than area of sector
Expanded misconception:
Finds area of entire geometric shape (such as circle, triangle, rectangle) rather than the specific part of the shape (such as sector, portion of triangle, or fractional area) required by the problem, often resulting in overestimation or incorrect application of formulas.

Misconception 974: Believes multiplying a positive by a negative gives a positive answer
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the rule for multiplying numbers of different signs, leading to incorrect assumptions about the signs of products, typically believing multiplying a positive by a negative gives a positive answer, rather than following the multiplication rule that a positive times a negative equals a negative.

Misconception 975: When solving an equation, multiplies instead of dividing 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly apply order of operations, specifically by performing multiplication instead of division when the two operations are applicable, often resulting in the reversal of the quotient or product.

Misconception 976: Thinks a Fibonacci sequence must start with 1, 1
Expanded misconception:
Misconceives the definition of a Fibonacci sequence, believing it requires initial values of 1 and 1, rather than any two consecutive numbers, and potentially overlooking generalizations or variations of the sequence.

Misconception 977: Confuses the sign for parallel lines as being dashes
Expanded misconception:
"Confuses the symbol for parallel lines (‖) with other similar-looking symbols or notation, such as dashes (-) or equals signs (=), leading to incorrect interpretation or application of geometric properties and relationships."

Misconception 978: When completing the square, believes the constant in the bracket is the coefficient of x
Expanded misconception:
When completing the square for expressions in the form ax^2 + bx + c, believes the constant in the bracket (i.e., the term within the parentheses that is squared) is the coefficient of x, rather than b/2, resulting in an incorrect binomial expression that doesn't maintain equivalence with the original expression.

Misconception 979: Thinks can combine the numerator and denominator after simplifying an algebraic fraction
Expanded misconception:
Thinks they can combine the numerator and denominator after simplifying an algebraic fraction, failing to follow order of operations and/or incorrectly applying equivalence properties of fractions.

Misconception 980: Hasn't lined up the place value columns when writing out the calculation
Expanded misconception:
Hasn't consistently lined up the place value columns, or misplaced the decimal point when writing out a multi-digit calculation, potentially resulting in incorrect regrouping or misaligned arithmetic operations.

Misconception 981: Believes inequalities cannot be combined
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct rules of inequalities when combining or manipulating them, such as incorrectly flipping the direction of the inequality sign when multiplying or dividing by negative numbers, neglecting to maintain the direction of inequalities under certain mathematical operations, or insisting that inequalities cannot be combined with other mathematical operations or with each other correctly.

Misconception 982: Calculates perimeter when asked for area
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly calculates or confuses one-dimensional measures (perimeter, length, or distance) with two-dimensional measures (area), failing to consider the differences between these geometric concepts when solving problems.

Misconception 983: When the dividend is a mixed number and the divisor is an integer, thinks they only divide the fraction by the integer.
Expanded misconception:
When the dividend is a mixed number and the divisor is an integer, thinks they only divide the fractional part of the mixed number by the integer, neglecting to convert the mixed number to an improper fraction first, and often resulting in a calculation error.

Misconception 984: Writes the numerator of an improper fraction as the number of wholes when converting to a mixed number
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the division of the numerator by the denominator when converting an improper fraction to a mixed number, instead directly writing the numerator as the number of wholes.

Misconception 985: Believes that repeated percentage change is equivalent to a single change by the sum of the percentages
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that repeated percentage changes have a compounding effect, incorrectly assuming that multiple consecutive percentage changes are equivalent to a single change by the sum of the percentages, rather than accounting for the multiplication of factors when applying each change sequentially.

Misconception 986: Does not understand that a remainder can be written as a fraction
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that a remainder represents a part of a whole and can be expressed as a fraction or an improper fraction in certain operations, such as division.

Misconception 987: Believes that the order of a worded calculation should be changed to follow BIDMAS 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to maintain the original context or operation order of a worded calculation, instead rearranging it to follow the conventional order of operations (BODMAS/BIDMAS), often resulting in an incorrect calculation by prioritizing operations based on the formula over the actual problem requirements.

Misconception 988: When converting a percentage to a decimal, just writes all the digits after a decimal point.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly apply the conversion rule of dividing the percentage value by 100 when converting a percentage to a decimal, instead simply placing a decimal point at an arbitrary position, often omitting the division step or misinterpreting the decimal position.

Misconception 989: Does not understand that area under a speed time graph gives distance because distance = speed x time
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the fundamental concept that the area under a speed-time graph represents the total distance traveled, often misunderstanding the relationship distance = speed x time or incorrectly interpreting the graph as representing a different physical quantity.

Misconception 990: Does not realise you can use equivalent fractions to break fractions up into smaller divisions
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that fractions can be expressed as equivalent fractions with different denominators, which can facilitate calculations or simplifications by breaking fractions down into more manageable or easily comparable components, such as dividing a fraction into smaller divisions, finding a common denominator, or simplifying fractions before performing arithmetic operations.

Misconception 991: Believes that when finding area under graph you can use the upper y value rather than average of upper and lower
Expanded misconception:
"Believes that when finding area under a graph in a numerical integration problem, can use the y-value of either the upper or lower boundary of the subinterval, or the y-value of an endpoint, rather than approximating the area using the average or midpoint of the subinterval."

Misconception 992: Thinks they find the length of a line between 2 points by adding coordinates, rather than subtracting
Expanded misconception:
Thinks they can find the length of a line between two points by incorrectly applying arithmetic operations to the coordinates (such as adding instead of subtracting), rather than using the correct method of subtracting the corresponding coordinates and applying the distance formula.

Misconception 993: Believes the length of the arms influence the size of the angle
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that angle size is determined by the amount of rotation between the sides, and is invariant to the lengths of the arms, mistakenly believing that longer arms correspond to larger angles and shorter arms to smaller angles.

Misconception 994: When completing the square in the form (x - a)^2 + b, believes b = a^2 + the original constant
Expanded misconception:
When completing the square in the form (x - a)^2 + b, incorrectly believes that the value of b is equal to the square of the value being subtracted from x (a^2) plus the original constant term, rather than a^2 being incorporated into the squared term and b being the adjusted constant.

Misconception 995: Believes the lowest common multiple is found my multiplying the numbers together
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply correct methods for finding the lowest common multiple, instead typically multiplying the numbers together, which is the method for finding the least common multiple of the factors of those numbers or a common multiple that is not necessarily the lowest.

Misconception 996: Does not know that units of volume are usually cubed
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognize or apply the conventional cubing of units when expressing volume measurements in algebraic problems, often leading to incorrect calculations or order-of-operations mistakes when dealing with units such as cubic meters, cubic feet, or other volume metrics.

Misconception 997: Subtracts instead of multiplies
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct operation, mistakenly substituting multiplication with subtraction when the numerical context or problem specifics require multiplication, leading to incorrect solutions.

Misconception 998: When the final stage of solving a problem is "variable to a power" = constant, assume the constant is the answer, without finding the root.
Expanded misconception:
When the final stage of solving a problem involves simplifying a variable raised to a power, assumes the final result is the constant itself, neglecting to isolate the variable or find the corresponding root if necessary, often due to a lack of understanding in handling exponentiation or root calculation in the context of algebraic equation-solving.

Misconception 999: Confuses the method of selection with the group selected in statistics
Expanded misconception:
"Confuses the method of selection (e.g., random sampling, stratified sampling, self-selection) with the group selected in statistics (e.g., sample population, target population), leading to incorrect conclusions about the data or statistical analysis."

Misconception 1000: Draws false conclusion from a pie chart
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly interpret proportions and relationships between data segments in a pie chart, potentially leading to incorrect inferences or conclusions due to misuse of visual information or neglect of actual numerical values.

Misconception 1001: Does not know how to divide a fraction by an integer
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct rules for dividing fractions, such as inverting the integer and changing the division operation to multiplication or treating the integer as a fraction with a denominator of 1, and instead performs incorrect procedures, including, but not limited to, dividing the numerator by the integer or multiplying the numerator by the integer.

Misconception 1002: Thinks the elevation is the plan view of a shape
Expanded misconception:
Thinks the elevation is the plan view of a shape, tends to confuse and misinterpret visual representations, failing to recognize that the elevation and plan view provide distinct and orthogonal views of an object or structure.

Misconception 1003: Believes the equation of a vertical line is in the form y = ax, where a is the x intercept
Expanded misconception:
Misconceives the equation of a vertical line as in the form y = ax, failing to recognize that a vertical line's equation is actually in the form x = a, where a is the constant x value defining the line.

Misconception 1004: Thinks that a number in front of a bracket means divide
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that a number preceding parentheses or brackets in a mathematical expression indicates division (e.g., incorrectly interprets '3(4)' as '3 divided by 4' rather than '3 times 4').

Misconception 1005: Carries out operations from left to right regardless of priority order, unless brackets are used
Expanded misconception:
Ignores or is unaware of the order of operations (PEMDAS/BODMAS), failing to prioritize calculations within brackets, exponents, multiplication and division (from left to right), and addition and subtraction (from left to right), when no brackets are present, often relying solely on the left-to-right sequence of operations.

Misconception 1006: Believes the mean is something divided by the number of categories given
Expanded misconception:
"Believes the mean is a value divided by the number of categories, groups, or subgroups, rather than the total number of data points, observations, or items being summarized."

Misconception 1007: Does not connect rotational symmetry to number of sides/vertices
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to understand or apply the relationship between rotational symmetry and the number of sides or vertices of a regular polygon, or incorrectly generalizes this relationship to non-regular polygons."

Misconception 1008: When dividing an integer by a fraction, thinks they multiply numerator and denominator by the integer
Expanded misconception:
Inverts the correct order of operations when dividing an integer by a fraction, incorrectly multiplies or inappropriately manipulates the numerator and denominator, or misunderstands equivalent ratios and thinks they need to multiply the numerator and denominator of the fraction by the integer instead of performing the correct division operation.

Misconception 1009: Does not know the meaning of bisect
Expanded misconception:
Does not know the meaning of bisect or apply it incorrectly, such as failing to find the midpoint, misunderstanding that bisecting a line means dividing it into two equal parts, or not recognizing that bisecting an angle means dividing it into two equal angles.

Misconception 1010: Believes the largest number in a set of numbers is always their highest common factor
Expanded misconception:
Believes the largest number in a set of numbers or most visible operand is always the result of a calculation or is more influential in determining the solution, disregarding mathematical operations and properties such as order of operations, equivalence, and properties of arithmetic.

Misconception 1011: Does not know the distributive property
Expanded misconception:
Does not know or incorrectly applies the distributive property of multiplication over addition and subtraction, resulting in failure to expand or factor expressions correctly, such as not recognizing a(b+c) as a*b + a*c.

Misconception 1012: Squares when asked to find the square root
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the operations of finding the square root and squaring a number, incorrectly applies the squaring procedure when the problem asks for the square root, and vice versa.

Misconception 1013: Answers as if there are 100 minutes in an hour
Expanded misconception:
Ignores the base unit system of 60 seconds in a minute and 60 minutes in an hour when converting between time units, leading to incorrect proportional reasoning and calculations.

Misconception 1014: Believes the frequency of the midpoint of a group is half the total frequency of the group
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly assumes that the median of a dataset is always calculated by averaging the total frequency of the dataset and then halving it, rather than finding the middle value of a ranked dataset.

Misconception 1015: When answering questions regarding adding items to get a new ratio, looks at the difference between the numbers in the original and new ratio rather than finding the size of a part
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that when adding or combining items to obtain a new ratio or proportion, it's necessary to find the absolute value or total quantity of a part or the entire set, rather than merely considering the difference or relative change between the numbers in the original and new ratio.

Misconception 1016: Confuses the hundreds and hundred thousands place value columns
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the placement of digits within large numbers, specifically misinterpreting the hundreds and hundred thousands place value columns in calculations involving decimal shifts, order of magnitude, or place value operations.

Misconception 1017: Does not understand how to solve linear two step equations
Expanded misconception:
Applies inverse operations incorrectly or in the wrong order when attempting to isolate a variable in a linear two-step equation, or fails to recognize the need for multiple steps to solve for the variable.

Misconception 1018: Thinks the fraction 1/n can express sharing n items between any number of people
Expanded misconception:
Thinks the fraction 1/n can express sharing n items between any number of people, rather than expressing one equal part out of n total equal parts, leading to confusion in understanding the difference between the concept of fractions and division.

Misconception 1019: Believes class width = frequency density
Expanded misconception:
"Believes that the class width of a histogram or bar chart directly represents the frequency density of each class, resulting in an incorrect interpretation or calculation of probabilities, frequencies, or other statistical measures, when in fact, frequency density is calculated as frequency divided by class width."

Misconception 1020: Believes it's not possible to solve simultaneous equations graphically
Expanded misconception:
Believes it's not possible to solve simultaneous equations graphically because they misunderstand the purpose of graphical representations in mathematics, fail to recognize that simultaneous equations can be represented as intersecting lines on a graph, or lack experience with applying graphical methods to solve systems of linear equations.

Misconception 1021: Believes an outlier is not determined by distance from the mean
Expanded misconception:
Believes an outlier is not determined by distance from the mean and instead relies on more simplistic or superficial characteristics such as visual appearance on a graph, position at the ends of a data set, or incorrectly applying threshold values, thus leading to incorrect identification or rejection of outliers.

Misconception 1022: Believes you multiply matching bases when carrying out a multiplication
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the exponentiation of both bases and coefficients in multiplication, instead incorrectly applying a simplistic or base-matching approach to exponents or coefficients when multiplying powers or terms with the same or similar base.

Misconception 1023: Believes that the complement of a set in a triple Venn consists of the elements that only belong to one other set
Expanded misconception:
Believes that the complement of a set in a Venn diagram consists of the elements that only belong to one or more of the other sets, but does not include elements that are common to multiple sets or elements outside the sets.

Misconception 1024: When solving an equation thinks they add when in fact they need to multiply
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct operation to solve an equation, such as mistakenly adding numbers when multiplication is required, or vice versa, often due to superficial recognition of similar problem structures or misinterpreted mathematical signs.

Misconception 1025: Forgets to include the intersection value when asked for total value of a set in a Venn diagram
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for overlapping values when calculating total quantities in Venn diagrams, leading to incorrect summation in intersection-based problems.

Misconception 1026: Does not reverse the order of operations when finding the input of a function machine
Expanded misconception:
Applies the order of operations incorrectly by failing to reverse or adjust the sequence when finding the input or inverse of a function or operation, or solving an equation, leading to incorrect identification of variables or parameters.

Misconception 1027: Believes the median is the frequency for the middle category in a table
Expanded misconception:
Fails to differentiate between measures of central tendency (mean, median, mode) and incorrectly assumes that the median in a grouped data set or table refers to the frequency of the middle category rather than the middle value when the data is arranged in order.

Misconception 1028: Multiplies up without finding unit price in proportion question
Expanded misconception:
Fails to find unit price or rate before applying proportion operations, often leading to incorrect multiplication or scale factor application, and neglecting to consider the ratio or relationship between quantities in proportion problems.

Misconception 1029: Does not understand that a line graph shows a relationship between 2 variables
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or interpret the fundamental purpose of line graphs, which is to represent the correlation between two variables, often overlooking the x-axis representing an independent variable, and the y-axis as the dependent variable, resulting in incorrect analysis or predictions of a given data set.

Misconception 1030: When dividing numbers with the same base divides the base numbers and the powers
Expanded misconception:
"When dividing numbers with the same base, incorrectly divides the base numbers and the powers independently, rather than first subtracting the exponents and keeping the base the same, as per the quotient rule of exponents."

Misconception 1031: Believes raising a negative number to an odd power will give a positive answer
Expanded misconception:
Misconception: Fails to recognize or apply the property that raising a negative number to an odd power results in a negative answer, and/or incorrectly assumes a change in sign when applying exponentiation to negative bases with odd exponents.

Misconception 1032: Confuses cube rooting with squaring a number
Expanded misconception:
Confuses cube rooting (finding the cube root of a number) with squaring a number (finding the result of multiplying the number by itself), leading to errors when identifying the correct operation or solving expressions involving powers or roots, such as ³√(x) versus x².

Misconception 1033: Does not know that you can have a decimal percentage
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand or recognize that percentages can be represented as decimal values or does not know how to convert between percent and decimal forms.

Misconception 1034: believes the complement of a set consists of elements which only belong to one set
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to recognize that the complement of a set consists of all elements which belong to the universal set but not to the original set, often mistakenly thinking it includes only elements that are exclusive to one set, ignoring the possibility of elements belonging to the complementary set that are also part of other related sets."

Misconception 1035: When multiplying a decimal less than 1 by an integer, gives an answer 10 times smaller than it should be
Expanded misconception:
When multiplying a decimal less than 1 by an integer, incorrectly shifts the decimal point one place to the left, resulting in an answer 10 times smaller than the correct product, due to a lack of understanding of decimal multiplication.

Misconception 1036: Only gives 1 value of x for a given value of y, when reading from a parabolic graph
Expanded misconception:
Only gives 1 corresponding value of y for a given x, or 1 value of x for a given y, when reading from a graph that represents an inverse relationship or a non-linear relationship such as a parabola, quadratic equation, or other non-linear equations of degree two or higher, despite the possibility of multiple corresponding values.

Misconception 1037: Believes time = speed x distance
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the correct formulas for calculating time, speed, and distance (e.g., t = d/s or t = d/r) and incorrectly applies the formula distance = speed x time, often due to misunderstanding or misapplying the relationship between these variables.

Misconception 1038: Thinks reciprocal button raises to a power
Expanded misconception:
Thinks the reciprocal button (1/x) raises a number to an inverse power instead of taking its reciprocal, leading to incorrect calculations involving exponentiation and root operations.

Misconception 1039: Believes finding a percentage of an amount involves adding the percentage to 10% of the amount
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that finding a percentage of an amount involves multiplying the amount by the percentage expressed as a decimal, and instead relies on an incomplete or incorrect method, such as adding the percentage to 10% of the amount, resulting in incorrect calculations when dealing with percentages.

Misconception 1040: Does not measure bearing clockwise from the north line
Expanded misconception:
Does not properly measure and/or apply angular directions, specifically neglecting to consider the starting point, direction, or unit of measurement when determining bearing, often resulting in clockwise or counter-clockwise errors from standard references (such as the north line or a given reference line).

Misconception 1041: Does not know a rhombus is a type of parallelogram 
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognize a rhombus as a specific type of parallelogram with properties that include opposite equal sides and opposite angles, often resulting in misapplication of parallelogram or quadrilateral properties instead of rhombus-specific properties.

Misconception 1042: When dividing a number written in standard form by an integer, thinks you divide the power on the ten rather than the number component.
Expanded misconception:
When dividing a number written in scientific or standard form by an integer, fails to apply exponent rules correctly and instead of dividing the numerical component, incorrectly modifies the power of 10 or applies incorrect operations to the exponent.

Misconception 1043: Mixes up cubes and cuboids
Expanded misconception:
Mixes up concepts of 3D shapes, specifically fails to distinguish between cubes and cuboids in terms of definition, properties, and applications, and may incorrectly apply formulas or characteristics intended for one shape to the other.

Misconception 1044: Misremembers the quadratic formula
Expanded misconception:
Misremembers the quadratic formula, such as incorrectly recalling the coefficient of the squared term or the sign between terms, substituting variables or values incorrectly, or incorrectly applying the plus-minus symbol to both roots.

Misconception 1045: Believes reading from a cumulative frequency diagram will give the frequency above a certain value, rather than below
Expanded misconception:
Believes reading from a cumulative frequency diagram will give the frequency above a certain value, rather than the cumulative frequency below, or in other words, incorrectly interprets the diagram as showing the number of data points exceeding a value instead of the total number of data points at or below that value.

Misconception 1046: Does not understand remainders in division
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly apply division operations, specifically not understanding or misapplying the concept of remainders, which may result in incorrect calculations when dividing numbers, including but not limited to, ignoring remainders, rounding down or up incorrectly, or misinterpreting the quotient or remainder in a division problem.

Misconception 1047: Estimates a measurement on a diagram rather than working it out
Expanded misconception:
Fails to perform calculations or follow procedural steps, instead relying on visual estimation, intuition, or mental shortcuts, often resulting in an incorrect answer.

Misconception 1048: When adding/subtracting fractions, adds/subtracts the denominators and multiplies the numerators
Expanded misconception:
When adding or subtracting fractions, incorrectly applies whole number arithmetic by adding or subtracting the denominators and multiplying the numerators, rather than finding a common denominator and following the correct order of operations for fraction addition and subtraction.

Misconception 1049: Misunderstands the process of making a variable the subject of an equation
Expanded misconception:
"Lacks understanding of order of operations and/or appropriate algebraic manipulations required to isolate a variable on one side of an equation, often resulting in failure to apply inverse operations correctly or add, subtract, multiply or divide both sides of the equation by the same incorrect value or in the incorrect order."

Misconception 1050: Thinks there are 10g in a kilogram
Expanded misconception:
Failed to understand the metric system conversion or incorrectly recalled the conversion factor between units, often resulting in misunderstanding that there are 10 gram units in a kilogram, rather than 1000 grams or 10 grams = 0.01 kilograms.

Misconception 1051: When multiplying by a multiple of 10, gives an answer 10 times bigger than it should be
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the concept that multiplying by a multiple of 10 simply involves appending zeros to the multiplicand, resulting in an answer that is not 10 times the correct result but rather the correct result shifted by a place value of 10.

Misconception 1052: Uses length scale factor for volume without cubing
Expanded misconception:
Applies linear scaling inappropriately to measurement units that require exponential application, such as converting between area or volume units without correctly squaring or cubing the scale factor, resulting in incorrect calculations.

Misconception 1053: Believes that when converting ratio to fractions, the denominator is the product of the parts of the ratio
Expanded misconception:
Believes that when converting a ratio to a fraction, the denominator is the product of the parts of the ratio, rather than the total number of parts or the larger quantity associated with the context of the ratio.

Misconception 1054: Performs subtraction in wrong order
Expanded misconception:
Fails to follow the correct order of operations, specifically performing subtractions from left to right instead of following the correct sequence as required by the context, often resulting in incorrectly swapping or reversing the order of the minuend and subtrahend.

Misconception 1055: Does not notice all changes in a sequence of visual patterns
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize a pattern change, interruption, or adaptation in a sequence, including missing or misinterpreting a single alteration, a shift in sequence direction, or a modification in the pattern's relationship or underlying rule.

Misconception 1056: Chooses an incorrect point in between the shapes when describing a 90 or 270 degree rotation
Expanded misconception:
Identifies an incorrect point of rotation or incorrectly measures the angle of rotation when describing 90 or 270 degree rotations in rotational transformations, such as choosing an endpoint of a shape segment instead of a vertex as the point of rotation or confusing the direction or measure of rotation.

Misconception 1057: Finds the unit fraction when asked to find a non-unit fraction of an amount
Expanded misconception:
Inappropriately identifies the unit fraction as the solution when asked to find a non-unit fraction of a quantity, possibly due to a lack of understanding of fractions as part of a whole or the concept of equivalent ratios.

Misconception 1058: Believes that in the nth term of a descending sequence, b is the difference between the term and the positive multiple of n
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply or understands incorrectly the relationship between terms in a sequence, specifically in a descending sequence, believing that n (or a multiple thereof) is subtracted by a fixed common difference b to obtain the nth term, rather than correctly applying the formula (e.g., an = a1 + (n-1)d for an arithmetic sequence, where d can be negative for a descending sequence).

Misconception 1059: Does not know the definition of the range
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand or apply the definition of the range of a data set, which may manifest in incorrect identification of the highest and lowest values, failure to account for outliers, or misinterpretation of the concept of range in the context of statistical analysis.

Misconception 1060: Thinks positive values in a column vector denote movement left and down (and vice-versa for negative values).
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the direction of movement in column vectors, incorrectly assuming that positive values correspond to left and down movements, and negative values correspond to right and up movements, or vice versa.

Misconception 1061: When asked to rotate by 90 degrees rotates by 180 degrees
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the direction and magnitude of rotation when applying rotational transformations, resulting in incorrect changes of orientation, such as rotating by 180 degrees instead of 90 degrees or incorrectly determining the clockwise or counterclockwise direction.

Misconception 1062: When using the capture-recapture method of sampling, believes you divide the number caught second by the number marked second to find the best estimate of population size
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly apply the capture-recapture method of sampling by incorrectly identifying the number of individuals caught or marked on one or both occasions, and incorrectly calculates the population size estimate by confusing the number marked and/or caught in the initial and secondary capture events, often specifically dividing the number caught second by the number marked second instead of applying the correct formula (N = (M * C) / R), where N is the population size, M is the number marked initially, C is the number caught second, and R is the number of marked individuals recaptured in the secondary event.

Misconception 1063: Adds rather than multiplies when asked for a total
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly interprets multiplication as repeated addition, resulting in an incorrect sum rather than the product, especially in problems that involve multiplication and addition operations.

Misconception 1064: Confuses the union and intersection of inequalities
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the union and intersection of inequalities, specifically, mistakenly applies the rules for combining compound inequalities, such as incorrectly treating 'or' as 'and' and vice versa, resulting in the incorrect identification of solution sets.

Misconception 1065: Does not recognise a unit of area in word form
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognise or apply correct unit conversions in word form (e.g., square units, square centimeters, square meters, etc.), particularly in problems involving area calculations.

Misconception 1066: Does not think that the compensation strategy for the multiplication of 2 numbers is valid.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or apply the distributive property and other compensation strategies, such as using the nearest ten or hundreds, to efficiently multiply multi-digit numbers or perform mental calculations, often resulting in incorrect or laborious calculations.

Misconception 1067: Confuses measures of turn (ie. degrees) and measures of length
Expanded misconception:
Confuses measurements of angle or rotation (e.g., degrees, radians) with measurements of length or distance (e.g., centimeters, meters, inches), failing to recognize the fundamental difference between units of angle and units of length.

Misconception 1068: Believes the fx column for calculating mean is value + frequency
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly apply the frequency concept when calculating the mean, mistakenly believing that the fx column represents the sum of a value added to its frequency rather than the product of a value multiplied by its frequency.

Misconception 1069: Assumes an angle is the size that it looks in the (not to scale) diagram
Expanded misconception:
Ignores scale indication and inaccurately perceives or utilizes angle measurements, lengths, or proportions directly from a diagram, even when explicitly stated as 'not to scale' or lacking a clear scale indicator.

Misconception 1070: Believes that dividing by 10 gives 1%
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands the relationship between place value and decimal conversions, specifically believing that dividing by 10 gives a percentage or that shifting the decimal point only one place is equivalent to finding one percent.

Misconception 1071: Confuses obtuse and acute angles
Expanded misconception:
Confuses properties or definitions of acute (less than 90 degrees) and obtuse (greater than 90 degrees) angles, potentially leading to incorrect classification or application of angle-related concepts.

Misconception 1072: Multiplies by the index
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly assumes 'index' or superscript in mathematical expressions is an instruction to perform multiplication instead of indicating exponentiation.

Misconception 1073: Believes if you add the same value to all numbers in the dataset the range will change
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the invariability of range when a constant value is added to all data points in a dataset, mistakenly believing that the range will change as a result of this operation, when in reality, adding the same value to all numbers shifts the entire dataset but leaves the relative differences between the numbers, and thus the range, unchanged.

Misconception 1074: Multiplies rather than divides
Expanded misconception:
The misconception is: Multiplies rather than divides when a division operation is indicated, failing to recognize the effect of the division symbol or operation within a given mathematical expression.

Misconception 1075: When asked to add fractions uses the method for multiplying fractions instead.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the difference between adding and multiplying fractions, applying the procedure for multiplying fractions (i.e., multiplying numerators and denominators or the cross-multiplication method in the case of fractions with different denominators, then simplifying or finding the least common denominator) to addition problems.

Misconception 1076: Cancels digits when dividing
Expanded misconception:
Ignores or improperly applies the division concept, possibly cancelling digits when dividing, instead of considering the actual quotient or remainder.

Misconception 1077: Believes all multiples of odd numbers are odd
Expanded misconception:
Believes all multiples of odd numbers are odd, failing to recognize that the parity (evenness or oddness) of the product depends on the other factor, resulting in an incorrect assumption that operations involving odd numbers always yield odd results.

Misconception 1078: Ignores numbers written as words in worded questions, and just calculates using those written in digits
Expanded misconception:
Ignores non-numeric representations of numbers in worded questions, such as numbers written as words or implied in the context, and only performs calculations using explicitly stated numeric values.

Misconception 1079: Believes factors add to give a number
Expanded misconception:
Believes factors add to give a product, rather than multiply to give a product.

Misconception 1080: Believes there are 20 minutes in an hour
Expanded misconception:
Misconceives time units, specifically believing that a standard hour consists of 20 or some other incorrect number of minutes, rather than 60 minutes, possibly leading to underestimation or miscalculation of time intervals or durations in various time-based problems.

Misconception 1081: When subtracting fractions, divides the numerators
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to find a common denominator and instead misapplies division rules; when subtracting fractions, divides the numerators and/or denominators rather than finding a common denominator or equivalently multiplying by the reciprocal of the divisor."

Misconception 1082: When multiplying with negative numbers, assumes any negative sign can be ignored
Expanded misconception:
Ignores the effect of a negative sign in a multiplication operation, as if all numbers were positive, resulting in a positive answer regardless of the original signs of the numbers.

Misconception 1083: Does not know the meaning of uniform cross section
Expanded misconception:
Has a limited or inaccurate understanding of 'uniform cross-section,' possibly resulting in miscalculations or incorrect applications in geometry and measurement problems, such as misunderstanding the concept of uniform cross-section in relation to area and perimeter calculations, incorrectly identifying the characteristics of shapes with uniform cross-sections, or failing to recognize when a shape does or does not have a uniform cross-section.

Misconception 1084: Has removed brackets but not performed the operation
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the order of operations (PEMDAS/BODMAS) by not evaluating expressions inside brackets or parentheses, instead only removing the brackets or parentheses without performing the necessary arithmetic operations.

Misconception 1085: Multiplied the powers rather than added them
Expanded misconception:
Failed to properly handle exponents and multiplied the powers of the base rather than adding them when applying the rule of the same base raised to different powers.

Misconception 1086: Thinks the perimeter of a sector is the same as arc length 
Expanded misconception:
Thinks the perimeter of a sector is equal to the length of the arc only (or the radius or diameter of the circle), without considering the chord or additional radii components.

Misconception 1087: Thinks the interior angles of a polygon add up to 100n
Expanded misconception:
Thinks the formula for the sum of interior angles of a polygon is incorrect, incorrectly generalizing it as (a fixed number, in this case, 100) times the number of sides 'n', rather than (n-2) times 180 degrees.

Misconception 1088: Believes you add the denominators when finding a fraction of another fraction
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct operation for finding a fraction of another fraction, specifically adding the denominators instead of multiplying the numerators and multiplying or dividing by the denominators according to the operation.

Misconception 1089: Cannot identify mutually exclusive events
Expanded misconception:
Cannot identify mutually exclusive events, including failing to recognize when two or more events cannot occur simultaneously and consequently applying incorrect probability calculations.

Misconception 1090: Doesn't recognise commutativity of addition with negative numbers
Expanded misconception:
Doesn't recognise or apply the commutative property of addition when dealing with negative numbers, mistakenly believing that changing the order of both positive and negative numbers affects the result.

Misconception 1091: Confuses a variable with a formula
Expanded misconception:
Interprets a variable within an equation or expression as a standalone formula or calculation, rather than recognizing its role as a placeholder for an unspecified value or a stored quantity within the problem's context.

Misconception 1092: Cannot spot a coefficient within a written scenario
Expanded misconception:
Fails to identify or interpret coefficients within written scenarios or word problems, or misunderstands their implications on mathematical operations, such as not recognizing that a number in a real-world context is intended to be multiplied by a variable or unit.

Misconception 1093: Has multiplied the value of the first term by the the term being asked
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the distributive property or multiplication correctly by incorrectly applying the operation to only one part of the problem, often multiplying the first term by a value from a different part of the expression or equation instead of following the proper order of operations or applying the operation as intended.

Misconception 1094: Believes when frequencies multiply by a scale factor in a table the mean also multiplies by that scale factor
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that when multiples of a quantity in a dataset, such as frequencies in a table, are scaled by a constant factor, the mean of the dataset also scales by the same factor, but the variability and spread in the data are affected in a different way, and the relationship between mean and other measures of central tendency and variability remains unchanged.

Misconception 1095: Believes that vectors of different magnitudes can be equal.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider magnitude in comparing vectors, believing that vectors of different magnitudes can be equal if they share the same direction, or incorrectly equating vectors with the same magnitude but different directions as unequal.

Misconception 1096: Thinks there are 10cl in a millilitre
Expanded misconception:
Thinks there are 10 milliliters (mL) in 1 centilitre (cL) rather than understanding that 1 centilitre equals 10 millilitres

Misconception 1097: Is not able to identify a situation where Pythagoras' Theorem is needed
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that Pythagoras' Theorem (a^2 + b^2 = c^2) is required to solve a problem involving a right-angled triangle with unknown hypotenuse or another side.

Misconception 1098: Finds the mean instead of the range
Expanded misconception:
Finds statistical measure incorrectly by calculating or selecting the mean (average) when the problem actually requires finding the range (difference between maximum and minimum values).

Misconception 1099: Believes gradient at a point = y coordinate/x coordinate
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the distinction between slope and coordinates, specifically believing that the gradient (slope) of a curve at a point is equal to the y-coordinate divided by the x-coordinate, rather than accurately evaluating the limit of the difference quotient or applying other differentiation rules.

Misconception 1100: Believes direct proportion means a 1:1 ratio
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the concept of proportional relationships, incorrectly assuming direct proportion implies a 1:1 ratio, rather than a consistent ratio or constant of variation between the variables involved.

Misconception 1101: Believes stratified samples should be evenly split
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider that stratified samples should be split proportionally to the population distribution, instead assuming equal distribution across all subgroups.

Misconception 1102: Believes that addition should be used when finding the difference between two values on a time series graph
Expanded misconception:
Misconceives subtraction as addition, failing to recognize that finding the difference between two connected points on a graph, particularly those representing consecutive time periods or intervals in a time series, requires a calculation of subtraction to accurately determine the change or growth between the values, and instead, incorrectly applies addition to find the supposed difference.

Misconception 1103: When reading integers on a number line, assumes each dash is 2
Expanded misconception:
Fails to accurately represent intervals or scale when reading or plotting integers on a number line, incorrectly assuming that each dash or unit always represents a fixed constant such as 2, rather than understanding that intervals can vary depending on the context or scale of the number line.

Misconception 1104: Simplifies integers when simplifying a mixed number
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly simplify or convert between mixed numbers and improper fractions, or simplifies unrelated integers, often resulting in an overreliance on approximations or disregarding the relationship between the whole number part, numerator, and denominator.

Misconception 1105: Rounds every number to the same degree instead of rounding to significant figures when estimating 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the number of significant figures in the given numbers when performing calculations and estimating answers, often resulting in incorrect rounding or retaining too many decimal places.

Misconception 1106: Divides 360 degrees by sum of sides rather than given amount of angles
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets angles in a geometric figure and incorrectly divides 360 degrees by the sum of the number of sides or the sum of the values of other given quantities rather than dividing by the actual given number of angles or using the corresponding angles in the correct relationship.

Misconception 1107: Converted hours to minutes instead of hours to seconds
Expanded misconception:
Converted units within the same dimension (e.g., time), but failed to apply the correct conversion factor, such as converting hours to minutes instead of hours to seconds.

Misconception 1108: Believes  c, in y=mx+c, will be the y value of the coordinate given despite the x coordinate not being 0.
Expanded misconception:
Failure to recognize that the constant term 'c' in a linear equation y=mx+c represents the y-intercept, requiring x to be 0, and mistakenly assuming it's the y-coordinate of a specific point on the line, without considering the accompanying x-coordinate.

Misconception 1109: On a cumulative frequency diagram, believes the total frequency is the top of the frequency axis rather than the top of the curve
Expanded misconception:
Does not distinguish between the y-axis scale and the cumulative total in diagrams, charts, or graphs; may mistake axis labels or chart limits for actual counts or totals, specifically on a cumulative frequency diagram, believing the total frequency to be the upper limit of the frequency axis rather than the y-coordinate of the rightmost point or the endpoint of the curve.

Misconception 1110: Does not think that -n + n = 0
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the additive inverse property, where adding two numbers with opposite signs (e.g. -n + n) always results in zero.

Misconception 1111: Does not understand the term 'linear'
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the concept of a linear relationship or equation, including but not limited to, mistaking non-linear relationships for linear ones, incorrectly applying linear formulas to non-linear problems, or confusing linear elements within a non-linear equation.

Misconception 1112: When using Pythagoras' theorem to find a missing side, thinks you multiply the sides together then square root
Expanded misconception:
When using Pythagoras' theorem to find the length of a missing side, mistakenly believes that the correct calculation involves multiplicatively combining the given sides' lengths (e.g., a*b) and then applying the square root, rather than adding the squares of the lengths and then taking the square root (i.e., √(a² + b²)).

Misconception 1113: When substituting a value into a non-linear term with a coefficient, multiplies by the coefficient then calculates the power.
Expanded misconception:
When substituting a value into a non-linear term with a coefficient, evaluates by incorrectly applying the order of operations, specifically multiplies the variable's value by the coefficient before calculating the power, instead of first calculating the power and then multiplying by the coefficient.

Misconception 1114: Believes they can change the subject of a formula just by swapping two letters
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that algebraic expressions or formulas have a specific structure and that altering or switching parts of it (such as variable letters) must be done systematically while preserving the underlying relationship or equation, and not simply by swapping letters.

Misconception 1115: Identifies the term-to-term rule rather than the next term in a sequence
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the iterative process or formula to find the next term in a sequence, instead relying on observing a term-to-term pattern or superficial relationship that does not hold true as the sequence progresses.

Misconception 1116: Multiplies coefficients and letters separately and writes as an addition
Expanded misconception:
Fails to combine the variables and constants correctly when multiplying or adding expressions with variables, possibly multiplying coefficients and variables separately and writing the result as an addition, or incorrectly adding or multiplying coefficients and variables with different letters.

Misconception 1117: When finding a missing length on similar shapes, just find the scale factor
Expanded misconception:
When finding a missing length on similar shapes, utilizes an overly simplistic or incomplete method (e.g., only finds the scale factor) and fails to account for proportional relationships, ratios, and/or mathematical operations between corresponding sides, resulting in inaccurate or incomplete calculations.

Misconception 1118: Thinks the smallest bar in a bar chart is the mode
Expanded misconception:
Thinks the mode in a bar chart or histogram is represented by or determined by the smallest/largest bar exclusively, rather than recognizing it as the class with the highest frequency, regardless of bar size.

Misconception 1119: Does not interpret the correct order of operations in a fraction when there is an addition on the numerator
Expanded misconception:
Does not correctly apply the order of operations within a fraction or between multiple operations, particularly when addition or subtraction is combined with other operations such as multiplication and division, and incorrectly assumes that operations in the numerator are always evaluated first or evaluated according to a left-to-right approach rather than following the standard order of operations.

Misconception 1120: Thinks there are 100 ml in a litre
Expanded misconception:
