"Fails to understand the correct conversion between milliliters and liters or incorrectly remembers that there are 100 milliliters (mL) in a liter (L), when in fact, there are 1,000 mL in 1 L."

Misconception 1121: Believes biased selection methods can be random
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that random sampling involves giving every member of the population an equal chance of being selected and mistakenly believes some biased selection methods can produce a truly random sample.

Misconception 1122: When enlarging a shape, thinks the scale factor divides the movement from the centre of enlargement to a vertex rather than multiplies
Expanded misconception:
When enlarging or reducing a shape, mistakenly believes that the scale factor divides or subtracts the distance of movement from the centre of enlargement to a vertex, rather than correctly multiplying or applying the scale factor as a ratio of the distances.

Misconception 1123: Subtracts rather than dividing to find unit price
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly performs or selects an arithmetic operation (e.g., subtraction instead of division) when the problem context requires a comparative calculation, such as finding unit prices, equivalent ratios or proportions, rates, or per item/quantity values.

Misconception 1124: When ordering numbers in standard form just looks at the decimal without multiplying by a power of 10
Expanded misconception:
Ignores the power of 10 when comparing or ordering numbers in standard form, instead prioritizing the decimal part only.

Misconception 1125: Multiplies rather than adds when answering worded problems
Expanded misconception:
"The student struggles to identify the correct operation in worded problems, frequently substituting addition with multiplication (or vice versa), resulting in an operation misapplication."

Misconception 1126: Believes the mean is always representative
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the limitations of using mean as a representative value, such as the impact of outliers, skewness, or multimodal distributions, and always selects it as the most appropriate measure of central tendency.

Misconception 1127: Believes the variable should remain in place after substituting its value
Expanded misconception:
Fails to replace and/or substitute variables with their corresponding numerical values during arithmetic operations or expressions, or incorrectly retains the variable in place of its value, leading to incorrect mathematical evaluations.

Misconception 1128: Does not know how to interpret a fraction worded problem 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the actual mathematical operation or relevant quantities involved when interpreting word problems containing fractions, such as misunderstanding the relationship between part and whole, misinterpreting the role of the denominator, or incorrectly identifying the mathematical operation required.

Misconception 1129: When finding a missing part in a ratio, divides rather than multiplies
Expanded misconception:
When working with proportions, incorrectly applies inverse operation (i.e., divides instead of multiplies, or vice versa) to find the missing part, indicating a lack of understanding of proportional relationships and ratio scaling.

Misconception 1130: Does not know how to solve linear equations involving fractions
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply or incorrectly applies the properties of fractions, such as the least common denominator, when solving linear equations that involve fractions, often resulting in arithmetic errors or improper simplifications.

Misconception 1131: When adding fractions, multiplies the numerators
Expanded misconception:
When adding fractions, multiplies the numerators, or fails to find a common denominator before adding the fractions, resulting in an incorrect sum.

Misconception 1132: Does not understand that a probability of 1 represents something that is certain
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand that a probability of 1 represents something that is certain, and/or has difficulty interpreting or applying unit probability values in various contexts such as interpreting probability distributions, calculating expected outcomes, or making decisions under uncertainty.

Misconception 1133: Does not recognise a quadratic graph
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognise or incorrectly identifies key features of a quadratic graph, such as the vertex, axis of symmetry, or the direction of the parabola (opening upwards or downwards), possibly due to lack of understanding of quadratic equation characteristics or graphical representation.

Misconception 1134: When solving an equation, adds instead of dividing
Expanded misconception:
Inverts the order of operations when dealing with division, frequently substituting addition for division and/or neglecting to prioritize division over addition when both operations are present in an expression or equation.

Misconception 1135: When subtracting a negative number, adds the absolute values and then writes the answer with a negative sign
Expanded misconception:
When subtracting a negative number, adds the absolute values and does not change the sign of the original number, resulting in an incorrect answer with the wrong sign or treating subtraction of a negative as a separate positive addition operation.

Misconception 1136: Believes that a general trend of an increase or decrease over time, on a statistical diagram, means that the slope must be in the same direction for the whole time period
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that a general trend or pattern over time on a statistical diagram, such as a graph or chart, does not imply a consistent rate of change or a uniform slope throughout the entire time period, and that fluctuations or changes in slope within the data points do not necessarily contradict the overall trend.

Misconception 1137: When squaring a variable with a power, thinks that the power is squared rather than doubled. 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the power rule of exponents correctly when an exponential expression is raised to another power, incorrectly squaring or cubing the initial exponent rather than doubling or tripling it.

Misconception 1138: Subtracts the smallest digit from the largest digit
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to apply correct procedures for arithmetic operations involving multiple steps or numbers, specifically simplifies complex calculations by subtracting the smallest digit from the largest digit, regardless of the actual operation required."

Misconception 1139: Confuses the lines y=x and y=-x
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets or confuses functions or relationships that are symmetric with respect to the x-axis, y-axis, or the lines y=x and y=-x, such as incorrect identification or representation of functions with x-y symmetry or mixing up inverse functions with their originals.

Misconception 1140: When simplifying surds, adds the number on the outside of the surd to the number under the surd
Expanded misconception:
When simplifying surds (e.g., radical expressions), incorrectly adds or combines the number outside the surd (coefficient or other surds with a different base) with the radicand (the number under the radical sign), instead of applying the correct rules of surd simplification and manipulation, such as separating the radicand and coefficient correctly and simplifying or combining only like terms.

Misconception 1141: Believes all grouped frequency table need a midpoint column
Expanded misconception:
Believes that all grouped frequency tables, including those with unequal intervals or open/closed intervals, require a midpoint column for accurate analysis, and fails to adapt to specific table structures or data representations.

Misconception 1142: When converting from an improper fraction to a mixed number, adds on the part left to make the whole, rather than subtracting it
Expanded misconception:
When converting from an improper fraction to a mixed number, incorrectly handles the whole number part by adding rather than subtracting the remainder, demonstrating an incomplete understanding of the relationship between whole numbers and fractions.

Misconception 1143: Mixes up the terms for the parts of a fraction
Expanded misconception:
Mixes up the terms for the parts of a fraction, incorrectly identifying the numerator or denominator or confusing their respective roles.

Misconception 1144: Believes the equation for a horizontal line is in the form xy = a, where a is the y intercept
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the equation forms of vertical and horizontal lines, mistakenly believing the standard form for a horizontal line is xy = a (instead of x = a or y = c, where c is the y-intercept) and possibly mistakenly interchanging other equation forms between horizontal and vertical lines.

Misconception 1145: When adding column vectors, multiplies rather than adds
Expanded misconception:
In operations involving vectors, incorrectly applies the multiplication rule for scalar quantities instead of applying the component-wise addition rule for vectors, such as treating vector addition as scalar multiplication or misinterpreting the concept of vector components.

Misconception 1146: When solving simultaneous equations, believes the terms must be of opposite signs in order to be eliminated.
Expanded misconception:
When solving simultaneous equations using the method of elimination, incorrectly believes that only terms with opposite coefficients or opposite signs can be eliminated by adding or subtracting the equations.

Misconception 1147: When solving a 'more than' problem, believes the first step is to halve the total rather than setting up an equation to help solve
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that 'more than' or 'greater than' problems often require algebraic thinking or equation-based strategies, and may instead inappropriately apply a proportional relationship (e.g., simply halving, doubling, or subtracting a fixed value) as a shortcut to solve such problems.

Misconception 1148: Uses an incorrect side length when splitting a composite shape into parts
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly decompose composite shapes into individual components by using an incorrect side length due to misidentification of shared boundaries, neglect of geometric properties, or incorrect application of spatial relationships.

Misconception 1149: Believes perpendicular is the term used to describe two lines that are parallel
Expanded misconception:
Believes that the terms describing line relationships, specifically 'perpendicular' and 'parallel', are used interchangeably, where perpendicular is incorrectly used to describe two lines that maintain the same distance and never intersect or intersect at some point other than at a 90-degree angle.

Misconception 1150: Does not believe you can scale up a proportional statement
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or apply proportional relationships, assuming that a mathematical principle or ratio only applies to a specific scenario or set of numbers, and is unable to scale it up or generalize it to different contexts or values.

Misconception 1151: Believes they can round when converting units
Expanded misconception:
Believes they can round numbers during intermediate calculations, such as when converting between units, without considering the possible loss of precision or accuracy in the final answer.

Misconception 1152: When dividing fractions, does the reciprocal of the first fraction instead of the second
Expanded misconception:
When dividing fractions, incorrectly inverts the reciprocal, typically by taking the reciprocal of the first fraction instead of the second, thus resulting in a flawed division by mistakenly inverting the divisor instead of the dividend.

Misconception 1153: Believes they can ignore the coefficient when expanding double brackets 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to handle coefficients properly when expanding double brackets, either by ignoring them, applying them to only one term, or incorrectly distributing them to multiple terms.

Misconception 1154: Believes midpoints are plotted on histograms
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly assumes midpoints must always be plotted on histograms, or fails to consider the impact of class interval or bin width on determining interval representation, resulting in misunderstandings about the representation of data distributions in histograms.

Misconception 1155: Starts at the wrong end of the dividend when dividing
Expanded misconception:
Fails to identify or align digits correctly when performing division, often starting at the wrong end of the dividend or misunderstanding number placement within the division process.

Misconception 1156: When dividing a fraction by an integer, divides the denominator by the integer
Expanded misconception:
When dividing a fraction by an integer, performs incorrect operations, such as dividing the denominator by the integer instead of multiplying the numerator and denominator by its reciprocal.

Misconception 1157: Rounds every number up when estimating
Expanded misconception:
Rounds every number up when estimating, failing to consider the context of the problem, operation, or relative magnitude of numbers involved, which may lead to incorrect approximation or calculation results.

Misconception 1158: Believes you can go above an amount of money when finding how much of something you can buy
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for budget constraints, believes it is possible to exceed or ignore available funds when calculating purchases or determining affordability, and incorrectly assumes the feasibility of buying items without considering the total cost or amount of money available.

Misconception 1159: When comparing non like surds, does not square the surds
Expanded misconception:
Fails to rationalize or square non-like surds when comparing or adding/subtracting them, often directly comparing or combining the coefficients or surds as if they were like terms.

Misconception 1160: Thinks you can find missing values in a given table by treating the row as a sequence of consecutive numbers
Expanded misconception:
Assumes table values are in a linear or arithmetic sequence, and infers or calculates missing data based on perceived patterns, rather than considering actual relationships, formulas, or underlying structures.

Misconception 1161: Thinks 1 square metre = 10 square centimetres
Expanded misconception:
Confuses square units and incorrectly applies conversion between them, misunderstanding that 1 square metre actually equals 10,000 square centimetres.

Misconception 1162: Mixes up the inequality signs in an error interval
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets or incorrectly applies inequality signs or interval notation, resulting in a reversal or incorrect representation of the solution, boundary or range in an error interval or inequality.

Misconception 1163: Does not understand you can round to numbers other than powers of 10
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand that numbers can be rounded to any significant figure, level of precision, or place value, not just powers of 10, and consistently rounds to the nearest ten.

Misconception 1164: Believes linear sequences cannot include fractions
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or apply the distributive property, or ignore operations within parentheses, or believes linear sequences cannot include fractions, decimals, or mixed numbers, and may also have difficulty with negative numbers or variables in such sequences.

Misconception 1165: Rounds to the nearest integer rather than one significant figure
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply precision and rounding rules correctly, rounding to the nearest integer in situations where rounding to a specific decimal place or significant figure is required.

Misconception 1166: When subtracting a fraction from a mixed number, assumes they subtract the numerator from the integer and the numerator of the mixed number
Expanded misconception:
Fails to convert a mixed number to an improper fraction and does not change the sign of the fraction's numerator when the operation changes from addition to subtraction, resulting in the incorrect manipulation of the numbers.

Misconception 1167: Does not recognise millimetres as a unit of length
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognise millimetres as a unit of length, instead applying operations or conversions as if millimetres were incorrectly perceived as other units of length (e.g. metres, centimetres, or other unit conversions).

Misconception 1168: Believes both the x and y co-ordinates of the x-intercept of a quadratic are derived from the constants in the factorised form.
Expanded misconception:
Misconception: Believes both the x-coordinate and the y-coordinate (mistakingly assuming y to be other than 0) of the x-intercept of a quadratic are derived from the constants in the factorised form, failing to recognize that the y-coordinate of an x-intercept is always 0.

Misconception 1169: Does not know that cubic means the same as cubed in units
Expanded misconception:
Does not know that cubic and cubed are synonymous terms for raising a number to the power of 3 in mathematical operations and units of measurement, leading to incorrect application and interpretation of cubed or cubic quantities.

Misconception 1170: Confuses the order of the digits in stick multiplication
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the order of the digits in multiplication problems, specifically reverses or incorrectly arranges the placement of single or multi-digit numbers within the multiplication process, leading to incorrect calculations and results.

Misconception 1171: When solving a problem using written division (bus-stop method), puts the dividend in the wrong place
Expanded misconception:
When solving a problem using written division (also known as long division or the bus-stop method), incorrectly places or orients the dividend (the number being divided) on the dividend line, such as placing it on the line beneath, writing it outside the line, or misaligning the digits.

Misconception 1172: Thinks any 2 values multiplied together can be denoted with a squared symbol
Expanded misconception:
Thinks any two values multiplied together can be denoted with a squared symbol, failing to recognize the convention that the squared symbol (²) specifically represents the result of multiplying a value by itself.

Misconception 1173: Uses the numerator of an improper fraction as the denominator of the equivalent mixed number
Expanded misconception:
Ignores or misunderstands the relationship between the numerator and denominator in improper fractions when converting to mixed numbers or simplifying, possibly using the numerator as the denominator or incorrectly inverting the values of the numerator and denominator.

Misconception 1174: Believes the mean from grouped data is the sum of the midpoints divided by something
Expanded misconception:
Believes the mean from grouped data is the sum of the midpoints divided by something (usually incorrectly using the number of midpoints, the sum of frequencies, or the highest frequency) rather than the sum of the product of each midpoint and its frequency, then divided by the sum of frequencies.

Misconception 1175: Uses base instead of height as h in area of a trapezium formula
Expanded misconception:
Applies incorrect variable assignment in the formula for the area of a trapezium, such as using the base instead of height or vice versa, demonstrating difficulty in deciphering and substituting different geometric components within formulas.

Misconception 1176: Confuses isosceles and equilateral triangles
Expanded misconception:
Confuses properties and definitions of different types of triangles, particularly isosceles (having two sides of equal length) and equilateral triangles (having three sides of equal length), often demonstrating difficulty in identifying or distinguishing between these two concepts, or disregarding the equal angle properties or the total number of equal sides in a given triangle.

Misconception 1177: Identifies the biggest value for the range
Expanded misconception:
Identifies the biggest value for the range, but fails to consider the operational constraints or limitations of the problem, often resulting in choosing an answer that, although numerically large, does not satisfy the conditions or rules of the given problem.

Misconception 1178: Does not count both edges when turning a corner when counting perimeter
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for the contribution of corner geometry to the perimeter when counting edges in a shape, including neglecting to count both edges or parts of edges that meet at a corner when turning a corner in a geometric figure, resulting in incorrect perimeter calculations.

Misconception 1179: Forgets to multiply up for the correct time frame in a worded question
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the context of the worded question and does not adjust calculations according to the specified time frame, unit, or rate, such as not scaling up or down for different durations, frequencies, or quantities.

Misconception 1180: Does not know the properties of a rectangle
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or apply basic geometric properties of a rectangle, such as opposite sides being equal in length, opposite angles being equal in measure, all internal angles adding up to 360 degrees, and/or the diagonals bisecting each other.

Misconception 1181: When solving an equation thinks they divide when in fact they need to multiply
Expanded misconception:
When solving an equation, consistently fails to recognize the operation required between variables or constants and incorrectly substitutes multiplication with division or vice versa, often resulting in a miscalculation of the solution.

Misconception 1182: Believes only the decimal places with dots above them recur, not the whole string
Expanded misconception:
Ignores or misunderstands the notation of recurring decimal expansions, specifically believing that only the decimal places with dots or bars above them (e.g., 0.1̇2̇3 or 0.12̄3) recur, not the entire repeating pattern.

Misconception 1183: Does not include units in answer.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider or apply the appropriate units of measurement or operation results in their answer, which may be due to a lack of understanding about unit consistency or omission in calculations.

Misconception 1184: Identifies the wrong part of a fraction model as the whole
Expanded misconception:
Misconception: Identifies the wrong part (e.g., numerator, denominator, or a subset of the parts) of a fraction model as the whole, or incorrectly relates the whole to a fraction's representation, leading to incorrect interpretation of equivalency, comparison, addition, or subtraction of fractions.

Misconception 1185: When rationalising the denominator, believes only the numerator or the denominator needs to be multiplied by the surd
Expanded misconception:
When rationalising the denominator, believes only the numerator or the denominator (but not both) needs to be multiplied by the conjugate or the surd to eliminate the radical, or fails to apply the necessary multiplication consistently.

Misconception 1186: Believes when identifying the mode you look at all values, not just frequencies, for the highest number
Expanded misconception:
Believes that when identifying the mode, the highest or largest data value is selected, rather than identifying the value that occurs most frequently.

Misconception 1187: Does not realise that gradient represents rate of change
Expanded misconception:
Does not realise that gradient represents instantaneous rate of change and fails to apply this understanding in solving problems involving slopes, derivatives, or other rate-related concepts, often leading to incorrect calculations or misinterpretations of graphical representations.

Misconception 1188: Reads the wrong scale on the protractor when measuring obtuse angles
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly measure obtuse angles by misreading the protractor scale, often resulting from confusion between the inner and outer scales or miscounting the degree markings when measuring angles greater than 180 degrees or in mixed units (e.g., degrees and minutes).

Misconception 1189: Does not understand that adding on to a multiple can give you the next multiple
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that adding a multiple to itself, or to another number, results in a new multiple of the original base number, and applies incorrect arithmetic reasoning to arrive at the incorrect solution.

Misconception 1190: When solving an equation, subtracts instead of dividing
Expanded misconception:
When solving an equation, incorrectly performs an operation by substituting a division operation with a subtraction operation, often due to misinterpretation of mathematical notation or misunderstanding of order of operations.

Misconception 1191: Believes that the sign changes when factorising an expression into single brackets
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the rules of sign changes when factorising expressions, including failing to distribute the negative sign when removing brackets, incorrectly changing signs of multiple terms or constants when factorising expressions into single brackets.

Misconception 1192: Believes all triangles have a right angle
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between different types of triangles (e.g., acute, obtuse, right, isosceles, scalene) and assumes all triangles have a right angle or special properties thereof.

Misconception 1193: Multiplies up without finding unit amount
Expanded misconception:
Fails to convert between units or scales by applying the correct multiplier, leading to incorrect multiplication without first identifying the fundamental quantity or unit amount.

Misconception 1194: Forgets to halve when finding the missing base angles of an isosceles
Expanded misconception:
Forgets to halve the given angle or supplement when finding the missing base angles of an isosceles triangle, leading to miscalculations of the equal angles.

Misconception 1195: Gives the change in value when asked for the percentage change
Expanded misconception:
Gives the change in value when asked for the percentage change, not recognizing that percentage change is the ratio of change in value to the original value, often expressed as a percentage (change in value/original value) and instead simply providing the change in value itself.

Misconception 1196: When solving an equation where there is a negative sign in front of the variable at the final stage, believes they can ignore it and treat it as if it's positive
Expanded misconception:
Ignores the presence or incorrectly handles the sign of a numerical value or variable, particularly at intermediate or final stages of calculations, often underestimating or neglecting the impact of negative signs, resulting in incorrect solutions.

Misconception 1197: Confuses frequency and frequency density
Expanded misconception:
Confuses frequency and frequency density: incorrectly equates the number of times a particular event occurs (frequency) with the rate of occurrence per unit of measurement (frequency density), or vice versa, failing to account for differences in the units of measurement or scale when comparing or interpreting data in histograms or other graphical representations.

Misconception 1198: Does not recognise that a linear sequence must increase or decrease by same amount
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognise that a linear sequence must have a consistent difference or rate of change between consecutive terms, either increasing or decreasing by the same amount, or does not apply this knowledge consistently to identify the correct pattern or next term.

Misconception 1199: Thinks that co-interior angles can lie on opposite sides of a transversal 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the properties of co-interior angles, mistakenly believing they can be non-adjacent angles lying on opposite sides of a transversal in a intersection of lines, rather than consecutive angles on the same side of a transversal.

Misconception 1200: Finds the IQR rather than the range
Expanded misconception:
Finds the interquartile range (IQR) in situations requiring the computation of a different measure of spread, such as range, mean absolute deviation, or variance, often indicating a misunderstanding of context or incorrect application of statistical concepts.

Misconception 1201: Finds the arc length instead of the area of the sector 
Expanded misconception:
Finds the arc length instead of the area of the sector or incorrectly applies the formula for the arc length to find the area of the sector, failing to multiply the central angle by the radius squared and divide by 360 or 2π.

Misconception 1202: Does not understand that an acute angle is between 0 and 90 degrees
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand that an acute angle is between 0 (exclusive) and 90 degrees (inclusive), or alternatively, mistakenly believes an acute angle is a broader range, such as up to 180 degrees or that it is the only possible range for an acute angle when some valid examples of acute angles actually approach 90 degrees, though aren't 90 degrees, themselves.

Misconception 1203: Believes that to find an angle for a pie chart sector you divide the total frequency by 360 and multiply by its frequency
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct proportional relationship in pie chart calculations, mistakenly dividing the total frequency by 360 and then multiplying by the sector's frequency, rather than correctly dividing the sector's frequency by the total frequency and multiplying by 360.

Misconception 1204: Multiplied by 100 rather than divided by 100
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly apply unit conversions or scale adjustments by inverting the operation order (i.e., multiplying when dividing is required, or vice versa), often involving operations with percentages, unit conversions, or decimal shifts.

Misconception 1205: Thinks a variable next to a number means addition rather than multiplication
Expanded misconception:
Thinks a variable or pronumeral immediately adjacent to a numerical value represents addition rather than implied multiplication due to the lack of an explicit operator or proper understanding of implicit multiplication rules.

Misconception 1206: Does not understand how to maintain equivalence in fractions
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that equivalent fractions must have the same value, resulting in incorrect operations or simplifications, such as incorrectly adding, subtracting, multiplying, or dividing fractions, or incorrectly converting between different forms.

Misconception 1207: Does not realize we can write a division as a fraction
Expanded misconception:
Does not realize that division can be expressed as multiplication by a reciprocal, failing to understand that a/b is equivalent to a * (1/b), and thus incorrectly handles division operations or translates between division and fraction representations, or recognizes division as being represented by a fraction but makes computation or conceptual errors when working with fractions and division.

Misconception 1208: Believes the subtraction button writes a fraction
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to understand that the subtraction operation is performed differently when subtracting positive numbers versus reversing the subtraction of fractions/dealing with negative numbers; specifically, incorrectly handles order of operations involving fractions or incorrectly assumes the subtraction operation yields a fraction or mistakenly interprets a fraction sign as the result of subtraction."

Misconception 1209: Believes a mixed number can contain an improper fraction
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the distinction between mixed numbers and improper fractions, and incorrectly believes a mixed number can contain an improper fraction or inappropriately combines mixed numbers with improper fractions in calculations, possibly indicating confusion with fraction representations or misunderstanding the definition of a mixed number.

Misconception 1210: Does not recognise millilitres as a unit of volume
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognise millilitres (or other metric units, such as litres, centilitres) as a unit of volume, failing to apply conversion principles between units within the metric system and/or incorrectly applying relationships between volume units and other units (e.g., grams for mass), leading to errors in calculations and applications involving liquid measurement.

Misconception 1211: Believes number of squares in a bar gives frequency on a histogram
Expanded misconception:
Believes number of squares in a bar on a histogram or a similar graphical representation directly corresponds to the frequency, rather than understanding that the area or height of the bar is proportional to the frequency, often disregarding the variable scale or bin width.

Misconception 1212: Thinks a greater number of shaded parts represents a greater fraction
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the relative size or proportion of the shaded parts to the whole, assuming that a greater number of shaded parts represents a greater fraction, regardless of the total number of parts or the context of the problem.

Misconception 1213: When finding the differences between terms in a sequence, believes they can do so from right to left 
Expanded misconception:
When finding the differences between terms in a sequence, incorrectly applies the operation from right to left instead of maintaining the correct left-to-right orientation or misinterprets the order of operations within a sequence.

Misconception 1214: When solving an equation, uses the same operation rather than the inverse.
Expanded misconception:
When solving an equation, uses the same operation rather than the inverse,  thus incorrectly assuming that operations have the same effect in both the forward and reverse directions, often failing to properly invert addition and subtraction or multiply and divide when solving for the variable.

Misconception 1215: Includes a data point with the value, when asked to find the number of data points above a value
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly includes or excludes a data point with the specific value when asked to find the number of data points above or below a given value, often due to a failure to understand boundary conditions or to accurately interpret the meaning of 'above' and 'below' in a mathematical context.

Misconception 1216: Believes a curve can show a constant rate
Expanded misconception:
Believes a curve can show a constant rate of change along the entire curve or part of it, failing to recognize that a curve implies a changing rate of change unless the curve is linear or has a specific uniform non-linear characteristic.

Misconception 1217: Assumes the perpendicular height of a triangle bisects the base
Expanded misconception:
Assumes the perpendicular height or altitude of a triangle always bisects the base, divides it into two equal segments, or forms two congruent triangles, often disregarding the properties of the specific triangle, such as isosceles, scalene, or right triangles.

Misconception 1218: Multiplies the numerators when adding fractions with a common denominator
Expanded misconception:
Ignores the requirement for a common denominator, and/or incorrectly adds the numerators and/or denominators when adding fractions, such as directly adding numerators of fractions with a common denominator.

Misconception 1219: Gives the value for the wrong variable when answering a simultaneous equation question
Expanded misconception:
Gives the value for a different variable, or a variable from a different equation or part of the problem, instead of providing the correct solution or answer to the specific question being asked when answering a simultaneous equation question or system of equations.

Misconception 1220: Does not include zero when counting over zero
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for zero when performing operations involving numbers with a zero digit, such as in addition, subtraction, or multiplication, often leading to incorrect calculations by ignoring or omitting the placeholder value of zero.

Misconception 1221: Converts a decimal to a fraction by writing 1 over the digits of the decimal
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly convert a decimal to a fraction by writing 1 or a power of 10 (in incorrect cases) over the digits of the decimal without considering the actual place value.

Misconception 1222: Forgets the denominator in probability
Expanded misconception:
Forgets the denominator in probability, including when working with fractions, proportions, or percentages to calculate or compare probabilities, or fails to reduce fractions that represent probabilities to their simplest form.

Misconception 1223: Uses numerators to convert fractions to ratio despite different denominators
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the effect of the denominator in fractions and instead uses the numerator to convert or compare fractions, often resulting in incorrect equivalencies or ratios.

Misconception 1224: Does not understand the diagram given in the question
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets or fails to interpret visual information presented in the diagram, table, or figure provided in the question, such as scale, proportions, labels, or symbols, leading to incorrect conclusions or calculations.

Misconception 1225: Believes you don't need to numerically order the data before calculating the median
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the importance of arranging data in numerical order before calculating the median, leading to incorrect middle value identification and/or consideration of all values without distinguishing between the middle and extreme values.

Misconception 1226: Does not recognise the distributive property
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognise or apply the distributive property of multiplication over addition or subtraction, often resulting in incorrect transformation or grouping of numbers and operations, such as failing to multiply each term in the expression or incorrectly combining like terms.

Misconception 1227: Forgets that a number without an indice is to the power of 1 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly interpret implicit exponents: forgets that a number without an exponent or indice is implicitly to the power of 1.

Misconception 1228: Believes sets are the same if the elements within them have a shared property
Expanded misconception:
Believes sets are the same if the elements within them have a shared property, ignoring the principle of extensionality where two sets are considered equal if and only if they contain the same elements, regardless of their internal ordering, arrangement, or additional common attributes.

Misconception 1229: Confuses chord and circumference
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between the length of a chord (a segment connecting two points on a circle) and the circumference (the distance around a circle), often resulting in incorrect calculations or units when solving problems involving circles.

Misconception 1230: Believes you cube the conversion factor when dealing with squared units
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that when dealing with squared or cubed units in dimensional analysis or conversions, the exponent should be applied to the conversion factor, rather than cubing or taking the power of the entire conversion factor when the unit is raised to a power.

Misconception 1231: Does not know that 7 and -7 are different
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between positive and negative numbers, often treating them as having the same magnitude or ignoring the existence and implications of negative numbers in mathematical operations.

Misconception 1232: Thinks the longest bar has the highest frequency on a pictogram, even if the symbols aren't evenly spaced
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for variations in symbol density in pictograms, assuming the longest bar or the bar with the most symbols corresponds to the highest frequency, and neglects the actual numerical value or key provided.

Misconception 1233: Believes that when eliminating a variable, if the signs of the terms with matching coefficients are the same, we add the equations.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly apply inverse operations or misunderstands the significance of signs when eliminating variables, incorrectly assuming that adding equations is always the approach when the signs of terms with matching coefficients are the same, rather than recognizing that same signs require subtracting the equations.

Misconception 1234: Believes f(x + a) translates the function up a units
Expanded misconception:
Believes f(x + a) translates the function up a units, instead of horizontally shifting or displacing the function's graph by a units to the left when a is positive, and to the right when a is negative.

Misconception 1235: When completing the square in the form (x - a)^2 + b, believes b is the difference between a and the constant of the original quadratic
Expanded misconception:
When completing the square in the form (x - a)^2 + b, incorrectly believes that the constant term 'b' is determined by a simple arithmetic relationship, such as a difference or sum, between the value of 'a' and the constant term of the original quadratic expression, rather than 'b' being the result of the entire algebraic process involved in completing the square.

Misconception 1236: Thinks the plan view is taken from an angle to include aspects of the side view and plan
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands the relationship between plan and side views, failing to recognize that plan views are taken from directly above, resulting in the inclusion of incorrect or irrelevant features from other views in their problem-solving.

Misconception 1237: Believes that 1 part of a bar model will always have a value of 1
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that parts of a bar model can represent different units or quantities, typically assuming a single unit or value of 1 for each part, and does not adjust accordingly when solving problems involving proportional or multi-unit representations.

Misconception 1238: Does not realise the lines at an intersection have to be straight in order to show vertically opposite angles
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that vertically opposite angles require the intersecting lines to be straight and not curved, in order to form right angles and apply associated properties to these angles in geometry.

Misconception 1239: Believes a cubic equation will have a quadratic graph
Expanded misconception:
Confuses cubic equations with quadratic equations, thinking a cubic equation will have a quadratic graph, and not understanding that cubic equations typically produce cubic graphs with one inflection point and may have one, two, or three real roots, whereas quadratic equations have parabolic graphs with one real turning point.

Misconception 1240: Thinks the first term of a sequence must be 1
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that a sequence may have any starting point and does not always begin with 1, often resulting in incorrect calculation or misunderstanding of the sequence pattern.

Misconception 1241: Subtracts instead of divides when division is written as a fraction
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets division operations when represented as fractions, mistakenly performs subtraction instead of division, and/or confuses division and subtraction syntax.

Misconception 1242: Does not recognise the similar shape as the shape has been rotated and the scale factor is less than 1
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognise the similar shape as the shape has been rotated or reflected, and may struggle with scale factors that are less than 1, or when the rotation and scaling result in a significant appearance change, indicating difficulty with visual or spatial reasoningskills and understanding of transformations that alter the shape's orientation and size.

Misconception 1243: Believes when given a fraction and an amount the question must be to find the fraction of the amount. 
Expanded misconception:
Ignores the specific operation required and assumes a division or multiplication operation when given a fraction and an integer, often proceeding as if the question asks for the fraction of the amount or an amount divided into fractional parts, rather than following the given instructions.

Misconception 1244: The student does not understand the place value of 3-digit numbers
Expanded misconception:
The student demonstrates a partial or complete lack of understanding of place value in 3-digit numbers, including incorrect representations of hundreds, tens, and ones, and/or inability to perform arithmetic operations involving 3-digit numbers while considering their place value correctly.

Misconception 1245: Believes a number written in standard form with a negative power creates a larger number than that with a positive power
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the concept of place value and magnitude in numbers written in standard form, incorrectly assuming that a negative exponent indicates a larger value than a positive exponent due to a lack of comprehension of the relationship between exponents and the base value.

Misconception 1246: When adding fractions, uses addition of the denominators as a common denominator
Expanded misconception:
When adding fractions, incorrectly uses the sum of the denominators as a common denominator instead of finding the least common multiple (LCM) of the denominators.

Misconception 1247: Assumes you cannot use the fact that multiplication is commutative when simplifying an algebraic term. 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the properties of operations (associative, commutative, or distributive) to rearrange or simplify algebraic terms, such as overlooking the commutative property of multiplication.

Misconception 1248: Rounds to the wrong degree of accuracy (rounds too little)
Expanded misconception:
Fails to maintain correct degree of precision by consistently rounding to a degree of accuracy that does not account for prior calculations or neglects to round intermediate results, often resulting in premature or unnecessary rounding.

Misconception 1249: Believes 'given that a, what is the probability of b?' means 'given that b, what is the probability of a?'
Expanded misconception:
Confuses conditional probability and reverses the direction of causality, failing to recognize that 'given that A, what is the probability of B?' (P(B|A)) inverts the casual relationship with 'given that B, what is the probability of A?' (P(A|B)), often leading to incorrect calculations and misunderstandings of conditional probability problems.

Misconception 1250: Correct scale but wrong value on protractor reading.
Expanded misconception:
Corrects scale on a protractor but applies the wrong value or incorrectly interprets the location of the degree mark on the protractor, resulting in a numerical answer close to the correct value but not accurate.

Misconception 1251: Correctly finds the reciprocal of a number, but believes it should be opposite in sign to the original number.
Expanded misconception:
Correctly identifies that the reciprocal of a number is obtained by flipping the numerator and denominator, but believes the resulting fraction should have an opposite sign to the original number, regardless of the context or operation being performed.

Misconception 1252: Doesn't know the area of a triangle formula
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply or recall the area of a triangle formula (A = 0.5 * base * height), possibly substituting it with a different geometric formula or incorrectly manipulating the variables.

Misconception 1253: Thinks 1 cubic metre = 100,000 cubic centimetres
Expanded misconception:
Thinks 1 cubic metre equals 100,000 cubic centimetres, failing to apply or recall unit conversions between cubic metres and cubic centimetres, specifically not recalling that 1 cubic metre (or 1,000 litres) is actually equal to 1,000,000 cubic centimetres (1 million millilitres or 1,000 litres) or not fully understanding the base-10 nature of the metric system's unit conversions.

Misconception 1254: Believes that if there are two unknowns in an equation, all integers must be possible solutions
Expanded misconception:
Believes that if there are two unknowns in an equation, or if multiple values could satisfy the equation due to insufficient constraints, all integers must be possible or equally valid solutions, without considering the need for a system of equations or unique constraints to arrive at a single solution.

Misconception 1255: Adds rather than multiplies for combined probabilities
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to apply the multiplication rule for independent events, adding probabilities instead of multiplying them when calculating combined probabilities in situations where events are dependent or independent."

Misconception 1256: Believes the difference of 2 squares method of factorising also works for the addition of 2 squares
Expanded misconception:
Misapplies the difference of squares factorization method, incorrectly assuming it can also be used for factoring the sum of two squares, failing to recognize that this formula only applies to expressions of the form a^2 - b^2 and not to expressions of the form a^2 + b^2.

Misconception 1257: Assumes a 1/4 of a whole is 25
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets fractions or decimals in relation to a whole, specifically assuming a one-fourth proportion is equal to 25 percent, rather than perceiving it as a proportional part of a 100 whole.

Misconception 1258: When calculating a scale factor, thinks they need to subtract rather than divide.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct mathematical operation to find a scale factor or proportion, specifically, incorrectly uses subtraction when the correct operation is division, or incorrectly uses division by the wrong factor, resulting in miscalculations of scaling and proportional relationships between quantities.

Misconception 1259: Believes division is sometimes commutative 
Expanded misconception:
Believes division is sometimes commutative, often wrongly assuming that switching the order of the dividend and divisor will yield the same result, or failing to recognize that division is not commutative due to a limited understanding of the operation's properties.

Misconception 1260: Gives number of sections of pie chart rather than the amount it represents
Expanded misconception:
Interprets graphical representations, such as pie charts, bar graphs, and histograms, as solely reflecting the number of sections, categories, or visual elements rather than understanding the underlying quantities or proportions they represent.

Misconception 1261: Thinks if you multiply a fraction by itself you get a whole
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that when a fraction is multiplied by itself, the result will always be a whole number, rather than applying the rules of multiplying fractions and understanding the outcome may still be a fraction or a mixed number.

Misconception 1262: Believes that they can fully factorise an expression by taking out any common factor, not just the HCF
Expanded misconception:
Believes that they can fully factorise an expression by taking out any common factor, not just the highest common factor, leading to an incomplete or incorrect simplification.

Misconception 1263: Does not understand the definition of a rational number when it is a surd
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the definition of a rational number when it is a surd, incorrectly identifying it as irrational due to the presence of a root symbol, failing to recognize that a rational number can be expressed as a ratio of integers even if it contains a square root that can be simplified to an integer.

Misconception 1264: Finds the median instead of the mode
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly identifies the central tendency or measure of position being asked for, and finds the median (the middle value, or average of two middle values in a data set with an even number of entries) instead of the mode (the most frequently occurring value) when requested, showing a misunderstanding of these measures of position and their respective definitions.

Misconception 1265: When describing a translation, goes from the image to the original
Expanded misconception:
Fails to follow the conventional mapping of an original to its image in a translation, instead inappropriately mapping the image back to the original, demonstrating a misunderstanding of the direction of the transformation.

Misconception 1266: When solving simultaneous equations, thinks they can ignore one of the variables
Expanded misconception:
When solving simultaneous equations, thinks they can ignore one of the variables or its associated equation, either by solving for one variable and not substituting it back into the other equation, or by only considering one equation as a constraint to find a solution.

Misconception 1267: Identifies a number as a common factor but doesn't identify a letter as a common factor
Expanded misconception:
Fails to generalize common factors beyond numerical values, often incorrectly assuming that common factors can only be numerical coefficients rather than variables or letters.

Misconception 1268: When solving simultaneous equations, thinks you only transform the algebraic terms, rather than the whole equation.
Expanded misconception:
When solving simultaneous equations, selectively applies algebraic operations or transformations to individual terms, but fails to apply these operations or transformations to the entire equation, and may ignore or disregard the equal sign and the corresponding terms on the other side of the equation.

Misconception 1269: Confuses left and right
Expanded misconception:
Fails to follow the conventional left-to-right and top-to-bottom reading direction of mathematical operations or incorrectly interprets the left-to-right and top-to-bottom orientation of mathematical symbols, resulting in incorrect ordering or placement of values or operations in a given expression.

Misconception 1270: Divides by 10 instead of 100 when converting a percentage to a decimal 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to adjust the divisor correctly when converting percentages to decimals, often resorting to a default division by 10, regardless of the percentage's place value.

Misconception 1271: Confuses chord and radius
Expanded misconception:
Confuses chord and radius, specifically struggles to distinguish between a straight line segment connecting two points on the circumference of a circle (chord) and the line segment extending from the center of the circle to its circumference or edge (radius).

Misconception 1272: Gives a rounded whole number instead of a decimal
Expanded misconception:
Fails to maintain precision in calculations, often neglecting to perform or record operations that result in decimal answers, incorrectly rounding or truncating to the nearest whole number, even when a more precise solution is required.

Misconception 1273: Thinks they start on the left when doing long multiplication 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct order of operations or ignores the importance of aligning digits and starting from the right when performing long multiplication and other multi-digit calculations.

Misconception 1274: Subtracts simultaneous equations when addition is required
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply correct operation, inverting operations such as addition and subtraction, or incorrectly handling the signs of constants and coefficients in equations, particularly in multi-step problems or when dealing with simultaneous equations, which can result in incorrect combinations of variable and constant terms when setting up and solving equations.

Misconception 1275: Does not relate integers and fractions
Expanded misconception:
Does not relate integers and fractions, such as incorrectly converting mixed units, misunderstanding order of operations involving fractions and integers, or failing to adjust calculations when combining or comparing integers and fractions.

Misconception 1276: Believes that the y intercept is (0, y) where y is any y coordinate on the line
Expanded misconception:
Believes that the y-intercept of a linear equation can be any point on the line where y is one of the y-coordinates, rather than being specifically the point where the line crosses the y-axis when x=0.

Misconception 1277: In algebra, thinks it's ok to write (for example) 0x rather than just 0
Expanded misconception:
In algebra, incorrectly includes constants or variables that do not affect the solution of an equation, such as writing (for example) 0x rather than just 0, or including unnecessary terms with coefficients of zero or one.

Misconception 1278: Believes the subscript in iteration notation is the value to be substituted
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the role of the subscript in iteration notation, mistakenly believing it represents the value to be substituted into the expression, rather than the number of times the expression is to be repeated or a pointer to the specific term within a sequence.

Misconception 1279: Confuses right angle triangles and equilateral triangles
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply distinct properties of right-angled and equilateral triangles, specifically ignores right angle triangle properties such as Pythagorean theorem, trigonometric ratios, or side length relationships, and/or misapplies properties unique to equilateral triangles, such as all angles being 60 degrees or all sides being equal in length.

Misconception 1280: When multiplying fractions, multiplies the numerator and adds the denominator
Expanded misconception:
When multiplying fractions, incorrectly applies addition or another operation to the denominator instead of multiplying, demonstrating a lack of understanding of fraction multiplication rules.

Misconception 1281: Does not understand that similar shapes and enlarged shapes will have the same size angles as the original
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand that similar shapes, enlarged shapes, reduced shapes, and other scaled transformations will preserve the size of corresponding angles as well as the ratio of corresponding sides, leading to incorrect applications of geometry principles.

Misconception 1282: When subtracting a negative thinks the answer must be negative 
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to recognize that subtracting a negative number results in an addition operation, leading to a misunderstanding of the sign of the result."

Misconception 1283: Believes a polygon can have curved sides
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the fundamental property that a polygon is a two-dimensional shape composed of straight line segments, and incorrectly identifies shapes with continuous curved boundaries, irregular boundaries, or circular arcs as polygons.

Misconception 1284: Thinks only like terms can be multiplied
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply distributive property and incorrectly assumes that multiplication can only be applied to like terms, resulting in incorrect distribution or lack thereof.

Misconception 1285: Believes a vertical line has equation y=
Expanded misconception:
Believes a vertical line is represented by the equation y = a constant value and mistakenly applies this concept to incorrectly identify the equation of an arbitrary line.

Misconception 1286: believes the complement of a set consists of anything that is not just in that set
Expanded misconception:
Believes the complement of a set consists of anything that is not just in that set, but fails to account for the universal set it is relative to, incorrectly including elements outside of the universal set or incorrectly excluding elements that are part of the relative complement.

Misconception 1287: Believes if you changed all values by the same proportion the range would not change
Expanded misconception:
Believes scaling a set of values by the same factor or proportion preserves the range or relative differences, and fails to recognize the impact of such scaling on measurements of variability and spread.

Misconception 1288: Believes the intersection on a Venn diagram represents the total of one set
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the intersection on a Venn diagram as representing the elements common to both sets, mistakenly interpreting it as the total number of elements in one or both sets, leading to incorrect conclusions about set relationships and operations.

Misconception 1289: Does not understand that the area of an enlarged shape is the area of the original multiplied by the square of the scale factor of the lengths.
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand that the area of an enlarged shape is the area of the original multiplied by the square of the scale factor of the lengths, and instead may apply the scale factor only once, or misunderstand how the scale factor affects the dimensions of the shape, or mistakenly apply a proportional increase in area that is not squared.

Misconception 1290: Reads the wrong scale on the protractor when measuring reflex angles
Expanded misconception:
Fails to accurately measure reflex angles by either reading the wrong scale on the protractor, misaligning the protractor with the angle's sides, or misinterpreting the relationship between the angle's measure and the protractor's scales.

Misconception 1291: Thinks they solve mx + c = a, by substituting in the value of a for x and therefore calculating ma + c
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to understand that in a linear equation of the form mx + c = a, the variable x should be isolated on one side, and instead incorrectly substitutes the constant term a for the variable x, resulting in miscalculations such as ma + c."

Misconception 1292: Does not realise that the area of a triangle is half the area of the contatining rectangle
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognise that the area of a triangle is half the area of the containing or equivalent parallelogram, including rectangular or non-rectangular enclosures, due to a lack of understanding of the triangle's relationship to its enclosing shape.

Misconception 1293: Identifies how much repeated use of the term-to-term rule changes a number but does not apply to the sequence
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognize or apply that a repeated mathematical operation (term-to-term rule) only changes each term's preceding value according to set rules, failing to account for correct cumulative changes and shifts in the overall sequence.

Misconception 1294: When solving an equation where there is a negative sign in front of the variable and its equivalent value, assumes the variable has a negative value
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly interpret the sign of a variable and its corresponding value in an equation when the variable is preceded by a negative sign, leading to incorrect assumptions about the variable's value, such as treating the variable as having a negative coefficient when in reality it is positive, or vice versa.

Misconception 1295: When finding part of the area of a shape, subtracts from the side length before multiplying but still subtracts the unshaded area too.
Expanded misconception:
In calculations involving partial area of shapes, incorrectly applies the process by subtracting from a side length before multiplying but still proceeds to subtract the unshaded area, failing to consider only the remaining dimensions or adjustments necessary after subtraction, and inaccurately combining these operations to determine the partial area.

Misconception 1296: Believes there are 90 degrees in a full turn
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand or recall that there are 360 degrees in a full rotation or turn (or circle), often confused with the 90-degree angle.

Misconception 1297: Believes you keep the divisor unchanged when dividing by a fraction
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that dividing by a fraction is equivalent to multiplying by its reciprocal, leading to incorrect retention of the original divisor value rather than inverting and multiplying.

Misconception 1298: Thinks multiple letters can be subtracted at once in Roman numerals
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that in Roman numerals, multiple letters can be added but not subtracted, or incorrectly applies the subtraction concept in a specific context, and mistakenly attempts to directly subtract or cancel multiple letters when the problem actually requires a different operation or conversion.

Misconception 1299: Ignores the zero when multiplying
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the concept that any number multiplied by zero equals zero, resulting in an incorrect or incomplete solution.

Misconception 1300: In direct and inverse proportion, forgets to find the value of k, and carries out calculations as though k were 1
Expanded misconception:
In direct and inverse proportion, neglects to find the value of the constant of variation (k) and incorrectly assumes it is equal to 1 or another arbitrary value, carrying out calculations without properly substituting k.

Misconception 1301: When subtracting a surd from an integer, just subtracts the number under the surd from the integer
Expanded misconception:
When subtracting a surd from an integer, fails to consider the properties of radicals and incorrectly applies standard whole-number subtraction rules, resulting in neglecting the square root symbol or misinterpreting the numerical relationship between integers and surds.

Misconception 1302: In ratio question where the difference between the sides is given, uses that as one part without dividing
Expanded misconception:
In ratio or proportion questions, mistakenly uses the difference between the parts as a single part, failing to divide it equally among the specified parts, thus misapplying the concept of equivalent ratios or proportion.

Misconception 1303: Believes y=-f(x) is a reflection in y=-x
Expanded misconception:
Believes transformations involve perpendicular line of reflections, specifically confusing the effect of y = -f(x) as a reflection across the line y = -x, rather than correctly perceiving it as a reflection across the x-axis.

Misconception 1304: Confuses lines of the form y = a and y= ax
Expanded misconception:
Confuses lines of the form y = a (where 'a' is a constant) and y = ax (where 'a' is the slope and 'x' is the variable), often incorrectly identifying one as the other or applying the properties of one to the other, such as assuming y = a has a slope or assuming y = ax has a constant y-value.

Misconception 1305: believes that a fraction with the largest denominator will be smallest regardless of the numerator
Expanded misconception:
Believes that a fraction with the largest denominator will be the smallest regardless of the numerator, and consistently makes errors by comparing fractions based solely on their denominators, ignoring the overall fraction value determined by the relationship between the numerator and denominator.

Misconception 1306: Finds the median instead of the range
Expanded misconception:
Finds the median or other measures of central tendency instead of calculating the range or does not account for the contextual differences in the use of these concepts when solving quantitative problems.

Misconception 1307: Finds the degrees for 1 person rather than the required number of people for a pie chart sector
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly translate real-world quantities to the mathematical representation of a pie chart, specifically neglecting to multiply the number of people by the corresponding degree measure, thus calculating degrees for a single representative rather than the required total number of people for a given sector.

Misconception 1308: Thinks tables of values are symmetrical about (0,0)
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that functions may not be symmetrical about a specific point, such as (0,0), and mistakenly assumes that if (a,b) is a point on a function's table of values, then (-a,-b) must also be a point on the table, or incorrectly generalizes this assumption to other tables of values or functions without considering the origin or specific function type.

Misconception 1309: Believes the numerator is the top of a fraction 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly identify and utilize the numerator and denominator in mathematical operations, such as adding, subtracting, multiplying, and dividing fractions, often incorrectly assuming the numerator is the entire top of a fraction or incorrectly using the terms "numerator" and "denominator" interchangeably.

Misconception 1310: When interpreting a pictogram, thinks each symbol stands for 1
Expanded misconception:
When interpreting a pictogram, incorrectly thinks that each symbol stands for one item or unit, failing to understand or account for the given scale or key that may indicate each symbol represents multiple items or units.

Misconception 1311: 
Has incorrect belief about the meaning of "formula"
Expanded misconception:
Has a fundamental misunderstanding or incorrect application of mathematical formulas, symbols, or equations, such as misinterpreting notations, conflating similar formulas, or failing to recognize valid substitutions or transformations of formulas.

Misconception 1312: Believes f(x) + a translates the function right a units
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands function transformations, specifically believes that a horizontal translation is achieved by adding a constant to the function itself (f(x) + a) rather than to the input (f(x - a) for a left translation and f(x + a) for a right translation).

Misconception 1313: Found the percentage but forgot to add to 100%
Expanded misconception:
Found the correct percentage increase/decrease or difference in percentage, but incorrectly reported it as the final answer instead of adding it to the base value (100%) or incorrectly applied it to the context.

Misconception 1314: Only uses the closest angle to the unknown when using the angles in a straight line relationship
Expanded misconception:
Only uses the closest angle to the unknown when using the angles in a straight line relationship, neglecting to apply the supplementary angles property that the sum of the angles on a straight line is 180 degrees.

Misconception 1315: Rounds to the wrong degree of accuracy (nearest ten rather than nearest whole number)
Expanded misconception:
Rounds answer to incorrect degree of accuracy, failing to consider the problem's specified or implied precision (e.g., rounding to nearest ten, hundred, or decimal place incorrectly when the problem requires rounding to the nearest whole number or specific decimal place).

Misconception 1316: Does not understand that the numerator and denominator of fractions represent groupings and have the same order of priority as brackets
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand that both the numerator and denominator in a fraction are evaluated according to the order of operations within their respective groupings before performing the division operation, with the same level of priority as expressions enclosed in brackets.

Misconception 1317: Identifies wrong place value column after decimal point
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly identify the place value column after the decimal point and/or confuses digits to the right of the decimal point with those to the left of the decimal point, resulting in miscalculations and/or misinterpretation of the numerical value.

Misconception 1318: Does not understand that shapes are congruent if they have the same size and shape
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand that shapes are congruent if they have the same size and shape, regardless of their orientation or location in the coordinate plane, and frequently fails to account for rotational and reflectional symmetries when determining congruence.

Misconception 1319: Adds instead of multiples
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply correct multiplication operation, instead adding numbers as if the multiplication symbol is an addition operator, or incorrectly substituting addition for multiplication in equations or expressions.

Misconception 1320: Multiplied by 10 rather than 100
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or apply order of magnitude changes, such as confusing multiples of ten with multiples of one hundred, leading to incorrect calculations involving place value, decimal shifts, or scaling.

Misconception 1321: Thinks angles which look the same on diagram will be equal
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that visually equal or seemingly symmetrical angles in a diagram will always be equal, neglecting the possibility of optical illusions, incorrectly interpreted visual cues, or misunderstanding of the actual angle measurements.

Misconception 1322: Does not know how many days are in a leap year
Expanded misconception:
Does not accurately account for the exception that a leap year has 366 days, instead applying the standard 365 days for a non-leap year.

Misconception 1323: When multiplying surds in the form ✓a × b✓c, believes the answer is  ✓(abc)
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly applies the distributive property when multiplying surds, believing that multiplying two surds in the form √a × √b = √(ab) can be generalized to multiplying surds where the radicands are not simply multiplied, such as √a × b√c, resulting in answers like √(abc) without considering the coefficients.

Misconception 1324: When solving an equation, believes that when they get to ax = b, b is the solution
Expanded misconception:
When solving a linear equation, incorrectly assumes the constant term isolated on one side of the equation (i.e., the value on the other side of the equation when the variable is multiplied by a coefficient and the equation is in the form ax = b or similar) is the solution, rather than recognizing that the variable's value is actually the constant term divided by the coefficient of the variable (e.g., x = b/a).

Misconception 1325: Has found area of triangle rather than area of square
Expanded misconception:
Confuses shape-specific area formulas or mistakenly applies a triangle area formula to a different shape, such as using 0.5 * base * height for a square or rectangle instead of length * width.

Misconception 1326: Does not know the meaning of square sign for right angle
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand or apply the geometric symbol of a square (□) to represent a right angle in shapes or diagrams, leading to incorrect interpretation or description of angles in geometric figures.

Misconception 1327: Does not know the meaning of attribute data
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand or correctly apply the concept of attribute data or is unable to distinguish between attribute data and other types of data, leading to misinterpretation or incorrect analysis of data characteristics that cannot be numerically measured, such as categorical or descriptive information.

Misconception 1328: When reading off from the left side of a back to back stem and leaf, mixes up the value of the leaf and the stem
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly interpret the structure of a back-to-back stem and leaf plot, often reversing or confusing the relationship between the stem and the leaf, incorrectly reading values when moving from left to right or right to left across the plot, and subsequently misinterpreting data distribution, median, or range.

Misconception 1329: Believes it is not possible to calculate the frequency for a pie chart sector despite being given enough information to do so
Expanded misconception:
Believes it is not possible to calculate the frequency for a pie chart sector despite being given sufficient information such as the central angle and total number of data points, often due to a lack of understanding of proportional relationships between the central angle and the portion of the data it represents.

Misconception 1330: Believes they can still have the variable on both sides when making it the subject
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to reverse or invert operations appropriately when isolating a variable, often resulting in moving the variable to the same side or keeping it on both sides of the equation when trying to make it the subject."

Misconception 1331: Believes angles in a quadrilateral sum to 450 degrees
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands the sum of interior angles in a quadrilateral as 450 degrees instead of 360 degrees, possibly due to the incorrect generalization of the sum of interior angles in a triangle being 180 degrees multiplied by the number of sides in a quadrilateral.

Misconception 1332: When adding, just writes the digit to the right of the number without considering place value
Expanded misconception:
Neglects the concept of place value, simply adds numbers by writing the digit to the right of the number without considering place value, or fails to regroup or carry digits during addition, potentially resulting in incorrect calculation of sums or miscalculation of the place of individual digits.

Misconception 1333: Halves base but still divides product by 2 when finding area of a triangle
Expanded misconception:
Applies the area formula for a triangle (Area = 1/2 base * height) incorrectly by halving the base and then also dividing the product by 2, instead of just multiplying the base by height and then halving the result.

Misconception 1334: Tries to multiply within place value columns during column multiplication
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the concept of shifting place value during column multiplication, instead attempting to multiply numbers within single columns, resulting in incorrect products and regrouping.

Misconception 1335: Believes volume of a cuboid is the area of the three unique faces multiplied together
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the formula for the volume of a cuboid as the product of length, width, and height, and instead misapplies the concept by attempting to calculate volume as the product of the areas of the three unique faces or misinterprets the formula as requiring the multiplication of all three unique face areas, leading to an incorrect calculation of volume as area of one face times the area of another face times the area of the third face (A1*A2*A3), where A1, A2, and A3 are the areas of the three unique faces.

Misconception 1336: Does not realise addition is commutative
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the commutative property of addition, failing to recognize that changing the order of addends does not affect the result of an addition operation.

Misconception 1337: Does not understand the phrase to the power of
Expanded misconception:
Ignores or misinterprets the concept of exponentiation, failing to evaluate expressions that include numbers 'to the power of' or raised to a certain power correctly, which may result in treating exponents as coefficients or simple addends.

Misconception 1338: Does not understand the effect of consecutive operations
Expanded misconception:
