Does not understand or inaccurately applies the effect of consecutive operations (e.g., order of operations, procedures for handling multiple operations with or without parentheses, or handling operations with the same precedence but reverse order), leading to miscalculations in mathematical expressions.

Misconception 1339: Calculated the hours and minutes separately
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly convert between units of time or calculates hours and minutes separately in time calculations, neglecting to carry over excess units or convert between units of time correctly when the calculation involves different units.

Misconception 1340: Misunderstands dashed lines as being the midpoint
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands dashed lines or other visual indicators as being the exact midpoint, median point, or axis of symmetry, rather than considering the possibility of other interpretations or translations depending on the context of the diagram or problem.

Misconception 1341: Multiplies instead of divides by 100 when converting a percentage to a decimal
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply conversion factor correctly when converting between percentage and decimal, specifically, when converting percentages to decimals, the student incorrectly multiplies by 100 instead of dividing by 100, or vice versa, resulting in an error of two orders of magnitude.

Misconception 1342: When adding decimals, does not keep place value the same as the original digits
Expanded misconception:
When performing arithmetic operations, particularly addition, with decimal numbers, fails to align corresponding place values correctly, and/or disregards the effect of decimal points on the numerical value.

Misconception 1343: When substituting into an algebraic term, replaces variables with numbers without multiplying.
Expanded misconception:
When substituting into an algebraic term that contains variables in multiplication or division operations, incorrectly replaces variables with numbers without applying the correct operation, such as simply replacing the variable without performing the required multiplication or division.

Misconception 1344: Estimates angle without using knowledge of degrees in quarter turns
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply knowledge of angular measurements, particularly degree values associated with quarter turns or right angles, resulting in an estimation error when solving problems involving angles or rotational motion.

Misconception 1345: Inserts brackets but not changed order of operation
Expanded misconception:
Inserts brackets around subexpressions or numbers, but fails to follow the correct order of operations within or among the bracketed expressions, often resulting in incorrectly applying operations from left to right regardless of priority order.

Misconception 1346: Mixes up squaring with repeating a digit
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands the concept of exponentiation and often confuses squaring (raising to the power of 2) with repeating a digit, and also has difficulty distinguishing between squaring and other forms of multiplication.

Misconception 1347: Believes a circle achieves what a perpendicular bisector does
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between the properties and functions of different geometric concepts, specifically believing that a circle serves the same purpose as a perpendicular bisector in dividing a line segment, intersecting shapes, or identifying midpoints, demonstrating a lack of understanding of unique geometric constructions and their applications.

Misconception 1348: Does not recognise a parallelogram
Expanded misconception:
Does not correctly identify or recognise a parallelogram, often due to misunderstanding its properties, such as opposite sides being equal and parallel, or having opposite angles that are equal, resulting in incorrect classification or confusion with other quadrilateral shapes.

Misconception 1349: Does not include negative numbers when calculating a range
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for negative values or zero when computing a range or comparing numerical values, leading to incorrect interpretation or application of mathematical operations involving non-positive numbers.

Misconception 1350: When simplifying a ratio, divides one side of the ratio but multiplies the other side.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the commutative property of multiplication and/or division consistently when simplifying ratios, often incorrectly applying an operation to only one part of the ratio.

Misconception 1351: Does not know the meaning of congruent
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that congruent shapes have the same size and shape, often resulting in incorrect assumptions or calculations when comparing geometric figures.

Misconception 1352: Believes cumulative frequency is plotted at the midpoint of the class
Expanded misconception:
Believes cumulative frequency is plotted at the midpoint of the class rather than correctly plotting the cumulative frequency at the upper or lower boundary of the class interval depending on whether the question requires accumulation below or above a certain point.

Misconception 1353: Believes two odd numbers multiply to give an even number
Expanded misconception:
Believes that in multiplication, even and odd properties are determined by the product of the parity (even/odd) of the individual numbers rather than applying correct rules of arithmetic, such as: an odd number multiplied by an odd number always gives an odd number.

Misconception 1354: Does not know how to calculate the position of a term in a sequence
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand or is unable to apply sequence or pattern rules to determine the position of a term or the nth term in a sequence, such as arithmetic or geometric sequence, including incorrectly applying sequence formulae, reversing or omitting critical sequence operations, or consistently misidentifying term positions.

Misconception 1355: Removes a zero from within the digits when rounding
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the full digit sequence, particularly ignoring or inappropriately removing zeros within or at the end of the number, leading to incorrect rounding, significant figure representation, or calculation results.

Misconception 1356: Confuses the use of LCM and HCF in real life examples 
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the use of LCM and HCF in real-life examples, often incorrectly selecting LCM instead of HCF in situations where the smallest common multiple is not required, or mistakenly using HCF when the purpose is to find the largest common factor or when dealing with situational questions that require them to determine the number of times something fits into a larger quantity, or vice versa.

Misconception 1357: Thinks only labelled dimensions need to be included when calculating the perimeter
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider all relevant dimensions or parts of a shape when calculating its perimeter, often only including labelled or obviously visible sides and neglecting unlabelled or implied ones.

Misconception 1358: Believes you can have decimals in a simplified fraction
Expanded misconception:
Failure to apply the basic principle that a fraction is in simplest form (or lowest terms) when the numerator and denominator have no common factors other than 1, often evidenced by retaining decimal points or incorrectly performing reduction, and incorrectly accepting fractions with decimals as simplified.

Misconception 1359: Does not know how to round up when a decimal place value column contains a 9
Expanded misconception:
Does not know how to round numbers to the nearest place value when a decimal place value column contains a 9, specifically struggles with identifying the correct place to round or increments the digit in the next higher place value column incorrectly.

Misconception 1360: When solving an equation, multiplies instead of adding
Expanded misconception:
When solving an equation, confuses the operation or order of operations, specifically misinterpreting or swapping addition and multiplication, often resulting in incorrect application of mathematical operations or incorrect calculation of results.

Misconception 1361: Does not recognise that addition is the inverse operation of subtraction
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the inverse relationship between addition and subtraction and incorrectly applies subtraction in place of addition, or vice versa, when solving equations or word problems.

Misconception 1362: Believes that the angle on a pie chart is the frequency multiplied by 10
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets the scaling of pie chart angles, specifically believing that the angle is proportional to the frequency or category value multiplied by a fixed arbitrary constant, rather than being directly proportional to the percentage or fraction of the whole.

Misconception 1363: Uses 2 letters to describe an angle
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands trigonometric notation and uses '2' to represent '2θ' (2 times theta) or incorrectly recognizes the angle identifier and thinks it is describing the angle in terms of digits instead of using Greek letters such as 'θ', 'α', 'β' to represent it.

Misconception 1364: Believes the mode is the frequency rather than the value/group
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between the concept of mode as the value or group that appears most frequently and the frequency of occurrence itself, mistakenly identifying the count of occurrences as the mode rather than the actual mode.

Misconception 1365: When ordering integers, orders from the digits at the end of the number instead of the digits at the start
Expanded misconception:
Prioritizes last digit when comparing numerical values, often focusing on the units place and neglecting the place value of the leading digits, leading to incorrect ordering or magnitude comparisons.

Misconception 1366: Subtracts instead of multiplies when using grid method
Expanded misconception:
Misuses or misapplies operation signs when applying procedural methods, such as substituting multiplication with subtraction, division, or addition when using strategies like the grid method, arrays, or area models.

Misconception 1367: Thinks a dashed line marking a region means the line is included
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets boundary notations, thinking a dashed line or a similar non-standard boundary marking in a mathematical diagram or graph indicates that the line or boundary is part of the solution or included in the defined region, rather than simply being a visual representation of it, possibly not included or partially included.

Misconception 1368: Confuses parallel and transversal
Expanded misconception:
Confuses properties and relationships involving parallel and transversal lines, often leading to incorrect identification of corresponding, alternate interior, alternate exterior, interior, or exterior angles.

Misconception 1369: Halves the radius to find the diameter
Expanded misconception:
Confuses key terms or properties of geometric shapes, particularly thinking that the diameter is equal to the radius or half the circumference, rather than being twice the radius or the longest possible distance across a circle passing through its center.

Misconception 1370: Does not believe a shape can have two correct names e.g. rectangle and parallelogram
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognize that geometric shapes can have multiple correct names or classifications due to shared properties or characteristics, and instead believes that each shape has a unique and singular name.

Misconception 1371: When drawing bar diagram for ratio, shows the total as the difference between parts
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the concept of a part-to-part ratio representation, incorrectly calculating the total or whole as the difference between the parts rather than as the sum of the parts, leading to an incorrect or incomplete bar diagram representation of ratio.

Misconception 1372: Ordered from largest to smallest
Expanded misconception:
Ordered numbers or values from largest to smallest without considering the specific mathematical operation or context, such as failing to consider the impact of negative numbers or zero, misunderstanding the concept of absolute value or the effect of operations on number magnitude.

Misconception 1373: Does not recognise a unit of time
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognise and appropriately apply the concept of a unit of time, including converting between different time units (e.g., seconds to minutes, minutes to hours), misunderstanding time intervals, or inappropriately applying time-related calculations.

Misconception 1374: When multiplying fractions, multiplies the denominator
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to invert and multiply when dividing by a fraction, or mistakenly applies multiplication rules when adding or subtracting fractions with unlike denominators, or misunderstands the role of the numerator and denominator when performing operations involving fractions."

Misconception 1375: Confuses the radius and theta when working with sectors 
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the radius and central angle (theta) when working with sectors, potentially using the radius in place of theta or substituting theta in place of the radius in calculations or incorrectly scaling the radius based on theta, or failing to adjust theta based on changes to the radius.

Misconception 1376: Thinks that a larger shaded area represents a larger fraction
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for the size and scale of different representations when comparing fractions, often mistakenly believing that a larger shaded area or a portion of a larger object corresponds to a larger fraction.

Misconception 1377: Square roots when asked for second multiple
Expanded misconception:
"When asked for a second multiple or alternative solution, provides or relies on square roots as an alternative, even when the original number does not have a multiple or square root relationship with the given problem."

Misconception 1378: When solving a problem using written division (bus-stop method), carries the wrong digit
Expanded misconception:
When solving a problem using written division (bus-stop method), carries the wrong digit, such as writing an incorrect digit in the quotient, remainder, or incorrect regrouping, indicating a misunderstanding of place value or the standard procedure for multi-digit division.

Misconception 1379: Rounds down instead of up
Expanded misconception:
Fails to round correctly, consistently rounding down instead of up when required by rules of arithmetic operations, such as half-up or half-even rounding methods.

Misconception 1380: When collecting like terms, assigns positivity or negativity incorrectly.
Expanded misconception:
When collecting like terms, assigns positivity or negativity incorrectly, often resulting from failure to apply the correct sign when combining terms, adding instead of subtracting or vice versa, or exhibiting inconsistent handling of negative signs.

Misconception 1381: Believes the gradient is where the line intersects the y axis
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the y-intercept with the gradient of a linear equation, incorrectly assuming the point where a line crosses the y-axis represents the rate of change of the line rather than the point at which x=0.

Misconception 1382: When given two similar objects and asked to find the length scale factor, believes the length scale factor is the area scale factor
Expanded misconception:
Confuses or misunderstands scale factor concepts, specifically that when given two similar objects and asked to find the length or linear scale factor, believes the length scale factor is the area or volume scale factor, or neglects to square/cube the scale factor when comparing quantities of differing dimensions.

Misconception 1383: When two digits sum to 10 or more during an addition problem, does not add one to the preceding digit
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the concept of regrouping or carrying in addition, specifically when two digits sum to 10 or more, neglecting to add one to the preceding digit or digits.

Misconception 1384: When reading decimals on a number line, assumes each dash is 0.5
Expanded misconception:
When reading decimals or fractions on a number line, assumes each equal dash or unit interval represents a fixed value of 0.1 or 0.5, regardless of the actual scale or unit value.

Misconception 1385: Struggles to draw 3D shapes on isometric paper
Expanded misconception:
Fails to accurately represent 3D geometric shapes, such as cubes and rectangles, on isometric paper by inappropriately drawing parallel lines, incorrect angles, or inconsistent scaling.

Misconception 1386: Confuses the method for finding the gradient and length of a line segment
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the method for finding the gradient (slope) of a line segment with the method for finding its length, such as incorrectly using the distance formula to find the gradient or vice versa.

Misconception 1387: Does not understand the effect of consecutive subtractions
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the concept that consecutive subtractions represent an equivalent addition of the negative counterpart, leading to incorrect ordering and calculation of operations.

Misconception 1388: Does not understand the term quadratic
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognize or apply the concept of quadratic equations, including the general form (ax^2 + bx + c = 0), characteristics of quadratic expressions, or common solution methods such as factoring, quadratic formula, or graphing.

Misconception 1389: Mistakes lines as parallel
Expanded misconception:
Mistakes non-intersecting lines as parallel, or mistakes intersecting lines that do not satisfy the alternate interior angles theorem as perpendicular or parallel, due to a lack of correct understanding of the properties of parallel and perpendicular lines.

Misconception 1390: Believes having the gradient and y-intercept is not enough information to write an equation for a straight line
Expanded misconception:
Believes having the gradient (slope) and y-intercept is not enough information to uniquely define and write an equation for a straight line in the slope-intercept form (y = mx + b).

Misconception 1391: Does not know how to find the y intercept when given two other sets of coordinates that lie on a straight line
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the slope-intercept form or two-point form to find the y-intercept of a linear equation given two other sets of coordinates on the same line, possibly due to a lack of understanding of the relationship between slope, intercept, and coordinates, or difficulty in selecting and applying the correct method for solving the equation.

Misconception 1392: Does not include brackets when required
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for the order of operations or operator precedence by not applying brackets, parentheses, or other grouping symbols correctly, or neglects to apply the correct order of operations when brackets or parentheses are present in an expression.

Misconception 1393: Does not know that a single letter labels a vertex
Expanded misconception:
Possesses inadequate understanding of coordinate plane terminology and notation, specifically unable to recognize that in the context of graphs and equations, a single letter often represents or labels a point or vertex rather than an operation or expression.

Misconception 1394: Identifies the first term rather than the next term in a sequence
Expanded misconception:
Fails to transition to the correct subsequent term within a sequence or pattern, often incorrectly selecting the first or preceding term, rather than the intended next term in the sequence.

Misconception 1395: When calculating volume expressed in cubes, counts the visible cubes only
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for non-visible parts when calculating volume, only considering the visible surface area or the number of cubes that are directly observable, neglecting any hidden or overlapped components that contribute to the overall volume.

Misconception 1396: Finds a percentage when asked for a fraction
Expanded misconception:
Finds a percentage when asked for a fraction, or vice versa, often due to a lack of understanding of the relationship between fractions and percentages, and how to convert between them.

Misconception 1397: Believes point will move diagonally when reflecting in a horizontal or vertical line
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that a reflection over a horizontal or vertical line results in a point moving in a straight line, parallel to the line of reflection, and instead incorrectly assumes that the point moves diagonally or at an angle, demonstrating a lack of understanding of the geometric properties of line reflections.

Misconception 1398: Divided by 100 rather than 1000
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or apply the place value difference between hundreds and thousands, resulting in incorrect calculations involving division or conversion by powers of 10.

Misconception 1399: Fails to identify any rotational symmetry 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to identify any rotational symmetry, incorrectly assuming the shape has no rotational symmetry or neglecting to consider all possible rotations (e.g., 90, 180, or 360 degrees) and number of times the shape looks the same during a rotation.

Misconception 1400: Believes dividing a negative by a negative gives a negative answer
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that dividing a negative number by a negative number results in a positive quotient, typically due to a misunderstanding of the rule for the sign of the quotient, or incorrect application of the rule that two negative signs make a positive.

Misconception 1401: Believes the sin graph relates to the cos function
Expanded misconception:
Confuses trigonometric identities or functions, specifically incorrect application of sine and cosine relationships, such as assuming the sine graph is equivalent to the cosine function, or failing to recognize the phase shift difference between the two.

Misconception 1402: Rounds down instead of up when next digit is 5
Expanded misconception:
Rounds down instead of up when the next digit is 5 or greater in a multi-digit arithmetic operation.

Misconception 1403: When two digits multiply to 10 or more during a multiplication problem, does not add carried value to the preceding digit
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands place value when regrouping in multiplication, failing to recognize that when partial products are 10 or greater, they require regrouping, leading to incorrect handling of carries or the tens place.

Misconception 1404: Believes that when multiplying an integer by a decimal greater than 1, you multiply by the integer component of the decimal but add on the decimal component multiplied by 10.
Expanded misconception:
Believes that when multiplying an integer by a decimal greater than 1, you separate the decimal into its integer and fractional components, then multiply the integer by the integer component and add the product of the integer and the fractional component multiplied by 10, or similarly, misapplies place value or incorrectly handles the conversion between decimal forms.

Misconception 1405: Cannot accurately estimate capacity
Expanded misconception:
Over/underestimates quantities or capacities due to inability to perform rough mental calculations or inaccurately compares magnitudes.

Misconception 1406: Does not recognise that interior and exterior angles are supplementary
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognise or consistently apply the fact that interior and exterior angles of a polygon are supplementary, including failing to identify the correct angle relationships around a point.

Misconception 1407: Confuses histograms with bar charts
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between histograms and bar charts, often incorrectly interpreting the x-axis as discrete categories rather than continuous values or intervals, or incorrectly assuming equal intervals in histograms, and not understanding that histograms are used for continuous data and bar charts are used for categorical or discrete data.

Misconception 1408: Does not realise that adding a larger number gives a larger answer
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to apply the concept of addition as a process that combines two or more values, resulting in a new total that is greater than each of the original values, often resulting in selecting an answer less than the sum, especially when the addends are of significantly different magnitudes."

Misconception 1409: Confuses 'and' with 'or' in probability
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct logical operator in probability, specifically confusing the intersection ('and') and union ('or') of events, which may result in incorrect calculations of probabilities for combined events.

Misconception 1410: Believes to find the next traingle number you add the previous two numbers together i.e. Fibonacci
Expanded misconception:
Believes that the sequence of triangle numbers follows a pattern similar to Fibonacci sequence, where each term is the sum of the two preceding terms, rather than correctly understanding that triangle numbers are generated by adding consecutive integers, starting from 1.

Misconception 1411: Describes term to term rules using position to term rules
Expanded misconception:
"Wrongly applies a position-based rule to individual terms within an expression, such as treating a term as a standalone entity based on its order or position, and ignores the effect of adjacent terms and overall expression properties."

Misconception 1412: Believes y=f(-x) is a reflection in y=x
Expanded misconception:
Believes y=f(-x) is a reflection in y=x, rather than y=-x, often mistakenly assuming that the negative sign affects the y-axis instead of the x-axis.

Misconception 1413: Assumes the negative sign in a power has no meaning
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or apply the properties of negative exponents and the effect of the negative sign on the entire power, often treating it as an indication of magnitude rather than a representation of the reciprocal or opposite of the base raised to a certain power.

Misconception 1414: Believes instant change of velocity is possible. 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or apply the concept of acceleration, specifically believes that an object's velocity can change instantaneously without a corresponding change in force or acceleration over time.

Misconception 1415: Assumes every dash on a scale represents an increase of 1
Expanded misconception:
Assumes every dash, tick mark, or interval on a number line or scale represents an equal and fixed increase, often one unit, without considering the actual scale or context provided.

Misconception 1416: Adds instead of multiplies
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct order of operations and/or incorrectly interprets arithmetic operations, specifically replacing multiplication with addition, as evidenced by summing the multiplicand and multiplier or treating the multiplication symbol as an addition symbol.

Misconception 1417: When solving an equation forgets to eliminate the coefficient in front of the variable in the last step
Expanded misconception:
When solving linear equations, neglects to eliminate the coefficient in front of the variable, often by not dividing or multiplying both sides of the equation by the coefficient before providing the final solution.

Misconception 1418: When the dividend is a mixed number and the divisor is an integer, thinks they only perform the calculation with the integers.
Expanded misconception:
Ignores the fractional part of the dividend when performing division operations involving a mixed number dividend and an integer divisor, incorrectly treating the mixed number as solely its whole number component.

Misconception 1419: Believes any lines that cross are perpendicular
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to understand the properties of intersecting lines, believing that any two lines that cross are necessarily perpendicular and/or that lines can only intersect at right angles, demonstrating a limited understanding of angles and geometric relationships."

Misconception 1420: Believes that when eliminating a variable, regardless of the signs of the terms with matching coefficients, we subtract the equations
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the signs of the terms with matching coefficients when eliminating a variable, always subtracting the equations instead of adding or subtracting them based on which operation eliminates the variable.

Misconception 1421: When factorising into double brackets, finds the sum of two values which give the non variable term in the expression rather than the product
Expanded misconception:
When factorising into double brackets (e.g., a^2 + bx + c), incorrectly identifies the coefficients for the terms in the double brackets by finding the sum of two values that correspond to the constant term (c) rather than the product of two values that multiply to the constant term (c) and add up to the coefficient of the middle term (b).

Misconception 1422: Does not recognise km as a unit of length 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognise or apply standard units of measurement, such as km, m, cm, mm, for length, leading to errors in calculations or conversions.

Misconception 1423: Struggles to estimate 3D volume
Expanded misconception:
Struggles to estimate 3D volume, specifically difficulties in visualizing or calculating the correct volume of rectangular or irregularly-shaped objects, including misunderstandings about proportional relationships between dimensions and how to convert between units of volume.

Misconception 1424: Thinks numbers greater than 1 cannot be converted into fractions
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that whole numbers, decimals, or other numerical values can be converted or represented as equivalent fractions, potentially due to a limited understanding of the concept of equivalence or the process of converting between different numerical forms.

Misconception 1425: Believes two lines of the same length are parallel
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that parallel lines require consistent width and no convergence, sometimes misconstruing vertical or horizontal lines of the same length as parallel, neglecting corresponding angles, or misapplying properties of parallel lines.

Misconception 1426: Thinks rule for calculating interior angles only applies to regular polygons
Expanded misconception:
Thinks rule for calculating interior angles of polygons (e.g., (n-2) * 180 degrees) only applies to regular polygons and not irregular polygons with equal or varying side lengths, or applies incorrectly to other specific polygons such as right-angled or obtuse-angled polygons.

Misconception 1427: Believes cube numbers are just multiples of 3
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the concept of perfect cubes and confuses them with either multiples of a number (e.g., cube numbers as simply multiples of 3), or with raising a number to an incorrect power (e.g., cube of a number as the number raised to the power of 2 or 4, rather than 3), or both.

Misconception 1428: Does not understand the meaning of "value"
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the concept of absolute or relative value, and/or has difficulty comparing and determining the magnitude of values in different mathematical contexts, often resulting in incorrect ordering or calculation errors.

Misconception 1429: Believes equivalent fractions are formed with addition not multiplication
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that equivalent fractions are formed by multiplying both the numerator and denominator by the same non-zero number, instead believing they are created by adding the same number to or subtracting the same number from the numerator and/or denominator.

Misconception 1430: Does not recognise that the intersection of a Venn diagram represents those belonging to both sets
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that sets or groups with overlapping membership require the intersection of Venn diagrams to be correctly represented, leading to incorrect inclusion or exclusion of shared characteristics when identifying commonalities or differences between those groups or sets; this includes misidentifying or underestimating the intersection area as separate membership or failing to consider it in calculations or conclusions.

Misconception 1431: Believes you add the whole to the numerators, ignoring denominators, when adding a mixed number to a proper fraction with the same denominator.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the rules of adding mixed numbers and proper fractions with the same denominator by incorrectly adding the whole number to the numerators and ignoring the denominators, resulting in incorrect regrouping of whole numbers and fractions.

Misconception 1432: When factorising into double brackets, believes the sum of the two values in the brackets equals bx rather than b
Expanded misconception:
When factorising quadratic expressions into the form ax^2 + bx + c = a(x + p)(x + q), believes the sum of the two values in the brackets (p and q) equals bx, rather than b; may mistakenly think that ax^2 and a(x + p)(x + q) can be expanded separately, leading to errors in identifying coefficients.

Misconception 1433: When factorising into single brackets, only factors out the numerical values and forgets to factorise possible variables
Expanded misconception:
Forges expressions into single brackets, but tends to factor out only numerical values or constants while neglecting or incorrectly handling variable terms, resulting in incomplete or inaccurate factorisation.

Misconception 1434: Believes any adjacent angles sum to 180 degrees
Expanded misconception:
Believes any two adjacent angles sum to 180 degrees, regardless of whether they form a linear pair or not, demonstrating a lack of understanding of the specific geometric conditions required for adjacent angles to be supplementary.

Misconception 1435: When subtracting from a negative thinks the answer must be positive
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the properties of negative numbers during subtraction operations, mistakenly believing that the result of subtracting a positive or negative value from a negative number will always be positive, thus leading to errors in determining the correct sign of the outcome.

Misconception 1436: Confuses cubic and exponential graphs
Expanded misconception:
Misapplies or confuses properties, characteristics, or behaviors of cubic and exponential functions, such as growth rates, curve shapes, inflection points, or asymptotes, which can result in difficulty identifying, describing, or distinguishing between the two types of graphs.

Misconception 1437: Assumes the fractional index denotes the root and power the other way around
Expanded misconception:
Assumes the fractional index denotes the root and power the other way around, leading to the incorrect reversal of the fractional exponent rules in calculations, such as interpreting a power with a rational exponent a^(m/n) as taking the nth power of a to the mth root of a.

Misconception 1438: Believes division of surds always results in an integer
Expanded misconception:
Believes division of surds (radicals or roots) always results in an integer or a simplified expression without a radical in the denominator, indicating a lack of understanding of rationalizing denominators and the properties of surds.

Misconception 1439: When solving simultaneous equations, assumes every variable has equal value.
Expanded misconception:
When solving simultaneous equations, assumes every variable has equal value, fails to consider the relationships between variables, or incorrectly applies the concept of substitution, causing incorrect treatments of variables during equation manipulation.

Misconception 1440: Believes 180 degrees is an obtuse angle
Expanded misconception:
Believes that angles greater than 90 degrees but less than 270 degrees are all considered obtuse angles, failing to recognize that a straight angle measures exactly 180 degrees.

Misconception 1441: Thinks decimal place value works the same as integer place value, ones will follow tenths
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that decimal place value differs from integer place value, specifically when calculating, comparing, or rounding decimals, and expects that the digits beyond the tenths place will follow the same rules as the ones place in integers.

Misconception 1442: Think cross section of a prism is the biggest face
Expanded misconception:
Misconceives the cross-sectional area of a prism, thinking it refers to the largest surface area, rather than the area of the shape formed by a cross-section cut perpendicular to the height of the prism.

Misconception 1443: Thinks that when raising a variable with a coefficient to a power, only the coefficient is affected by the power
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply exponent rules, mistakenly thinking that when raising a variable with a coefficient to a power, only the coefficient is affected by the power, and neglects to raise the variable to that power.

Misconception 1444: Does not understand that the negative outside the bracket means answer will be negative
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that a negative sign outside parentheses or brackets indicates a change in sign for all terms within the parentheses or brackets, resulting in a negative answer.

Misconception 1445: Believes you find the angle for a pie chart sector by doing its frequency multiplied by total frequency
Expanded misconception:
The conception that pie chart angles can be directly calculated by multiplying a category's frequency or value by the total frequency, rather than using the correct proportion or percentage of the data, and failing to apply the formula 360 * (category frequency or value / total frequency or sum of values).

Misconception 1446: When calculating perimeter, only adds two sides
Expanded misconception:
"When calculating the perimeter of a shape, fails to consider adding all sides, instead only adding two adjacent sides or a portion of the total sides required, possibly due to misunderstanding the concept of a closed shape or incorrect recall of the perimeter formula."

Misconception 1447: Writes the remainder as the digit after the decimal point
Expanded misconception:
Fails to fully perform division and instead writes the remainder as the digit after the decimal point, not converting it to an actual decimal by dividing by the divisor.

Misconception 1448: Believes the solutions of a quadratic equation are the negative of the absolute values of the constants in the factorised form
Expanded misconception:
Believes the solutions of a quadratic equation, particularly when in factored form ax^2 + bx + c = (x - r)(x - s), are the negative of the absolute values of the constants (r or s) in the factorized form, rather than the actual roots or solutions, which may be positive or negative and can be obtained by solving for x.

Misconception 1449: Believes rounding numbers down would give an overestimate
Expanded misconception:
Ignores the role of rounding in estimation, specifically believing that rounding numbers down (rather than up) would give an overestimate when it would actually result in an underestimate.

Misconception 1450: Does not understand how to apply sohcahtoa
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand how to apply the SOH-CAH-TOA trigonometric ratios correctly to find side lengths or angles in a right-angled triangle, specifically confusing or incorrectly substituting sine, cosine, or tangent values or misunderstanding their relationships with the opposite, adjacent, and hypotenuse sides.

Misconception 1451: Assumes that the square root is the only type of root
Expanded misconception:
"Assumes that the square root is the only type of root present in an expression, leading to neglect of other roots, and/or incorrectly applying square root rules and properties to other types of roots, such as cube roots or nth roots."

Misconception 1452: When converting ratio to fractions, puts the other side of the ratio as the denominator rather than the total
Expanded misconception:
When converting a ratio to a fraction, incorrectly places the corresponding quantity as the denominator instead of using the total or the sum of the parts as the denominator.

Misconception 1453: Subtracts from left to right, ignoring the word ‘from’
Expanded misconception:
Fails to interpret the word 'from' as requiring a subtraction operation in the reverse order, instead incorrectly distributing the subtraction operation from left to right.

Misconception 1454: Believes they can eliminate a variable without making the coefficients the same in simultaneous equations
Expanded misconception:
Attempts to solve systems of linear equations without eliminating variables by making the coefficients the same through multiplication by scalar values, such as adding or subtracting equivalent equations, failing to consider that the elimination method requires coefficients to be the same in order for the variable to be truly cancelled out.

Misconception 1455: Calculates circumference when asked for area
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between related geometric properties, specifically confuses perimeter or circumference of a shape with its area, and selects a formula or calculates incorrectly as a result.

Misconception 1456: Believes linear graphs can be curved
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the properties of linear and nonlinear functions, specifically believing that linear graphs can have curved or rounded shapes and/or exhibit curvature, whereas in reality, linear functions always have a constant rate of change and a straight-line graph.

Misconception 1457: Does not understand the interpretation of the fx column in a frequency table
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the interpretation of the frequency (fx) column in a frequency table, specifically that it represents the total count or frequency of occurrences for each value or category, and fails to use it to calculate summary statistics such as mean or total sum, or mistakenly uses it interchangeably with other columns, such as the x or score column.

Misconception 1458: Believes 0 multiplied by a number gives the number
Expanded misconception:
"Lacks understanding of the zero property of multiplication, believing that 0 multiplied by a number will yield that number, rather than 0."

Misconception 1459: Does not recognise that a surd will simplify to a whole number and not longer be a surd if the number under the surd is a square number
Expanded misconception:
Fails to identify and simplify surds to whole numbers when the radicand is a perfect square, resulting in unnecessary retention of the square root symbol in calculations.

Misconception 1460: Identifies second decimal place value when asked for the second significant figure
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly distinguishes between decimal place value and significant figures, particularly when identifying the second significant figure, often mistakenly assuming it to be the second digit after the decimal point rather than considering the position of the digit within the overall numerical value.

Misconception 1461: Believes parallel lines have gradients that multiply to give -1
Expanded misconception:
Believes parallel lines have gradients that multiply to give -1, assuming this relationship applies to all pairs of lines, not accounting for lines where the concept is not applicable (e.g. horizontal lines or vertical lines) and failing to consider that this property is unique to perpendicular lines.

Misconception 1462: Believes height represents frequency on a histogram
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that the area of the bars on a histogram, not the height, represents the frequency or relative frequency of each category or interval, leading to misinterpretation of the distribution of data.

Misconception 1463: When adding fractions, adds the numerators and multiplies the denominators
Expanded misconception:
Fails to find a common denominator before adding fractions, instead mistakenly treating fraction addition as addition of the numerators and multiplication of the denominators, disregarding the fundamental rule that fractions must have a common denominator for addition to be valid.

Misconception 1464: When solving simultaneous equations graphically, they think the solutions are the y-intercepts
Expanded misconception:
When solving simultaneous linear equations graphically, they mistakenly identify the solution as either the y-intercepts of one of the lines, the x-intercepts of one of the lines, or any point on one of the lines rather than the point of intersection where the two lines meet.

Misconception 1465: Uses outcome as numerator in probability 
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to understand the concept of probability as a ratio of favorable outcomes to total outcomes; consistently uses either the favorable outcome or the total outcome as the numerator or denominator incorrectly, or misinterprets the role of the outcome in calculating probability."

Misconception 1466: Thinks there are 100kg in a ton
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recall or apply the correct conversion rate between commonly used units of measurement, specifically confusing the metric unit of a ton (1000 kilograms or 1000 litres of water) with another unit or the base-ten unit misconception, possibly substituting it with the unit of a hundred (or 100 units) such as kilogram or litres.

Misconception 1467: Believes the power button writes a fraction
Expanded misconception:
Fails to follow order of operations, specifically, incorrectly treating the exponentiation operator (power) as a multiplication operation or not applying it at all.

Misconception 1468: Orders integers based on first digits without taking into account place value
Expanded misconception:
Orders integers or numbers based on leading digits or the most significant digits without considering or taking into account the concept of place value or the proportional relation of each digit within a number, resulting in incorrect or incomplete comparisons of numerical values.

Misconception 1469: Does not recognise when simplifying a fraction may be helpful
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognise or apply simplification of intermediate results or fractions when performing multi-step calculations, potentially leading to incorrect or overly complex solutions.

Misconception 1470: Does not know the meaning of the word "product"
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the term "product" to refer to the result of multiplication, possibly confusing it with other mathematical operations or concepts.

Misconception 1471: Mixes up colder and hotter when comparing temperatures
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct order when comparing temperatures, mixing up 'colder' and 'hotter' due to a lack of understanding of the concept of high and low temperatures on the temperature scale.

Misconception 1472: Believes that they can reverse a percentage decrease by dividing by the decimal multiplier of the percentage itself, not the multiplier for the percentage decrease.
Expanded misconception:
Believes that they can reverse a percentage change by applying the inverse operation with the wrong factor, in that they incorrectly use the decimal multiplier of the percentage itself instead of the decimal multiplier representing the proportional decrease (or increase), leading to miscalculations when attempting to 'undo' the percentage change.

Misconception 1473: Believes adding two numbers finds their lowest common multiple
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply basic arithmetic operations correctly, specifically, mistakenly uses concepts of multiplication or factors, such as finding the lowest common multiple instead of adding numbers.

Misconception 1474: Forgets to add on the starting value when identifying a position on a number line
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly account for the starting value or reference point when identifying a position on a number line, resulting in misinterpretation of distances or coordinates.

Misconception 1475: Believes y=f(-x) is reflection in the x-axis
Expanded misconception:
Believes y=f(-x) is a reflection in the x-axis, rather than recognizing it as a reflection of the function f(x) in the y-axis.

Misconception 1476: Thinks 1 square metre = 1000 square centimetres
Expanded misconception:
"Ignores conversion between units of area, often confusing square centimetres (cm²) with square metres (m²), specifically thinking that 1 square metre equals 1000 square centimetres due to treating square units similarly to linear units (1 metre = 100 centimetres)."

Misconception 1477: When using a scale factor greater than 1, thinks you multiply by the scale factor to go from the larger to the smaller shape.
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly applies scale factor conversations, thinking that to transform from a larger shape to a smaller shape, you multiply by the scale factor, rather than dividing by it.

Misconception 1478: When expressing one quantity (x) as a fraction of another (y), believes the answer is 1/x.
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to understand the concept of proportions and ratios, incorrectly inverting the fraction when expressing one quantity as a proportion of another, believing the answer is 1/x or x in the denominator instead of the correct form x/y."

Misconception 1479: Thinks you multiply by the numerator and add the denominator when converting a mixed number to an improper fraction
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply correct order of operations when converting between mixed numbers and improper fractions, specifically adding the denominator to the product of the denominator and the whole number, or multiplying by the numerator when it should be added, resulting in an incorrect fraction conversion.

Misconception 1480: Does not recognise the notation for parallel sides
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply or recognise mathematical notation for shapes with parallel sides (e.g. || for parallel lines, or the symbol for parallel sides in a triangle), leading to incorrect assumptions or solutions.

Misconception 1481: When describing a translation, miscounts when crossing an axis
Expanded misconception:
When describing a translation, miscounts or incorrectly accounts for the number of units moved, particularly when crossing an axis, resulting in an inaccurate representation of the movement or final position.

Misconception 1482: Believes you only need to multiply the integer by the integer in a integer x decimal calculation
Expanded misconception:
Fails to realize that when multiplying a decimal by a whole number, the whole number must be converted into a decimal with a zero in the tenths place and then follow the standard rules of decimal multiplication, leading to errors in calculation when omitting this conversion step and only multiplying the integer part.

Misconception 1483: Believes vertically opposite angles sum to 90 degrees
Expanded misconception:
Misapplies or misunderstands angle properties: confuses vertically opposite angles with complementary angles, mistakenly believing that vertically opposite angles are supplementary to a specific angle or sum to 90 degrees.

Misconception 1484: Thinks 1l = 10 000 cubic centimetres
Expanded misconception:
Confuses or incorrectly converts between units of volume or capacity (e.g., milliliters, liters, and cubic centimeters) and fails to recall that 1 liter (L) is equal to 1,000 milliliters (mL) or 1,000 cubic centimeters (cm³), often demonstrating an off-by-a-factor-of-ten error.

Misconception 1485: When given an error interval for a decimal that ends in a zero, thinks you give the error interval as if the zero was not present (e.g. 1dp rather than 2dp)
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to fully acknowledge the precision of a given decimal value ending in one or more zeros, and instead applies error intervals or rounding rules as if the trailing zeros were insignificant or absent."

Misconception 1486: When using the capture-recapture method for sampling, believes you subtract the number marked second from the number caught second to find the best estimate of population size
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for individuals captured twice in the capture-recapture method for population estimation, specifically incorrectly applying subtraction instead of the correct application of the Lincoln-Petersen estimation formula to calculate the minimum estimate of population size.

Misconception 1487: Believes you can square root a negative number
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that the square root of a negative number is undefined in the real number system, leading to incorrect mathematical operations or results when encountering negative numbers under a square root.

Misconception 1488: Confuses negative and positive coordinates when working with graphs in four quadrants
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly interpret the concept of negative and positive coordinates when working with graphs in four quadrants, often resulting in the incorrect placement of points, slope miscalculations, or orientation confusion, possibly due to misunderstandings about the relationship between the x-axis, y-axis, and the quadrants.

Misconception 1489: Believes an outlier is the smallest value in a data set, even if this value is close to the mean
Expanded misconception:
Believes an outlier is always the smallest or largest value in a data set, disregarding the actual definition of an outlier as a value that is significantly distant from the mean and deviates from the overall pattern of the data set.

Misconception 1490: Writes fraction rather than fraction of amount
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly interprets the concept of division as a change in unit, or misunderstands that division should yield a proportion of the original quantity, resulting in writing the fraction form or a part of the original amount rather than correctly scaling it to the required proportion or resulting value.

Misconception 1491: Thinks there are 100kg in a tonne
Expanded misconception:
Confuses units of measurement, particularly in the mass or weight system, incorrectly equating 1 tonne with 100 kilograms instead of 1,000 kilograms, or fails to apply conversion factors in metric unit calculations.

Misconception 1492: Believes the whole and remainder are the other way when changing an improper fraction to a mixed number
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly convert between improper fractions and mixed numbers, often incorrectly switching or misinterpreting the whole number part and the fractional remainder.

Misconception 1493: Thinks the positive and negative terms in a sequence will have the same absolute values
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that terms with opposite signs but the same absolute values in a sequence may cancel or partially offset each other, rather than having equivalent or additive effects.

Misconception 1494: Believes 0 is not a real solution to an equation
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or consider zero as a valid or real solution to mathematical equations or expressions, possibly due to misunderstanding the concept of zero in algebra or neglecting to check if zero satisfies the equation.

Misconception 1495: Thinks the fraction 1/n can express sharing any number of items between n people
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands the concept of division and fractions, believing that the fraction 1/n only represents sharing a single whole item into equal parts for n people, rather than understanding that it can represent the reciprocal of n, and thus apply to sharing any given quantity into n equal parts.

Misconception 1496: Ignores grouping within a fraction and carries out the division first
Expanded misconception:
Ignores or misapplies the order of operations within fractions or expressions with multiple operations and groups, specifically neglecting to recognize or apply the proper order of operations within a fraction, such as following the order of operations inside parentheses or when working with multinumbers or multidenominators.

Misconception 1497: Thinks the plan view is taken from an angle to include aspects of the side view
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets or incorrectly applies conventional drawing projections, specifically believing that two or more distinct views (e.g. plan and side elevation) can be effectively combined within a single view, or that a single view can be taken from an angle not aligned with standard orthographic projections.

Misconception 1498: Does not understand that a scale factor of enlargement greater than -1, will create a larger image on the other side of the centre of enlargement from the original
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to recognize that a scale factor of enlargement greater than 1 (regardless of its sign) results in a larger image on the opposite side of the center of enlargement, implying a misunderstanding of the relationship between scale factors, image size, and orientation in enlargement transformations."

Misconception 1499: Believes that in y = mx+c, c is the gradient
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the roles of slope (m) and y-intercept (c) in linear equations in slope-intercept form, believing that c represents the rate of change or slope rather than the point at which the line crosses the y-axis.

Misconception 1500: When calculating a scale factor, thinks the division is the other way around.
Expanded misconception:
When calculating a scale factor, mistakenly believes the scale factor is found by dividing the original measurement by the new measurement, rather than the new measurement by the original measurement.

Misconception 1501: Multiplied the base numbers
Expanded misconception:
Ignores exponent rules and only multiplied the base numbers, neglecting to consider exponentiation or evaluation order.

Misconception 1502: When calculating the group width of a group from a grouped frequency table, believes this is found by midpoint multiplied by frequency
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly calculates the group width of a group from a grouped frequency table by using an incorrect formula, often by misunderstanding that the group width is simply the upper limit minus the lower limit, and mistakenly thinking it is the midpoint of the class multiplied by the frequency, the frequency alone, or the range of the data.

Misconception 1503: When drawing a histogram, thinks you plot group midpoint on the horizontal axis
Expanded misconception:
When drawing a histogram, incorrectly believes that the category/group labels should represent the midpoint value, rather than the boundaries or interval of the data range, and plots accordingly, often resulting in misaligned or misinterpreted graphical representations.

Misconception 1504: When multiplying surds, adds the numbers under the roots
Expanded misconception:
When multiplying surds, incorrectly applies the rules by adding the numbers under the roots instead of multiplying them.

Misconception 1505: Thinks co-interior angles are equal
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that all pairs of angles in the same relative position when two lines intersect are equal, and fails to distinguish between co-interior, corresponding, and alternate angles.

Misconception 1506: Believes transforming some terms in simultaneous equations is sufficient
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply transformations to all relevant terms or variables in simultaneous equations, resulting in an incomplete or inconsistent solution.

Misconception 1507: Carries out operations from left to right regardless of priority order
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the order of operations (BODMAS/BIDMAS/PEMDAS), carrying out arithmetic operations from left to right regardless of priority order, thus neglecting the effect of parentheses, exponents, multiplication and division, or addition and subtraction.

Misconception 1508: Assumes the item has not been replaced in a probability question where it has actually been replaced
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for the impact of replacement on probability in situations where objects are put back or reused, leading to incorrect assumptions of independent or mutually exclusive events, and consequently, the calculation of probabilities as if the events were without replacement.

Misconception 1509: Does not realise that all regular shapes of a certain type will be similar
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply properties of similarity in regular shapes, specifically not recognizing that all regular polygons with the same number of sides share proportional side lengths and congruent angles, leading to incorrect assumptions about shape lengths, angle measurements, or scaling factors.

Misconception 1510: Believes subtraction is commutative 
Expanded misconception:
Believes arithmetic operations, specifically subtraction, follow the commutative property where the order of the numbers being subtracted does not affect the result, e.g., a - b = b - a.

Misconception 1511: Does not understand that the probability of something not happening is 1 minus the probability of it happening
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the complement rule in probability, specifically not understanding that the probability of an event not occurring is equal to 1 minus the probability of the event occurring, which may be evident in incorrect calculations or interpretations of conditional probabilities or complementary events.

Misconception 1512: When sketching graphs of factorised quadratics, does not see that the negative of the non variable terms in the brackets give the x intercepts or the roots of the graph.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the relationship between the constants in the factorized quadratic equation and the x-intercepts, assuming either that only the factors with variables provide roots or incorrectly identifying roots as numerical values rather than x values, leading to errors in identifying the x-intercepts of the graph.

Misconception 1513: Does not know the meaning of perpendicular
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand that two lines are perpendicular if and only if they intersect at a right angle (90 degrees) and may incorrectly assume perpendicularly based on line position or another angle, or fails to recognize perpendicular relationships between lines or shapes in various geometric contexts.

Misconception 1514: Adds instead of subtracts
Expanded misconception:
Adds instead of performing the correct operation (addition, subtraction, multiplication, or division), often resulting from a failure to understand the operation symbol or its context in the mathematical expression.

Misconception 1515: When asked to take off a fraction of an amount, subtracts the fraction itself
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the concept of division in relation to fractions or percentages, instead incorrectly using subtraction, often when interpreting phrases such as 'take away a fraction of', resulting in an insufficient or incorrect calculation of the portion being removed.

Misconception 1516: Performs addition ahead of any other operation
Expanded misconception:
Performs addition ahead of any other operation, regardless of the presence of multiplication, division, or exponentiation, and possibly due to misunderstanding the conventional order of operations.

Misconception 1517: Finds unit amount but doesn't multiply up
Expanded misconception:
Finds unit amount but doesn't scale up by multiplying to find total quantity in multi-digit or complex multiplication problems, or in multi-step word problems involving scaled quantities.

Misconception 1518: Mixes up smallest and largest when ordering decimals
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly compare or order decimals, often due to reversing the relationship between the smallest and largest numbers, or incorrectly applying rules for ordering integers to decimals.

Misconception 1519: Assumes that a graph always starts at the origin
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider that graphs do not always start at the origin (0, 0), leading to errors in interpreting and analyzing graphs that have non-standard starting points, are shifted or translated, or represent real-world data with non-zero starting values.

Misconception 1520: Does not recognise litres as a unit of volume
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognize units of volume or conversions between different units of volume, particularly with metric units such as millilitres (mL) and litres (L), or applies incorrect conversions between these units.

Misconception 1521: Thinks that scale factors, other than one, can leave the dimensions of shapes unchanged
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that scale factors, other than 1, can leave the dimensions or proportional relationships of shapes unchanged, and does not apply the multiplication of scale factors to all corresponding measurements.

Misconception 1522: Believes the mean is total frequency divided by sum of categories given
Expanded misconception:
Believes the measure of central tendency, such as mean, is calculated by summing the frequencies or values and then dividing by the sum or combination of the categories or intervals, rather than the total number of observations or values.

Misconception 1523: Does not know how to say a fraction
Expanded misconception:
Does not know how to apply or manipulate fractions correctly, such as adding a number to or dividing another number by a fraction, forgetting to convert between improper fractions and mixed numbers, or having trouble simplifying fractions with different denominators.

Misconception 1524: Believes adding b to any integer gives a multiple of b
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the concept of multiplication or distributive property, incorrectly assuming that adding a number to any integer will result in a multiple of that number.

Misconception 1525: Confuses written 'teen' numbers with their corresponding multiples of 10
Expanded misconception:
Confuses written 'teen' numbers with their corresponding multiples of 10 or tens; may incorrectly perceive 'ten' as 'teen' or vice versa, leading to arithmetic errors, such as treating '13' as if it were '10' or adding an extra '10' in calculations involving teens.

Misconception 1526: Believes that they only need to write non-zero digits when giving a number in digits
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly represent the place value of numbers by omitting leading zeros, often when only non-zero digits are provided or when using numerical shorthand, leading to incorrect computation or answers.

Misconception 1527: Does not know how to find missing lengths in a composite shape
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply appropriate geometric principles to identify and calculate missing lengths in composite shapes, such as not recognizing the relationship between component shapes or not utilizing relevant formulas or properties, resulting in incorrect solutions.

Misconception 1528: Does not know the meaning of alternate
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or apply the concept of alternate or indirect relationships, often interpreting them literally, such that they will reverse or misunderstand the meaning of words like 'not,' 'none,' 'never,' or similar terms that signal a reversal of the statement's original intent.

Misconception 1529: Rounds to the wrong degree of accuracy (significant figures rather than decimal places)
Expanded misconception:
Rounds intermediate or final calculations to the wrong degree of accuracy, either by rounding to an incorrect number of significant figures or decimal places, or by not maintaining the required precision throughout the calculation.

Misconception 1530: Believes a steeper downwards line on a graph means a slower decrease
Expanded misconception:
Confuses slope with rate of change, and specifically, believes that a steeper downwards line on a graph always means a slower decrease, rather than a faster decrease, and may have difficulty interpreting the magnitude and direction of slope in various contexts.

Misconception 1531: Believes you plot frequency rather than cumulative frequency on a cumulative frequency diagram
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the distinction between frequency and cumulative frequency, resulting in incorrect plotting of frequency values rather than cumulative totals on cumulative frequency diagrams.

Misconception 1532: Forgets to include values outside of the Venn diagram when finding the total
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider or include the entire sample space or universal set when solving problems involving Venn diagrams or set theory, and instead makes calculations based solely on the values within the diagram or relevant subsets.

Misconception 1533: When adding powers of 10 adds onto the wrong column
Expanded misconception:
"When performing arithmetic operations involving powers of 10 or multiple-digit numbers, incorrectly applies place value concepts by failing to align numbers or incorrectly carrying values, and subsequently adds onto the wrong column, leading to errors in calculation."

Misconception 1534: Thinks that doubling and then doubling again is not the same as multiplying by 4
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that repeated doubling is equivalent to multiplication by powers of 2, and does not apply the exponentiation property of multiplication consistently in problems involving multiplication by 4 or other powers of 2.

Misconception 1535: Believes that when factorising a quadratic, they can choose any two numbers that multiply to give the constant
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to consider the coefficient of the middle term when factorising a quadratic, thus mistakenly believing they can choose any two numbers that multiply to give the constant, without checking if the sum of these numbers corresponds to the coefficient of the middle term."

Misconception 1536: Divides length of original by length of image rather finding length of image divided by length of original to find an enlargement scale factor.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct ratio when finding scale factors, specifically inverting or misinterpreting the proportion, such as mistaking the enlargement scale factor by dividing the original length by the image length instead of the correct method of dividing the image length by the original length.

Misconception 1537: Believes that in completed square form, the x co-ordinate of the turning point has the same sign as the constant in the brackets.
Expanded misconception:
Believes that in completed square form (y = a(x-h)² + k), the x-coordinate (h) of the vertex or turning point always has the same sign as the constant term within the brackets, failing to recognize that the x-coordinate is actually determined by the coefficient of x in the quadratic expression before completing the square.

Misconception 1538: Does not take into account the whole amount when using a bar model to find a fraction
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the entire quantity or the whole when partitioning or dividing, often resulting in errors with equivalent fractions or proportions in various visual models, such as bar models or ratio tables.

Misconception 1539: Does not consider that all three angles in a triangle sum to 180 degrees
Expanded misconception:
Does not consider or apply the triangle angle sum property (i.e., the sum of the three interior angles of a triangle always equals 180 degrees), leading to incorrect calculations or assumptions about angles in a triangle.

Misconception 1540: Does not know how to simplify algebraic fractions
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the rules of algebra to simplify fractions, including not factoring out common terms, cancelling out common factors between the numerator and denominator, and performing required mathematical operations such as multiplying or dividing under a common denominator.

Misconception 1541: Believes reflection in y = -x changes sign of y coordinate only
Expanded misconception:
A. Misinterpreter of coordinate transformations: believes reflection in y = -x changes sign of y coordinate only, and does not consider its effect on the negative sign of the x-coordinate, or incorrectly adjusts for it, resulting in an error when determining the image after a reflection.

Misconception 1542: Believes that a fraction means dividing the denominator by the numerator
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the relationship between the numerator and denominator, believing that a fraction a/b implies a division of the denominator by the numerator (i.e., b ÷ a), rather than the numerator by the denominator (i.e., a ÷ b).

Misconception 1543: Does not realise that a pair of coordinates can be used to find missing parts of an equation
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the relationship between coordinates and the equation of a line, and does not realise that a pair of coordinates can be used to find missing parts of an equation, such as the slope, y-intercept, or x-intercept, by applying concepts like the slope formula or the point-slope form.

Misconception 1544: Thinks a variable next to a number means write the digits together
Expanded misconception:
Thinks a variable next to a number means write the digits together, rather than understanding that the variable is being multiplied by the number and requires an operation to be evaluated.

Misconception 1545: Believes you find the surface area of a prism by finding the total area of the unique faces
Expanded misconception:
Believes that the surface area of a prism is found only by summing the areas of the unique faces, without accounting for the existence and area of the otherwise identical, but separate, faces.

Misconception 1546: Believes the horizontal axis on a cumulative frequency diagram is labelled upper bound
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that the horizontal axis on a cumulative frequency diagram is typically labelled with the lower bound of each class interval, and instead mistakenly associates the values on this axis with the upper bound.

Misconception 1547: Has forgotten to convert cm to m
Expanded misconception:
Fails to convert units of measurement to the required standard unit when necessary for calculation, such as converting centimeters to meters when required for operations involving meters.

Misconception 1548: Thinks that a unit fraction can be simplified further
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly applies fraction simplification rules to unit fractions, thinking they can be reduced to a lower value, despite the numerator being 1.

Misconception 1549: When asked to multiply fractions, uses the method for adding instead
Expanded misconception:
When asked to multiply fractions, uses the method for adding fractions (or another operation) instead, resulting in adding or substituting the denominators (and sometimes numerators) rather than following the correct procedure of multiplying the numerators and denominators.

Misconception 1550: Believes interior angles are outside a shape
Expanded misconception:
Believes interior angles are outside a shape, incorrectly identifying exterior angles as interior and potentially resulting in incorrect calculations of sums, measures, or properties of angles within a polygon.

Misconception 1551: Does the reciprocal of both fractions when dividing fractions
Expanded misconception:
Does the reciprocal of only the second fraction, but not multiplying it by the first fraction when dividing fractions.

Misconception 1552: Believes term to term rule is applied to position number, not previous term
Expanded misconception:
Misapplies the concept of order of operations, specifically misunderstands the concept of term-by-term calculation or sequentially performing operations from left to right, by incorrectly applying conventions to position within an expression rather than considering the operation performed on or with the preceding term.

Misconception 1553: Thinks that we can use O as zero for place value in Roman numerals
Expanded misconception:
Lacks understanding that Roman numerals do not use a zero placeholder and assumes incorrect representations using 'O' as a zero for place value in Roman numerals, instead of recognizing the absence of a symbol in a given place value, or misinterpreting the concept of Roman numeral representation entirely.

Misconception 1554: Does not know how to identify common factors from algebraic terms
Expanded misconception:
Does not know how to identify and factor out the greatest common factor (GCF) from algebraic terms, often resulting in incorrect simplification or combination of like terms.

Misconception 1555: Believes that we cannot calculate the median of a dataset that contains negative numbers
Expanded misconception:
Has an inaccurate understanding that certain statistical operations or calculations (e.g., median, mean, mode, range) cannot be applied or are invalid when dealing with negative numbers, decimals, fractions, or other non-integer values in a dataset.

Misconception 1556: Writes digits in the order of the pictorial representation rather than using place value columns
Expanded misconception:
Writes digits or values in the order they appear in the problem or representation, rather than aligning or reorganizing them using standard column or place value structures, such as place value columns in arithmetic operations or coordinate order in geometry.

Misconception 1557: Has an incorrect assumption about the definition of a reciprocal
Expanded misconception:
Has an incorrect assumption about the definition of a reciprocal, such as mistakenly equating it with its inverse, thinking it requires a change in operation from multiplication to division without considering the original constants or variables being inverted.

Misconception 1558: Believes dividing a positive by a negative gives a positive answer
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the change in sign that occurs when dividing a positive number by a negative number, consistently resulting in an incorrect positive answer.

Misconception 1559: Believes a downward slope on a speed-time graph shows positive acceleration
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between speed and acceleration, mistakenly interpreting a downward slope on a speed-time graph as increased velocity or positive acceleration, rather than recognizing it as a decrease in speed, and therefore, as negative acceleration or deceleration.

Misconception 1560: Mistakes the number of rows for the total frequency in a frequency table
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that the number of rows or categories in a frequency table represents the different groups or categories being observed, and mistakenly uses this count as the total frequency or total number of observations, rather than summing the actual frequencies for each category.

Misconception 1561: Believes the mean is total frequency divided by something
Expanded misconception:
