Believes the mean is total frequency divided by something, such as the total of another data set, an unrelated number, or another statistical measure, rather than the total count of values or frequency.

Misconception 1562: When substituting into an algebraic term, believes they can disregard the coefficient
Expanded misconception:
"When substituting into an algebraic term, believes they can disregard or incorrectly manipulate the coefficient (e.g., ignore multiplication, treat the coefficient as an exponent), thus performing incorrect arithmetic operations that affect the overall solution."

Misconception 1563: Thinks the order of events does not matter in a question about combined probability with no replacement
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the dependence between events in a combined probability scenario with no replacement and assumes the order or sequence of events does not affect the overall probability.

Misconception 1564: Does not recognise time as a variable from a worded scenario
Expanded misconception:
Fails to identify time as a variable, or misinterprets the intended unit of time (e.g., hours, minutes, seconds), when solving problems presented in worded scenarios or real-world contexts, often resulting in arithmetic errors or incorrect applications of formulas that include time as a variable.

Misconception 1565: Miscounts the area of a shape when there are half squares
Expanded misconception:
Fails to accurately account for partial units or fractional parts of a shape when calculating the area, particularly when encountering regions such as half squares, resulting in an incorrect total area calculation.

Misconception 1566: Adds or subtracts from numerator first when solving an equation with a fraction
Expanded misconception:
Fails to follow the correct order of operations when solving equations with fractions, specifically not following the rule to perform operations on the numerator or denominator according to the rules of the equation, often incorrectly adding or subtracting from the numerator first rather than the denominator or the entire fraction in accordance with the given operation.

Misconception 1567: Does not recognise suitable statistical diagrams for comparing results over time
Expanded misconception:
Fails to identify and distinguish suitable statistical diagrams (e.g. line graphs, bar charts, scatter plots, etc.) for comparing and illustrating trends, patterns, or correlations of data over time, regularly misusing or confusing them with other types of diagrams.

Misconception 1568: Includes the x variable when giving the equation of a horizontal line
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly formulates equation of a horizontal line by either explicitly including the variable x or failing to recognize that the equation should be in the form of y = constant, resulting in an equation that incorrectly accounts for x.

Misconception 1569: Uses term-to-term rule as numerical part of nth term rule
Expanded misconception:
Applies term-to-term rule as numerical part of nth term rule, incorrectly assuming that all sequences can be defined by multiplying the previous term by a common ratio or constant factor.

Misconception 1570: Does not understand the 3-letter convention for labelling angles
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or apply the three-letter convention for labelling angles, including incorrectly switching the middle letter of the angle to another point or using incorrect intermediate points, resulting in incorrect angle identification or calculation.

Misconception 1571: When writing standard form does not give the correct power of ten
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly apply the rules of standard form (exponential notation), specifically not adjusting the exponent to represent the correct power of ten for the given numerical value, or using an incorrect placement of the decimal point within the coefficient.

Misconception 1572: Translates rather than reflects across a line of symmetry
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the concept of reflection to line symmetry, mistakenly performing translations or rotations, resulting in an incorrect final image.

Misconception 1573: When scaling the perimeter multiplies the number of sides by the scale factor
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the concept that when scaling a two-dimensional figure, the perimeter is scaled by the scale factor, but does not incorrectly assume that multiplication is not required; rather, incorrectly applies it by multiplying the number of sides, rather than the lengths of the sides, by the scale factor.

Misconception 1574: Struggles to visualise 2D triangles in a cuboid
Expanded misconception:
Struggles to visualise and understand the spatial relationships between 2D geometric figures, such as triangles, and 3D objects, such as cuboids, making it difficult to accurately identify cross-sectional shapes or predict resulting shapes when a 3D object is sliced.

Misconception 1575: Includes too many possibilities when calculating a combined event in probability
Expanded misconception:
Includes too many possibilities when calculating a combined event in probability by failing to properly account for overlapping or mutually exclusive events, and not adjusting for these in the calculation, likely due to a lack of understanding of the concept of conditional probability or the inclusion-exclusion principle.

Misconception 1576: Divides rather than multiplies in a related calculation questions
Expanded misconception:
Confuses multiplication with division in calculations involving related operations, such as associating similar operations and reversibly applying inverse operations, leading to incorrect results.

Misconception 1577: Adds instead of multiplies when using grid method
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the multiplication operation correctly when using the grid method, instead treating the column and row values as numbers to be added together.

Misconception 1578: When dividing a fraction by an integer, subtracts the integer from the denominator
Expanded misconception:
When dividing a fraction by an integer, subtracts or performs incorrect operations (e.g., multiplication, addition) with the integer rather than inverting and multiplying, indicating a lack of understanding of standard division procedures for fractions and mixed numbers.

Misconception 1579: Does not realise that in standard form the decimal we use must be between 1 and 10
Expanded misconception:
Fails to follow or misapplies the convention that the coefficient in standard form must be between 1 and 10, often resulting in incorrectly placing the decimal point.

Misconception 1580: When drawing a cumulative frequency diagram, thinks you plot the frequency on the horizontal axis
Expanded misconception:
When drawing a cumulative frequency diagram, incorrectly plots the frequency on the horizontal axis instead of the cumulative frequency, demonstrating a misunderstanding of the relationship between frequency and cumulative frequency in graphical representation.

Misconception 1581: Thinks that two side views are interchangeable
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that different side views (e.g. front, side, top) provide distinct information and instead assumes that multiple views depict essentially the same information or views, leading to the interchangeability of side views.

Misconception 1582: Thinks terms in linear sequence are in direct proportion
Expanded misconception:
Thinks terms in linear sequence are in direct proportion, regardless of the actual proportional or nonlinear relationship between quantities, often leading to the assumption that the result can be obtained simply by adding or multiplying the corresponding terms in a pattern or sequence.

Misconception 1583: Does not recognise the origin as the starting point for coordinates
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly apply coordinate geometry by neglecting to treat the origin as the reference point (0,0) for plotting or determining locations in a coordinate system.

Misconception 1584: Believes if a point is reflected in the line y=b then the y coordinate of the point will become 'b'
Expanded misconception:
Misconstrues line of reflection, incorrectly assuming that when a point is reflected across a horizontal line (y=b), the y-coordinate of the reflected point will always become 'b', rather than maintain the same distance from the line of reflection but on the opposite side.

Misconception 1585: Believes distance = speed/time
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between formulas for distance, speed, and time, specifically misapplying the formula d = rt (distance equals rate multiplied by time) by inverting the variables, leading to incorrect calculations when the correct formula should be d = rt, v = d/t (speed equals distance divided by time), or t = d/v (time equals distance divided by speed).

Misconception 1586: When asked to read a value from a graph, thinks they give the corresponding value on the other axis, rather than reading from the graph
Expanded misconception:
Fails to identify the variable represented on the given axis and, instead, mistakenly reads or retrieves an unrelated value from the other axis when interpreting graphical representations, often resulting in a reversal or mismatch of x and y variable values, or incorrectly identifies a point on the graph by mixing up independent and dependent variables.

Misconception 1587: when reading a dash on a number line counts 1 dash too few when working out the divisions
Expanded misconception:
When working with divisions on a number line, miscounts the number of dashes between numbers, often counting 1 dash too few or misinterpreting a dash as representing a full unit, rather than a fraction or portion of a unit, leading to inaccurate calculations.

Misconception 1588: Thinks only angles on parallel lines can be alternate
Expanded misconception:
Thinks only angles formed by a transversal intersecting two parallel lines can be alternate, overlooking other configurations such as alternate interior or exterior angles formed by a transversal intersecting multiple non-parallel lines, or failing to identify other angle relationships that meet the criteria for being alternate.

Misconception 1589: Thinks the division sign means to subtract
Expanded misconception:
Thinks the division sign means to subtract, multiply, add, or perform an operation other than the intended mathematical division operation.

Misconception 1590: Believes any two inequalities can be combined into a double inequality
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that certain mathematical operations or inequalities cannot be combined or used interchangeably, such as incorrectly combining or converting between single and double inequalities, incorrectly applying transitive or commutative properties to inequalities, or improperly combining different types of inequalities, and incorrectly assumes that standard operations such as addition or multiplication can always be applied to inequality equations, demonstrating a lack of understanding of inequality properties and operations.

Misconception 1591: Rounds to the nearest hundredth, rather than one significant figure
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply appropriate rounding rules, consistently rounding to the nearest hundredth or a fixed decimal place, rather than adjusting the number of significant figures according to the context or specific mathematical operation.

Misconception 1592: Thinks the number of items give the number of different possible arrangements rather than using the product rule for counting
Expanded misconception:
Thinks the total number of items gives the total number of different possible arrangements rather than using the product rule for counting, where each item or event contributing to the total number of arrangements independently, often misunderstanding that each item or event has multiple possible outcomes that multiply together to give the total number of arrangements.

Misconception 1593: When simplifying an algebraic fraction, only looks for number factors
Expanded misconception:
When simplifying an algebraic fraction, only looks for number factors and fails to consider variable or algebraic factors, often resulting in incomplete simplification or a failure to cancel common factors between the numerator and the denominator.

Misconception 1594: Believes the proportion for a pie chart sector is equal to the frequency
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that the proportion for a pie chart sector represents the relative value of the data, which is usually calculated by dividing the frequency by the total, often resulting in incorrectly equating the angle or proportion of the pie chart sector with the raw frequency rather than the normalized frequency or relative proportion.

Misconception 1595: Believes the first multiple of a is 2a
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the concept of a multiplier and incorrectly assumes the first multiple of a number is always twice the original number, leading to miscalculations when multiplying by other numbers.

Misconception 1596: Has found the total as a percentage of the amount being asked
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that the question is asking for a specific numerical solution, instead calculating a value as a percentage or proportion of a specific part of the original quantity, leading to an incorrect unit or numerical value.

Misconception 1597: Believes multiplying two negatives gives a negative answer
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand or consistently apply the rules of signed numbers, specifically that two negative numbers multiplied together or a positive number multiplied by two negative numbers results in a positive product, or demonstrates a general misconception about the sign of a product resulting from the multiplication of two negative numbers.

Misconception 1598: Believes the solution to an equation with matching coefficients of variables on both sides will always be 0
Expanded misconception:
Believes the solution to an equation with matching coefficients of variables on both sides will always be 0, as they mistakenly apply the idea that any expression with identical values on both sides of an equation must have a difference of 0, rather than recognizing that the constant terms may prevent the solution from being zero.

Misconception 1599: Adds rather than divides when answering worded problems
Expanded misconception:
Adds rather than divides (or performs incorrect inverse operations) when interpreting or answering word problems due to misinterpreting the context or misunderstanding the mathematical operation required to solve the problem, often resulting from a failure to recognize or understand the language or keywords that indicate division or other inverse operations.

Misconception 1600: Thinks a lower average means the data is more consistent
Expanded misconception:
Thinks a lower average means the data is more consistent, and fails to consider the actual dispersion or spread of the data points, such as variance or standard deviation, when evaluating data set consistency.

Misconception 1601: Does not understand that you can have percentages greater than 100%
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand that percentages can indeed be greater than 100%, failing to recognize that percentages represent proportions or ratios, and that values exceeding 100% indicate more than the total or reference value, rather than strictly being an error or impossibility.

Misconception 1602: Believes the sum of change in x and change in y gives the distance between points
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the Pythagorean theorem to find the distance between two points in a coordinate plane, and instead, incorrectly calculates distance as the sum of the absolute values of the horizontal and vertical changes between the points.

Misconception 1603: Confuses displacement-time graphs with velocity-time graphs
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between graphs representing different physical quantities, specifically confusing displacement-time graphs (showing the relationship between an object's position and time) with velocity-time graphs (showing the relationship between an object's velocity and time), indicating a misunderstanding of kinematic concepts and graph types.

Misconception 1604: Thinks there are 10 centimetres in 1 mm.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand or recall conversion relationships between units of measurement within the metric system, specifically incorrectly assuming that 1 millimeter (mm) contains a certain number of centimeters (cm), such as 10, rather than recognizing 1 cm as equal to 10 mm.

Misconception 1605: When rounding up from a 9, does not add one on to the digit to the left
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly apply the rules of rounding, specifically when rounding up from a 9, does not add one to the digit to the left, potentially resulting in incorrect digits in the remaining answer, often due to neglecting to correct the carry in higher place values or misunderstanding the concept of regrouping.

Misconception 1606: Believes the coefficent of x in an expanded quadratic comes from multiplying the two numbers in the brackets
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct exponent rule and incorrectly assumes the coefficient of the linear term in an expanded quadratic expression (ax+b)(cx+d) comes solely from the product of the two numbers in the brackets (a*c), rather than recognizing it is also the result of a+bc+ad and the actual result requires additional computation.

Misconception 1607: Believes the gradient of perpendicular lines just have opposite signs
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that the gradients of perpendicular lines are negative reciprocals of each other, mistakenly believing they only have opposite signs.

Misconception 1608: Believes parts of a 3D diagram that look straight class as edges
Expanded misconception:
Fails to differentiate between edges and other lines in 3D diagrams, incorrectly assuming that lines that appear straight and parallel are edges of the shape rather than possibly being other features such as parts of the axes or visual cues for depth.

Misconception 1609: Believes the decimal point button writes a fraction
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognize the distinction between a number's decimal representation and its fractional representation, leading to incorrect operations when working with numbers containing decimals, and may incorrectly apply the decimal point button to create a fraction or assume that a decimal is an exact representation of a fraction in all calculations.

Misconception 1610: Misunderstands the concept of common factors in algebra
Expanded misconception:
Fails to identify or incorrectly applies the greatest common factor, resulting in incorrect simplification or manipulation of algebraic expressions.

Misconception 1611: Does not understand that coordinates can be decimals
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider that coordinates can be non-integer values, such as decimals or fractions, or incorrectly assumes that all coordinates are whole numbers, resulting in miscalculations or misinterpretation in geometric contexts.

Misconception 1612: Confuses the shaded and unshaded regions on an inequality diagram
Expanded misconception:
Fails to accurately differentiate between the shaded and unshaded regions on an inequality diagram, leading to incorrect identification of solution sets, often due to misinterpreting the direction of the inequality sign or misunderstanding the concept of a boundary line, resulting in incorrect interval or set notation.

Misconception 1613: Believes constructing a triangle with given SAS requires a compass
Expanded misconception:
Believes constructing a triangle with given two sides and the included angle (SAS) requires a compass, rather than understanding that this can be accomplished using a straightedge and other basic geometric construction techniques.

Misconception 1614: Believes factors of algebraic terms/expressions will only be numbers
Expanded misconception:
Believes factors of algebraic terms/expressions will only be numbers, failing to recognize that factors can also be algebraic expressions or variables themselves, and thus incorrectly assuming that only numerical values can be factored out or accepted as factors.

Misconception 1615: Does not know the symbol for proportionality 
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognize, understand, or correctly apply the meaning and notation of proportional relationships, such as not recognizing the equivalence of a given ratio (e.g., 5:3) and a proportional expression (e.g., 3x = 5y or y/x = 5/3) or not understanding that the tilde (~) or equal signs (=) with colons ":" or proportionality constant notation (e.g., y ∝ x or y = kx) are symbols for proportional relationships.

Misconception 1616: Fails to consider all possible outcomes
Expanded misconception:
Ignores or overlooks one or more possible outcomes, solutions, or cases when solving a problem or evaluating a mathematical expression, leading to incorrect conclusions or incomplete answers.

Misconception 1617: Believes a reciprocal equation will have a linear graph
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that reciprocal relationships, such as rate inversely proportional to another variable, typically produce non-linear or curved graphs, incorrectly anticipating a linear relationship and corresponding graph when given a reciprocal equation or problem scenario.

Misconception 1618: Mixes up cubing and multiplying by 3
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the operation of cubing a number (raising it to the power of 3) with multiplying the number by 3, or vice versa, and incorrectly applies this operation in a given expression.

Misconception 1619: When dividing a decimal by a whole number, ignores decimal point and just divides the digits
Expanded misconception:
When dividing a decimal number by a whole number or an integer, ignores the decimal point and just divides the digits as if the decimal point did not exist, failing to adjust the decimal place accordingly.

Misconception 1620: Believes zero cannot be raised to a power
Expanded misconception:
Lacks understanding that zero raised to any positive power equals zero, and/or may think that the result is undefined or that any number raised to the zeroth power is not equal to one, or incorrectly applies exponent rules to zero.

Misconception 1621: When dividing a fraction by an integer, multiplies both the numerator and denominator by the integer
Expanded misconception:
When dividing a fraction by an integer, incorrectly applies division rules by multiplying both the numerator and denominator by the integer, instead of multiplying the numerator by the reciprocal of the divisor.

Misconception 1622: Divides by 1000 instead of 100 when converting a percentage to a decimal
Expanded misconception:
Ignores the percent sign and instead of dividing by 100 to convert a percentage to a decimal value, incorrectly divides by 1000, resulting in a decimal value that is ten times smaller than the correct conversion.

Misconception 1623: When asked for the mean of a list of data, does not count any zeros in the data when working out what to divide by
Expanded misconception:
Fails to include all values, including zero, when determining the count or total of data points, resulting in an incorrect calculation of statistical measures such as the mean.

Misconception 1624: Multiplies the number of whole units by the numerator when converting from a mixed number to an improper fraction
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly apply the conversion procedure when changing a mixed number to an improper fraction, specifically by incorrectly multiplying or not multiplying the number of whole units by the denominator and then adding the numerator, often mistakenly multiplying the whole units by the numerator instead.

Misconception 1625: Uses difference as index instead of coefficient in nth term rule
Expanded misconception:
Applies the nth term rule by considering the difference between consecutive terms as the coefficient 'd', rather than understanding that 'd' represents the common difference to be multiplied by the term number, often leading to incorrect application of the formula for both arithmetic and geometric sequences.

Misconception 1626: Has partitioned the digits when doing long multiplication
Expanded misconception:
Has difficulty in lining up and correctly placing digits when partitioning numbers for long multiplication, resulting in misplaced or shifted decimal points or digits, or incorrect carry between partial products.

Misconception 1627: Believes ratios can be combined without equalizing common terms
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the need for common units or equivalent ratios before performing operations involving proportions, ratio comparisons, or combining ratios in various mathematical contexts.

Misconception 1628: When multiplying involving a multiple of 10, gives an answer 10 times smaller than it should be
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for the effect of a zero when multiplying by a multiple of 10, resulting in answers 10 times smaller than expected, possibly due to a misunderstanding of place value or a tendency to ignore the zero when performing calculations.

Misconception 1629: writes the remainder as the divisor divided by 10
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly simplifies or reduces fractions by using a common denominator divisor rule where the remainder is treated as the divisor being divided by a factor of the dividend, often 10.

Misconception 1630: Does not realize we can simplify a term that involves powers
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to recognize the opportunity to simplify expressions containing powers, exponents, or roots prior to performing other operations, and therefore tends to perform calculations in an order that is not mathematically efficient or correct."

Misconception 1631: When asked for a specific term in a sequence gives the term after
Expanded misconception:
When asked for a specific term in a sequence, gives the term after or before the correct term instead of identifying the correct one, possibly due to a misunderstanding of sequence indexing or a careless error in counting.

Misconception 1632: Believes a decimal with one significant figure is a multiple of 10 when converted to a percentage, regardless of place value
Expanded misconception:
Believes a decimal with limited significant figures must result in a percentage that is a multiple of a power of 10 (e.g., 10, 20, 50, etc.), regardless of actual place value, resulting in the incorrect assumption that any given decimal can be directly converted to a specific percentage with the same property of ending in a power of 10.

Misconception 1633: When asked to rotate by 180 degrees rotates by 90 degrees
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for full rotational symmetry, often incorrectly rotating by 90 degrees instead of 180 degrees, indicating a difficulty in comprehending the magnitude of rotations and the completion of full turns.

Misconception 1634: When performing mental multiplication, multiplies accurately by a multiple of 10 but then does not know how to adjust their answer 
Expanded misconception:
When performing multiplication, accurately multiplies by a multiple of 10 but then fails to generalize or apply the resulting pattern to other multipliers, leading to multiplication errors that do not correctly account for the multiplier's ones place, and often results in answers that are multiples of 10.

Misconception 1635: Believes gradient is the difference between the change in x and the change in y
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that the gradient (slope) of a line is a measure of the rate of change of y with respect to x, often incorrectly thinking it is the difference between the change in x and the change in y, rather than their ratio.

Misconception 1636: Believes nth term rule can be given in words
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that nth term rules should be expressed algebraically and assumes a verbal or simplified description is sufficient for defining a sequence.

Misconception 1637: Has used the decimal point to separate numerator and denominator
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to understand the correct representation of fractions and improper use of decimal points, mistakenly separating the numerator and denominator with a decimal point instead of using it to represent a decimal equivalent."

Misconception 1638: Counts repeated factors as two separate factors
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that identical repeated factors can be simplified or combined, thus counting them as two separate operations or entities rather than simplifying the calculation.

Misconception 1639: Believes adding two negatives gives a positive answer
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the rule of operations with negative numbers, specifically mistakenly believing that the product or sum of two negative numbers is positive, possibly due to confusion with other mathematical operations or misunderstanding of the concept of negation.

Misconception 1640: Believes the centre of rotation is always the origin
Expanded misconception:
Believes the centre of rotation is always the origin or the initial stated point, failing to consider alternative points of rotation or neglecting the impact of coordinate system changes.

Misconception 1641: When collecting like terms, only simplifies when there is an addition
Expanded misconception:
"When collecting like terms, only simplifies when there is an addition, and fails to apply the distributive property and combine terms across different operations, including subtractions or involving constants, due to a limited understanding of algebraic manipulations."

Misconception 1642: When adding roots, adds the numbers under the roots
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly applies properties of arithmetic operations by treating roots as regular numbers, and adds the numbers under the roots instead of recognizing that roots represent quantities that cannot be directly combined using basic arithmetic operations.

Misconception 1643: Believes that when increasing by a percentage over 100, they do not need to add an extra 1 to the decimal multiplier for the original amount
Expanded misconception:
Believes that when increasing by a percentage over 100, they do not need to add an extra 1 to the decimal multiplier representing the original 100%, resulting in an error where they only multiply by the percentage value as a decimal, rather than adding 1 to the decimal.

Misconception 1644: Confuses collecting like terms with multiplication
Expanded misconception:
Confuses collecting like terms with multiplication, often incorrectly adding or multiplying coefficients or variables, and fails to recognize that collecting like terms involves adding or subtracting the same variables raised to the same power.

Misconception 1645: Thinks there are 10ml in a litre
Expanded misconception:
Thinks there is a fundamental misunderstanding of metric unit conversions, specifically the number of milliliters in a liter, resulting in incorrect calculations, possibly due to confusion between milliliters and liters or a lack of understanding of the conversion factor (1 liter = 1000 milliliters).

Misconception 1646: In ratio question where one side of ratio is given, uses that as one part in ratio without dividing
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that in a ratio or proportion, given one side or part, they must determine the total number of parts by dividing the whole into equivalent parts based on the given information, instead directly using the given part as one of the total parts.

Misconception 1647: Believes that numbers are read aloud digit by digit 
Expanded misconception:
Interprets multi-digit numbers as separate digits or applies operations to digits individually rather than considering the place value of digits within a multi-digit number, which can result in incorrect calculations or comparisons.

Misconception 1648: Believes dividing two positives will give a negative answer
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that the sign of the result in multiplication and division is solely determined by the signs of the operands, leading to incorrect expectations that multiplying or dividing two positive numbers will result in a negative answer.

Misconception 1649: Believes that all recurring decimals can be converted to a fraction by writing the digits of the decimal over 9
Expanded misconception:
Believes that all recurring decimals can be converted to a fraction by directly writing the digits of the decimal over the digit 9 or over a number composed of 9s repeating the same number of times as the repeating block in the decimal, regardless of the actual conversion process and without considering the position of the recurring block.

Misconception 1650: Only includes full hours when measuring time in minutes
Expanded misconception:
Only considers partial time periods in whole units (hours, days) without fully accounting for fractional parts when converting between units of time (e.g., hours to minutes, days to hours).

Misconception 1651: Multiplies when converting to a larger unit
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly convert between units by inappropriately multiplying or dividing when changing the unit of measurement, including consistent errors such as multiplying when going to a larger unit or dividing when going to a smaller unit.

Misconception 1652: Thinks there are 100mm in a centimetre
Expanded misconception:
Lacks understanding of metric measurement unit conversions, specifically believes there are 100 millimeters in a centimeter (instead of 10 millimeters).

Misconception 1653: Misunderstands how to adjust proportions for different sample sizes in stratified sampling
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands how to adjust proportions for different sample sizes in stratified sampling, specifically by failing to apply weights or misapplying weights to adjust for variations in sample size across subgroups, resulting in biased or inaccurate population estimates.

Misconception 1654: Believes y=f(-x) is a reflection in y=-x
Expanded misconception:
Believes transformation of the form f(-x) only represents reflection across the line y=-x, rather than the line y=x.

Misconception 1655: When adding fractions, changes to common denominator but leaves numerators the same
Expanded misconception:
Failing to update the numerators after finding a common denominator when adding fractions.

Misconception 1656: Believes that one point is enough to specify a line
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that two distinct points are necessary to uniquely define a line, instead mistakenly believing that a single point, or a single point and other insufficient information, is sufficient to specify a line.

Misconception 1657: Doubles the diameter when finding the radius
Expanded misconception:
Fails to differentiate between radius and diameter when solving problems, often incorrectly doubling or halving a given measurement to find the radius or diameter.

Misconception 1658: Does not link Pythagoras Theorem to finding distance between two points
Expanded misconception:
Does not apply Pythagoras Theorem or distance formula correctly to find the distance between two given points on a coordinate plane or in a right-angled triangle, often resulting in incorrect calculations or failure to recognize the relationship between the coordinates.

Misconception 1659: Finds one part of a ratio but doesn't multiply that by the number of parts needed
Expanded misconception:
Finds one part of a ratio but fails to multiply that by the total number of parts needed, resulting in an incomplete calculation or failure to scale the ratio accordingly.

Misconception 1660: When using inequality notation, thinks "greater than/less than or equal to" symbols are instead the "greater than/less than" symbols and vice versa
Expanded misconception:
When using inequality notation, incorrectly interprets or confuses "greater than/less than or equal to" symbols with "greater than/less than" symbols and vice versa, often disregarding the correctness of the equal case, leading to incorrect conclusions or calculations.

Misconception 1661: Confuses circumference and diameter
Expanded misconception:
Confuses circumference and diameter, often incorrectly substituting one for the other as if they were equivalent or forgetting to apply the appropriate conversion factor (π) when switching between the two.

Misconception 1662: Does not know the y intercept is where the x coordinate is 0
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand that the y-intercept of a line is the point at which the line crosses the y-axis, specifically where the x-coordinate equals 0, and may incorrectly identify it as a different point or fail to identify it altogether.

Misconception 1663: Thinks halving is the same as multiplying by 2
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the reciprocal relationship between multiplication and division operations, specifically believing that halving (or division by 2) is equivalent to multiplying by 2.

Misconception 1664: Mixes up squaring and halving
Expanded misconception:
Confuses or misapplies mathematical operations involving exponents, particularly squaring (raising to the power of 2) and halving (dividing by 2), or incorrectly treats numbers as either squared or halved when the actual operation is the opposite.

Misconception 1665: Believes when frequencies multiply by a scale factor in a table the median also multiplies by that scale factor
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between measures of central tendency that are sensitive to the scale of the data (such as mean), and those that are position-based and not directly affected by proportional changes in data values (such as median), believing that proportional adjustments to data, like multiplying by a scale factor, would impact all statistical measures in the same way.

Misconception 1666: When factorising into single brackets, factorises the first term correctly but subtracts the number outside the bracket with the second term in the expression to find the second term in the bracket
Expanded misconception:
When factorizing quadratic expressions or polynomials into single brackets, incorrectly applies the product-sum rule by correctly identifying the first term but then naively subtracting the constant (or the coefficient of another term) from the second term to find the second term in the bracket, rather than correctly finding the product of the coefficient of the second term and the first term in the bracket.

Misconception 1667: When comparing unit fractions believes that fractions with larger denominators are greater in size.
Expanded misconception:
When comparing fractions, specifically unit fractions or fractions that represent different sized parts of a whole, believes that fractions with larger denominators are greater in size due to the larger number, regardless of the actual proportional relationships.

Misconception 1668: Calculates area when asked for perimeter
Expanded misconception:
Confuses or incorrectly applies properties of geometric shapes, including calculating the area instead of perimeter, perimeter instead of area, or incorrectly applying formulas for specific shapes.

Misconception 1669: In 3 letter angle notation, gives a wider angle that contains the shaded angle
Expanded misconception:
In 3 letter angle notation, incorrectly labels the angle by choosing a wider angle that contains the shaded angle, rather than the specific angle being referenced, resulting in incorrect identification or naming of angles due to a lack of precision in notation.

Misconception 1670: Does not realise that multiplication can be broken down into factors
Expanded misconception:
Does not realise that multiplication can be broken down into factors, resulting in incorrect partitioning, regrouping, or calculation of products, especially when dealing with multi-digit numbers or more complex algebraic expressions.

Misconception 1671: Does not know how to find area by counting squares on a grid
Expanded misconception:
Does not know how to find area by accurately counting and combining the number of squares within a figure on a grid, possibly due to errors in identifying partial squares, not accounting for gaps or overlaps, or misunderstanding the concept of unit squares as a measure of area.

Misconception 1672: Confuses the order of operations, believes addition comes before multiplication 
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to follow the correct order of operations as per the precedence rules, incorrectly assuming that addition/subtraction operations should be evaluated before multiplication/division, regardless of the absence of brackets, leading to incorrect calculations."

Misconception 1673: Multiplied by 1000 instead of 100
Expanded misconception:
Fails to accurately apply conversion factors or place value shifts, often demonstrated by mistaking the multiplier or divisor in decimal or percentage calculations, or multiplying/dividing by an incorrect power of 10 (e.g., multiplying by 1000 instead of 100, or by 100 instead of 10).

Misconception 1674: Believes distance is the area under the graph on a displacement time graph
Expanded misconception:
Misconstrues distance and area, believing the area under a displacement-time graph represents the total distance traveled, rather than the total displacement or change in position.

Misconception 1675: Changes wrong place value column when rounding
Expanded misconception:
Changes wrong place value column when rounding numbers, often involving a failure to correctly identify the rounding digit or misunderstanding of the direction of rounding, resulting in adding digits to a higher or lower place value column, or rounding to a different column.

Misconception 1676: When adding or subtracting surds, does not simplify the surd before adding the numbers in front of the surd
Expanded misconception:
Fails to simplify the radicand or does not rationalize the denominator when adding or subtracting surds, resulting in incorrect combination of the coefficients of the surds.

Misconception 1677: Thinks the range is just the first value subtracted from the last value, without ordering
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider that the range of a data set requires arranging the numbers in sequential order before finding the difference between the highest and lowest values.

Misconception 1678: Confuses perimeter and area
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between perimeter (distance around a shape) and area (total space inside a shape), often incorrectly applying formulas or concepts relevant to one to the other.

Misconception 1679: Uses addition instead of the associative property of multiplication
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the associative and/or commutative properties of operations, leading to incorrect grouping or ordering of factors and mistaken applications of arithmetic properties, such as adding or subtracting instead of multiplying or using the incorrect order of operations.

Misconception 1680: Does not realize we can omit the multiplication symbol in algebraic expressions
Expanded misconception:
Does not realize that multiplication can be implied by juxtaposition or omission of the multiplication symbol in algebraic expressions, leading to errors in interpreting and writing mathematical expressions.

Misconception 1681: Assumes grouped data is evenly distributed within each group
Expanded misconception:
"Assumes grouped data is evenly distributed within each group, ignoring actual data distribution or interval, and fails to account for the midpoint or the specific values within the group when making calculations or estimates."

Misconception 1682: Confuses inequality symbols with equality
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to correctly apply the meaning of inequality symbols (e.g., <, >, ≤, ≥) and treats them as if they had the same meaning as the equals sign (=), resulting in incorrect solutions or comparisons, particularly in contexts involving algebra, inequalities, and mathematical equations."

Misconception 1683: Thinks the length of the hypotenuse is the sum of the two shorter sides
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the Pythagorean theorem to find the length of the hypotenuse in a right-angled triangle, instead incorrectly assuming it is the sum of the lengths of the two shorter sides.

Misconception 1684: When finding the difference between two marks on a scale, counts lines instead of gaps
Expanded misconception:
When finding the difference between two marks on a scale, incorrectly interprets the units by either counting the lines (ticks) between the marks or counting the gaps between the lines (ticks) instead of using the correct approach of counting the spaces or gaps between the marks, often due to confusion between scale intervals and tick marks.

Misconception 1685: Confuses angle of a sector with the diameter
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between central angle measurements and other circle properties, such as diameter, circumference, or radius, when solving problems related to sector areas, arc lengths, or circle geometry.

Misconception 1686: Confuses diameter and radius
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between diameter and radius, which may manifest as using the diameter instead of the radius (or vice versa) in calculations, misunderstanding the relationship that the diameter is twice the radius, or incorrectly applying formulas involving diameter or radius.

Misconception 1687: Gives one fewer order of rotational symmetry than there is
Expanded misconception:
Undercounts the number of rotational symmetries an object has, typically due to overlooking one axis or failing to consider the identity rotation (i.e., a 360-degree rotation) as a valid rotational symmetry.

Misconception 1688: Believes a line showing a constant rate of increase corresponds to a non-prism shape
Expanded misconception:
Confuses shape characteristics with graphical representations of measurements, specifically believing that changes in shape appearance are directly related to trends in rate of increase or change, rather than understanding that a constant rate of increase is a characteristic of a prism's volume, area, or other measurable attributes, regardless of its non-linear or polygonal shape.

Misconception 1689: Believes the largest number in a set of numbers is always their lowest common multiple
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to consider multiple number relationships and incorrectly assumes that the largest number in a set of numbers is always their lowest common multiple, often resulting in selecting answers based solely on the magnitude of numbers involved, rather than on the operations applied to them."

Misconception 1690: Assumes an addition pyramid works in a different way to how it actually does
Expanded misconception:
Assumes an addition or multiplication calculation pyramid works in a different way to how it actually does, incorrectly applying procedures related to regrouping, place value, or operation priority.

Misconception 1691: Believes the solution of mx + c = 0 is x =0
Expanded misconception:
Overlooks the necessity of isolating x and solving for its value by subtracting c from both sides and then dividing or factoring out co-efficients of x when dealing with linear equations in the form mx + c = 0.

Misconception 1692: Mixes up total and part when forming expressions from worded problems
Expanded misconception:
Mixes up total and part when forming expressions from worded problems, specifically confusing the total with a component or part of the whole, such as using a part of a quantity to represent the entire quantity in an equation, or representing the total when a specific portion is required.

Misconception 1693: Does not know the meaning of common, eg. common factor
Expanded misconception:
Has difficulty understanding or recognizing key mathematical concepts, such as common terms (e.g., common factor, common denominator), resulting in incorrect application of mathematical principles or formulas.

Misconception 1694: Believes that a discriminant of 0 means there are no real solutions
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between the discriminant being zero (which means one repeated real solution, not necessarily no real solutions) and non-existent real solutions.

Misconception 1695: Thinks you add all the bricks in a row in an addition pyramid.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the place value representation in an addition pyramid and mistakenly thinks the pyramid is a direct representation of a series of additions, leading to incorrect computation by adding all the bricks in a row.

Misconception 1696: Heads towards zero when subtracting from a negative
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the concept of 'direction' in subtraction with negative numbers, leading to a tendency to head towards zero when subtracting from a negative, instead of moving further away from zero.

Misconception 1697: Does not understand the link between the sign of the root and the sign in the bracket
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands the relationship between the sign of the radicand (the number within the square root symbol) and the resulting sign of the square root in algebraic expressions containing radicals, possibly causing incorrect handling of plus and minus signs.

Misconception 1698: Uses the whole base rather than half the base when forming a right angled triangle from an equilateral triangle
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly apply geometric properties when altering shapes, specifically using incorrect proportions of the original shape (e.g., the whole base rather than half the base) when creating smaller shapes, such as right-angled triangles from equilateral triangles.

Misconception 1699: Thinks there are 60 degrees in a full turn
Expanded misconception:
Misconceives that a complete rotation or a full turn is not equal to 360 degrees, specifically thinking there are 60 minutes in an hour and using this to incorrectly calculate the number of degrees in a circle as 60, rather than multiplying the 60 minutes in an hour by the 6 sections of 60 in a circle to reach the correct answer of 360.

Misconception 1700: Believes you need to know the value of all algebraic variables before any general conclusions can be made
Expanded misconception:
Believes you need to know the exact numerical value of all algebraic variables, constants, or unknowns before any general conclusions, manipulations, or calculations can be made, rather than recognizing that variables can be manipulated or conclusions drawn based on their relationships, patterns, or structural properties.

Misconception 1701: Believes the solutions of a quadratic equation are the absolute values of the constants in the factorised form
Expanded misconception:
This expanded misconception would read:

"Fails to consider the possibility of negative solutions or incorrectly believes the solutions of a quadratic equation are always the absolute values or absolute values of the constants in the factorised or vertex form, rather than the actual solutions which may involve both positive and negative values."

Misconception 1702: Does not know the 9 timestable
Expanded misconception:
"Overlooks memorized multiplication facts, such as not recalling the 9 times table correctly, to complete the calculation."

Misconception 1703: When using a 180 degree protractor to measure reflex angles gives the value of the acute or obtuse angle that is a complement to 360
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider that when using a 180 degree protractor, reflex angles (angles greater than 180 degrees) must be calculated by measuring the adjacent acute or obtuse angle and then subtracting that value from 360, rather than directly taking the protractor reading.

Misconception 1704: Believes gradient = change in x/change in y
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands the concept of gradient, incorrectly believing it to be calculated as the change in the x-coordinate divided by the change in the y-coordinate, rather than the change in the y-coordinate divided by the change in the x-coordinate, or Δy/Δx.

Misconception 1705: Rounds to the nearest integer when estimating
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the order of operations or exact calculation when solving mathematical expressions, often relying on estimation by rounding intermediate results to the nearest integer inappropriately.

Misconception 1706: When calculating perimeter, only adds the marked lengths
Expanded misconception:
Fails to include unmarked lengths or non-numeric side lengths in the perimeter calculation, and does not recognize the importance of considering all sides when measuring the perimeter of a shape or object.

Misconception 1707: Misinterprets the meaning of angles on a straight line angle fact
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets the relationship between angles on a straight line angle fact, including mistakenly assuming the sum of adjacent angles on a straight line is not 180 degrees, incorrectly identifying vertical angles or corresponding angles from previously encountered information, or incorrectly assuming supplementary or complementary angles are equivalent.

Misconception 1708: Does not understand place value within a number
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand place value within a number, failing to distinguish between digits in various positions (hundreds, tens, ones, etc.), and neglects the concept of shifting place value through multiplication or division operations that require regrouping.

Misconception 1709: Believes a factor pair doesn't have to be 2 integers
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that a factor pair must consist of two integers that, when multiplied together, yield the original product, and does not consider the order or uniqueness of factors.

Misconception 1710: When finding a percentage of an amount, assumes they divide the percentage by 10, but not the amount, before multiplying
Expanded misconception:
Fails to convert percentage to decimal correctly by not considering the entire calculation; typically divides the percentage by 10, but not the base amount, leading to incorrect intermediate steps and ultimately an incorrect solution, especially in percentage-related multiplication problems.

Misconception 1711: Is not familiar with the names of the different parts of a fraction
Expanded misconception:
Demonstrates limited understanding of fraction components, specifically naming or identifying numerator, denominator, or other essential parts correctly, leading to errors in fraction-related calculations or problem-solving.

Misconception 1712: Includes all faces when finding surface area of a compound shape, including those which are stuck together
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for overlapping or shared surfaces when finding the total surface area of a compound shape, mistakenly including all individual faces without subtracting the areas that are hidden or in contact due to the combination of the shapes.

Misconception 1713: Believes inverse function notation means multiplying by negative 1
Expanded misconception:
Believes inverse function notation (f^(-1)(x) or g(f^(-1)(x))) means multiplying the function's argument by negative 1 or changing the function's sign, rather than finding a new function that undoes the original function's output.

Misconception 1714: When completing the square, believes the constant in the bracket is the square root of the coefficient of x
Expanded misconception:
When completing the square, mistakenly believes the constant term added inside the bracket is the square of half the coefficient of x, rather than half the coefficient of x squared, or incorrectly interprets the process of adding and subtracting the square of half the coefficient of x to complete the square, rather than solely adding and subtracting a constant that agrees in sign with the original expression.

Misconception 1715: Converts a fraction to a decimal by using only the numerator after the decimal point
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the denominator when converting a fraction to a decimal, resulting in the placement of the numerator directly after the decimal point without proper division.

Misconception 1716: Goes the wrong direction in the sequence when identifying term-to-term rule
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly identify or apply term-to-term rule in a sequence, including errors in identifying patterns, increments, or decrements, resulting in applying the rule in the wrong direction or assigning incorrect attributes to consecutive terms.

Misconception 1717: Swaps the dividend and divisor in order to get an integer answer
Expanded misconception:
Swaps the dividend and divisor when performing division in multi-step calculations, often resulting in an incorrect calculation in an attempt to achieve an integer answer or whole number solution, particularly when faced with division operations that involve fractions or decimals.

Misconception 1718: Thinks there are 100 seconds in an hour
Expanded misconception:
Thinks there are 100 seconds in 1 minute or confuses base units of time (e.g., seconds in a minute, minutes in an hour, etc.) while performing calculations involving units of time.

Misconception 1719: Believes a rhombus has one pair of equal sides
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands the properties of quadrilaterals, specifically believing that a rhombus is only partially defined by having one pair of equal sides, rather than recognizing that a rhombus by definition must have all four sides of equal length.

Misconception 1720: Identifies the term-to-term rule rather than the next term
Expanded misconception:
Identifies the term-to-term rule rather than the next term in a sequence or series, failing to consider the relationship between consecutive terms to infer the subsequent term, and instead relying solely on the identifiable pattern between specific terms.

Misconception 1721: Does not recall the steps required to draw an angle
Expanded misconception:
Does not recall or apply the appropriate steps and/or sequential order required to draw a precise angle using a protractor or other geometric drawing tools, such as identifying the relevant angle measurement, aligning the protractor, and accurately marking the angle.

Misconception 1722: Believes a subtraction cannot be partitioned into separate subtractions
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that a subtraction can be decomposed or partitioned into separate subtractions, omitting the potential rearrangement or reordering of numbers to simplify the calculation, such as a - (b + c) = (a - b) - c.

Misconception 1723: Assumes all journeys of the same distance take the same amount of time
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider factors affecting journey duration, such as mode of transportation, speed, terrain, traffic, or other external influences when comparing journeys of equal distance.

Misconception 1724: When completing the square, believes that a is always half the x-coefficient. 
Expanded misconception:
When completing the square for a quadratic expression of the form ax^2 + bx + c, mistakenly believes that the value being added at the end is always x-coefficient divided by 2 or x-coefficient squared divided by 4, without considering if the x^2 term is not in its simplest form or if a is the absolute coefficient (i.e., ignoring potential negative coefficients of x^2 that may affect the value being added).

Misconception 1725: Does not recognise inches (") as a unit of length
Expanded misconception:
Fails to identify or correctly apply common measurement units, such as inches ("), feet ('), yards, or misinterprets the relationship between different units, leading to incorrect solutions or conversions.

Misconception 1726: Believes there is more than one line which passes through a given pair of points
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to recognize the fundamental concept in geometry that through any two distinct points, there is exactly one line, and instead, believes that multiple lines can pass through the same pair of points, often due to an incomplete understanding of point-line relationships or visual misinterpretation of geometric figures."

Misconception 1727: Rounds the wrong way when interpreting a real-life context
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply appropriate rounding strategies when interpreting real-life contexts, such as rounding up in situations that require additional resources or leniency, and rounding down in situations that require precision or exact amounts, resulting in incorrect estimation or approximation in practical problems involving money, time, or other quantifiable measures.

Misconception 1728: When solving a pair of simultaneous equations, thinks they need to make the coefficients of both variables the same
Expanded misconception:
When solving a pair of simultaneous equations, thinks that a necessary condition to solve the equations is to make the coefficients of both variables the same, even if this complicates the arithmetic or if one of the variables can be more easily eliminated by subtraction or addition without making the coefficients equal.

Misconception 1729: When solving simultaneous equations, finds the correct numbers to transform the equations, but uses them the wrong way around.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly apply substitution or elimination method in solving simultaneous equations by misassigning coefficients or constants from one equation to the other, resulting in incorrect equation transformation and solution.

Misconception 1730: When adding decimals with a different number of decimal places, lines up the digits incorrectly
Expanded misconception:
Fails to align decimal points properly when adding or subtracting decimals with a different number of decimal places, resulting in incorrect placement of digits or loss of precision.

Misconception 1731: Makes an assumption about the squares on a scale representing either 1 or 10
Expanded misconception:
Makes an assumption about the scale representation, such as assuming that each square consistently represents either 1 or 10 units, without considering the actual scale or units given in the problem.

Misconception 1732: Thinks a factor tree splits a number into a pair of numbers that sum to the given number.
Expanded misconception:
Thinks a factor tree splits a number into a pair of numbers that sum to the given number, rather than identifying pairs of factors that multiply to the given number.

Misconception 1733: Confuses tangent and chord
Expanded misconception:
Confuses tangent and chord, possibly by incorrectly identifying the tangent line as the line connecting the center of the circle to the midpoint of the chord, or vice versa, and does not understand that a tangent line is perpendicular to the radius at the point of tangency, unlike a chord which intersects the circle at two distinct points.

Misconception 1734: Thinks that we can remove a division symbol and just write the variables next to each other
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the order of operations or properly handle division, often treating the operation as if it can be disregarded, resulting in the incorrect assumption that variables or numbers can simply be concatenated or written next to each other without performing the division.

Misconception 1735: Does not realise a quadratic must be in the form ax^2+bx+c=0 to find the values for the quadratic formula
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that a quadratic equation must be in the standard form ax^2 + bx + c = 0, and/or neglects to rearrange the equation to the correct form, before applying formulas, such as the quadratic formula, to solve for unknown variables or find key features of the quadratic function.

Misconception 1736: Believes clock times can be written with a decimal point rather than a colon
Expanded misconception:
Lacks understanding that standard time notation requires hours and minutes to be separated by a colon rather than a decimal point.

Misconception 1737: Only looks at the difference between one pair of terms when discerning the pattern
Expanded misconception:
Fails to analyze the overall pattern or relationship among multiple terms, focusing solely on the local difference between adjacent or nearby terms, and neglecting to apply this understanding consistently to make valid deductions about the entire sequence or pattern.

Misconception 1738: Uses sin when cos is required
Expanded misconception:
Applies trigonometric functions inappropriately or out of context, such as using sine when cosine is required due to misunderstanding the relationships between sine, cosine, and the angles involved.

Misconception 1739: Does not understand changing decimals to fractions
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the concept of decimals as fractions or does not convert decimals to fractions correctly in mathematical operations or problems, leading to incorrect calculations or solutions.

Misconception 1740: Does not recognise the properties of a kite
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognise the properties of a kite, including its symmetries (line and rotational), angles, side lengths, and diagonals, in particular, that it has two pairs of adjacent sides of equal length and one pair of opposite angles that are equal.

Misconception 1741: Confuses diameter and chord
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly uses diameter in place of chord, or vice versa, in calculations and problem-solving, failing to understand the distinct geometric properties and relationships of these two line segments in a circle.

Misconception 1742: Adds or subtracts the degree of accuracy from the original number
Expanded misconception:
Fails to perform or incorrectly applies mathematical operations, such as rounding, truncating, or approximating, that affect the degree of accuracy of the original numbers, leading to incorrect results.

Misconception 1743: Believes that the coefficient of x represents the gradient even when a line is not in the form y = mx+c
Expanded misconception:
Believes that the coefficient of x represents the gradient without considering the specific linear equation form, mistakenly applying the concept of gradient to equations where the coefficient of x does not solely represent the slope, such as equations in the form x + y = a or more complex forms.

Misconception 1744: Rounded to nearest 100 instead of 1sf
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly apply significant figure rounding rules, rounding to the nearest 100 (or another incorrect power of 10) instead of rounding to the relevant level of precision (such as 1 significant figure) required by the problem or calculation.

Misconception 1745: Believes a quadratic graph can be a straight line
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that a quadratic function will always result in a curved graph unless it simplifies to a linear or constant function due to a zero or constant coefficient of the squared term, leading to an incorrect identification of quadratic graphs as straight lines.

Misconception 1746: Does not know the term 'diameter'
Expanded misconception:
Does not know or incorrectly applies the term 'diameter' (or its equivalent in different geometric contexts) and is therefore unable to correctly interpret problems involving circumference-related concepts.

Misconception 1747: When using the capture-recapture method of sampling, believes you multiply the number caught second by the number marked second to get the best estimate of population size
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the principles of the capture-recapture method correctly, specifically misunderstanding the formula and mistakenly multiplying the number of individuals caught in the second capture by the number of marked individuals in the second capture, rather than using the correct formula that involves the number of marked individuals in the first capture and the number of recaptured marked individuals in the second capture.

Misconception 1748: Believes that when a shape touches the mirror line, all of its vertices will stay the same when reflected
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply line reflection properties correctly, specifically believing that when a shape shares an edge or a vertex with the mirror line, all or some of its vertices on the line of reflection will remain stationary or unchanged, rather than being correctly flipped over the line.

Misconception 1749: When dividing fractions, does the reciprocal of both fractions
Expanded misconception:
When dividing fractions, incorrectly inverts or fails to invert the reciprocal of both fractions, or misunderstands the role of reciprocal in fraction division.

Misconception 1750: Adds "100" to end of written value rather than multiplying by 100
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly interprets or applies place value, specifically failing to recognize the effect of multiplying or converting a value to a higher denomination (e.g., tens to thousands), resulting in an incorrect addition of the original value instead of shifting its decimal place or multiplying by the corresponding power of 10.

Misconception 1751: Does not realise we can borrow from the preceding digit in a subtraction problem
Expanded misconception:
Does not realise or apply the concept of regrouping or borrowing from a preceding digit in a multi-digit subtraction problem, often resulting in incorrect calculation of values when a digit being subtracted from is less than the digit being subtracted, and may lead to incorrect answers or negative results in such operations.

Misconception 1752: Believes the arrows for parallel lines mean equal length
Expanded misconception:
Misapplies the concept of equal length and parallel lines, mistakenly associating the parallel line notation (|| or →) with line segments of the same length, rather than recognizing it solely as an indication of lines that will never intersect due to equal slopes.

Misconception 1753: Believes a direct proportion graph can have a negative gradient
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the fundamental properties of direct proportion relationships, specifically believing that a graph representing a direct proportion can have a negative gradient, despite the fact that a direct proportion relationship requires a positive gradient that intersects the origin.

Misconception 1754: Thinks two-tailed inequalities can have a constant in the middle
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that two-tailed inequalities, also known as compound inequalities or combined inequalities, require a variable, expression, or value being compared against a range defined by two constants on either side, and instead mistakenly assumes or uses a constant in the middle, rather than the variable, resulting in incorrect setup or solution of inequalities.

Misconception 1755: Incorrectly factorises a quadratic
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly factorises a quadratic, such as incorrectly identifying the product or sum of the factors, failing to consider the middle term, incorrectly applying the method of factorisation by grouping or splitting the middle term, or incorrectly applying the method of factorisation by difference of squares or perfect square trinomials.

Misconception 1756: Thinks you need to just remove the percentage sign to convert from a percentage to a decimal
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the operation of division by 100 when converting from a percentage to a decimal, instead simply removing the percentage sign.

Misconception 1757: Lists outcomes rather than counts how many outcomes
Expanded misconception:
Fails to enumerate outcomes, instead incorrectly listing or describing possible outcomes in a manner that doesn't actually calculate or account for their quantity.

Misconception 1758: Can not recognise all factor pairs of numbers
Expanded misconception:
The student struggles to identify or recall all possible factor pairs of a given number, potentially leading to miscalculations or oversights when solving multiplication-related problems or identifying equivalent ratios.

Misconception 1759: Assumes decimal point can be ignored when given a decimal percentage to convert to a fraction
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly account for decimal point when converting between decimal percentages and fractions, often resulting in loss of significant zeros, incorrect placement of the decimal, or neglecting to divide by 100.

Misconception 1760: Reads the position of a number from the space between the line marked, rather than the line marked
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets numerical values or scale units by reading the position of a number from the space or interval between the lines (or marks) on a graph, chart, or number line, rather than from the actual lines or marks themselves, often resulting in incorrect or misplaced values.

Misconception 1761: Does not understand what each part of a bar model represents as a fraction
Expanded misconception:
Struggles to comprehend the representation of fractions, including parts and wholes, in bar models, leading to incorrect allocation or calculation of quantities, such as incorrectly identifying the relationship between the parts or misunderstanding the proportional relationship between the parts and the whole.

Misconception 1762: Thinks that when drawing the plan view, the top layer of the shape needs to be drawn in the row above 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply 2D plan view principles to 3D shapes, believing that the top layer of the shape must be drawn in the row above the base layer, regardless of orientation or 2D representation, resulting in incorrect placement or alignment of shapes in the plan view.

Misconception 1763: Confuses arc and diameter
Expanded misconception:
Confuses arc and diameter terms or properties, such as using the measure or length of an arc when a diameter was required, or vice versa.

Misconception 1764: Estimated when not appropriate
Expanded misconception:
Estimated when not appropriate, such as when the question requires an exact answer, or when the question explicitly states that estimation is not allowed, implying a lack of understanding of the context and instructions, or an over-reliance on estimation strategies.

Misconception 1765: Does not understand how to answer generalised statements (eg. by testing values to check for always, sometimes, never)
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand how to answer generalised statements, such as those involving conditional phrases (e.g. always, sometimes, never), and may instead rely on specific examples or anecdotal evidence, failing to test or verify the statement's applicability across a range of scenarios or cases.

Misconception 1766: Thinks x^2 - a^2x is a valid form of difference of two squares
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly identify the difference of two squares formula as (x + a)(x - a) or (a + x)(a - x), and instead incorrectly interprets or simplifies expressions such as x^2 - a^2x, or mistakenly applies the formula, often neglecting the requirement for the middle term (2ax) in a perfect square trinomial.

Misconception 1767: Does not realise that a pictogram needs a title or question
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the importance of contextualisation in data interpretation and does not realise that a pictogram needs a title, key, or clear question to provide sufficient context for accurate interpretation.

Misconception 1768: Believes the y-intercept of a line is the solution to its equation
Expanded misconception:
Believes the y-intercept of a linear equation is either the solution to the equation, or uses it as the final answer, instead of realizing that it represents the point where the line crosses the y-axis, providing only one coordinate value of a solution point for the equation on a coordinate plane.

Misconception 1769: Does not know how to read information off a pie chart
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand how to extract information from a pie chart, including difficulties with interpreting total percentages, reading proportions of different sectors, and comparing relative sizes of different segments.

Misconception 1770: Believes the proportion for a pie chart sector is 1 divided by the number of degrees
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that the proportion of a pie chart sector is calculated as the angle measure of the sector divided by 360 degrees (or in radians, divided by 2π), rather than simply assuming a fixed proportion of 1 divided by the total number of degrees in the pie.

Misconception 1771: Believes the solution of a quadratic inequality is always the interval between the roots
Expanded misconception:
Ignores or misunderstands the existence of critical regions and directions of parabola, failing to account for the actual solution set, and incorrectly assumes that the solution to quadratic inequalities is always the interval strictly between the real roots of the corresponding quadratic equation.

Misconception 1772: Believes a reciprocal graph comes from a cubic equation
Expanded misconception:
Confuses graph types and equation degrees, often believing that specific graph shapes, such as reciprocal graphs, logarithmic graphs, or asymptotic graphs, are directly derived from a particular polynomial equation degree, such as cubic, quadratic, or linear, rather than recognizing the unique characteristics and equation transformations that define these graph types.

Misconception 1773: Adds rather than subtracts when answering worded problems
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for the correct operation, frequently replacing subtraction with addition, due to misinterpreting the context or misunderstanding the mathematical representation of the problem, often resulting in incorrect calculation.

Misconception 1774: When multiplying a surd by an integer to write as one surd, squares the number under the surd rather than the number in front
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the exponent rule to the rational coefficient when multiplying a surd by an integer, incorrectly squaring the number under the radicand instead of multiplying the rational coefficient.

Misconception 1775: Stops iteration too early when solving equations
Expanded misconception:
Fails to completely solve equations or iterative problems by terminating the process prematurely, often due to a misunderstanding of the operation sequence or oversight of remaining variables, constants, or unresolved steps.

Misconception 1776: Believes they solve mx + c = 0 by substituting in x = 0
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that solving linear equations of the form mx + c = 0 requires manipulating the equation to isolate the variable, and instead incorrectly substitutes a fixed value for the variable, often x = 0, missing the step of adding or subtracting a value to both sides or dividing by a coefficient.

Misconception 1777: Confuses the graph of 1/x with 1/x² when both are shown
Expanded misconception:
Has difficulty distinguishing between the graphical representations of inverse functions with square terms (e.g., 1/x²) and those without (e.g., 1/x), often leading to incorrect identification or interpretation of their respective shapes and asymptotes.
