Misconception 1778: Believes you subtract the number marked second from the estimated population to find the number marked first when using the capture-recapture method
Expanded misconception:
Misconstrues the capture-recapture method by failing to account for double counting and incorrectly applying subtraction, leading to the belief that the population initially marked can be found by subtracting the number of individuals in the second catch from the estimated total population size, rather than recognizing that the first population is actually estimated through the ratio of marked individuals in the second capture and the total number of individuals in the first capture.

Misconception 1779: Does not recognise that a shape translated would not change orientation
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply transformation properties, specifically that translation preserves a shape's orientation, size, and shape, in contrast to rotations or reflections.

Misconception 1780: Thinks all dimensions given must be used when calculating the area
Expanded misconception:
Thinks all given dimensions must be used in calculations regardless of relevance, resulting in incorrect application of formulas or overcomplication of calculations, often due to a lack of understanding of which dimensions are necessary for a specific area calculation.

Misconception 1781: Does not think we can have negative powers in standard form
Expanded misconception:
Does not think we can have negative powers in standard form, or misunderstands the concept of negative exponents and their representation in scientific or standard notation.

Misconception 1782: Does not recognise miles as a unit of length
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognise or apply units of measurement in word problems, such as not recognising miles as a unit of length, especially when calculating distances or speeds.

Misconception 1783: Confuses pyramid with prism
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between different polyhedra, specifically confuses a pyramid (a three-dimensional solid object with a polygon base and a set of triangular faces that meet at the apex) with a prism (a three-dimensional solid object with two identical faces that are polygons, and all other faces are rectangles), leading to incorrect identification or application of formulas for calculations such as volume, surface area, or other geometric properties.

Misconception 1784: Believes a horizontal line can show a constant rate of change
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets the concept of a horizontal line in a graph as always indicating a constant rate of change, failing to consider that a horizontal line only indicates a constant rate of change when comparing proportional or percentage values, and neglecting other interpretations such as a minimum or maximum in a curve or no change in a single measured value over time.

Misconception 1785: Added the base numbers
Expanded misconception:
Added the base numbers without performing the required operation, such as ignoring the exponent or multiplication sign, resulting in an incorrect solution that merely combines the original numbers.

Misconception 1786: Converts a fraction to a percentage by writing the numerator followed by the denominator
Expanded misconception:
Converts a fraction to a percentage either by incorrectly writing the numerator followed by the denominator, or failing to divide the numerator by the denominator before multiplying by 100.

Misconception 1787: When talking about money "taken" in a retail context, thinks that means take-away rather than add
Expanded misconception:
Interprets the term "taken" in a transactional context as implying subtraction, instead of considering it a synonym for deduction, discount, or a fee added to a total.

Misconception 1788: Uses slant heights instead of parallel sides to find area of a trapezium
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between properties required for calculations, specifically mistakenly using measurements that are not parallel (e.g. slant heights) instead of required parallel sides when finding the area of trapeziums or other quadrilaterals that rely on base and height calculations.

Misconception 1789: Thinks the elevation is taken from an angle to include aspects of the other side
Expanded misconception:
Thinks the elevation is taken from an angle to include aspects of the other side, rather than understanding that elevation represents a 2D side view from a perpendicular angle, often resulting in the incorrect inclusion of features from adjacent or opposite sides of an object.

Misconception 1790: Does not know the correct notation for labelling a side length
Expanded misconception:
Struggles with symbolic or algebraic representation of lengths, resulting in confusion or misuse of notation for labelling a side length, which may manifest as incorrect units, incomplete labels, or mix-ups in assignment of variables or values.

Misconception 1791: Does not know that vertically opposite angles are equal
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the concept of vertically opposite angles and/or lacks the ability to apply the property that vertically opposite angles are equal, regardless of the orientation of intersecting lines.

Misconception 1792: Has multiplied the base number and the power
Expanded misconception:
Has multiplied the base number and the power, rather than raising the base to the power, indicating a lack of understanding of exponentiation as repeated multiplication.

Misconception 1793: Does not count end number when counting
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to include the last unit or end value in a sequence or calculation, leading to undercounting or incorrect results in various mathematical operations such as addition, subtraction, multiplication, and division, or when determining quantities in counting tasks."

Misconception 1794: Does not recognise that choosing 2 items at once means the first item does not get replaced in a probability question
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for the impact of sequential selection without replacement on probability outcomes, incorrectly applying concepts of replacement or independent events to dependent scenarios where the selection of one item affects the likelihood of subsequent choices.

Misconception 1795: Believes you subtract the smaller digit from the larger digit in column subtraction, regardless of position
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly align digits in column subtraction and incorrectly applies place value concepts by always subtracting the smaller digit from the larger digit, regardless of their position within the number.

Misconception 1796: Thinks a positive x value in a column vector means to move left rather than right
Expanded misconception:
Misconceives directionality in a coordinate system and mistakenly applies the opposite direction, specifically, thinking a positive value for a given axis (e.g., x in a column vector) indicates movement in the opposite direction (e.g., left instead of right) when interpreting graphical representations or performing transformations.

Misconception 1797: Assumes the range of a set of data starts at zero
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider that the range of a set of data does not necessarily start from zero, but rather from the lowest actual value in the set, leading to miscalculation of range, scale, or interval.

Misconception 1798: Believes 1 is a prime number
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the definition of prime numbers, specifically that a prime number must have exactly two distinct factors (1 and itself), and mistakenly considers the number 1 as prime due to lack of understanding of this definition or its exceptions.

Misconception 1799: When converting fraction to ratio, thinks you just use the numerator and denominator as the numbers in the ratio 
Expanded misconception:
When converting a fraction to a ratio, incorrectly assumes that the numerator and denominator can be directly used as the numbers in the ratio without considering the direction or order of the ratio, such as identifying the part-to-whole or part-to-part relationship.

Misconception 1800: Believes multiplying two positives gives a negative answer
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the rule of signs in multiplication, specifically believing that multiplying two positive numbers or two negative numbers always yields a negative result, rather than the correct outcome.

Misconception 1801: Believes they always divide by the conversion factor when converting units
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply conversion factors correctly, specifically consistently dividing by the conversion factor instead of considering the direction of conversion and inverting the conversion factor when necessary.

Misconception 1802: Adds instead of subtracts when finding the area of part of a shape
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the concept of subtraction in geometric calculations, such as adding instead of subtracting areas when finding the area of a composite shape or the area of a part of a shape (e.g., a shape with a hole) by incorrectly treating it as a combination of separate addition operations or disregarding the need for subtraction to account for shared or overlapping regions.

Misconception 1803: Believes they can divide by a variable without checking whether it could equal zero
Expanded misconception:
Does not account for potential division by zero in algebraic expressions, failing to consider that a variable might take on a value that leads to an undefined mathematical operation.

Misconception 1804: When dividing fractions, multiplies the numerators and multiplies the denominators
Expanded misconception:
When dividing fractions, inverts the second fraction's numerator and denominator but then incorrectly multiplies the numerators and multiplies the denominators instead of multiplying the first fraction's numerator by the second fraction's denominator and multiplying the first fraction's denominator by the second fraction's numerator.

Misconception 1805: Does not realise that subtracting a negative is the same as adding it's additive inverse
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the concept of double negatives or additive inverses in arithmetic operations, resulting in incorrect handling of expressions containing subtraction of negative numbers, and instead treats subtraction of negatives as a literal mathematical operation rather than as the equivalent of addition.

Misconception 1806: Only recognises the negative form of a quadratic graph
Expanded misconception:
Only recognises simple quadratic graphs in the standard form ax^2 + bx + c where a is negative (i.e., facing downwards), but struggles to identify more complex quadratic graphs, such as those with transformations (e.g. stretches, shifts), in non-standard forms (e.g. factored or vertex form), or with positive 'a' values (i.e. facing upwards) due to relying solely on visual inspection rather than algebraic understanding.

Misconception 1807: When multiplying mixed numbers, forgets to multiply the fraction
Expanded misconception:
When multiplying mixed numbers, fails to convert the mixed number to an improper fraction before performing the multiplication, specifically neglecting to multiply both the whole number part and the fraction part.

Misconception 1808: does not realise that the product of prime factors will also be a factor of the original number 
Expanded misconception:
does not realise that the product of prime factors, as well as any combination of these prime factors, will also be a factor of the original number.

Misconception 1809: Rounds too little when estimating
Expanded misconception:
Rounds or truncates values too aggressively when estimating or performing calculations, resulting in loss of precision or significant digits.

Misconception 1810: Thinks x = 1 at the x axis
Expanded misconception:
"Thinks a variable always has a constant or default value (e.g. x = 1, y = 0) along an axis when solving equations or graphing functions, rather than understanding that variables can represent a range of values."

Misconception 1811: Believes that the compensation strategy for the multiplication of 2 numbers can only be used one way round.
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to recognize that the multiplication relationship (e.g., a × b = c) can be applied in different combinations and permutations (e.g., b × a = c), resulting in incorrect reversal or inability to generalize a multiplication compensation strategy when the operation order or factors are altered."

Misconception 1812: When multiplying fractions, adds the denominators and multiplies the numerators
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to apply the correct procedure for multiplying fractions, resulting in the addition of denominators and/or multiplication of numerators, instead of multiplying both numerators and denominators separately."

Misconception 1813: Estimates the length of diagonal lines rather than working out based on horizontal and vertical measurements
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply Pythagorean theorem or principles of distance calculation when working with right-angled triangles or diagonal distances, instead relying on visual estimations of length for diagonal lines rather than calculating the actual length based on horizontal and vertical measurements.

Misconception 1814: Confusion over whether multiplication or division is the correct way to solve the problem.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recall or consistently apply the correct operation (in this case, either multiplication or division) to a given problem or selects an operation that results in a reasonable solution using a conflicting operation due to incomplete or inaccurate understanding of mathematical relationships.

Misconception 1815: When given the perimeter of a regular polygon, subtracts instead of divides to find each side length
Expanded misconception:
When given the perimeter of a regular polygon, fails to apply the inverse operation, subtracts the number of sides instead of dividing to find the length of each side.

Misconception 1816: Believes the probability on all branches travelling in the same direction on a tree diagram are the same, despite changing events
Expanded misconception:
Fails to update probabilities in a tree diagram after each event, assuming that each branch in the same direction, regardless of the changing events or dependencies, has an equal probability despite the influence of precedent events or outcomes.

Misconception 1817: Rounds every number down when estimating
Expanded misconception:
Rounds every number down when estimating or disregards the impact of rounding on the overall calculation, leading to an oversimplification of the problem and resultant errors in the solution.

Misconception 1818: Thinks you can find missing values in a given table by treating the row as linear and adding on the difference between the first two values given.
Expanded misconception:
Thinks you can find missing values in a given table by treating the row as linear and using an incorrect pattern recognition approach, such as adding on the difference between consecutive values or applying an incorrect multiplication factor, to calculate or extrapolate missing data.

Misconception 1819: Confuses order of sides in a trigonometric ratio
Expanded misconception:
Confuses order of sides in a specific trigonometric ratio (e.g., sine, cosine, or tangent) such as incorrectly using the opposite side in place of the adjacent side, or vice versa, when identifying the correct trigonometric function in a right-angled triangle.

Misconception 1820: Uses the denominator when converting from fractions to ratio, rather than numerator.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly convert fractions to ratios or decimals, often inverting the numerator and the denominator or using the wrong component of the fraction during conversion, such as using the denominator instead of the numerator.

Misconception 1821: When finding the nth term rule for a quadratic sequence, believes that the coefficient of n squared is the second difference
Expanded misconception:
When finding the nth term rule for a quadratic sequence, believes that the coefficient of n squared is the second difference, ignoring the fact that the second difference is actually twice the coefficient of n squared.

Misconception 1822: Thinks they multiply the numerator and denominator together to convert a fraction to a percentage 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct conversion process for fractions to decimals or percentages, specifically by multiplying the numerator and denominator together instead of dividing the numerator by the denominator and then multiplying by 100 to get a percentage.

Misconception 1823: When there are two modes, finds the mean of these values and gives that as the mode
Expanded misconception:
When there are multiple modes in a dataset, incorrectly calculates the mean of these modes and gives that as the single mode rather than recognizing and reporting the presence of multiple modes.

Misconception 1824: Counts the starting number when counting backwards
Expanded misconception:
Counts the starting number as one of the items when counting backwards or performing subtraction, failing to exclude the initial value in the calculation.

Misconception 1825: Does not fully simplify fraction
Expanded misconception:
Demonstrates a lack of understanding of order of operations and/or division, often resulting in partial or incomplete simplification of fractions.

Misconception 1826: Believes the solution of a quadratic is its turning point
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly identifies the solution of a quadratic equation as the vertex or turning point of the parabola, rather than finding the x-intercepts or roots by applying factoring, quadratic formula, or other problem-solving strategies.

Misconception 1827: Believes the upper bound of an interval in a grouped frequency table is equivalent to the group width of the interval
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly assumes the upper bound of an interval in a grouped frequency table is the exact value or the group width, rather than recognizing it as an endpoint that includes the value up to but not including the next interval's lower bound.

Misconception 1828: Does not understand inequality notation
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand inequality notation, specifically the concepts of greater than (>), less than (<), greater than or equal to (≥), and less than or equal to (≤), often misinterpreting the direction of the inequality, mistaking the concept for an equation, or confounding the meaning of the inequality symbols.

Misconception 1829: Believes they must change the direction of the inequality when dividing through by a positive number.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that the direction of an inequality remains unchanged when both sides are divided by a positive number, as opposed to a negative number, which would require the direction to be reversed.

Misconception 1830: Thinks area of a circle = radius x pi 
Expanded misconception:
Thinks the area of a circle is equal to the radius multiplied by pi, rather than the radius squared multiplied by pi, often indicating a lack of understanding of the relationship between the radius and area formulas and a tendency to oversimplify geometric formulas.

Misconception 1831: Does not know the properties of a cylinder
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the geometric properties of a cylinder, including its definition as the set of all points at a fixed distance from a line (axis), and struggles to apply formulas such as those for surface area and volume, as well as understanding the relationships between radius, diameter, height, and base area.

Misconception 1832: Confuses linear and reciprocal graphs
Expanded misconception:
Confuses linear and reciprocal graphs, specifically having difficulty in identifying and applying characteristics such as the presence or absence of asymptotes, rates of change, and axis intercepts, and sometimes inverting or misinterpreting key features such as increasing, decreasing, or constant trends in linear and reciprocal functions.

Misconception 1833: When drawing a histogram, thinks you plot frequency on the horizontal axis
Expanded misconception:
When drawing frequency plots such as histograms or bar charts, incorrectly assigns the x-axis (horizontal axis) to represent frequency, magnitude, or quantity, rather than the categorical, qualitative, or continuous variable being measured, and the y-axis (vertical axis) to represent the variable being measured, rather than the frequency, proportion, or density.

Misconception 1834: Confuses an equation with an expression
Expanded misconception:
Confuses an equation with an expression, often including not understanding when the equation equals zero or requires a numerical value for one of the variables, or incorrectly assuming the solution for an equation can be the value of an individual term rather than the entire equation.

Misconception 1835: When finding the area of a parallelogram finds base x perpendicular height but divides the answer by 2 
Expanded misconception:
The misconception that the student incorrectly applies the formula for the area of a triangle to that of a parallelogram, multiplying the base by the perpendicular height but incorrectly dividing the result by 2, possibly due to confusing it with the area formula for triangles or a misunderstanding of the properties of a parallelogram.

Misconception 1836: Believes lines of symmetry must be vertical or horizontal
Expanded misconception:
Believes lines of symmetry in 2D shapes must always be vertical or horizontal and cannot be diagonal, therefore incorrectly assuming symmetry only occurs along the x or y-axis.

Misconception 1837: Names one of the faces of a 3D shape rather than the 3D shape name
Expanded misconception:
Identifies or names only one of the attributes, components or 2D faces of a 3D shape rather than recognizing or recalling the overall 3D shape name, or relies on a characteristic such as a specific number of sides or orientation, rather than the shape's holistic properties or standard name.

Misconception 1838: Confuses the roles of initial capture, recapture, and total population in the capture-recapture method
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands the roles of initial capture, recapture, and total population in the capture-recapture method, leading to incorrect interpretations of Lincoln-Petersen estimates, potentially resulting in miscalculations of population sizes due to misconceptions about the relationship between the initially captured sample, the recaptured subset, and the overall population being studied.

Misconception 1839: Does not know how to use a bar model to write division facts
Expanded misconception:
Does not know how to or incorrectly applies the concept of inverse operations, including using a bar model to represent division facts, leading to incorrect conversions between multiplication and division, and struggling to derive related facts or solve division problems.

Misconception 1840: Thinks the fraction button on a calculator converts fractions to decimals
Expanded misconception:
Insufficient understanding of calculator functionality, specifically thinking that the fraction button or equivalent functions directly convert mixed numbers or improper fractions into decimal form without proper simplification or conversion process, leading to incorrect numerical calculations or handling of decimal equivalents.

Misconception 1841: Thinks the number of wholes in a mixed number represents the tens from the numerator of an improper fraction
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the equivalent relationship between mixed numbers and improper fractions, specifically thinks the number of wholes in a mixed number represents the tens place from the numerator of an improper fraction rather than combining with the denominator.

Misconception 1842: Confuses the number of regions in a Venn diagram as the total number of entries in the diagram
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between the number of regions in a set diagram (e.g., Venn diagram) and the total count of elements, resulting in incorrect calculations or interpretations that mistakenly equate regions with the overall quantity of data or entries.

Misconception 1843: When using interpolation to estimate frequency that is above a certain value, believes they can add the estimated frequency up to that value in its class, rather than the estimated frequency after it
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider continuity of data when interpolating frequencies, and instead believes the interpolated frequency can be combined with the endpoint value of the interval, rather than replacing it with the interpolated frequency in the adjacent interval.

Misconception 1844: Uses length scale factor for area without squaring 
Expanded misconception:
Applies numeric scale factors to two-dimensional quantities without considering the compounding effect of dimensions, such as squaring or cubing scaling factors for area or volume calculations.

Misconception 1845: When multiplying involving a multiple of 10, just multiplies the digits and ignores the zeros 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct rules for place value when multiplying or dividing by multiples of 10, often resulting in incorrect digit placement or neglecting the identity element of multiplication (i.e., the zero).

Misconception 1846: Is not able to spot a proportional relationship pictorially unless they are laid out in groups
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or identify proportional relationships in visual representations unless the quantities are explicitly grouped or illustrated in a discrete, easily comparable format.

Misconception 1847: Confuses the part and the whole in fraction of an amount problems
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between a fractional part of a quantity and the entire quantity itself in problems involving fractions, often resulting in incorrect calculations or missing requisite conversions when evaluating parts of a whole in mathematical expressions or real-world scenarios.

Misconception 1848: Thinks the y coordinate of a point on the x axis is 1
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to understand the coordinate geometry, specifically thinking the y-coordinate of any point on the x-axis is non-zero (e.g., thinks it's always 1), rather than recognizing that any point on the x-axis has a y-coordinate of 0."

Misconception 1849: Believes the gradient of a line is given by the x intercept
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly identifies the gradient of a line as being equivalent to either its x-intercept or its y-intercept, and possibly confuses the relationship between the gradient, x-intercept, and y-intercept in general.

Misconception 1850: Mixes up square rooting and fourth rooting
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the operation and properties of square roots with those of higher-order roots, such as fourth roots or nth roots, often resulting in incorrect calculations or misuse of symbols when solving equations involving radicals.

Misconception 1851: Does not know the properties of an isosceles triangle
Expanded misconception:
Does not demonstrate understanding of the properties of an isosceles triangle, particularly that isosceles triangles have two sides of equal length, resulting in two equal angles and a symmetrical shape, but may not recognize the specific implications of these properties in various geometric contexts or problems, such as identifying equal angles, applying base and side congruence, or recognizing that an isosceles triangle's height bisects the vertex angle and the base.

Misconception 1852: When adding an integer to a fraction, thinks you add the integer to the numerator
Expanded misconception:
When adding an integer to a fraction, thinks you add the integer to the numerator without accounting for the denominator, or incorrectly assumes that adding the integer to the numerator directly yields the total sum.

Misconception 1853: Thinks an enlargement of any scale factor keeps side lengths the same
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly assumes that all transformations involving scale factors are equivalent to translations, failing to recognize that scale factors other than one inherently change the dimensions or proportions of a shape, and mistakenly applying properties of rigid movements to non-rigid transformations involving enlargement.

Misconception 1854: Confuses tangent and radius
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between the tangent line to a circle and the radius, possibly due to incorrect application of properties or misconceptions about their geometric relationships.

Misconception 1855: Believes that if one number in a product decreases, the answer must increase. 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the inverse relationship between factors in multiplication, believing that a decrease in one factor will result in an increase in the product, rather than a decrease.

Misconception 1856: Thinks multiplication and addition are the same
Expanded misconception:
Lacks understanding of operation hierarchy and treats multiplication and addition as interchangeable operations, often resulting in either adding or multiplying values in a manner inconsistent with the given mathematical expression.

Misconception 1857: Believes a cubic equation will have a linear graph
Expanded misconception:
Believes that an equation's graph shape can be solely determined by the degree of the polynomial on the variable's exponent, ignoring other factors such as variable coefficients, constant coefficients, and operation combinations.

Misconception 1858: Does not compensate when subtracting
Expanded misconception:
Fails to adjust or compensate when a value is subtracted within an equation, such as not accounting for borrowing or considering the effect of the subtraction operation on the overall calculation's value.

Misconception 1859: Thinks the mode in a stem and leaf diagram  is the stem that has the most leaves 
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets the concept of the mode in a stem and leaf diagram: Confuses the concept of the mode as the stem that has the most leaves, rather than the actual number(s) that appear most frequently within the data set.

Misconception 1860: Believes a reciprocal graph can be a straight line
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that the reciprocal of a function will always result in a non-linear curve or a hyperbola, and incorrectly assumes that a reciprocal graph can be a straight line or does not understand that a reciprocal function is undefined at points where the original function is zero.

Misconception 1861: Rounds to nearest 50 not 20
Expanded misconception:
Miscalculates or misapplies rounding rules, typically rounds to the nearest multiple of a convenient unit such as 50 or 10, rather than the correct unit specified in the problem.

Misconception 1862: Performs addition ahead of division
Expanded misconception:
Fails to adhere to the order of operations or forgets to prioritize division over addition, leading to incorrect calculation when both operations are present in the same expression.

Misconception 1863: Thinks 1l = 10 cubic centimetres
Expanded misconception:
Confuses units of volume between milliliters (mL) and units of length between millimeters (mm), centimeters (cm), or incorrectly assumes an equivalence between 1 liter (L) and a small unit of volume such as 10 cubic centimeters (cm³), 1 liter equaling 1000 cubic centimeters (cm³).

Misconception 1864: Does not realise that the order is important in a ratio
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand or apply the concept of ordered operations or operations with specific priority, such as in ratios or fractions, ignoring the importance of the order of elements.

Misconception 1865: When dividing numbers with different bases, subtracts the powers and subtracts the base numbers.
Expanded misconception:
When dividing numbers with different bases, mistakenly subtracts the exponents (or powers) and subtracts the base numbers, failing to recognize that division with different bases requires a common base or conversion, and incorrectly applying the rule for division of powers with the same base.

Misconception 1866: Confuses additive and multiplicative relationships
Expanded misconception:
Confuses additive and multiplicative relationships, such as incorrectly applying addition when multiplication is required, or vice versa, resulting in a computational error due to a misunderstanding of the operation or concept involved.

Misconception 1867: Finds unit price then forgets to multiply up
Expanded misconception:
Fails to scale up by multiplying the unit price or rate by the total quantity after correctly calculating it, resulting in an incorrect final answer.

Misconception 1868: Believes lines are parallel if they have the same y intercept 
Expanded misconception:
Believes lines are parallel if they have the same y-intercept or equal slope value, or same x-intercept, regardless of other characteristics.

Misconception 1869: Believes that the larger the divisor, the larger the answer.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that division involves finding a quotient by sharing or grouping into equal parts, and instead, incorrectly associates larger divisors with larger results due to a misunderstanding of the relationship between the divisor and quotient in division operations.

Misconception 1870: Thinks there are 10000mm in a metre
Expanded misconception:
"Insufficient understanding of unit conversion between millimeters and meters, often resulting in incorrect placement of decimal points or misunderstanding the relationship between metric prefixes, which may lead to answers equivalent to 10000mm or 1000cm in 1 meter."

Misconception 1871: Thinks factorising a quadratic means factorising the variable terms into a single bracket, and leaving out the constant
Expanded misconception:
Thinks factorising a quadratic equation involves expressing only the terms containing variables as a product of simpler expressions, typically neglecting the constant term or incorrectly handling its relationship with the rest of the equation.

Misconception 1872: Believes they only need to multiply the first and last pairs of terms when expanding double brackets
Expanded misconception:
Believes they only need to multiply the first and last pairs of terms when expanding double brackets, ignoring or misapplying the middle term and the resulting combinations of the individual terms.

Misconception 1873: Believes the median is the midpoint of the range
Expanded misconception:
Believes the median is the midpoint or average of the highest and lowest values in a dataset, rather than the middle value when the data is ordered from least to greatest.

Misconception 1874: Believes the median is the frequency for the category in which it is located
Expanded misconception:
Believes the median is the frequency for the category in which it is located, or interprets the median as a measure of frequency, mode or most common value, rather than as a measure of the middle value in a data set when it is ordered from the smallest to the largest.

Misconception 1875: Has used a subtraction sign to represent division
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets division operation and may have difficulty representing division symbolically; uses a subtraction sign to represent division, such as writing '6 - 2' to represent '6 ÷ 2'.

Misconception 1876: Counts leading zero as a significant figure
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that leading zeros are not significant figures and therefore incorrectly includes them in calculations involving numbers with leading zeros in whole number or decimal form.

Misconception 1877: Thinks rectangles have rotational symmetry or order 4
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly applies the concept of rotational symmetry, specifically thinking that all rectangles have an order of 4 rotational symmetry, and fails to recognize that some rectangles may only have an order of 2 rotational symmetry or no rotational symmetry at all.

Misconception 1878: In inverse proportion, believes that if one variable is a reciprocal, the other variable will be the reciprocal as well. 
Expanded misconception:
In inverse proportion relationships, mistakenly assumes that when one variable is expressed as a reciprocal (1/x), the other variable will also be the reciprocal of its original value, resulting in incorrect application of equations and flawed understanding of inverse variation.

Misconception 1879: Believes midpoint of an interval is used rather than total frequency to calculate the sector angle for a pie chart
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands the concept of proportionality in pie charts, incorrectly assuming that the midpoint or beginning of an interval, rather than the total frequency or proportion, should be used to calculate the sector angle.

Misconception 1880: Mixes up greater than and less than symbols
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly apply basic inequality properties, specifically confusing the greater than (>) and less than (<) symbols, potentially leading to incorrect ordering or comparison of numerical values.

Misconception 1881: Halves when asked to find the cube root
Expanded misconception:
Misapplies exponent rules by halving when asked to find the cube root, rather than recognizing the need to isolate the base by raising both sides to the reciprocal power of the exponent (i.e., 1/3) in the case of cube roots.

Misconception 1882: Believes square numbers are triangle numbers
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between different types of figurate numbers, specifically conflating the properties and definitions of square numbers (a²) and triangular numbers (n*(n+1)/2), illustrating a lack of understanding of the distinct patterns and formulas governing these sequences.

Misconception 1883: Uses only the base and perpendicular height when calculating the area of a trapezium
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct formula for the area of a trapezium by only considering the average height between the two parallel sides (or using an incorrect average) or by using only the base and perpendicular height, rather than averaging the lengths of the two parallel sides and multiplying by the height.

Misconception 1884: Thinks numbers are a multiple of their index
Expanded misconception:
Assumes that the result of an arithmetic operation or the value of a quantity must be related to the position or index of the number in the sequence of options, rather than being determined by the actual mathematical operation or underlying concept.

Misconception 1885: Thinks a power raised to a power leads to addition of the powers
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets the concept of exponentiation by incorrectly applying the rule of multiplying exponents when adding the same base to itself, instead applying this rule when raising a power to a power, resulting in incorrect addition of exponents.

Misconception 1886: Does not understand that base angles in an isosceles are equal
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand that base angles in an isosceles triangle are equal, resulting in incorrect assumptions about angle relationships, failure to identify isosceles triangles, and inability to apply properties of isosceles triangles to solve problems or find missing angle measures.

Misconception 1887: Adds instead of subtracts when asked to find the difference
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct operation, often adding instead of subtracting, when asked to find the difference between two quantities.

Misconception 1888: Does not know the information each vertical line of a box plot represents
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the representation of key statistical elements within a box plot, specifically failing to recognize that each vertical line represents a quartile (Q1, median, or Q3) or whisker, and its respective relationship to the surrounding quartiles, and overall distribution of the data.

Misconception 1889: Believes f(x - a) translates the function up a units
Expanded misconception:
Believes f(x - a) translates the function up a units, rather than to the right by a units.

Misconception 1890: Thinks the multiplication sign means to add
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to understand the operation denoted by the multiplication symbol, mistakenly interpreting it as addition, and consequently treats multiplication as an operation that combines numbers through a simple addition process."

Misconception 1891: Believes they can cross-cancel before flipping the second fraction when dividing fractions
Expanded misconception:
Fails to invert the divisor and multiply when dividing fractions, instead attempting to cross-cancel or directly divide numerators and denominators, revealing a lack of understanding of the algorithm for fraction division.

Misconception 1892: Does not recognise the sin graph
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognise the general properties of the sine function graph, including amplitude, period, and key features such as maximum and minimum values, and typical shape.

Misconception 1893: Believes the y intercept is given by the difference between the x and y coordinates of any point on the line, not just the point (0, c) 
Expanded misconception:
Believes that the y-intercept of a linear equation is the difference between the x and y coordinates of any point on the line, rather than the point where the line intersects the y-axis (0, c), and may apply this misconception to find the y-intercept using non-origin point coordinates, instead of identifying and using the point where x equals zero.

Misconception 1894: Confuses the part and the whole in missing number subtraction problems
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the part and the whole in missing number subtraction problems, specifically having trouble identifying whether a given number is the minuend, subtrahend, or difference, often resulting in incorrect calculations or reversal of the entire subtraction operation.

Misconception 1895: Does not realise that all circles are similar
Expanded misconception:
Does not realise that all circles are similar and that any two circles are scaled versions of each other, leading to proportional relationships in radius, diameter, circumference, and other circle-related measurements.

Misconception 1896: When finding the gradient from the equation of a line in the form ax + by = c, believes b/a is the gradient
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that when finding the gradient from the equation of a line in the form ax + by = c, the coefficient of x (a) should be the denominator rather than the numerator, leading to incorrect gradient calculation, and instead incorrectly calculates the gradient as b/a when given the equation in the form ax + by = c, rather than -b/a, particularly in cases where the equation needs to be rearranged into the slope-intercept form or other standard formats.

Misconception 1897: Confuses vertically opposite angles and angles around a point
Expanded misconception:
Confuses vertically opposite angles (angles that are opposite each other at a point where two lines intersect) and angles around a point (the sum of the angles around a point is 360 degrees) when identifying, calculating, or applying properties and relationships of angles in different geometric contexts.

Misconception 1898: When adding decimals, just adds the digits and ignores place value
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the place value of decimal numbers, treating decimal points as if they do not exist or incorrectly aligning decimal places, resulting in a digit-by-digit addition that disregards the actual decimal values.

Misconception 1899: Thinks the missing length has to be half the length of the side given
Expanded misconception:
Thinks the missing length in a geometric shape must be a fixed proportion or fraction of another given length, such as half, a quarter, or a specific ratio, when in fact the relationship between the lengths is more complex.

Misconception 1900: Thinks two variables next to each other represent addition
Expanded misconception:
"Thinks two variables or numerical values next to each other, with or without an intervening sign or operation symbol, always represent addition, rather than a lack of explicit operations or an algebraic term waiting to be simplified or evaluated."

Misconception 1901: Subtracted base numbers as well as powers
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to recognize that the exponentiation operation (raising a base to a power) has a higher order of operations priority than the subtraction operation, subtracting base numbers but not correctly applying exponents and therefore incorrectly manipulating numbers with powers in expressions."

Misconception 1902: Gets the minutes and hours the wrong way around
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets or misapplies time in a manner that inverts units or gets the hours and minutes, days and years, or other temporal components the wrong way around in calculations or conversions.

Misconception 1903: Thinks all lines on a net will form edges in 3D
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to account for hidden lines or lines representing folds in a 2D net, incorrectly assuming they all represent external edges when the shape is constructed in 3D."

Misconception 1904: Believes the numbers in factorising should multiply to give the coefficient of x and add to give the constant
Expanded misconception:
Misuses the relationship between the coefficients of quadratic equations and their factorised forms, specifically believing that when factorising expressions in the form of ax^2 + bx + c, the numbers in the brackets should multiply to give the coefficient of x (b) and add to give the constant term (c) rather than multiply to give the product of a and c and add to give the coefficient of x (b).

Misconception 1905: Believes that when finding bounds you round to the nearest whole number
Expanded misconception:
Believes that when finding bounds or estimating quantities, they consistently, inappropriately, or oversimplify by rounding to the nearest whole number, failing to account for relevant rules or considerations in rounding, such as rounding to a specific decimal place, or rounding rules in calculations involving upper and lower bounds.

Misconception 1906: Gives 3-letter angle notation in the wrong order
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly apply angle notation conventions by reversing or incorrectly sequencing the letters that represent the vertices or vertex order when naming a three-letter angle.

Misconception 1907: Believes an interior angle in a regular polygon can be found using 360 subtract (180 over number of sides)
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly applies the formula for the sum of interior angles of a polygon, specifically substituting incorrect or irrelevant values into the formula (e.g., using 180 divided by the number of sides) to find an interior angle, rather than using the correct formula: (n-2)*180 divided by n, where n is the number of sides in a regular polygon.

Misconception 1908: Ignores numerical terms when setting up an equation from a question
Expanded misconception:
Fails to translate numerical information given in the problem into a corresponding mathematical expression or equation, often omitting or misinterpreting numerical coefficients, constants, or other numerical terms essential for accurately modeling the situation.

Misconception 1909: Does not consider the denominator when adding/ subtracting fractions. Adds/subtracts the numerator and makes the denominator the one with the highest value.
Expanded misconception:
Does not consider the denominator when adding or subtracting fractions. Instead, inappropriately applies whole number addition or subtraction strategies by adding or subtracting the numerators and either keeping one of the original denominators, making the denominator the one with the highest value, or averaging the denominators, resulting in an incorrect representation of the resulting fraction.

Misconception 1910: Confuses relative frequency and expected frequency
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between relative frequency (observed proportion of a sample) and expected frequency (theoretical number of occurrences based on probability), possibly due to misunderstanding the concepts, misapplying formulas, or ignoring the given probability values, leading to incorrect calculations or interpretations of probability and statistical results in various distributions and experiments.

Misconception 1911: Only multiplies second term in the expansion of a bracket
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the distributive property when expanding expressions with brackets, only multiplying the second term by the factor outside the brackets, and incorrectly treating the first term or other elements inside the brackets as constants or neglecting to multiply them by the factor outside.

Misconception 1912: Gives the probability out of the number of non-successes rather than the total
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that probability is calculated as the number of successful outcomes divided by the total number of outcomes, and mistakenly bases calculations on non-successful or unsuccessful outcomes instead.

Misconception 1913: Gives the smallest whole when asked for the smallest fraction shaded, presented visually
Expanded misconception:
Gives the smallest whole when asked for the smallest fraction or the fewest number of items shaded, demonstrated visually, often apparent in circle or rectangle diagrams where the individual doesn't account for partial sections in relation to the whole, and/or does not consider fractions that have values less than one.

Misconception 1914: Converts fraction to percentage by multiplying the denominator by 10
Expanded misconception:
Fails to convert fractions to percentages correctly and misapplies the concept that multiplying or dividing by 10 is used to convert between standard and decimal forms, incorrectly assuming it is applicable to fractions and percentages.

Misconception 1915: When multiplying mixed numbers thinks they add the integers
Expanded misconception:
When multiplying mixed numbers, incorrectly thinks that the operation involves adding (or simply combining) the integer parts and/or incorrectly handles the fractional parts, without properly converting to improper fractions or following the order of operations.

Misconception 1916: When subtracting fractions, subtracts the numerators and multiplies the denominators
Expanded misconception:
"In operations involving fractions, inappropriately applies whole-number subtraction and multiplication rules, such as incorrectly subtracting the numerators and multiplying or incorrectly adding the denominators, rather than following the correct order of operations and fraction properties."

Misconception 1917: Confuses the meaning of parallel and perpendicular
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the meaning of parallel and perpendicular lines, often incorrectly identifying pairs of lines with no intersection points as perpendicular, or lines that never intersect as not being parallel.

Misconception 1918: Does not fully simplify ratio
Expanded misconception:
Does not fully simplify a fraction or ratio by failing to cancel out common factors between the numerator and the denominator, or neglects the order of operations when simplifying complex ratios.

Misconception 1919: Believes that a fractional index also means you need to find the reciprocal
Expanded misconception:
Believes that a fractional index (e.g. 1/2, 1/3, etc.) in a mathematical expression indicates taking the reciprocal of a value instead of properly interpreting it as a root or power operation.

Misconception 1920: Forgotten that anything to the power of 0 equals 1
Expanded misconception:
 Forgotten that zero exponent rule states that any number (except zero itself) raised to the power of 0 equals 1, and may not properly evaluate expressions containing zero exponent.

Misconception 1921: Does not understand laws of indices
Expanded misconception:
Does not apply, or misapplies, the laws of indices, including the product rule, quotient rule, or power rule, resulting in incorrect manipulation and simplification of algebraic expressions.

Misconception 1922: Gets the dividend and divisor the wrong way round when doing long division
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the dividend and divisor roles, or inverts the dividend and divisor, when performing division operations such as long division, potentially also applying the wrong operation to the wrong part of the equation.

Misconception 1923: Thinks a greater range means more consistent data
Expanded misconception:
Thinks a greater range means more consistent, reliable, or representative data, and/or confuses the concept of range with other statistical measures such as precision or accuracy, indicating a lack of understanding of how to interpret data and the importance of central tendency and variability.

Misconception 1924: Believes that a number divided by itself equals 0
Expanded misconception:
Believes that a number divided by itself, or any number to the power of zero, or any of the identity properties of division (i.e., 1 or a number divided by itself), equals 0, rather than the correct result of 1, indicating a lack of understanding of the basic properties of arithmetic operations and algebraic expressions.

Misconception 1925: Believes nth term rules for patterns must be given in standard an + b form
Expanded misconception:
Believes nth term rules for patterns must be given in standard an + b form, and fails to recognize that patterns can be represented by alternative forms, such as sequences in reversed terms, or in different equation structures like ax^n + b.

Misconception 1926: Believes you use the half of the base length to find the area of an isosceles triangle, following using it to find the perpendicular height
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly applies properties of one geometric concept to another: incorrectly uses the formula for the area of certain triangles (e.g. a right triangle or a right-angled isosceles triangle with a half base formula) in finding the area of an isosceles triangle or misunderstands a half relationship inherent to one step of finding an area of a particular triangle to incorrectly assume half a base is used in every or all property formulas or steps of finding the area of a triangle, such as when identifying the base to use the Pythagorean Theorem or length of two congruent sides to apply the triangle area half base times height formula.

Misconception 1927: Does not know the definition of a prism
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the definition of a prism, including its characteristics, properties, and relationship to other geometric shapes, or confuses it with other three-dimensional shapes such as a pyramid or a polyhedron.

Misconception 1928: Mixes up the numerator and denominator
Expanded misconception:
Mixes up the numerator and denominator when dealing with fractions, resulting in incorrect order, leading to incorrect calculations or manipulations such as addition, subtraction, multiplication, or division of fractions, often substituting the numerator for the denominator and vice versa.

Misconception 1929: Does not understand the concept of inverse operations in algebra
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or apply the principle of inverse operations, where addition and subtraction are inverse operations and multiplication and division are inverse operations, resulting in incorrect grouping or order of operations, or incorrect assumption of equivalent expressions or equations.

Misconception 1930: Believes it's ok to change the scale on an axis for the negative values
Expanded misconception:
Fails to maintain consistent unit scales on coordinate axes, often altering scale on the negative side of an axis, leading to incorrect graph interpretations and analyses.

Misconception 1931: Believes the opposite side is where the adjacent side is in a right angled triangle
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between opposite and adjacent sides in a right-angled triangle, incorrectly associating the opposite side with the value or location of the adjacent side when working with trigonometric ratios or solving problems involving right-angled triangles.

Misconception 1932: Counts partial symbols as if they were whole on pictograms
Expanded misconception:
Includes partial representations or sections of pictorial images as whole quantities, misinterpreting the visual depiction of data.

Misconception 1933: Believes that the reciprocal of a number is equal to that number (1/x = x)
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the concept of reciprocal and inverse relationships, believing that the reciprocal of a number is equal to the number itself (1/x = x), rather than applying the correct operation of flipping the fraction (1/x ≠ x).

Misconception 1934: Multiplied by the scale factor rather than divided in direct proportion problems
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to recognize that direct proportion problems involving scaling require division by the scale factor, rather than multiplication, resulting in incorrect calculations when scaling down or scaling up quantities."

Misconception 1935: Divides length of original by length of image to find scale factor, rather than image by original.
Expanded misconception:
Reverses the orientation of original and image when calculating the scale factor, either by mistakenly dividing the length of the original by the length of the image or by using the formula 'scale factor equals original divided by image' instead of the correct formula 'scale factor equals image divided by original'.

Misconception 1936: Believes the integer can be written before or after the surd eg. 3√2 = √2 3
Expanded misconception:
Misplaces or incorrectly orders components of a mixed radical expression, including integers and surds, by writing the integer before or after the surd when it should only be before, or vice versa (e.g., 3√2 ≠ √2 3, √2 3).

Misconception 1937: Believes subtracting a positive number from a negative number makes the answer less negative
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the concept of subtracting a positive number from a negative number, mistakenly believing it decreases the magnitude of the negative number instead of increasing it, often resulting from a lack of understanding in the direction of the number line.

Misconception 1938: Ignores zeros when giving the value of an integer
Expanded misconception:
Ignores zeros at the end, beginning, or within a numeric value, failing to recognize their place-holding significance or multiplicative effect, and incorrectly assumes their placement does not affect the overall numeric value.

Misconception 1939: Difficulty with translating a mathematical answer into a real world context
Expanded misconception:
Difficulty with translating or interpreting mathematical solutions/approaches to fit real-world contexts or practical applications, including struggles with unit conversion, meaningful comparison, and logical scaling.

Misconception 1940: Believes a kite has bisecting diagonals
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that only certain types of quadrilaterals (such as rectangles, squares, and rhombuses) necessarily have bisecting diagonals, and incorrectly applies this property to other shapes (including kites) or misconstrues the defining characteristics of these shapes.

Misconception 1941: Confuses the order of operations, believes subtraction comes before multiplication 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to follow the correct order of operations, specifically prioritizing subtraction or additive inverses over multiplication, or does not apply the operations hierarchy consistently (e.g., the conventional priority sequence of parentheses, exponents, multiplication and division, and addition and subtraction, or BIDMAS/BODMAS in some regions).

Misconception 1942: Chooses the wrong 3 letters to describe an angle
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets or incorrectly recalls standard three-letter angle notation conventions, such as Acute (A), Obtuse (O), or Right (R), resulting in incorrect angle classification.

Misconception 1943: Includes the intersection of a Venn diagram when asked to find the value of a set only
Expanded misconception:
Includes the intersection of a Venn diagram when the problem only asks to find the value of a set, demonstrating an inability to distinguish between union and intersection, and neglecting to consider the problem's specific instructions.

Misconception 1944: Confuses the radius with the radius squared in the equation of a circle
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the radius with the radius squared in the equation of a circle, such as incorrectly substituting 'r' for 'r^2' or vice versa in formulas like the equation of a circle (x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2, resulting in incorrect calculations or misplaced squared values.

Misconception 1945: Believes an upward slope on a distance-time graph means the movement has stopped
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the slope of a distance-time graph with instantaneous speed or velocity, incorrectly interpreting a positive slope as movement with increasing speed, a zero or near-zero slope as movement coming to a stop or remaining stationary, and a negative slope as movement in the reverse direction, when in reality, a positive slope on a distance-time graph indicates a constant or increasing magnitude of movement speed that is always in the positive or forward direction.

Misconception 1946: Believes numbers are divisible by numbers which sum to their factors
Expanded misconception:
Believes numbers are divisible by numbers which sum to their factors, overlooks the necessity for factor products to equal the original number, and fails to recognize non-arithmetical sum-to-factor misconceptions are valid heuristics.

Misconception 1947: Writes more than three repeated symbols next to each other in Roman numerals
Expanded misconception:
Writes more than three of the same numerals next to each other in Roman numerals, failing to apply the subtraction rule and instead relying solely on additive properties or demonstrating a fundamental misunderstanding of Roman numeral syntax and conventions.

Misconception 1948: Believes the inverse of square rooting is doubling
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the inverse operation of square root, specifically, incorrectly assuming that the inverse of square rooting is doubling a number instead of squaring it.

Misconception 1949: Believes reflection in y=-x switches the signs of the coordinates, but not their numbers
Expanded misconception:
Believes that reflection across the line y = -x only switches the signs of the coordinates, rather than switching the x and y values with their signs negated (i.e., (x, y) becomes (-y, -x), but incorrectly thinks it becomes (-x, -y)).

Misconception 1950: forgets to include the common factors when calculating the lowest common multiple
Expanded misconception:
Omits or incorrectly uses the greatest common divisor, common factors, or product of the numbers when calculating the least common multiple, often resulting in an inaccurate or oversimplified solution.

Misconception 1951:  Does not believe that a negative multiple of a vector is in the opposite direction
Expanded misconception:
Does not believe that a negative scalar multiplier changes the direction of the resulting vector, incorrectly assuming the sign of the scalar has no effect on the direction of the product, such as vector orientation in physics, linear algebra, or geometry problems.

Misconception 1952: Confuses class width and frequency when calculating frequency density
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between class width and frequency when calculating frequency density, leading to the incorrect assumption that frequency density is a direct measure of class frequency rather than frequency per unit of class width.

Misconception 1953: Makes an assumption about line segments being equal within a shape
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider or verify the equality of line segments within a shape, possibly due to misinterpretation of visual cues or neglecting to apply geometric properties and theorems, such as assuming opposite sides of a quadrilateral are equal without verifying it is a parallelogram or other specific type of quadrilateral.

Misconception 1954: Divides when converting to a smaller unit
Expanded misconception:
Fails to take into account the direction or purpose of unit conversion, incorrectly divides when converting from a larger unit to a smaller unit instead of multiplying, and vice versa.

Misconception 1955: Thinks that a percentage can be converted to a fraction using a denominator of 10 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that a percentage can be converted to a fraction using a denominator of 100, or incorrectly assumes a percentage can be directly converted to a fraction without considering the magnitude of the percentage, and often mistakenly uses a denominator of 10 instead.

Misconception 1956: Doubles side length for similar shape instead of using correct scale factor
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct scale factor when comparing similar shapes, incorrectly doubling side lengths or applying an arbitrary scaling method, rather than using the actual scale factor provided or calculated from given proportions.

Misconception 1957: Reads 10,000 as 1000
Expanded misconception:
Misplaces or ignores a digit, particularly a zero, leading to incorrect handling of place value when reading numbers, such as reading numbers with trailing zeros as if the zero was not present.

Misconception 1958: Believes the smaller number is always subtracted from the larger, regardless of the order of the calculation
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the importance of maintaining the order of operands in subtraction operations, often resulting in consistently subtracting the smaller number from the larger number, despite the actual calculation presented, leading to incorrect calculations and misunderstandings of mathematical operations.

Misconception 1959: Does not know that a solid is a 3D shape
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand that a solid is a three-dimensional shape with length, width, and height, and/or confuses it with two-dimensional shapes due to a lack of recognition or understanding of its properties and attributes.

Misconception 1960: Does not realise that the sum of the two shorter sides must be greater than the third side for it to be a possible triangle
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the Triangle Inequality Theorem, which states that for any triangle, the sum of the lengths of any two sides must be greater than the length of the remaining side, often resulting in the incorrect assumption that a triangle can be formed from any three line segments with lengths that do not satisfy this criterion.

Misconception 1961: Fails to identify mirror line
Expanded misconception:
Fails to identify or recognize the concept of reflection and its relationship to the mirror line, including identifying the mirror line as an axis of reflection when multiple lines are present, failing to orient the shape correctly with respect to the mirror line, or incorrectly applying transformations as a result of this misunderstanding.

Misconception 1962: Doesn't take into account cost when calculating expected return/profit
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the potential costs or expenses associated with an investment or decision when evaluating the expected outcome or calculating profit, ultimately leading to an incorrect assessment of the overall value or benefit.

Misconception 1963: Performs subtraction right to left if priority order means doing a calculation to the right first
Expanded misconception:
Fails to follow the correct order of operations, specifically when priority order requires performing operations from left to right or following the order of parentheses, exponents, multiplication and division, and addition and subtraction, and instead incorrectly applies a right-to-left approach for operations with the same priority, such as subtraction.

Misconception 1964: Estimates obtuse angles as 90 degrees or less
Expanded misconception:
Estimates obtuse angles as 90 degrees or less, often overlooking the fact that the angle measure can exceed 90 degrees due to a lack of understanding of angular measurement concepts or a reliance on visual cues rather than precise calculations.

Misconception 1965: Thinks you need to just add a % sign to a decimal to convert to a percentage
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that converting a decimal to a percentage requires multiplication by 100 and adding the percentage sign, merely adding a percentage sign to a decimal without scaling the value.

Misconception 1966: Finds the median instead of the mean
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly calculates the average by finding the middle number (median) instead of summing all numbers and dividing by the total count (mean).

Misconception 1967: Counts a side length more than once when counting perimeter due to not marking a starting point
Expanded misconception:
Fails to keep track of the starting point when measuring perimeter, resulting in incorrectly counting a side length more than once, or omitting a side due to incorrect accounting of boundary traversal.

Misconception 1968: Mixes up width and height of a rectangle
Expanded misconception:
'Fails to distinguish between the width and the height of a rectangle, leading to errors in calculating perimeter, area, or interpreting measurement results in problems involving rectangles.'

Misconception 1969: Mixes up square numbers and even numbers
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between square numbers (numbers obtained by squaring an integer) and even numbers, often considering all even numbers as square numbers or vice versa, due to limited or incomplete understanding of the properties and definitions of these concepts.

Misconception 1970: Confuses isosceles and right angle triangles
Expanded misconception:
Confuses properties of isosceles triangles (e.g., two sides equal and two angles equal, excluding the vertex angle) with properties of right angle triangles (e.g., one right angle, trigonometric ratios), showing an inability to apply or recognize distinct geometric characteristics and theorems applicable to each type of triangle.

Misconception 1971: Confuses the inverse operations of addition and subtraction when rearranging equations
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly rearrange equations due to confusion between inverse operations, specifically mistakenly replacing addition with subtraction or visa versa when isolating variables.

Misconception 1972: Subtracts rather than multiplies when answering worded problems
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets or misreads mathematical operations described in word problems, such as adding instead of subtracting, dividing instead of multiplying, or substituting operations (e.g., subtracts rather than multiplies, or multiplies instead of finding a percent).

Misconception 1973: Divides instead of multiplies when converting a decimal to a percentage
Expanded misconception:
Fails to follow the correct order of operations when converting between different units or formats, including mistakenly dividing or inverting operations, such as dividing instead of multiplying when converting a decimal to a percentage, or multiplying by an incorrect conversion factor.

Misconception 1974: Believes the gradient of a line is the root of its equation
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets the relationship between a linear equation's slope and its other components, specifically believing the gradient of a line is directly related to the solution or root of its equation, rather than being the coefficient representing the rate of change.

Misconception 1975: Does not understand the question
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to fully comprehend or misinterprets the question, leading to incorrect application of mathematical operations, misunderstanding of contextual information, or incorrect identification of key variables, resulting in a distractor selection that reflects a lack of overall understanding of the problem."

Misconception 1976: When multiplying a fraction by an integer, multiplies both numerator and denominator by the integer
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the distributive property of multiplication over fractions, specifically when multiplying a fraction by an integer, incorrectly multiplying both the numerator and denominator by the integer instead of just the numerator.

Misconception 1977: Confuses section and whole when using a bar model
Expanded misconception:
Confuses a part of a unit (subset or section) with the entire unit or the whole when representing or interpreting a bar model, often leading to incorrect identification or calculation of lengths, totals, or proportions.

Misconception 1978: Subtracts instead of adds when answering worded problems 
Expanded misconception:
"Confuses the operation between addition and subtraction in worded problems, substituting one for the other when interpreting the problem's intention or applying arithmetic operations, often due to a misinterpretation of the context or keywords."

Misconception 1979: Believes that they can reverse a percentage increase by decreasing the new value by the same percentage, and vice versa
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the inverse operation correctly when reversing percentage changes, specifically believing that to restore the original value after a percentage increase or decrease, they can apply the same percentage change in the opposite direction, rather than using the inverse percentage change or reversing the original calculation.

Misconception 1980: When multiplying fractions, adds the numerators and the denominators
Expanded misconception:
When multiplying fractions, incorrectly applies addition instead of multiplication to either the numerators or the denominators, or both, resulting in either an added numerator and an added denominator, or incorrect application of the operation to just one of them.

Misconception 1981: Does not know how to compare decimal numbers
Expanded misconception:
Lacks understanding of decimal number comparison, including failing to consider the same whole number part, ignoring the tenths, hundredths, or thousandths place when comparing digits, or incorrectly assuming the magnitude of decimal numbers based solely on their whole number or tenths place part.

Misconception 1982: When asked to find data points above a certain value, gives the number of data points below this value
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between data points above and below a specific value or threshold, consistently providing the count or description of the data points on the opposite side of the threshold.

Misconception 1983: When dividing involving a multiple of 10, gives an answer 10 times smaller than it should be 
Expanded misconception:
When dividing or multiplying by a multiple of 10, fails to consider the correct placement of the decimal point or the effect of the multiplier/divisor, often resulting in an answer that is 10 times smaller or larger than expected.

Misconception 1984: Adds when calculating volume instead of multiplying
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the formula for calculating volume by mistakenly using addition instead of multiplication, such as adding length and width to height instead of multiplying them together.

Misconception 1985: Has multiplied base by slant height and perpendicular height to find area of a triangle
Expanded misconception:
Has multiplied base by either the slant height or the perpendicular height instead of height, to find the area of a triangle, failing to identify or apply the correct relationship between base and perpendicular height in calculating the area.

Misconception 1986: When writing ratio from diagram, writes total as one side
Expanded misconception:
When writing a ratio from a diagram, fails to distinguish between the parts and the total, incorrectly writing the total as one side of the ratio instead of identifying and representing the specific parts.

Misconception 1987: When completing the square in the form (x - a)^2 + b, believes b = the original constant
Expanded misconception:
When completing the square in the form (x - a)^2 + b, believes the value of b is equal to the original constant in the quadratic equation, rather than the original constant adjusted for the value that was added to (x - a)^2 during the process of completing the square.

Misconception 1988: Rounds up instead of down
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply correct rounding rules,  consistently rounding up when the decision digit is exactly halfway between two numbers (i.e., 0.5 or .05, etc.) instead of using specific rounding rules as per conventional mathematics or the given instructions.

Misconception 1989: Believes a coordinate cannot be placed beyond the bounds of the grid shown
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that the coordinate plane extends infinitely in all directions and incorrectly assumes that point locations are limited to the visible grid, leading to incorrect plotting or identification of points outside the displayed boundaries.

Misconception 1990: Fails to reflect across mirror line
Expanded misconception:
Fails to take into account or apply the concept of line symmetry, failing to recognize that corresponding parts on either side of a mirror line (line of symmetry) are reflected images of each other, and incorrectly applying operations or transformations that do not respect this symmetrical property.

Misconception 1991: Assumes any variable in a sum on the numerator can be divided by the denominator
Expanded misconception:
Assumes that any term (including variables) in the numerator of a fraction can be cancelled or divided by the denominator, failing to recognize that factoring or canceling out terms in a fraction requires common factors or matching coefficients between the numerator and denominator.

Misconception 1992: Believes extreme values can affect the mode
Expanded misconception:
Believes extreme values can affect the mode, failing to understand that the mode is the value that occurs most frequently in a dataset, regardless of its numerical value, and that outliers or extreme values do not necessarily influence the mode.

Misconception 1993: Believes total in a grouped table is found by counting rows
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that quantities or aggregated values in a grouped table, such as sums or counts, are typically calculated by combining data from multiple rows within each group, rather than simply counting the number of rows present.

Misconception 1994: When completing the square in the form (x - a)^2 + b, believes b = -a^2 regardless of the original constant
Expanded misconception:
