Fails to recognize that the constant term b in the expression (x - a)^2 + b is not solely determined by a^2, but rather is the result of a^2 plus any original constant term from the quadratic equation being transformed, and incorrectly assumes b = -a^2 regardless of the original constant or algebraic manipulations involved in completing the square.

Misconception 1995: Mixes up shortest and longest when ordering times
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct ordering rules when comparing times (e.g., AM/PM, 12-hour/24-hour clocks), specifically mixing up the shortest time with the longest time or vice versa, often leading to incorrect application of time comparisons or sequences.

Misconception 1996: Only includes fully shaded squares when counting area
Expanded misconception:
Only includes fully shaded squares and/or partial squares if they can be easily combined to form a whole square when counting area, failing to accurately account for fractional parts of squares or area units.

Misconception 1997: Believes vertically opposite angles sum to 180 degrees
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly applies definition of supplementary angles to vertically opposite angles, believing vertically opposite angles always sum to 180 degrees, rather than always being equal.

Misconception 1998: When finding which term represents the median, thinks you divide the total number of terms by 2
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly handle cases where the total number of terms is odd or even when finding the middle value(s), incorrectly dividing the total number of terms by 2 and/or not considering whether to take the middle value or the average of two middle values when calculating the median.

Misconception 1999: Does not interpret the correct order of operations from a worded problem
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the order of operations correctly, often resulting in calculation errors due to incorrect ordering or prioritization of mathematical operations when transitioning from a worded problem to a mathematical expression.

Misconception 2000: Believes multiplying by 0.1 is the same as multiplying by 10
Expanded misconception:
Believes multiplying or dividing by a decimal is the same as or equivalent to multiplying or dividing by its whole number equivalent without considering the decimal place shift.

Misconception 2001: When solving an equation,  subtracts instead of multiplying
Expanded misconception:
When solving an equation, incorrectly substitutes the operation 'addition' for 'multiplication' or 'subtraction' for 'multiplication', often due to procedural errors or careless mistakes in executing mathematical operations.

Misconception 2002: When dividing a decimal by an integer, divides by the wrong power of 10 when reinserting the decimal.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly reinsert the decimal point when dividing a decimal number by an integer, leading to errors in place value and potentially resulting in answers that are off by a power of 10 or its multiples.

Misconception 2003: Believes multiply all numbers in a prime factor venn diagram gives the value of one sets number
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider set relationships and instead assumes multiplying all numbers or values in a Venn diagram's prime factor representations yields the cardinality of one set or the entire set system.

Misconception 2004: Confuses frequency polygons with cumulative frequency diagrams
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the graphical representation of discrete data distributions for summary and comparison purposes (frequency polygons) with graphical representation used for displaying accumulation of frequencies (cumulative frequency diagrams), often leading to incorrect interpretation or calculation of cumulative frequencies, exceedance probabilities, or percentiles.

Misconception 2005: Multiplies all given dimensions when calculating the length of the hypotenuse in a right angled triangle
Expanded misconception:
Applies the formula for area or volume of a shape to a problem involving the Pythagorean theorem, multiplying given dimensions instead of using the correct formula to calculate the length of the hypotenuse in a right-angled triangle.

Misconception 2006: Believes the mean is total divided by sum of categories given
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between the number of values in each category (frequency) and the number of categories when calculating the mean, incorrectly assuming that the total value should be divided by the number of categories rather than the total number of values or data points.

Misconception 2007: Believes the number of vertices on a 3D shape match those of the 2D cross section
Expanded misconception:
Believes that the properties of a 3D shape, such as the number of vertices, edges, or faces, directly correspond to or match those of its 2D cross-sections or net representations, without considering the actual geometric transformations or structure of the shape.

Misconception 2008: When substiting into an algebraic term, raises the coefficient to the power of the variable rather than multiplying the coefficient and the variable..
Expanded misconception:
When substituting into an algebraic term, incorrectly handles the interaction between coefficients and variables, such as raising the coefficient to the power of the variable instead of multiplying the coefficient by the variables or misinterpreting the operation between them.

Misconception 2009: Assumes they add all values in a worded problem, thereby not interpreting the problem correctly
Expanded misconception:
Fails to accurately interpret the worded problem, leading to an incorrect operation or inclusion of irrelevant values, often resulting in adding all numerical values present rather than understanding the context and required mathematical operations.

Misconception 2010: Does not understand power notation
Expanded misconception:
Does not correctly interpret the meaning and scope of exponentiation and power notation, frequently displaying difficulties in differentiating between numerical bases, exponents, and coefficients, thereby resulting in miscalculations or misinterpretations in algebraic or arithmetic expressions.

Misconception 2011: When simplifying surds, leaves the square root of the biggest square number under the root and puts the other number on the outside
Expanded misconception:
When simplifying surds, leaves the square root of the largest square number factor under the radical sign, and the remaining non-simplified expression as a coefficient outside, revealing a lack of understanding of prime factorization and proper radical simplification.

Misconception 2012: When reading a dash on a number line does not take into account the number at the start or the width of each division
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly interpret number lines, specifically neglecting to account for the starting point, scale, or width of each division when determining the position or value represented by a dash or other mark.

Misconception 2013: When drawing a histogram, thinks you plot cumulative frequency on the horizontal axis
Expanded misconception:
"When drawing a histogram, incorrectly uses the horizontal axis to represent cumulative frequency instead of the individual categories or classes, often confusing the axis roles or misunderstanding the concept of histograms and frequency distribution."

Misconception 2014: Believes if a point is reflected in the line x=a then the x coordinate of the point will become 'a'
Expanded misconception:
Believes incorrectly applies the concept of reflection in a coordinate plane, specifically in relation to a vertical line x=a, thinking that the x-coordinate of the reflected point will always become 'a', rather than being replaced by its mirror image, with the x-coordinate becoming '2a - x' (where x is the original x-coordinate), while the y-coordinate remains unchanged.

Misconception 2015: Forgot to square both parts of the fraction
Expanded misconception:
Forgot to square both the numerator and the denominator when squaring a fraction, or incorrectly applied exponent rules when dealing with fractions or rational expressions.

Misconception 2016: Believes the nth term of a descending sequence will have a positive coefficient of n
Expanded misconception:
"Applies naive expectations about the behavior of variables in a sequence, specifically assuming that an nth term in a descending sequence will always be directly proportional to n with a positive coefficient, failing to consider potential negative coefficients or other mathematical relationships that can indicate decreasing quantities."

Misconception 2017: Mixes up square rooting and multiplying by 2 or doubling
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between operations involving exponents and multiplication, specifically, confuses square rooting with multiplying or dividing by 2, doubling, or halving, and applies these operations incorrectly.

Misconception 2018: Thinks a divide and a negative sign next to each other makes a minus
Expanded misconception:
Misleads two consecutive operations - one involving division using a slash or a horizontal or vertical line and the other involving subtraction (represented by a negative sign) - by either reordering, omitting, or incorrectly combining those operations.

Misconception 2019: When there's a negative sign in the question, thinks the answer must be negative
Expanded misconception:
When there is a negative sign present in an equation or expression, the student mistakenly applies it globally, incorrectly assuming that it affects the overall sign of the solution, rather than only the specific values or components it is associated with.

Misconception 2020: When simplifying surds, does not use the largest square number to simplify
Expanded misconception:
When simplifying surds, fails to identify or factor out the largest perfect square number from the radicand to simplify the expression, resulting in an unnecessarily complex or incorrect simplified form.

Misconception 2021: Does not recognise when a fraction can be simplified
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognise when a fraction can be simplified, and instead treats it as an equivalent complex fraction or performs calculations that could be simplified, resulting in incorrect or overly complicated solutions.

Misconception 2022: 
Has incorrect belief about the meaning of "expression"
Expanded misconception:
Has a limited or incorrect understanding of the concept of expressions, potentially leading to difficulties in evaluating or manipulating algebraic expressions, incorrectly simplifying or combining like terms, or not recognizing that expressions can represent unknown quantities or variable relationships.

Misconception 2023: Thinks when finding a percentage you divide by the percentage being asked
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that when finding a percentage, the percentage being asked should be the divisor instead of being converted to a decimal or used as a multiplier.

Misconception 2024: Confuses the variable and frequency columns in a frequency table
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly distinguish between the variable and frequency columns in a frequency table, incorrectly interpreting data such as treating frequency values as variable values or vice versa.

Misconception 2025: Does not realise a triangular prism is a 3D shape
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to recognise that some specific geometric shapes, including but not limited to triangular prisms, can have different dimensions and are indeed three-dimensional, instead considering them to be 2D or not distinguishing between flat shapes and objects with height or depth."

Misconception 2026: Reflects instead of rotates
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply appropriate transformation properties, reflecting instead of rotating, and vice versa, when solving geometry or spatial problems, leading to incorrect orientation or configuration of shapes.

Misconception 2027: Believes that an + b is still a multiple of a
Expanded misconception:
Adds b to a without considering the effect of b on the multiplicative relationship between a and the result, assuming the product of a and a certain number is preserved even when additional terms are included.

Misconception 2028: When writing a number between zero and one in standard form, thinks the power is the negative of the number of decimal places
Expanded misconception:
When writing a number between zero and one in standard form, incorrectly assumes the power of 10 is the negative of the number of decimal places immediately after the decimal point without necessarily considering the placement of the decimal point or the digits before or after it.

Misconception 2029: Believes non-unit multiples of vectors can be the same length
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that scalar multiplication of a vector changes its length, mistakenly believing that multiplying a vector by a non-unit multiple preserves its original magnitude.

Misconception 2030: Believes that fractions with larger denominators are greater in size
Expanded misconception:
Believes that fractions with larger denominators are greater in size, and may prioritize the magnitude of the denominator over other relevant factors, such as the numerator, possibly due to a partial understanding of the concept of equivalent ratios or a misunderstanding of how the size of the denominator affects the value of a fraction.

Misconception 2031: Misremembers the exact values of trigonometric functions at standard angles
Expanded misconception:
Misremembers or inaccurately recalls the exact values of trigonometric functions at standard angles (0°, 30°, 45°, 60°, 90°) and their corresponding radian measures, leading to errors in calculation and problem-solving.

Misconception 2032: Believes the gradient of a distance-time graph is the acceleration
Expanded misconception:
"Believes the gradient of a distance-time graph is the acceleration, rather than recognizing it as a measure of velocity; and confuses the concept with the gradient of a velocity-time graph, which actually represents acceleration."

Misconception 2033: Rounds to the wrong degree of accuracy (1 decimal place rather than nearest whole number)
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct degree of precision or rounding when performing calculations, such as rounding to the wrong number of significant figures, decimal places, or not rounding to the nearest whole number when necessary.

Misconception 2034: Finds an equivalent improper fraction when asked for a mixed number
Expanded misconception:
Finds an equivalent improper fraction or fails to separate the whole number part when asked for a mixed number, possibly resulting in an incorrect or incomplete mixed number calculation.

Misconception 2035: When reading integers on a number line, assumes each dash is 10
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the concept of scaled number lines, assuming each unit mark (dash) represents the same consistent interval, often incorrectly defaulting to increments of 1, 10, or other powers of 10, and neglecting to consider the actual scale provided.

Misconception 2036: Identified the interior not exterior angle at a point
Expanded misconception:
Identified the interior rather than the exterior angle at a point; specifically, confused the angle to be measured at a point on a line intersecting or forming an angle, fails to consider the relationship between exterior and interior angles, and does not account for how angles at a point are defined in geometric figures.

Misconception 2037: Believes switching the gradient and y-intercept gives a parallel line
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that changing the slope (gradient) of a linear equation, rather than the y-intercept, is necessary to create a parallel line, as parallel lines have the same slope but different y-intercepts.

Misconception 2038: Believes a common denominator can only be the lowest common multiple
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that the least common multiple (LCM) or lowest common denominator is just one of many common denominators, and incorrectly believes that the LCM is the only valid or acceptable common denominator for adding or subtracting fractions.

Misconception 2039: Thinks you need to just add a % sign to a number to make it a percentage
Expanded misconception:
Fails to convert a number, ratio, or fraction to its corresponding percentage by not applying the necessary mathematical operations (e.g., multiplying by 100 or dividing by a given value), instead incorrectly assuming that simply adding a percentage sign (%) to the number is sufficient.

Misconception 2040: Has estimated the length of the missing side length by looking at the image  rather than calculating it
Expanded misconception:
Tends to rely on visual estimation or spatial reasoning when solving mathematical problems involving geometric figures, rather than applying mathematical concepts or formulas to calculate precise measurements or values.

Misconception 2041: Fails to reflect across reflection line without a diagram
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to understand or apply line symmetry properties, neglecting to reflect a shape or point across a given line of symmetry, often requiring visual or diagrammatic support to recognize the concept."

Misconception 2042: Can not square root a negative number
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or apply the concept of imaginary numbers, and/or incorrectly assumes that mathematical operations involving square roots of negative numbers are not valid or result in an error.

Misconception 2043: Selects y>a solutions instead of y<a solutions for a quadratic inequality
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the direction of the inequality when solving quadratic inequalities, leading to selectively choosing solutions where y is greater than a constant or expression, when in fact the correct solutions should satisfy y being less than a constant or expression.

Misconception 2044: Does not know the units column is before the decimal point
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the concept of place value, specifically that the units column is the first column to the left of the decimal point and place value order increases as you move from right to left.

Misconception 2045: Writes a variable to the power of n as nx
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between the concept of exponentiation and multiplication, incorrectly rewriting a variable raised to a power 'n' as the product of 'n' instances of the variable, resulting in 'nx' instead of 'x^n'.

Misconception 2046: When collecting like terms, treats subtractions as if they are additions
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for the negative sign when combining like terms, resulting in incorrect handling of subtractions as if they are equivalent to additions, and incorrect calculation of the resulting expression.

Misconception 2047: Believes infinite gradient is not possible in real life. 
Expanded misconception:
Believes an infinite or undefined quantity (e.g., infinite slope or undefined gradient) is not possible or does not have real-life implications, or cannot be graphically represented in mathematical equations or functions, despite various real-world scenarios that exhibit such properties.

Misconception 2048: Has not noticed the difference between 'subtract' and 'subtract from'
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to understand the directional implications of subtraction, often treating 'subtract a from b' and 'subtract b from a' as equivalent, leading to incorrect reversal of operands or incorrect handling of subtraction order."

Misconception 2049: Believes a gap of 2 is a gap of 1 on a numberline
Expanded misconception:
Fails to accurately represent or calculate unit increments on a number line, consistently under or overestimating the distance between numbers, specifically interpreting a gap of 2 or more as a gap of 1, leading to incorrect placement and calculation of numbers.

Misconception 2050: Does not recognise a unit of length
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish or incorrectly applies units of measurement, particularly those of length, which may include misunderstandings of prefixes (e.g., centi-, milli-, kilo-), unit conversions, or selecting an answer without regard for the implied unit of measurement.

Misconception 2051: Does not correctly apply the commutative property of multiplication
Expanded misconception:
Does not correctly apply the commutative property of multiplication, such as switching the order of factors in multiplication, e.g., incorrectly considering a*b to be different from b*a, or applying this incorrect principle to calculation errors.

Misconception 2052: Believes all numbers written in standard form must be smaller than any numbers written in ordinary form
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the relationship between standard and ordinary form, believing that standard form numbers are always smaller, and incorrectly applies this rule when comparing numbers or performing operations, such as in multiplying or dividing by very large or very small numbers, or when solving equations involving different number forms, especially in the context of exponents, scientific notation, or significant figures.

Misconception 2053: When reading integers on a number line, assumes each dash is 1
Expanded misconception:
When reading integers on a number line, assumes standard or implied uniform intervals or unit lengths between dashes regardless of the context, and neglects the scale or labels of the number line.

Misconception 2054: Believes the number of jumps between two numbers on a numberline represents the sum of those 2 numbers 
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets the representation of number relationships on a number line, mistakenly believing that the distance or number of jumps between two numbers represents the sum or combined quantity of those two numbers, rather than the actual difference or interval between them.

Misconception 2055: When multiplying a decimal by an integer, ignores decimal point and just multiplies the digits
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply or account for the decimal point when performing operations involving decimals, including multiplication by integers, and treats the decimal as if it were a whole number.

Misconception 2056: When calculating surface area, only includes visible faces
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for all relevant faces or features when calculating surface area or perimeter, often only considering those that are immediately visible or visible from a single perspective, and neglecting hidden or obscured faces, edges, or sides.

Misconception 2057: Thinks that the scale on axes has to match with the number of squares
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that the scale on axes has to match with the number of squares or grid lines, and fails to differentiate between the scale and the number of divisions on a graph, leading to incorrect calculations or representations in graphical problems.

Misconception 2058: Assumes there are only 2 terms in the nth term formula for a quadratic sequence
Expanded misconception:
Assumes there are only 2 terms (linear relationship) in the nth term formula for a quadratic sequence, neglecting the squared or quadratic term, and therefore fails to account for the increasing difference between consecutive terms.

Misconception 2059: Ignores unlabelled angles when applying angle sum rules
Expanded misconception:
Ignores unlabelled angles when applying angle sum rules such as interior angles of polygons or other angle sum properties (e.g. opposite angles of a cyclic quadrilateral, vertically opposite angles) to the problem.

Misconception 2060: When solving simultaneous equations graphically, they think the solutions are the x or y intercepts
Expanded misconception:
When solving simultaneous equations graphically, they incorrectly believe that the solutions are always the x or y intercepts of the individual lines, rather than the point of intersection between the two lines.

Misconception 2061: Believes that when converting ratio to fractions, the denominator is the difference between the parts of the ratio
Expanded misconception:
Believes that when converting a ratio to a fraction, the numerator and/or denominator are determined by incorrect assumptions, such as using the difference between the parts as the denominator, or using the larger part as the numerator and the smaller part as the denominator, without considering other factors that may be involved in the proportion, and/or without ensuring that the resulting fraction correctly represents the relationship between the parts in the ratio.

Misconception 2062: Believes you multiply all of the values given when using the capture-recapture sampling method
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the appropriate calculations in the capture-recapture sampling method, often mistakenly multiplying all given values or not correctly accounting for the overlap between the two samples, resulting in an incorrect estimation of the population size.

Misconception 2063: When calculating volume expressed in cubes, counts the faces of all the cubes
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the concept of volume correctly by counting individual components, such as faces of cubes, edges, or sides, rather than understanding that volume in cubic units is a measure of the total number of unit cubes that fit inside a 3D object.

Misconception 2064: Finds the range of possible values of the variable, rather than the range of recorded values
Expanded misconception:
Finds the range of possible or theoretical values of the variable, based on mathematical or contextual constraints, rather than the range of actual or recorded values present in the given dataset or context.

Misconception 2065: Believes when calculating a probability the denominator will be the total of one of the criteria rather than the overall total that is required (gives a given that answer)
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly identify the sample space, incorrectly taking the denominator in probability calculations as a subset or individual category total, rather than the overall total or sample space, thus leading to a distorted probability ratio.

Misconception 2066: Does not know multiplying by a unit fraction is equivalent to: dividing by its denominator value
Expanded misconception:
Does not know multiplying by a unit fraction is equivalent to dividing by its denominator value, thus not effectively scaling down the original quantity, or fails to recognize unit fractions as factors that affect proportion, leading to incorrect proportional reasoning and calculations.

Misconception 2067: Thinks there are 10000ml in a litre
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands the conversion between milliliters and liters, specifically believing that 1 liter is equal to 10,000 milliliters rather than 1,000 milliliters.

Expanded misconception:
Does not know how to factorise quadratic expressions using the 'product-sum method' or factorising by splitting the x-term coefficient into two numbers that meet the condition to add to give the coefficient of the x term and multiply to give the constant term (non-variable term).

Misconception 2069: Believes if there are two numbers with the same frequency, there isn't a mode
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that a dataset can have multiple modes if two or more values have the same highest frequency.

Misconception 2070: Believes that when factorising a quadratic equation with a coefficient of x squared, the two constants in the brackets are still the solutions of the equation with opposite signs
Expanded misconception:
Ignores the coefficient of x squared when factorising a quadratic equation into linear factors or finding the roots, and incorrectly assumes that the two constants in the factorised form are the solutions to the original equation with opposite signs.

Misconception 2071: Shares equally instead of in given ratio
Expanded misconception:
Ignores the given ratio and divides the quantity or proportion by the total number of components instead, effectively sharing it equally among them, rather than respecting the specified ratio or proportion.

Misconception 2072: Confuses formulae for area of triangle and area of rectangle
Expanded misconception:
Confuses formulae for area of triangle (A = 0.5 * b * h) and area of rectangle (A = l * w), mistakenly applying properties of one shape to another when solving area-related problems.

Misconception 2073: Converts a fraction to a decimal by placing all values of the fraction after the decimal point
Expanded misconception:
Converts a fraction to a decimal incorrectly by either placing the numerator after the decimal point without considering the denominator or placing both the numerator and denominator after the decimal point without performing division.

Misconception 2074: When describing the relationship between the parts in a ratio, replaces the : with a decimal point to say how many times bigger a part is than the other
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between the colon in a ratio and a division operation, often replacing the colon with a decimal point or division symbol, and misinterpreting the resulting value as a factor or multiplier describing the relationship between the parts.

Misconception 2075: Misunderstands mixed number notation 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to accurately interpret or apply the values within mixed numbers, incorrectly handling the relationship between the whole number part and the fractional part, possibly treating them as separate entities, adding or multiplying them incorrectly, or misunderstanding the equivalence between mixed numbers and improper fractions.

Misconception 2076: Believes an algebraic term with the same coefficient as the value of a constant term can be subtracted to give zero
Expanded misconception:
Lacks understanding of equivalence in algebraic expressions; specifically, mistakenly believes that algebraic terms with the same numerical coefficient as a constant term can be combined or eliminated by simple arithmetic operations to give zero, failing to consider variables and like terms in the equation.

Misconception 2077: Believes that when eliminating a variable, if the signs of the terms with matching coefficients are different, we subtract the equations.
Expanded misconception:
Believes that when eliminating a variable in a system of linear equations, if the signs of the terms with matching coefficients are different, we always subtract the equations, without checking if addition or subtraction would lead to the cancellation or creation of like terms with nonzero coefficients.

Misconception 2078: Simplifies a fraction by adding or subtracting the same amount from the numerator  and denominator

Misconception 2079: Believes that points on an axis have equal x and y coordinates
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or apply the concept of independent x and y coordinates of a point in a Cartesian coordinate system, often mistakenly assuming that the coordinates have a specific relationship or that points on an axis have equal x and y coordinates, or defaulting to a simplified or symmetric assumption where the values would be the same.

Misconception 2080: Confuses the y-intercept with the turning point of a curve
Expanded misconception:

Misconception 2081: Thinks there are 7 hours in 1 day

Misconception 2088: When multiplying variables, thinks they add coefficients as well as powers
Expanded misconception:

Misconception 2089: When asked to find a scale factor, assumes direction of enlargement does not matter

Misconception 2090: Finds the area of the whole grid instead of the shaded shape

Misconception 2094: Confuses all possible outcomes with options available

Misconception 2095: When reading graph, chooses nearest marked value on axis instead of reading accurately
Expanded misconception:
When reading a graph, consistently chooses the nearest marked value on an axis instead of taking the time to accurately read off the value from the points on the graph, which can be between two marked values, by estimating or interpolating, resulting in inaccurate or imprecise data interpretation and incorrect solution.

Misconception 2096: When adding negatives believes that they can just add their absolute values and add a negative sign to the answer
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the effect of adding or subtracting negative numbers, resulting in addition of absolute values followed by addition of a negative sign, rather than applying the correct rules for operation with negative numbers.

Misconception 2097: Confuses prime factors and factors
Expanded misconception:
Confuses prime factors and factors, often misunderstanding that prime factors are the prime numbers that multiply together to give the original number, while factors are all the numbers that divide the original number without leaving a remainder, leading to incorrect applications in simplification, multiplication, or division of expressions.

Misconception 2098: Multiplies all terms together when simplifying an expression
Expanded misconception:

Misconception 2099: Believes negative powers in standard form have the same impact as positive powers

Misconception 2100: Divides rather than multiplies when answering worded problems
Expanded misconception:

Misconception 2101: Believes we move down a number line when we add
Expanded misconception:
Believes that math operations such as addition and subtraction only involve moving in one direction along the number line, failing to recognize that subtraction can represent movement in the opposite direction, or that specific operations might actually represent a change from the original position in either a positive or negative direction, rather than just a one-way movement.

Misconception 2102: Believes adjacent sides of a rhombus are always perpendicular

Misconception 2106: When asked to find an angle given in three letter convention, thinks the angle you want to find will be located at the vertex labelled with the last letter

Misconception 2107: Student thinks that negative decimals behave the same as positive decimals when positioning a decimal between two consecutive negative integers, placing the decimal with a greater absolute value to the right.

Misconception 2109: Counts on in the wrong direction on a number line

Misconception 2110: Identifies a common multiple of two numbers when asked to consider a common multiple of three numbers

Misconception 2112: Does not understand how to find percentages of an amount

Misconception 2115: Believes any of the angles can be used in the trigonometric formula for area of a triangle

Misconception 2116: Does not round up when an answer is non-sensical in a real-life context

Misconception 2126: Miscounts when subtracting over zero

Misconception 2127: Thinks the gradient of a line includes the variable x

Misconception 2129: Increases by the given percentage rather than finding the percentage of an amount

Misconception 2135: Thinks positive or negative sign affects the term before it

Misconception 2142: Does not know that to factorise a quadratic expression, to find two numbers that add to give the coefficient of the x term, and multiply to give the non variable term

Expanded misconception:
Does not know that to factorise a quadratic expression, the necessary steps involve finding two numbers that add to give the coefficient of the x term and multiply to give the constant term (non-variable term), correctly applying the sign of these numbers from the constant term, and effectively using these numbers to construct the factored form.

Misconception 2143: Adds instead of multiplying when expanding bracket
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to apply the order of operations when expanding brackets, specifically treating the multiplication operation within the brackets as an addition operation, leading to incorrect partial products."

Misconception 2144: Believes a number written in standard form cannot have a lot of decimal places
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that a number in standard form (e.g. 3.456) can have any finite number of decimal places; may incorrectly assume standard form is limited to a certain number of decimal places.

Misconception 2145: Believes an upward slope on a distance-time graph means travelling back towards the starting point.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the concept of velocity as represented on a distance-time graph, incorrectly interpreting an upward slope as movement in the opposite direction towards the starting point, rather than as an increase in distance from the starting point, regardless of the direction of travel.

Misconception 2146: Believes the line y = mx - c will cross the y axis at c
Expanded misconception:
Believes that in the linear equation y = mx - c or y = mx + c, the value of c directly represents the y-intercept without considering the sign, leading to incorrect identification of the point at which the line crosses the y-axis.

Misconception 2147: Believes they should multiply each numerator by its original denominator when adding/subtracting fractions
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the concept of a common denominator and instead, incorrectly multiplies each numerator by its original denominator when adding or subtracting fractions, likely due to a misunderstanding of the relationship between equivalent ratios.

Misconception 2148: When simplifying, thinks they write 0 in front of a negative number in the final answer, instead of just writing the negative number
Expanded misconception:
When simplifying or solving mathematical expressions, consistently and incorrectly incorporates a leading zero in the final numerical answer for negative values, often as a carryover from calculations involving place value or placeholders in arithmetic operations, rather than correctly writing just the negative sign and the numerical value.

Misconception 2149: Converted seconds to minutes instead of seconds to hours
Expanded misconception:
Failed to convert units of time properly, specifically converting seconds directly to minutes when the context or question clearly requires conversion from seconds to hours.

Misconception 2150: Does not know the 6 timestable
Expanded misconception:
Demonstrates a lack of recall or inconsistent application of the 6 times table, including difficulty with mental calculation or fact retrieval of multiplication and division facts within this table.

Misconception 2151: Thinks you cannot find the range if there are an even number of values
Expanded misconception:
Thinks you cannot find the range or has incorrect understanding of calculating the range when there is an even number of values, such as finding the middle values instead of the maximum and minimum values.

Misconception 2152: Believes every number is the mode when you have data with no mode
Expanded misconception:
Fails to differentiate between data with no mode and data with multiple modes, often mistakenly assuming that every value or a specific value in the dataset is the mode due to the absence of a clearly defined mode or modal value.

Misconception 2153: Thinks the category with the most common bar height is the mode
Expanded misconception:
Thinks the category with the most common or highest frequency bar height in a histogram or bar chart is the mode, rather than identifying the mode as the value that appears most frequently in the dataset, possibly due to confusing frequency distributions in graphical representations with the calculation of the mode in raw data.

Misconception 2154: Thinks finding a fraction of an amount means subtracting from that amount
Expanded misconception:
Thinks finding a fraction of an amount (e.g., 1/2 of a number) means subtracting or dividing the fraction's denominator from the amount instead of multiplying the amount by the fraction, or confuses the process of finding a fraction with subtraction or division in contexts where fractions are involved.

Misconception 2155: Reflects in an axis, instead of the given mirror line
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the specific mirror line or reflection axis and instead reflects the figure across a standard axis (e.g. x-axis, y-axis, or origin) or reflects across an axis other than the given mirror line.

Misconception 2156: Thinks alternate angles are always equal
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that alternate angles are only equal when the lines that form the angles are parallel and transversal, often incorrectly applying properties of parallel lines and transversals to non-parallel situations.

Misconception 2157: Believes that when eliminating a variable, if the signs of the terms with matching coefficients are the same, we do the operation matching the sign (+ if positive, - if negative)
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly applies the sign rule when adding or subtracting unlike terms in algebraic expressions by always adding like terms when their coefficients have the same sign and similarly incorrectly subtracting like terms when their coefficients have the same sign, failing to recognize that the operation (addition or subtraction) between like terms depends solely on the operation symbol between them, regardless of the sign of their coefficients.

Misconception 2158: Multiplies rather than divides when answering worded problems
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly follow operation instructions in worded problems, specifically incorrectly performing multiplication operations when division or a different arithmetic operation is required.

Misconception 2159: Believes the fx column for calculating mean is value/frequency
Expanded misconception:
Believes the function column for calculating mean from a grouped frequency distribution is the product of value and frequency, instead of the correct approach where each value is multiplied by its frequency before summing these products and then dividing by the total frequency.

Misconception 2160: In repeated percentage change, believes the second change is only a percentage of the first change, without including the original
Expanded misconception:
In repeated percentage change problems, believes the second change is only a percentage of the first change, rather than a percentage of the resulting value obtained after applying the first change, without taking into account the compounding effect on the original value.

Misconception 2161: Confuses octagon and hexagon
Expanded misconception:
Has difficulty distinguishing between polygons with a similar number of sides, specifically confusing octagons (8-sided shapes) with hexagons (6-sided shapes) due to incomplete understanding of their definitions, properties, or spatial visualization.

Misconception 2162: When rounding to decimal places and the only digits after the decimal point will be zeros, just gives the integer as the answer.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to acknowledge that when rounding to a specific number of decimal places, zeros should still be included if the digits after the decimal point fall within the given decimal place criteria, even if they result in zeros being added to the rounded answer.

Misconception 2163: Attempts to simplify ratio by subtracting from both sides
Expanded misconception:
Attempts to simplify ratios or proportions by incorrectly manipulating the equation, such as by subtracting the same value from both sides, rather than applying equivalent operations, like multiplying or dividing by the same non-zero value, resulting in an incorrect simplification or an altered relationship between the quantities being compared.

Misconception 2164: Believes a number written in standard form is smaller than another number written in standard form if the number between 1 and 10 is smaller
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the exponent when comparing numbers written in standard form, mistakenly believing that the number with the smaller coefficient (or the number between 1 and 10) is smaller, regardless of the relative sizes of the exponents.

Misconception 2165: Thinks that we cannot calculate the range of negative numbers
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to recognize the absolute value or magnitude of negative numbers, leading to incorrect comparisons and calculations, particularly when determining the range of a set that includes negative values."

Misconception 2166: Adds up the variable column when asked for total frequency
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply appropriate statistical or mathematical operations when calculating totals or frequencies, specifically adding up values in a category or variable column, rather than summing frequencies or counts, leading to incorrect total or frequency calculations.

Misconception 2167: When a subtraction of one positive number from another, results in a negative answer, they believe the answer is the sum of those 2 numbers with a negative sign put in front.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the difference between additive inverse and changes in magnitude when subtracting one positive number from another, instead treating the result as a negated sum, resulting in an incorrect sign or magnitude.

Misconception 2168: Assumes a 1:1 conversion ratio between different currencies
Expanded misconception:
Assumes a 1:1 conversion ratio between different currencies or units, failing to account for conversion rates or proportional relationships in calculation or decision-making.

Misconception 2169: Believes that prime numbers can have more than two factors
Expanded misconception:
Believes that prime numbers do not strictly have exactly two distinct factors, resulting in incorrect factorization or simplification of expressions, and an inability to identify prime numbers as those divisible solely by 1 and themselves.

Misconception 2170: Subtracts numerators when dividing fractions with a common denominator
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the division rule for fractions with the same denominator correctly, specifically subtracting or incorrectly manipulating the numerators rather than inverting the second fraction and multiplying.

Misconception 2171: Uses the total as the numerator of the fraction rather than the denominator.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly identify the denominator when dealing with fractions, often mistakenly using the total or incorrect value as the denominator, resulting in incorrect calculations or equivalencies.

Misconception 2172: Subtracts instead of adding when asked to find a total
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the operation required by the question context, specifically mistakenly applying subtraction when the task requires addition to find a total, resulting from a misunderstanding of arithmetic operation indicators or an inability to interpret question requirements accurately.

Misconception 2173: Does not realise that division can be used to find the missing value in a multiplication problem
Expanded misconception:
Does not apply division to find the missing value when given a multiplication relationship, may only consider division as the inverse of division operations with clear division notation.

Misconception 2174: Believes a parallelogram cannot have right angles
Expanded misconception:
Believes that some specific properties (e.g. right angles, obtuse angles, equal side lengths) are mutually exclusive with the definition of a particular quadrilateral (e.g. parallelogram, rectangle, square), leading to incorrect assumptions about the possible characteristics of that quadrilateral.

Misconception 2175: Mixes up squaring and multiplying by 2 or doubling, and not square rooted
Expanded misconception:
Fails to accurately distinguish between exponential operations, specifically confusing squaring (e.g., a^2 or x^2) and multiplying by 2 (or doubling), often also neglecting to apply square roots when necessary.

Misconception 2176: Confuses objective and subjective
Expanded misconception:
Confuses objective and subjective - incorrectly applies personal opinions or perspectives to problems requiring factual, formula-based, or evidence-supported solutions.

Misconception 2177: Does not know that a reflection would not change the size of the shape
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that certain geometric transformations, such as reflections, rotations, and translations, preserve the size and shape properties of an original object, and instead incorrectly assumes that these transformations alter the object's dimensions.

Misconception 2178: Believes mixed numbers cannot be simplified
Expanded misconception:
Fails to identify or perform necessary simplifications of fractions or mixed numbers in calculations, such as removing common factors, resulting in overly complex or incorrect answers.

Misconception 2179: Assumes that an arrow on a number line starts at zero
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the actual starting value when interpreting arithmetic operations on a number line, assuming that an arrow always begins at zero unless explicitly indicated otherwise.

Misconception 2180: Divides instead of multiplies when calculating total frequency
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to recognize the order of operations or does not correctly account for the difference between addition and multiplication in calculating total frequency, specifically divides instead of multiplies when given the option, or incorrectly converts a multiplication operation into a division operation."

Misconception 2181: Performs addition ahead of subtraction
Expanded misconception:
Fails to follow the order of operations, performing addition and/or subtraction before other operations such as multiplication and division when the format calls for operations to be performed from top to bottom and left to right within the same level of the order of operations.

Misconception 2182: Believes given two sides and one angle of a triangle there are multiple ways to draw the triangle
Expanded misconception:
Believes that given two sides and one angle of a triangle (SSA), the remaining side and angle measurements are uniquely or ambiguously determined by the given angle, leading to incorrect assumption that there are multiple possible triangles or a lack of unique solutions.

Misconception 2183: Confuses consecutive and prime numbers
Expanded misconception:
Confuses properties and characteristics of consecutive numbers (integers that follow one another in order) and prime numbers (whole numbers greater than 1 with exactly two distinct factors: 1 and the number itself).

Misconception 2184: Believes the equation of a straight line is y=-mx+c
Expanded misconception:
Applies incorrect slope-intercept form by mistakenly reversing the sign of the slope term, often resulting in the equation y = -mx + c instead of y = mx + c.

Misconception 2185: Believes negative numbers with a larger absolute value are greater
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the concept of negative numbers and their placement on the number line, incorrectly assuming that a negative number with a larger absolute value is greater than a negative number with a smaller absolute value.

Misconception 2186: Thinks the missing length has to be half the length of the opposite side
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply or misinterprets the Pythagorean theorem and/or related geometric principles when finding missing lengths in triangles, specifically thinking the missing length must have a direct proportional relationship with the other side(s), such as being half or twice the length of another side.

Misconception 2187: When multiplying a decimal less than 1 by an integer, gives an answer 100 times smaller than it should be
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly account for the inverse relationship between decimal values less than 1 and multiplication results, often resulting in answers that are significantly smaller than the actual result due to incorrectly shifting the decimal point multiple places to the left.

Misconception 2188: Does not understand that enlargements can make shapes make shapes bigger, smaller and the same size
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand that enlargements can make shapes bigger (increase in size), smaller (decrease in size), or the same size (no change in size), and/or fails to recognize that these changes occur uniformly in all parts of the shape, maintaining the properties of the original shape.

Misconception 2189: Believes isosceles triangles cannot have right angles
Expanded misconception:
Believes isosceles triangles cannot have right angles, or that a triangle cannot be both isosceles and right-angled, indicating a lack of understanding of the properties of triangle classifications.

Misconception 2190: Denominator used for other parts rather than total parts
Expanded misconception:
Denominator used for other parts rather than total parts; incorrectly associates denominator with the numerator of an individual part, rather than correctly identifying the denominator as the total number of parts in proportion or percentage calculations.

Misconception 2191: When writing a number as a percentage of another number gets them the wrong way around
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the concept of percentages as a proportion or ratio, specifically confused about identifying which value represents the base and which value represents the part when writing a number as a percentage of another number.

Misconception 2192: Does not know you can find the mean from a pictogram
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that various types of graphs, including pictograms, charts, and diagrams, can be used to calculate or interpret statistics such as the mean, mode, range, and median, or inaccurately assumes that only numerical data presented in tables or lists can be used for statistical calculations.

Misconception 2193: Confuses tangent and circumference
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between the tangent of an angle in a right triangle (which involves the ratio of the opposite side length to the adjacent side length) and the concept of the circumference of a circle (the distance around the circle), often leading to incorrect calculations or substitutions of formulas related to these concepts.

Misconception 2194: Assumes the scale is the same when comparing two similar diagrams
Expanded misconception:
The student assumes that two or more similar diagrams have the same scale or proportions, failing to consider or verify if the scales are consistent when making comparisons or calculations.

Misconception 2195: Does not know the definition of a prime number
Expanded misconception:
Does not know the definition of a prime number or misunderstands it as requiring only that the number is odd, or greater than 1, or a specific pattern of factors, rather than the correct requirement that it be a positive integer greater than 1 that has exactly two distinct positive divisors: 1 and itself.

Misconception 2196: Believes the number of bars gives the total on a bar chart
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the scale on a bar chart and assumes the number of bars, or the end point of a bar, directly represents the total or absolute quantity being measured.

Misconception 2197: Thinks you multiply parallel sides to find the area of a trapezium
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that to find the area of a trapezium, you multiply the lengths of the two parallel sides instead of taking the average of the parallel sides and multiplying it by the height.

Misconception 2198: Finds the mode instead of the median
Expanded misconception:
Finds a measure of central tendency (mode) that relies on the frequency of values, rather than the middle value (median), typically due to a misunderstanding of the definitions or contexts where each measure is applicable.

Misconception 2199: Divides by the numerator and multiplies by the denominator when finding a fraction of an amount
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct operation when finding a fraction of an amount, specifically mistakenly dividing by the numerator (or inverting the numerator) and multiplying by the denominator (instead of multiplying by the numerator and dividing by the denominator) in fraction calculations

Misconception 2200: Believes that when dividing an integer by a power of 10 you add zeroes after the decimal point
Expanded misconception:
Fails to shift the decimal point correctly when dividing by a power of 10, resulting in added zeroes after the decimal point instead of moving the decimal point to the left.

Misconception 2201: Does not know the definition of a composite number
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand or recall the definition of a composite number, which is a positive integer that has at least one positive divisor other than 1 and itself, often leading to incorrect identification or analysis of composite numbers in various mathematical contexts.

Misconception 2202: Thinks we have to add the base numbers as well as the powers when multiplying indices
Expanded misconception:
Multiplying indices incorrectly by adding base numbers and/or their respective powers instead of multiplying the base numbers and adding the powers, revealing a lack of understanding of exponent rule application in algebraic operations.

Misconception 2203: Lines up the digits incorrectly when doing a subtraction involving two numbers with a different amount of digits
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly align numbers with differing numbers of digits when performing subtraction, neglecting to add zeros as placeholders to maintain correct place value alignment, resulting in an incorrect answer.

Misconception 2204: Believes the area of a rectangle is calculated by length add width
Expanded misconception:
Believes the area of a rectangle is calculated by length plus width, rather than length multiplied by width, resulting in incorrect application of the area formula in various mathematical contexts and problems.

Misconception 2205: Thinks the largest number in a stem and leaf diagram comes from the first leaf next to the largest stem
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider entire data set in a stem and leaf diagram, incorrectly assuming that the largest value is necessarily obtained by pairing the largest stem with its first or immediately adjacent leaf, rather than considering the entire leaf set or the maximum possible value resulting from the combination of a stem and any of its corresponding leaves.

Misconception 2206: Added the values together instead of finding the percentage
Expanded misconception:
Added the values together instead of applying the correct percentage operation (e.g., to find a sum involving a percentage increase or decrease, or as a part of more complex percentage-related calculations), such as incorrectly interpreting a percentage-based problem or failing to consider the base value when calculating percentages.

Misconception 2207: Believes we can categorise data as primary or secondary without knowing the source
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between primary and secondary data, assuming that the type of data is determined by its inherent characteristics rather than its origin or source.

Misconception 2208: When writing ratio, writes total as one side
Expanded misconception:
When writing a ratio, incorrectly includes the total quantity as one side of the ratio, rather than considering the relative quantities or parts being compared.

Misconception 2209: Confuses an expression with a variable
Expanded misconception:
Confuses an expression with a variable, interpreting standalone expressions as having a different value or performing operations on the wrong part of the expression, such as substituting variables incorrectly or neglecting to perform operations within or on the variable.

Misconception 2210: Ignores colours or design on shape when looking for symmetry
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the attributes of a shape (including but not limited to colour, design, size, and orientation) as relevant to identifying line or rotational symmetry, often incorrectly assuming only the shape's outline or basic form is considered when determining symmetry.

Misconception 2211: Believes interquartile range is the sum of the lower and upper quartiles
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that the interquartile range (IQR) is actually the difference between the upper quartile (Q3) and the lower quartile (Q1), not their sum, and incorrectly calculates IQR by adding Q1 and Q3 instead of subtracting Q1 from Q3.

Misconception 2212: Mixes up smallest and largest when ordering fractions
Expanded misconception:
Has difficulty comparing and ordering fractions, and may incorrectly place the smallest or largest fraction in the wrong position, such as reversing the order of fractions, placing the fraction with the largest numerator or denominator first, or misunderstanding the concept of equivalent fractions when comparing them.

Misconception 2213: Assumes regular shape refers only to a square
Expanded misconception:
Assumes a regular shape refers only to a square, and/or overlooks or fails to apply general properties of regular shapes, such as equal side lengths and equal interior angles, in various geometric contexts.

Misconception 2214: Thinks that in the equation y=a, a is the gradient
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that in a linear equation of the form y = a, incorrectly interprets 'a' as the gradient or slope, rather than the y-intercept.

Misconception 2215: Does not recognise when to find a factor from a worded question
Expanded misconception:
Fails to identify mathematical factors or relationships from worded questions, may misinterpret question context or overlook operation-dependent clues that require finding a factor, resulting in the incorrect application of mathematical operations or formulae.

Misconception 2216: Believes the mean from grouped data something divided by the sum of the midpoint values
Expanded misconception:
Believes the mean from grouped data is calculated by dividing the sum of frequencies by the sum of the midpoint values, rather than using the sum of the product of the midpoints and their respective frequencies.

Misconception 2217: Thinks subtracting a negative makes your answer smaller
Expanded misconception:
Thinks subtracting a negative number always results in a decrease in the original value, rather than recognizing that subtracting a negative is equivalent to adding a positive.

Misconception 2218: Assumes that opposite angles are equal in a quadrilateral
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that opposite angles are only equal in a parallelogram (a specific type of quadrilateral), or overgeneralizes this property to all types of quadrilaterals.

Misconception 2219: Believes any co-interior angles are supplementary, not just those in parallel lines
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that co-interior angles are supplementary only when they are formed by a transversal intersecting two parallel lines, mistakenly applying this property to all co-interior angles.

Misconception 2220: Mixes up term in a sequence and position of a term
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets or confuses the order of operations, the commutative or associative properties of a mathematical sequence, or mistakenly exchanges terms based on their positional value rather than their actual value, potentially indicating difficulty in retaining or applying sequence relationships.

Misconception 2221: Has made an order of operations mistake when writing a formula from a given situation
Expanded misconception:
Has misapplied the order of operations when translating mathematical expressions from situational descriptions and/or mathematical models, often incorrectly applying the priority rules of multiplication and division over addition and subtraction, resulting in misplaced or incorrectly ordered operations within the formula.

Misconception 2222: Out by 10 when subtracting
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly handle regrouping in subtraction or repeatedly subtracts ones, tens or hundreds digits resulting in a solution that is consistently out by 10 or a multiple of 10.

Misconception 2223: When dividing fractions, divides the denominators by each other
Expanded misconception:
"When operating with fractions, incorrectly applies whole number rules, specifically when dividing fractions, divides the denominators by each other instead of inverting and multiplying, revealing a lack of understanding of fraction division rules."

Misconception 2224: Does not recall larger square numbers
Expanded misconception:
"Does not recall or apply larger perfect square numbers accurately within calculation or algebraic problems, potentially leading to incorrect substitution, simplification, or solution."

Misconception 2225: Thinks signs are interchangeable when adding or subtracting positive and negative numbers
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly apply the rules of arithmetic operations with positive and negative numbers, often treating signs as interchangeable when adding or subtracting, such as adding a negative number as if it were positive, subtracting a negative number as if adding a positive, or incorrectly handling the signs in a series of operations, resulting in an incorrect result.

Misconception 2226: Incorrect use of scale on a diagram
Expanded misconception:
Incorrect use or interpretation of scale on a diagram, failing to recognize the ratio or proportion between real-world measurements and their representation on the diagram, or misunderstanding the relationship between units, leading to miscalculations or incorrect conclusions.

Misconception 2227: Subtracts rather than adds when using Pythagoras to find the hypotenuse
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct Pythagorean theorem operation, specifically subtracting rather than adding the squared lengths of the legs, which results in incorrect calculation of the hypotenuse.

Misconception 2228: Does not know what a Fibonacci sequence is
Expanded misconception:
Lacks understanding of the Fibonacci sequence definition, properties, or generation rules, such as the recursive relationship between consecutive numbers, where one number is the sum of the two preceding ones, often typically starting with 0 and 1.

Misconception 2229: Believes the mean is something divided by total
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the correct calculation of mean, instead believing it is a value divided by the total number of values or items, rather than the sum of all values divided by the total number of values.

Misconception 2230: substitutes incorrectly
Expanded misconception:
Substitutes incorrectly, possibly due to misunderstanding or misremembering the value, unit, or formula of a variable, constant, or coefficient, or incorrectly swaps values between the question stem and the options provided.

Misconception 2231: Thinks cross section of a prism can be any face
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that a cross-section of a prism can be any face, rather than a specific face that is cut in a way that is parallel to its base, resulting in a two-dimensional representation of the original shape with the same properties, such as a rectangle or square in the case of a rectangular or square prism.

Misconception 2232: Thinks that perpendicular lines do not meet at 90 degrees
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the definition of a right angle, incorrectly assuming that perpendicular lines may not intersect at exactly 90 degrees in all scenarios, or neglects to consider the properties of perpendicular lines when solving problems.

Misconception 2233: Thinks the reciprocal of a fraction is what number adds to that fraction to make one whole.
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the reciprocal of a fraction with its complement, or thinks that the reciprocal is the value needed to be added to the original fraction to make a sum of one (i.e., incorrectly applies the concept of adding fractions to make a whole, rather than understanding that the reciprocal of a fraction is obtained by swapping the numerator and the denominator).

Misconception 2234: Forgets to change the numerators when finding equivalent fractions
Expanded misconception:
Fails to adjust or incorrectly adjusts the numerators when attempting to find equivalent fractions, often modifying only the denominators or neglecting to maintain an equivalent ratio.

Misconception 2235: Gives the first significant figure when asked for the second
Expanded misconception:
Fails to maintain the correct number of significant figures throughout the calculation, specifically gives an intermediate answer with an incorrect number of significant figures or does not use the least precise measurement to determine the number of significant figures in the final answer, such as gives the first significant figure when asked for the second, rounds intermediate calculations too early, or incorrectly uses trailing zeros in intermediate calculations.

Misconception 2236: Does not know that co-interior angles sum to 180 degrees
Expanded misconception:
Does not apply or misunderstands the properties of co-interior angles, failing to recognize that when two lines are intersected by a transversal, the co-interior angles sum to 180 degrees and often incorrectly sums or relates these angles.

Misconception 2237: Thinks the more digits a number has the greater it is, regardless of place value
Expanded misconception:
Thinks the magnitude of a number is determined solely by the number of digits it has, regardless of place value, and fails to consider the actual value of the digits in each place, often leading to incorrect comparisons and operations.

Misconception 2238: Divides rather than multiplies when scaling up in direct proportion problems
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct operation, often dividing instead of multiplying, when scaling up in direct proportion problems due to a misunderstanding of proportional relationships.

Misconception 2239: Thinks there are 50 weeks in a year
Expanded misconception:
Lacks understanding of time units and conversions, particularly overestimating or misremembering the number of weeks in a year, and potentially applying this error to calculations involving yearly or weekly rates, frequencies, or quantities.

Misconception 2240: When factorising a quadratic with a non-unit coefficient of x squared, believes that coefficient will be in front of both x terms in the factorised form
Expanded misconception:
When factorising a quadratic with a non-unit coefficient of x squared, incorrectly believes that the coefficient will be in front of both x terms in the factorised form, or fails to correctly distribute the coefficient to only one of the terms, and may assume that the coefficients from the original expression directly translate to the factors without adjusting for proper term-wise correspondence.

Misconception 2241: Confuses triangular prism with pyramid
Expanded misconception:
Confuses triangular prism with a triangular pyramid, leading to incorrect identification or application of three-dimensional geometric shapes, including but not limited to, mislabeling a polyhedron with two congruent and parallel triangular bases as a triangular pyramid, or vice versa, and demonstrating an incomplete understanding of the distinct properties and definitions of these shapes.

Misconception 2242: Converts a fraction to a decimal by placing the denominator after the decimal point
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly convert a fraction to a decimal by incorrectly applying conversion rules, such as placing the denominator after the decimal point instead of performing the division or incorrectly placing the decimal point after fraction-to-decimal conversion.

Misconception 2243: Believes quartiles are at 1/4 and 3/4 through the range of the data
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands the concept of quartiles, incorrectly assuming they are points at the 1/4 and 3/4 marks of the total range of the data, rather than being points that split the data into four equal parts, where the first quartile (Q1) is the median of the lower half, the third quartile (Q3) is the median of the upper half, and the second quartile (Q2) is the overall median.

Misconception 2244: Thinks there are 10,000g in a kilogram
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the prefix-specific conversion factors, incorrectly believing that 'kilo' denotes a factor of 10 times larger than 'centi', resulting in the misconceived notion that there are 10,000 grams in a kilogram, rather than the correct 1000 grams.

Misconception 2245: When using the formula to solve a quadratic equation, believes that a, b and c are always taken from the 1st, 2nd and 3rd terms, respectively, of the quadratic. 
Expanded misconception:
When using the formula to solve a quadratic equation, believes that the coefficients a, b, and c are always taken from the 1st, 2nd and 3rd terms, respectively, of the quadratic expression in a standard ascending order (from lower to higher powers of the variable), without regard to the actual corresponding ax^2, bx, and c constants, and may also assume the equation must be in ascending powers of the variable or with specific constant term formatting.

Misconception 2246: Answers as if there are 10 seconds in a minute when changing from seconds to minutes
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to correctly convert between units of time by not properly applying conversion factors, such as incorrectly assuming there are 10 seconds in a minute, rather than 60, resulting in errors in calculations involving time conversion."

Misconception 2247: Does not understand that a number is divisible by the factors of its factors
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand that a number is divisible by the factors of its factors, including failing to recognize inherited divisibility relationships and not applying divisibility rules transitively.

Misconception 2248: Halves when asked to find the square root
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly applies the operation of halving rather than taking the square root, possibly indicating a lack of understanding of the concept of square roots or an inconsistent application of mathematical operations.

Misconception 2249: Confuses calculations for cumulative frequency and estimated total from a grouped frequency table
Expanded misconception:
Has difficulty distinguishing between calculations for cumulative frequency and estimated total from a grouped frequency table, often mistakenly applying the formula for one when attempting to find the other, such as incorrectly summing group frequencies instead of calculating the midpoint value or inappropriately adding cumulative totals.

Misconception 2250: Believes the difference between triangle numbers remains the same
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that the difference between consecutive terms in a sequence of triangular numbers increases by 1 for each successive pair, such as the difference between the nth triangle number and the (n+1)th triangle number being (n+1) instead of remaining constant.

Misconception 2251: Chooses the correct scale when measuring angles on a protractor but starts from the wrong number when the angle is in between two labelled dashes
Expanded misconception:
Fails to accurately determine the starting point on a protractor, even when correctly identifying the scale, by not adjusting the measurement origin when the angle is between two labelled dashes, leading to incorrect angle measurements.

Misconception 2252: Believes you multiply the constant difference by the term number to get the term value
Expanded misconception:
Believes you multiply the constant difference by the term number, rather than adding the constant term to the product of the constant difference and term number, when finding the nth term of an arithmetic sequence.

Misconception 2253: Believes the position number in a sequence does not have to be an integer
Expanded misconception:
Considers non-integer or non-whole number values as potential positions or indices in a sequence, series, or pattern, failing to recognize that counting and ordering typically require whole number increments, and may also have difficulty understanding the implications of fractional, negative, or zero positions in a sequence.

Misconception 2254: Removes the decimal point when asked to round a decimal to a whole number
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply rounding rules correctly, specifically ignoring the digit immediately to the right of the decimal point and removing the decimal point without considering the value of the digit being rounded or failing to increase the whole number by 1 when the digit to the right is 5 or greater.

Misconception 2255: Believes you subtract the number caught second from the estimated population to find the number marked first when using the capture-recapture method
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly apply the principles of the capture-recapture method by incorrectly reversing the roles of the first and second captures, believing that the number caught second represents the initial population that needs to be corrected for the marked animals, thus incorrectly calculating the total population estimate.

Misconception 2256: Believes the constant in an expanded quadratic comes from adding the two numbers in the brackets
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the relationship between the constant term in an expanded quadratic equation and the product of the coefficients of the linear terms and the constant terms within the brackets, instead incorrectly assuming the constant term is simply the sum or a straightforward arithmetic operation of the numbers in the brackets.

Misconception 2257: Believes you always reduce the numerator and denominator by 1 when writing the probability for a second pick without replacement on a tree diagram
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply correct rules of probability for dependent events, specifically incorrectly reducing the numerator and/or denominator by a fixed value (e.g., 1) instead of the actual number of items removed from the total set, when determining the probability of subsequent events in a without-replacement scenario, such as in tree diagrams.

Misconception 2258: Believes when writing a number in standard form as an ordinary number you write any digits in the base (omitting the decimal point) and do not account for the power of 10
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the implications of the exponent when expressing numbers in scientific notation or standard form, specifically believes that when converting a number in standard form to an ordinary number, one merely omits the decimal point without adjusting the digits according to the power of 10, neglecting the critical role of the exponent in denoting the place value shift.

Misconception 2259: Believes the mean is something divided by the sum of the categories given
Expanded misconception:
 Белieves the mean is something divided by the sum of the categories, rather than the count of the categories, typically resulting from a miscalculation or misunderstanding of the formula for calculating the mean or average.

Misconception 2260: Identifies a multiple of a number but not a common multiple of a group of numbers
Expanded misconception:
