Identifies a multiple of one or more numbers in the problem, but fails to recognize or incorrectly identifies the least common multiple (LCM) or a common multiple that satisfies the conditions of a group of numbers.

Misconception 2261: Believes if shapes have rotational symmetry they must have line symmetry
Expanded misconception:
Believes that if a shape has rotational symmetry, it must also have reflection line symmetry, or overemphasizes the connection between rotational and line symmetry, often expecting shapes with rotational symmetry to exhibit reflection line symmetry, even if the specifics of the shape and its axis of rotation do not support this.

Misconception 2262: Gets order of ratio wrong
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct order of operation when working with ratios, proportions, or fractions, often incorrectly inverting or reversing the order, or applying operations out of sequence.

Misconception 2263: Does not understand the root power of 4
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the properties of exponentiation, specifically failing to recognize or correctly apply the power of 4 (e.g., squaring twice, or the result of a number raised to the power of 4), which may result in miscalculations involving numbers raised to the power of 2, such as incorrectly calculating the result of an expression containing a number squared and then squared again.

Misconception 2264: When subtracting a power of 10, subtracts from the wrong column
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly align decimal places or columns when subtracting a power of 10, resulting in incorrect regrouping or borrowing.

Misconception 2265: Thinks that probabilities of an event occurring or not occurring must be equal
Expanded misconception:
Confuses probabilities with equal likelihood, failing to consider that probabilities of an event occurring and not occurring must sum to 1, but are not necessarily equal, often relying on an intuitive but incorrect assumption of 50/50 chance.

Misconception 2266: Believes angles around a point sum to 180
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that angles around a point sum to 360 degrees, not 180 degrees, unless specifically dealing with a straight line or a linear pair.

Misconception 2267: Chooses an incorrect point on the object when describing a 90 or 270 degree rotation
Expanded misconception:
Identifies an incorrect point of rotation, rotational angle, or order of rotation, often choosing an endpoint, a misleading visual cue, or misunderstanding the rotational effect on a specific object or part of an object during a 90 or 270 degree rotation.

Misconception 2268: Thinks the x coordinate of a point on the y axis is 1
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to recognize that points on the y-axis have an x-coordinate of 0, incorrectly assuming x is non-zero, often assuming it to be 1 or another small value."

Misconception 2269: When finding the distance between coordinates, includes the starting number
Expanded misconception:
When finding the distance between two coordinates, incorrectly calculates the distance by including the starting point or initial value as a reference point, rather than determining the absolute difference between the two coordinates along a given axis or scale.

Misconception 2270: In a function machine, confuses an operation with the input
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the role or operation of an input, output, or function variable in a function machine, such as mistaking an input for a result, an output for an input, or incorrectly applying the function operation to the wrong quantity, often by treating the operation as the input itself.

Misconception 2271: When solving a problem that requires an inverse operation (e.g. missing number problems), does the original operation
Expanded misconception:
"When solving a problem that requires an inverse operation (e.g. missing number problems, balancing equations), incorrectly identifies, applies, or reverses the operation, failing to recognize or apply the inverse relationship between operations (e.g. addition/subtraction, multiplication/division)."

Misconception 2272: When dividing involving a multiple of 10, gives an answer 10 times bigger than it should be 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for a factor of 10 when multiplying or dividing by multiples of 10, resulting in answers significantly larger or smaller than expected.

Misconception 2273: Subtracts instead of divides
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to apply the correct operation between division and subtraction, often mistakenly replacing division with subtraction or neglecting the priority of operations when both are present in an expression."

Misconception 2274: When dividing variables, thinks they subtract coefficients as well as powers
Expanded misconception:
When dividing variables or algebraic expressions with exponents, incorrectly applies the quotient rule by subtracting the coefficients in addition to subtracting the exponents, instead of only subtracting the exponents while keeping the coefficients unchanged or simplifying them separately.

Misconception 2275: Believes midpoint calculation is just half of the difference
Expanded misconception:
Believes that midpoint calculation only involves averaging or taking half of the difference between two numbers without considering the actual midpoint formula or failing to add the result to one of the endpoints, resulting in incorrect midpoint determination.

Misconception 2276: Has considered the percentage rather than the percentage of the amount
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly apply percentages by considering the percentage value itself rather than calculating the percentage of the relevant quantity or amount.

Misconception 2277: When factorising into double brackets, finds the correct values for the non variable terms but swops the plus and minus sign
Expanded misconception:
When factorising quadratic expressions into double brackets, the student correctly identifies the values of the non-variable terms (constants) but incorrectly assigns the signs to the variable terms, either swapping the positive and negative signs or misapplying the underlying rules for determining the signs in the factorised form.

Misconception 2278: Subtracts rather than divides when answering worded problems
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets division or sharing problems as subtraction problems, often due to a lack of understanding of the question context or key words that indicate division or sharing operations.

Misconception 2279: Adds rather than multiplies when using the product rule for counting
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the fundamental counting principle, specifically the product rule, by incorrectly adding rather than multiplying the number of options in each event, leading to an underestimation of the total number of possible outcomes.

Misconception 2280: Believes change in y for gradient is always calculated from the origin, regardless of the y-intercept of the line
Expanded misconception:
Believes change in y for gradient is always calculated from the origin, or the point where the line intersects the x-axis, regardless of the y-intercept or the interval between two given points on the line.

Misconception 2281: Does not understand which months are in the Spring
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the conventional seasons or their corresponding months in the Northern or Southern Hemisphere, as applied to a specific problem context.

Misconception 2282: Believes that in y=mx+c, m is the y-intercept
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets the slope-intercept form of a linear equation (y = mx + c), incorrectly believing that the slope (m) is the y-intercept, confusing it with the constant term (c), which is the actual y-intercept.

Misconception 2283: When finding the missing length on similar shapes, just finds the difference between the given lengths
Expanded misconception:
When finding the missing length on similar shapes, just finds the difference between the given lengths rather than applying the concept of proportional scaling and ratio to find the actual length.

Misconception 2284: When asked for a specific term in a sequence just gives the next term
Expanded misconception:
When asked for a specific term in a sequence, incorrectly gives the next term without understanding the underlying pattern or formula governing the sequence.

Misconception 2285: In a function machine, confuses the first operation and the input
Expanded misconception:
In a function machine, confuses the input value with the output value or mistakenly applies the wrong input value for the given function operation, or reverses the order of operations within the function, such as mistakenly inputting the result of a previous operation as the starting value.

Misconception 2286: Believes the length of the line in a velocity time graph gives the distance (rather than the area underneath that line).
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the relationship between velocity and distance in a velocity-time graph, incorrectly assuming that the length of the line or the magnitude of the velocity value directly represents distance, rather than recognizing that the area under the velocity-time graph gives the total displacement or distance.

Misconception 2287: Believes multiplying together all prime factors gives the highest common factor
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the concept of highest common factor correctly, believing that multiplying all prime factors of the given numbers together gives the highest common factor, rather than identifying the shared prime factors raised to the lowest power they appear with in either number.

Misconception 2288: When multiplying decimals, does not realize that the order and position of the digits (relative to each other) has to remain constant.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the importance of positional value and digit placement when multiplying decimals, often arranging numbers in incorrect positions or losing track of decimal places during the multiplication process.

Misconception 2289: Confuses the signs for straight lines as being dashes
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands or confuses the representation or notation of mathematical objects, specifically interpreting the symbolism for negative numbers '-', subtraction '-', or other negations, as being equivalent to or getting confused with dashes, or other punctuation marks.

Misconception 2290: Uses the same value for both coordinates
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly apply mathematical operations or transformations to one or both coordinates of a point, resulting in an error where the same incorrect value is used for both the x and y coordinates, often indicating a lack of understanding in coordinate system transformations.

Misconception 2291: When solving simultaneous equations, thinks they can't multiply each equation by a different number
Expanded misconception:
When solving simultaneous linear equations, doesn't recognize that equations can be multiplied by different constants to facilitate elimination or substitution methods, resulting in overly complicated or incorrect solutions.

Misconception 2292: Does not understand that factors are divisors which split the number into equal groups
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the concept of factors as divisors that split a number into equal, non-empty, and whole groups, failing to recognize that factors come in pairs and are essential for multiplication and division relationships within a number.

Misconception 2293: Thinks the orientation of the side view does not matter when identifying the direction of view
Expanded misconception:
Has difficulty understanding the relationship between an object's orientation and its projected side view, often assuming the orientation of the side view is arbitrary or irrelevant when determining the direction of view, leading to misinterpretations and incorrect analyses of spatial representations.

Misconception 2294: Believes that Mid point multiplied by the Frequency in a grouped frequency table gives the frequency for that group.
Expanded misconception:
Believes that the midpoint multiplied by the frequency in a grouped frequency table gives the frequency for that group, rather than being a method used to estimate the contribution of the group to the total sum or mean.

Misconception 2295: Believes probabilities can be greater than 1
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or apply the fundamental property of probability, which always lies between 0 and 1, inclusive, and therefore may assign probabilities exceeding 1 or calculate probabilities incorrectly, ignoring or misinterpreting the constraints on probability values.

Misconception 2296: Believes Fibonacci numbers are triangle numbers
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly identifies or applies properties of other sequences or number patterns to Fibonacci numbers, specifically, mistakenly assuming Fibonacci numbers are a different type of number sequence such as triangle numbers, square numbers, or perfect numbers.

Misconception 2297: Believes total frequency in a table is found by counting rows
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that total frequency in a table is found by summing the counts or frequencies in each row or category, and instead, incorrectly counts the number of rows, assumes each row contributes equally to the total frequency, or misunderstands the concept of frequency as being related to the number of categories or rows rather than the actual counts.

Misconception 2298: Mixes up smallest and greatest when ordering positive integers
Expanded misconception:
Fails to accurately compare and order positive integers, often reversing the relationship between the smallest and greatest values, resulting in incorrect placement or magnitude misconceptions when selecting among options.

Misconception 2299: Believes any sample gives an equal chance of selection
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the concept of representativeness and equal likelihood in probability, and believes any sample, regardless of size or method of selection, gives an equal chance of selection.

Misconception 2300: Forgets to include a marked right angle when performing angle calculations
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider or apply properties of special right-angled triangles, or neglects to incorporate given angle measures, including marked right angles, when performing calculations involving angle relationships, trigonometric ratios, or geometric transformations.

Misconception 2301: Believes the equation of a horizontal line is in the form x = ay, where a is the y intercept
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the equation of a horizontal line with a non-standard form: incorrectly believes the equation of a horizontal line is in the form x = ay, where a is actually the y-intercept, rather than the standard form y = b, where b is the y-intercept.

Misconception 2302: Believes lines inside the shape are included when finding perimeter
Expanded misconception:
Believes lines or segments enclosed within the shape are part of its boundary and should be included when calculating the perimeter.

Misconception 2303: When asked to find data points below a certain value, gives the number of data points above this value
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly identify the relationship between a given threshold value and the corresponding data point values, resulting in incorrect consideration or reversal of the comparative criteria (e.g., incorrectly counting values above the threshold when asked for values below, or vice versa).

Misconception 2304: Believes that Mid-point x Frequency in a grouped frequency table gives an accurate total, rather than an estimate.
Expanded misconception:
Believes that the product of mid-point and frequency in a grouped frequency table gives an exact total, rather than an estimate, failing to account for the variability within each class interval and not recognizing that this method provides an approximation.

Misconception 2305: Ignores place value when adding a whole number and a decimal
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for place value when adding or subtracting a whole number and a decimal, resulting in incorrect alignment of digits or omission of zeros when converting between whole number and decimal representations.

Misconception 2306: Applies BIDMAS in strict order (does not realize addition and subtraction, and multiplication and division, are of equal priority)
Expanded misconception:
Applies BIDMAS in strict sequential order rather than considering the equal priority of adjacent operations within the same level of the hierarchy (specifically, addition/subtraction and multiplication/division).

Misconception 2307: Thinks you can divide the variables and the numerical values by different numbers when simplifying an algebraic fraction
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that when simplifying algebraic fractions, the same operation must be applied to both the numerator and the denominator, and that dividing or multiplying variables and numerical values by different numbers can alter the fraction's value.

Misconception 2308: Converts a fraction to a decimal by placing a decimal point between the numerator and denominator
Expanded misconception:
Converts a fraction to a decimal by incorrectly placing a decimal point between the numerator and denominator, rather than performing the correct division of the numerator by the denominator to obtain the decimal equivalent.

Misconception 2309: Thinks they can just give the probability of the first event for a combined probability
Expanded misconception:
Ignores the second or subsequent events in a probability problem and thinks the answer is simply the probability of the first or only the most likely event, failing to consider the combined or conditional nature of probabilities in the question.

Misconception 2310: Does not know how to find the gradient of a straight line from two points
Expanded misconception:
Does not know or apply the correct formula for finding the gradient of a straight line from two points, including selecting the correct coordinate differences or considering negative gradients, which may result in incorrect slope or steepness calculations.

Misconception 2311: Divides rather than adds when answering worded problems
Expanded misconception:
Confuses or misinterprets mathematical operations in worded problems, selecting the incorrect operation, such as division instead of addition, and performs the calculation correctly.

Misconception 2312: Thinks congruent shapes are the same shape but can be different sizes
Expanded misconception:
Confuses congruence with similar figures or size, may think that congruent shapes can have different sizes or proportions, or may believe shape similarity implies exact shape sameness even if scale is different.

Misconception 2313: Mixes up discrete and continuous data types
Expanded misconception:
Can be confused between or misinterpreted characteristics of discrete data types (e.g., counts, integers) and continuous data types (e.g., measurements, ratios), such as identifying mean or median as the most appropriate measure of central tendency, or attempting to group or categorize continuous data as if it is discrete.

Misconception 2314: Multiplies instead of divides to find one part given the total
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply division correctly when solving for a part of a total, often mistakenly applying multiplication, which would find the total given a part rather than the intended operation of finding the part given the total.

Misconception 2315: Confuses negative and positive correlation
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the concepts and applications of negative and positive correlation, possibly thinking that a negative correlation occurs when two variables move in the same direction or when one variable decreases as the other increases, or that a positive correlation occurs when two variables move in opposite directions, and/or misinterprets the direction or strength of the relationship between variables.

Misconception 2316: Mixes up squaring and multiplying by 2 or doubling
Expanded misconception:
Mixes up squaring and multiplying by 2 or doubling, such as incorrectly applying exponentiation rules, confusing the meaning of 'squared' with 'multiplied by 2', or failing to recognize the difference between the numerical outcomes of doubling a value and squaring it.

Misconception 2317: When asked for the next term in the sequence subtracts one from the last term given, despite the sequence not going down in ones.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or apply the underlying pattern or rule governing a sequence, instead using a simplistic or linear approach, such as subtracting one from the last term given, to predict the next term in a sequence, even when the sequence does not follow a uniform increment or decrement.

Misconception 2318: Has used the wrong data point on the graph
Expanded misconception:
Has incorrectly interpreted or used the wrong data point on the graph, such as misreading axis values, confusing x and y coordinates, or identifying a local extreme instead of the correct maximum or minimum.

Misconception 2319: subtracts the highest from the lowest when calculating the range
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct order of operations when calculating range or misinterprets the concept of range, such as subtracting the largest value from the smallest value or misunderstanding the relative positions of the smallest and largest values in a dataset.

Misconception 2320: Subtracts rather than divides when scaling down in direct proportion problems
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply correct mathematical operations when scaling in direct proportion problems, such as incorrectly subtracting a value instead of dividing or multiplying by the scaling factor.

Misconception 2321: does not know the meaning of the word digit
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the concept of place value or the meaning of the word digit, resulting in incorrect identification or manipulation of individual digits within a number, potentially confusing or misinterpreting the number's value or magnitude in relation to the position or value of its individual digits.

Misconception 2322: Believes a percentage of a number will be a multiple of the percentage
Expanded misconception:
Fails to maintain the proportion or scale in percentage calculations, believing a percentage of a number is always a whole multiple of the percentage itself rather than a fraction of the original value.

Misconception 2323: When ordering decimals, believes the decimal point is not relevant
Expanded misconception:
"When ordering decimals, believes the decimal point is not relevant, and therefore compares decimals based solely on the numerical value of the digits before or after the decimal point, failing to consider the place value of each digit and leading to incorrect comparisons and ordering of decimal numbers."

Misconception 2324: Believes the y-intercept of a line is the root of its equation
Expanded misconception:
"Confuses key concepts in linear equations, specifically believing that the y-intercept of a line always represents the root (x-intercept) of its corresponding equation, and may incorrectly identify or manipulate these values when solving or graphing linear equations."

Misconception 2325: Believes data implies equal chance of selection
Expanded misconception:
Believes categorical data implies equal chance of selection or equal probability, despite absence of information regarding selection procedures or probabilities.

Misconception 2326: Multiplies rather than adds 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the order of operations, incorrectly prioritizing multiplication over addition, leading to the incorrect assumption that the numbers should be multiplied rather than added within a specific calculation.

Misconception 2327: Does not understand that a space can be used to separate the thousands column from the hundreds column
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply proper numeral notation conventions, including but not limited to, the use of commas or spaces to separate thousands, millions, or billions, leading to incorrect interpretation of numerical values and resulting calculations.

Misconception 2328: When using the capture-recapture method, believes the number caught second is the numerator and the number marked second is the denominator
Expanded misconception:
When using the capture-recapture method or any method requiring paired values for calculations (e.g., mark-release-recapture, probability calculations), mistakenly identifies the pairs of values (e.g., initial capture vs. marked population or initial capture vs. second capture) and confuses which should be the numerator and which should be the denominator in subsequent calculations.

Misconception 2329: Thinks the remainder is the difference between the dividend and the next biggest multiple
Expanded misconception:
Thinks the remainder in division is the difference between the dividend and the next biggest multiple, rather than the amount left over after an exact number of multiples of the divisor have been subtracted from the dividend.

Misconception 2330: Rounds incorrectly by changing multiple place values
Expanded misconception:
Rounds incorrectly by changing multiple place values, such as moving from tenths to ones or from hundreds to thousands, rather than maintaining the original place value during calculations.

Misconception 2331: Believes the angle in a pie chart sector is equal to the frequency
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that the angle in a pie chart sector is proportional to the frequency or value it represents, rather than being equal to it.

Misconception 2332: Uses slant height instead of perpendicular height to find area of a trapezium
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between slant height and perpendicular height in calculations involving the area of trapeziums, often leading to incorrect application of formulae that require right angles or perpendicular measurements.

Misconception 2333: Thinks there are 10 million mm in 1 km
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly apply unit conversion between kilometer and millimeter within the metric system, often assuming there are 10 million mm in 1 km rather than the correct conversion of 1 km = 1,000,000 mm.

Misconception 2334: Does not know that angles around a point sum to 360
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the concept that the sum of angles around a point (or a full turn) is 360 degrees, leading to incorrect calculations or applications involving rotational symmetry or angular measurements.

Misconception 2335: When multiplying decimals, multiplies rather than divides when reinserting the decimal.
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to recognize the effect of decimal multiplication on place value, not adjusting the position of the decimal point by dividing, instead incorrectly multiplying or in other cases failing to adjust at all, resulting in incorrect placement of the decimal point."

Misconception 2336: Confuses co-interior and corresponding angles
Expanded misconception:
Confuses properties and relationships between co-interior angles (angles formed when two lines intersect two other lines and are between the two original lines) and corresponding angles (angles in the same relative position in each of two intersecting lines) when dealing with parallel lines or line segments, possibly leading to errors in determining angle measures or relationships.

Misconception 2337: Struggles to visualise how an object will look in 3D when given a plan view, front and side elevation
Expanded misconception:
"Struggles to visualise the spatial relationships between different components and how they fit together, having difficulty to project and interpret two-dimensional plan views, front and side elevations into correct three-dimensional forms."

Misconception 2338: Believes the lower quartile is the lowest value in a list
Expanded misconception:
Fails to differentiate between position-based measures of spread, such as percentiles (including quartiles) that divide data into groups, and endpoint-based measures, such as minimum and maximum values, and instead mistakenly equates the lower quartile with the lowest value in a list.

Misconception 2339: Believes that reciprocals are undefined for negative numbers
Expanded misconception:
Stores the sign of the original value separately and neglects its effect when simplifying the reciprocal, or incorrectly assumes that the reciprocal operation does not accommodate or produce undefined results for negative input numbers due to an assumption that negative divisors are not allowed in division operations.

Misconception 2340: Does not recognise when to find a multiple from a worded question
Expanded misconception:
Fails to identify or interpret the key mathematical operation required by the context of the worded question, particularly in situations where a multiple or factor needs to be determined based on the given information.

Misconception 2341: Does not know how to calculate the sum of interior angles
Expanded misconception:
Does not know how to calculate the sum of interior angles of a polygon (e.g., using the formula (n-2) × 180°, where n is the number of sides) or fails to apply it correctly to different types of polygons.

Misconception 2342: Believes they add the conversion factor when converting between units
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct operation when converting between units, typically mistakenly adding the conversion factor instead of multiplying or dividing, or incorrectly multiplying/dividing without consideration of the conversion factor's relationship or direction, resulting in a loss or gain of unit context.

Misconception 2343: Believes total frequency in a table is given by the sum of the categories given
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider that some categories in a table may overlap or that the table may not be exhaustive and includes unknown or unspecified categories, resulting in incorrectly assuming that the total frequency in a table is given by the simple sum of the categories provided.

Misconception 2344: When counting partial symbols on pictogram, uses the fraction rather than the amount it represents
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly interpret partial symbols on pictograms or equivalent representations by improperly applying the fractional value rather than the corresponding quantity or amount it represents.

Misconception 2345: Counts half-squares as full squares when calculating area on a square grid
Expanded misconception:
Fails to accurately count units or convert fractions of units to whole units when calculating area on a grid-based figure, including incorrectly counting or rounding half-squares as full squares, or neglecting to account for the proportional value of broken squares in relation to the entire grid.

Misconception 2346: When subtracting decimals with a different number of decimal places, lines up the digits incorrectly
Expanded misconception:
When subtracting decimals with a different number of decimal places, lines up the digits or decimal points incorrectly, failing to properly align corresponding digits or add zeros to make the numbers have the same number of decimal places.

Misconception 2347: Believes class width divided by frequency density gives frequency in a histogram
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands the concept of frequency density in histograms, believing it can be converted to frequency by dividing the class width by the frequency density, failing to recognize that frequency density is actually the rate of occurrence per unit, and that frequency is obtained by multiplying frequency density by the class width.

Misconception 2348: Believes the number of edges on a 3D shape match those of the 2D cross section
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to distinguish between 2D cross-sectional representations and 3D geometric shapes, incorrectly assuming that the number of edges or features present in a 2D cross-section directly corresponds to the number or configuration of edges or features in the 3D shape."

Misconception 2349: Forgets that a number divided by itself is 1
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the mathematical identity property of division, where any number (except zero) divided by itself equals 1.

Misconception 2350: When multiplying a decimal less than 1 by an integer, gives an answer 10 times bigger than it should be
Expanded misconception:
When multiplying a decimal less than 1 by an integer, fails to shift the decimal point and instead gives an answer that is an order of magnitude larger than it should be, often resulting in an answer that is 10 times bigger than the correct result.

Misconception 2351: Believes to find the angle for a pie chart sector you calculate 360 divided by the frequency of that sector
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply correct proportionality when calculating angles for a pie chart, specifically incorrectly assuming that the angle for each sector is solely determined by dividing 360 degrees by the frequency of that sector, rather than dividing 360 degrees by the total number of data points and then multiplying by the frequency or proportion of the sector.

Misconception 2352: Writes the index as a digit on the end of a number
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply exponentiation rules, instead misinterpreting exponents as ordinary digits and incorrectly writing the index as a digit on the end of a number in exponential expressions.

Misconception 2353: Does not divide by 2 when calculating the area of a trapezium
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the formula for the area of a trapezium correctly, specifically omitting or incorrectly applying the operation of dividing the sum of the lengths of the parallel sides by 2, or incorrectly substitutes values into the formula.

Misconception 2354: When describing a translation, identifies the correct number of jumps but mixes up the directions .
Expanded misconception:
When describing a translation, identifies the correct number of units moved in one or both horizontal and/or vertical directions but fails to accurately assign the corresponding direction (north, south, east, west, left, right, up, or down), thus resulting in an incorrect overall translation.

Misconception 2355: Does not know how to find order of rotational symmetry
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand or apply the concept of rotational symmetry, failing to identify the number of times a figure can be rotated by a certain angle to align with the original figure, possibly due to confusion with reflection symmetry or inability to recognize rotational symmetry patterns.

Misconception 2356: Thinks a difference of one part in a ratio means the quantities will differ by one unit
Expanded misconception:
Thinks a difference in one part or any proportion in a ratio means the quantities will differ by that unit, failing to consider the relationship between the parts and the context of proportional relationships.

Misconception 2357: Thinks sign for parallel lines just means opposite
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to understand the concept of opposite and parallel lines in geometry, believes 'parallel' or 'perpendicular' signs are solely indicative of lines being on opposite sides, rather than also representing non-intersecting lines or intersecting at a right angle."

Misconception 2358: Believes even numbers can have odd multiples
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the relationship between the parity of a number and the parity of its multiples, specifically believing that an even number can have odd multiples when, in reality, even numbers always have even multiples.

Misconception 2359: Believes division is commutative 
Expanded misconception:
Applies commutative property of addition to division, resulting in incorrect reversal of dividend and divisor.

Misconception 2360: Thinks the scale factor is found by adding the lengths of two corresponding sides together
Expanded misconception:
"Incorrectly uses the lengths of corresponding sides to determine scale factor by adding the lengths of two corresponding sides together or adding/subtracting some other combination of lengths, rather than finding the ratio of corresponding sides."

Misconception 2361: Believes a fraction out of a number other than 100 represents a percentage
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly assumes that a fraction out of a number other than 100 represents a percentage, failing to recognize that a percentage is a fraction of 100, and thus, distractor calculations often add a denominator of 100 or divide by 100 unnecessarily, or incorrectly convert a fraction into a percentage.

Misconception 2362: Believes angles on a straight line add to 360 degrees
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or apply the property that the sum of angles on a straight line is 180 degrees in Euclidean geometry, possibly due to the incorrect assumption that the sum of angles around a point or in a full circle applies to a straight line.

Misconception 2363: Assumes fractions with prime numerators cannot be simplified
Expanded misconception:
Lacks understanding of fraction simplification rules, particularly assuming fractions with prime numerators or odd/even relationships between numerator and denominator cannot be simplified.

Misconception 2364: Believes all possible outcomes must have equal probability
Expanded misconception:
May incorrectly assume equiprobability in scenarios with no indication of equal likelihood, failing to account for the actual probability distribution or neglecting to consider relevant factors influencing the outcome in various probability-based problem contexts.

Misconception 2365: Confuses vertically opposite angles with angles on a straight line
Expanded misconception:
Fails to differentiate between angles on a straight line (supplementary angles) and vertically opposite angles (equal angles), often incorrectly applying properties of one type to the other.

Misconception 2366: Mixes up the words “to” and “past” when telling the time
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the prepositions used to express time, resulting in incorrect usage of 'to', 'past', and 'half past' (e.g., writing "12:05" as "5 to 12" instead of "5 past 12").

Misconception 2367: Confuses dependent and mutually exclusive events
Expanded misconception:
Confuses dependent and mutually exclusive events, either by incorrectly assuming the probability of two or more events is the sum of their individual probabilities (for dependent events) or the product of their individual probabilities (for mutually exclusive events, where the probability of occurrence of one event reduces the probability of another), failing to recognize the concept of conditional probability, and/or neglecting the intersection of sample spaces in probability calculations.

Misconception 2368: Converts a fraction to a percentage by placing a decimal point between the numerator and denominator
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly handles or misunderstands fraction to percentage conversions by not realizing that direct placement of a decimal point is not a suitable method, failing to multiply the fraction's numerator by its reciprocal or by 100 before performing the percentage calculation.

Misconception 2369: Does not know protractor should be positioned with one side of the angle at 0
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly align the protractor when measuring angles, often ignoring the need to position one side of the angle at the 0-degree mark or misunderstanding the relationship between the angle's sides and the protractor's markings.

Misconception 2370: Uses angles on a straight line in the wrong context 
Expanded misconception:
Uses angles on a straight line in the wrong context, such as incorrectly applying angle properties reserved for geometric shapes (e.g. triangles, quadrilaterals) or mistakenly assuming parallel or perpendicular lines have measureable angles between them when they do not intersect.

Misconception 2371: Believes a direct proportion graph is a horizontal line
Expanded misconception:
Believes a direct proportion graph is a horizontal line, mistakenly equating direct proportionality with no change in the dependent variable, or assuming that the constant of proportionality is zero or one.

Misconception 2372: Believes that when factorising a quadratic with a coefficient of x squared, the two constants in the brackets still add to give the coefficient of x
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly applies the "split the middle term" method when factorising quadratic expressions with leading coefficients other than 1, incorrectly assuming that the two numbers multiplied to give the product of the coefficient of x^2 and the constant term also add to give the coefficient of x, regardless of the leading coefficient.

Misconception 2373: Does not understand parts must be equal in a fraction diagram
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand parts must be equal in a fraction diagram, failing to recognize that each part represents an equivalent portion of the whole, potentially leading to incorrect segmentation, scaling, or comparison of fractions.

Misconception 2374: Believes a quarter of 2 is 0.25
Expanded misconception:
Believes fractions or portions of small whole numbers (especially those less than or equal to 4) can be calculated as a simple decimal conversion, rather than following actual proportional division.

Misconception 2375: Does not equate the value of a number with the place value column it is in
Expanded misconception:
Does not correctly equate the value of a number with the place value column it is in, and/or may not consistently demonstrate an understanding of positional notation, often resulting in calculation errors when a digit's place value affects the overall quantity.

Misconception 2376: When finding the nth term of a linear sequence, thinks the the first term is the coefficient in front of n.
Expanded misconception:
When finding the nth term of a linear sequence, incorrectly identifies the coefficient of 'n' as the first term of the sequence, rather than the common difference, and does not realize that the first term is typically a constant term or a separate value in the formula 'a + (n-1)d' or 'an + b'.

Misconception 2377: Does not know the definition of term or term-to-term rule
Expanded misconception:
Does not demonstrate understanding of or consistently applies, a specific term or term-to-term rule, including but not limited to: formula, concept, principle, theorem, definition, equivalence, or identity relevant to the problem.

Misconception 2378: Divides total amount by each side of the ratio instead of dividing by the sum of the parts
Expanded misconception:
Fails to sum the parts of the ratio before dividing the total amount by it, resulting in incorrect proportional reasoning.

Misconception 2379: Unable to equate two divisions with a single division
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that multiple division operations can be simplified or combined into a single operation, or incorrectly applies division rules when multiple divisions are involved, such as not evaluating them from left to right or not recognizing that multiple divisions with the same denominator are equivalent to a single division with the combined numerators.

Misconception 2380: Believes f(x) - a translates the function up a units
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the direction of vertical translations in function notation, specifically believing that when 'a' is subtracted from f(x), the function shifts upward by 'a' units, rather than downward.

Misconception 2381: Confuses the lower bound and upper bound, and writes the lower bound as a value that would end in recurring 9's, for example
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the lower bound and upper bound of a range, incorrectly stating the lower bound when solving a math problem, and often mistakenly representing the value with repeating digits (typically 9's) as if representing a value that approaches but doesn't equal the actual upper bound.

Misconception 2382: Believes that y = m +cx is the equation of a straight line
Expanded misconception:
Misconceives the general equation form of a linear equation in slope-intercept or point-slope formats, incorrectly substituting variables or operations, specifically swapping or misplacing terms/variables in linear equations, such as mistakenly using y = m + cx instead of y = mx + c.

Misconception 2383: When multiplying by 1 gives an answer of 1 (for a number other than 1)
Expanded misconception:
"When multiplying a number by 1, incorrectly assumes the result will always be 1, regardless of the actual number being multiplied, rather than understanding that multiplying by 1 leaves the number unchanged."

Misconception 2384: When simplifying surds, does not square root the largest square number that goes into the number under the surd before putting it in front of the surd
Expanded misconception:
Fails to simplify radicals fully by not factoring out the largest perfect square from under the radical sign, preventing the expression of the largest square root outside the radical symbol.

Misconception 2385: Thinks that the lower and upper bound for a truncated number can be found in the same was as when it has been rounded
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the distinction between rounding and truncation, specifically believing that the lower and upper bound for a truncated number can be found in the same way as when it has been rounded, resulting in incorrect estimation of range or interval of possible values.

Misconception 2386: Believes there is a multiplicative relationship between values of terms in a linear sequence e.g. 10th term is double 5th term
Expanded misconception:
Believes there is a multiplicative relationship between values of terms in an arithmetic sequence, incorrectly assuming that each term's value changes multiplicatively instead of additively, often overestimating or underestimating the rate of change and incorrectly applying proportional reasoning to arithmetic sequences.

Misconception 2387: When finding a fraction of a fraction, divided by the whole fraction instead of the denominator
Expanded misconception:
Fails to invert and multiply when finding a fraction of a fraction, often resulting in dividing by the whole fraction instead of the denominator.

Misconception 2388: Uses formula for area of a parallelogram when finding the area of a trapezium
Expanded misconception:
Inappropriately applies a formula for a specific shape to find the area of a different shape that shares some similarities, in this case, using the formula for the area of a parallelogram (base × height) when attempting to find the area of a trapezium, despite the trapezium requiring the formula for the average of the two parallel sides multiplied by the perpendicular distance between them (0.5 × sum of the parallel sides × distance), revealing a lack of understanding for shape-specific formulas or properties.

Misconception 2389: When using a function machine, confuses the input and the output.
Expanded misconception:
When using a function machine, confuses the input (the value that is put into the function) and the output (the value produced by the function), leading to incorrect interpretation or reversal of function operations.

Misconception 2390: Believes rounding to 1 significant figure means rounding to the nearest integer, regardless of the value of the number
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply correct rounding rules, specifically believing that rounding to a particular number of significant figures always means rounding to the nearest whole number or to the nearest place value (e.g., tens, hundreds) regardless of the value or decimal placement of the number.

Misconception 2391: Believes the median is the middle category in a table, ignoring the frequencies
Expanded misconception:
Believes the median corresponds to the middle category in a grouped data table or set of discrete categories, ignoring the actual frequencies or distribution of values within categories, and not properly ordering or ranking the data.

Misconception 2392: Rounds to the wrong degree of accuracy (rounds too much)
Expanded misconception:
Fails to maintain the required degree of precision, often rounding intermediate results or final answers too aggressively, which leads to a loss of significant figures and inaccurate solutions.

Misconception 2393: Believes the midpoint of grouped data is equivalent to the group width
Expanded misconception:
Believes the midpoint of grouped data is equivalent to the group width, such that if the data is grouped into intervals, they incorrectly assume the central tendency (mean, median, or mode) is identical to or directly proportional to the group width or range, rather than understanding that the actual midpoint should be calculated based on the individual data points or the position of the interval.

Misconception 2394: Does not know the properties of an equilateral triangle
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply properties of an equilateral triangle, such as equal side lengths, equal interior angles, or the relationship between altitude and side lengths in problem-solving or calculation, leading to an incorrect answer choice.

Misconception 2395: When asked for the value of a digit, just gives the digit
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the context or operational requirements of a problem, relying solely on the literal interpretation of numbers or digits presented, often resulting in neglecting required mathematical operations or property applications.

Misconception 2396: Adds a zero onto the end of units multiplied by units when solving multiplication problems using the grid method
Expanded misconception:
Adds a zero onto the end of units when multiplying by a single-digit or two-digit number, often due to a misunderstanding of place value and the effect of zero as a placeholder in multiplication problems, particularly when using the grid method.

Misconception 2397: Does not realise we can find a missing length if we know the base of a parallelogram and its area. 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the formula for the area of a parallelogram (A = bh) to find an unknown dimension (base or height) when the other dimension and the area are given.

Misconception 2398: Thinks you can divide terms by different factors when simplifying an algebraic fraction
Expanded misconception:
Thinks you can divide terms by different factors when simplifying an algebraic fraction, including incorrectly factoring numerators or denominators, disregarding requirements for common factors or equivalent ratios, and simplifying fractions with missing or incomplete steps.

Misconception 2399: when dividing a fraction by an integer, both multiplies the denominator and divides the denominator
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct operation for dividing a fraction by an integer, and instead exhibits one or both of the following errors: inappropriately multiplies the denominator and/or fails to reciprocate and multiply, illustrating a lack of understanding of the rule for dividing fractions by whole numbers.

Misconception 2400: Believes that if the numerators of 2 fractions are equal then the fractions are equal, regardless of the denominator
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to consider the relative size and importance of denominators when comparing fractions with the same numerators or similar characteristics, often resulting in conclusions that assume equal fractions based solely on the equality of the numerators."

Misconception 2401: Answers as if there are 60 hours in a day
Expanded misconception:
Answers as if there are 60 hours in a day or fails to adhere to the conventional time unit conversions, such as incorrect handling of hour to minute, minute to second, or day to hour conversions in time-related problems.

Misconception 2402: When asked for the value of a digit, gives an answer 10 times too small
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to consider place value, resulting in an answer that is consistently off by a factor of 10, often due to neglecting to account for the tens or hundreds place when presented with multi-digit numbers."

Misconception 2403: Believes the columns in a base are multiples not powers of the base
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or incorrectly applies the concept of exponentiation within a base system, mistakenly treating positional values as multiples rather than powers of the base, leading to incorrect calculations involving place values and numerical representations.

Misconception 2404: When using the capture-recapture method of sampling, believes you multiply the number marked first by the number marked second to get the best estimate of population size
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly applies the capture-recapture method by misunderstanding the formula for estimating population size, specifically by multiplying the number of individuals caught or marked in the first event by the number caught or marked in the second event, rather than using the formula that involves the total number caught in the first event, the number caught in the second event, and the number of individuals recaptured.

Misconception 2405: Arithmetic error when dividing by 8
Expanded misconception:
Arithmetic error when dividing by numbers with multiple factors, frequently demonstrated by incorrectly converting or simplifying fractions with divisors of 8 by failing to properly divide or struggling with conversions, such as in decimal-to-fraction or percent-to-fraction conversions.

Misconception 2406: When multiplying a fraction by an integer, believes the integer will become the denominator of the fraction
Expanded misconception:
When multiplying a fraction by an integer, inverts the correct operation by either multiplying the numerator by the integer and keeping the denominator unchanged, or believes the integer will become the denominator of the fraction while leaving the numerator unchanged, resulting in an incorrect product that does not follow the order of operations.

Misconception 2407: Believes an arithmetic sequence can have a common second difference rather than first difference
Expanded misconception:
Confuses or generalizes common properties of arithmetic sequences to other sequences or mistakenly applies properties of higher-order sequences, specifically believing that a sequence can have a common second difference (or another higher-order difference) rather than a common first difference.

Misconception 2408: Does not realise that subtracting a larger number will give a smaller answer
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply correct subtraction principles, resulting in errors when subtracting larger numbers from smaller numbers, including not realiseing that subtracting a larger number will yield a negative result or a smaller answer, and confusing the relative difference in value between the numbers being subtracted.

Misconception 2409: Believes you place the digits next to each other for a multiplication sample space diagram
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the concept of multiplication as repeated addition, and instead incorrectly visualizes the sample space diagram by placing the digits next to each other or writing the multiplicand only once, rather than creating an array or list that represents each possible outcome of the multiplication event.

Misconception 2410: Believes an outlier has to be a whole number
Expanded misconception:
Believes an outlier always takes the form of a distinct, whole number value and fails to recognize that an outlier can be any abnormal or irregular data point, including non-integer or decimal values that are significantly different from the majority of the dataset.

Misconception 2411: When multiplying large numbers, splits numbers into addends rather than factors
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that multiplication is not simply the sum of identical addends, and instead of finding the product, decomposes the multiplicand into addends and sums them up repeatedly, or incorrectly splits the multiplicand into smaller parts and treats them as separate operations rather than as factors.

Misconception 2412: Does not understand which place value is the one that determines if a number rounds up or down
Expanded misconception:
Fails to identify the correct place value that determines whether a number rounds up or down, often mistakenly considering the adjacent or incorrect place value when applying rounding rules.

Misconception 2413: When finding the value of a number given in roman numerals, adds the value of all letters together without considering the position of the letter.
Expanded misconception:
When finding the value of a number given in roman numerals, fails to apply the rules for subtractive notation, instead adding the value of all letters together without considering the position of the letter or the relative values of adjacent letters (e.g. IV = 4 since I is subtracted from V, not I + V = 6).

Misconception 2414: Believes a variable must remain unknown and cannot be substituted with a value
Expanded misconception:
Believes that variables are always placeholders for unknown values and fails to recognize that they can be substituted with actual values or used as surrogates for known quantities within mathematical expressions or equations.

Misconception 2415: Thinks that square root is found by dividing by 4
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that square root is found by dividing by a power of 2 (not limited to dividing by 4), indicating a misunderstanding of the square root operation as division instead of a unique mathematical function that yields a value that, when multiplied by itself, gives the original number.

Misconception 2416: Finds the difference in a coordinate pair rather than the midpoint
Expanded misconception:
Finds the difference or average of coordinates in an incorrect manner when calculating a midpoint, such as finding the difference between x-coordinates and y-coordinates or incorrectly averaging the x-coordinates and y-coordinates separately.

Misconception 2417: Does not use the associative property of multiplication to find other factors of a number
Expanded misconception:
Does not use or apply the associative or commutative properties of multiplication to rearrange calculations or identify equivalent factorizations of a number, often resulting in incorrect calculations or failure to recognize simpler factor combinations.

Misconception 2418: Believes the universal set symbol means any elements not within a circle. 
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands the universal set symbol, mistakenly believing it represents the complement of a set (elements not within a particular set) rather than the entire domain from which all elements within the sets in question are drawn.

Misconception 2419: Combines digits instead of applying the mathematical function
Expanded misconception:
Combines digits instead of applying the mathematical function, such as simply adding or merging numbers without performing the required operation, or mistakenly blending the digits from multiple numbers in a calculation.

Misconception 2420: Thinks direct proportion rules apply to inverse proportion problems
Expanded misconception:
Misapplies concepts of direct proportion to inverse proportion relationships, assuming that as one quantity increases, the other quantity also increases, rather than having a reciprocal relationship where one quantity increases as the other decreases.

Misconception 2421: Believes a rectangle has one pair of equal sides
Expanded misconception:
Confuses properties of rectangles with those of other quadrilaterals or polygons, specifically believes a rectangle has one pair of equal sides, whereas in fact, opposite sides of a rectangle are equal in length.

Misconception 2422: Tries to add or subtract unlike terms
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or apply the commutative, associative, or distributive properties of addition and subtraction, leading to adding or subtracting unlike terms, such as different units or variables with different coefficients.

Misconception 2423: Confuses isosceles and scalene triangles
Expanded misconception:
Confuses isosceles and scalene triangles by misunderstanding the number of equal sides each type of triangle has, or having incorrect knowledge of the definitions of isosceles (two sides of equal length) and scalene (no sides of equal length) triangles, often leading to incorrect identification, properties, or calculations involving these triangles.

Misconception 2424: Believes a line segment can be opposite an axis
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that in various mathematical contexts, a line segment cannot be opposite an axis, including but not limited to confusion between identifying opposite sides of an angle and understanding the relationship between line segments and axes in graphing and geometry, and mistakenly considering a line segment's orientation or position relative to an axis in problems involving angles, shapes, and coordinate planes.

Misconception 2425: Believes both solutions of a quadratic will have the same absolute value, even when the graph is not symmetrical about the y axis
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the possibility of different real solutions for a quadratic equation when the graph is not symmetrical about the y-axis, incorrectly assuming that both solutions will always have the same absolute value due to a misunderstanding of how a, b, and c coefficients in the quadratic equation impact the x-intercepts.

Misconception 2426: When finding the median from a even dataset does not understand we must find the midpoint of the two values in the middle
Expanded misconception:
When finding the median from an even dataset, does not understand that we must find the midpoint (average) of the two middle values, instead often incorrectly choosing the lower or higher middle value as the median, or incorrectly applying the median calculation used for odd-numbered datasets.

Misconception 2427: Does not divide by 2 when finding midpoint
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly apply division operations or halves quantities in relevant mathematical contexts (e.g., finding midpoints, calculating average rates, or scaling proportional relationships).

Misconception 2428: When drawing bar diagram for ratio, shows the total amount as the total of one side
Expanded misconception:
When drawing a bar diagram to represent a ratio, incorrectly shows the total amount as being equal to one side of the ratio, rather than the total sum of both parts, failing to account for the proportional relationship between the two quantities.

Misconception 2429: Believes finding a fraction of an amount means multiplying by the denominator
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly divide or find a fractional part of a number, mistakenly applying incorrect procedures such as multiplying by the denominator, considering the fraction as a whole number, or ignoring the division operation.

Misconception 2430: Does not consider fractional parts when solving proportion problems
Expanded misconception:
Fails to account for or improperly handles decimal or fractional components when solving mathematical problems, including proportions, percentages, or calculations involving mixed numbers, leading to incorrect or oversimplified solutions.

Misconception 2431: When two digits multiply to 10 or more during a multiplication problem, does not add one to the preceding digit
Expanded misconception:
When two digits multiply to 10 or more during a multiplication problem in a multi-digit multiplication, the student fails to apply the rule of carrying over, resulting in an incorrect sum.

Misconception 2432: Does not know the properties of a parallelogram
Expanded misconception:
Does not know the properties of a parallelogram, specifically the relationships between opposite sides and angles, and/or assumes that properties unique to specific types of parallelograms (such as rectangles or rhombi) apply to all parallelograms.

Misconception 2433: Does not know how to calculate with exchange rates
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand how to apply exchange rates to calculate equivalent amounts in different currencies or incorrectly handles the conversion process (e.g. fails to invert or multiply/divide correctly by the exchange rate), often resulting in arithmetic errors when solving problems involving multiple currencies.

Misconception 2434: Ignores position/value of midpoint when trying to label a point on scale
Expanded misconception:
fails to consider the proportional spacing of numbers on a number line or scale when estimating or identifying the position of a point, ignoring the midpoint or intermediate values and instead relying on inaccurate visual estimation.

Misconception 2435: Reads one point on a time series graph instead of all the values from a given time period
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the trend or overall change in a time series graph by either selecting only one data point to draw a conclusion, or incorrectly extrapolating from a single point, rather than analyzing the pattern across a given time period.

Misconception 2436: Believes the solutions of a quadratic equation are the constants in the factorised form
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that the constants in the factorised form of a quadratic equation represent the roots or solutions only when the equation is set equal to zero and the factors are linear, often treating the constants as solutions in other contexts or when the factors are non-linear.

Misconception 2437: Believes a downward slope on a distance-time graph means travelling away
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets the representation of direction on a distance-time graph, specifically believing that a downward slope always indicates movement away from a reference point, rather than considering the context and direction of the motion or the sign convention used.

Misconception 2438: Does not copy a given method accurately
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply a mathematical procedure or algorithm correctly, including not following the correct order of operations, not using the correct formula or equation, or inaccurately performing calculations within a given method, resulting in an incorrect answer.

Misconception 2439: Does not understand that removing an outlier will impact the range
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the impact of outlier removal on measures of spread and central tendency, particularly that the range will decrease or increase significantly when an outlier is removed, and thus may lead to incorrect inferences about the effect of removing data points on data analysis.

Misconception 2440: Finds the circumference instead of the area
Expanded misconception:
Finds the circumference instead of calculating the area, often indicating a lack of understanding or confusion between the formulas for the circumference (C = 2πr) and area (A = πr^2) of a circle.

Misconception 2441: Thinks multiplying by a number is the same as raising to the power of that number
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that multiplication by a number is equivalent to exponentiation, thus failing to distinguish between the operations and incorrectly applying arithmetic properties of multiplication to exponentiation or vice versa.

Misconception 2442: When calculating a scale factor, multiplies instead of dividing.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the reciprocal relationship between scale factors and proportionally applies multiplication when the operation required is division or inversely, when applying a scale factor to reverse the change or convert between measurements.

Misconception 2443: Thinks y = ? is the x axis
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the axes in a coordinate system, specifically thinking that y = ? (or a horizontal line) represents the x-axis instead of a horizontal line, often swapping x and y values.

Misconception 2444: When converting compound measures into different units, thinks you multiply when the unit of the divisor has become smaller
Expanded misconception:
When converting compound measures into different units, incorrectly thinks that when converting from a larger unit to a smaller unit, the conversion factor must always be multiplied (not divided), or conversely, mistakenly divides when converting from a smaller unit to a larger unit, often due to a misunderstanding of the relationship between unit conversion and multiplication/division operations.

Misconception 2445: Mixes up squaring and addition
Expanded misconception:
Mixes up squaring (exponentiation with a base of 2) and addition or multiplication operations involving the number 2, resulting in incorrect order of operations or incorrect application of arithmetic operations.

Misconception 2446: Believes the difference between triangle numbers decreases by 1 each time
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the constant increase in the difference between consecutive triangle numbers, specifically that the difference between successive triangle numbers always increases by 1 each time, rather than remaining constant or decreasing.

Misconception 2447: Believes the reciprocal button writes a fraction
Expanded misconception:
Confuses function of reciprocal button with manually writing a fraction, or incorrectly believes reciprocal operation is equivalent to literally writing a fraction over 1, resulting in miscalculation of reciprocal of numbers or algebraic expressions.

Misconception 2448: Cannot evaluate if an estimate is an underestimate or overestimate
Expanded misconception:
Cannot evaluate the reasonableness or accuracy of a calculation result, including the ability to identify if an answer is an underestimate or overestimate, by comparing the result to the expected range or by checking the units of the answer.

Misconception 2449: Done a different calculation to the one given
Expanded misconception:
Performed an incorrect or unrelated mathematical operation, substituted different numbers, or altered the original calculation, resulting in an answer that deviates from the correct solution.

Misconception 2450: Does not half sum of parallel sides when finding area of a trapezium
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the midpoint of the sum of parallel sides (or average of parallel sides) and only uses it crudely, or not at all, when trying to find the area of a trapezium.

Misconception 2451: When multiplying a vector by an integer does not multiply all parts of the vector
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distribute the scalar multiplication operation across all components of the vector, incorrectly applying the operation to only one or a subset of components.

Misconception 2452: Believes a parallelogram has one pair of equal sides
Expanded misconception:
Fails to fully understand the properties of a parallelogram, specifically believing it has only one pair of equal sides, rather than two pairs of equal sides, reflecting a lack of comprehension of the fundamental attributes of this quadrilateral.

Misconception 2453: Believes particular is the term used to describe two lines at right angles
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the difference between 'perpendicular' and 'particular', and incorrectly uses 'particular' to describe two lines at right angles, indicating a possible confusion between a non-technical term and a technical term in geometry.

Misconception 2454: Confuses corresponding and alternate angles
Expanded misconception:
Confuses corresponding angles with alternate interior angles, alternate exterior angles, or vertical angles, demonstrating a lack of understanding of the relationships between angles formed by a transversal cutting through two lines, including failure to recognize congruent angle pairs.

Misconception 2455: Tries to apply sohcahtoa to a non right angled triangle
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the limitations of SOHCAHTOA (sine, cosine, and tangent in right-angled triangles) and inappropriately applies it to non-right angled triangles, or incorrectly assumes that trigonometric ratios only apply to right-angled triangles, without considering alternative methods such as the law of cosines or sines for more complex triangle types.

Misconception 2456: Finds the mode rather than the range
Expanded misconception:
Finds the mode (the value that appears most frequently) rather than the range (the difference between the highest and lowest values), indicating a misunderstanding of statistical measures and their calculations.

Misconception 2457: When asked for the mean of a list of data, gives the median
Expanded misconception:
When asked for the mean (average) of a list of data, incorrectly gives the median (middle value) instead of actually calculating the sum and dividing by the number of values.

Misconception 2458: Multiplies by the denominator instead of dividing when finding a fraction of an amount
Expanded misconception:
Misapplies fraction of a quantity operations by inverting or misinterchanging roles of the numerator and denominator, often resulting in unnecessary multiplication by the divisor, instead of performing required division when finding a fraction of an amount.

Misconception 2459: Believes alternate angles sum to 180 degrees
Expanded misconception:
Believes alternate angles in a variety of contexts, including angles around a point, vertically opposite angles, or around a transversal with two intersecting lines, sum to 180 degrees regardless of the actual relationship, often leading to incorrect application of angle properties.

Misconception 2460: Does not recognise an example of the angles in same segment theorem
Expanded misconception:
Does not recognise or apply the angles in the same segment theorem property that angles subtended by the same chord in a circle are equal, often resulting in incorrect calculations or angle relationships in circle geometry problems.

Misconception 2461: Believes that the larger the dividend, the smaller the answer.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the inverse relationship between the dividend and the quotient when dividing by a given divisor, often incorrectly assuming the larger the dividend, the smaller the answer, and unaware that the quotient actually increases as the dividend increases, given a constant divisor.

Misconception 2462: Confuses the position of the median with its value
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the position of the median with its actual value in a dataset, incorrectly considering the median's numerical value to be its position within the ordered data set, or vice versa.

Misconception 2463: When multiplying an integer by a fraction, believes the numerator of the fraction will become the denominator
Expanded misconception:
When multiplying an integer by a fraction, incorrectly applies reciprocal operations, where they either believe the numerator of the fraction becomes the denominator or incorrectly reverses the addition of the integer, either entirely or partially.

Misconception 2464: Believes the sides of a rectangle must be horizontal and vertical, not diagonal
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that a rectangle's sides can be oriented at any angle, rather than being strictly horizontal and vertical, and believes that a quadrilateral with diagonally oriented sides is not a rectangle due to a misunderstanding of the geometric properties of rectangles.

Misconception 2465: Believes reflection in y = -x changes sign of x coordinate only
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize or apply the inverse nature of reflection across the line y = -x, and instead only considers the effect on the x-coordinate, neglecting the associated change in the y-coordinate's sign.

Misconception 2466: Uses tan when sin is required
Expanded misconception:
Inappropriately applies trigonometric functions in a problem, specifically incorrectly choosing between sine, cosine, and tangent despite the type of angle or ratio mentioned, often showing a lack of understanding of SOH-CAH-TOA or the trigonometric relationships between the sides of a triangle.

Misconception 2467: Does not know the meaning of the word equidistant 
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand or apply the concept of equidistant, where two or more points are in a position that is the same distance from a reference point, as required in various mathematical contexts, such as geometry, transformations, or coordinate systems.

Misconception 2468: Does not know that three quarters of a turn is 270 degrees
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the relationship between degrees and fractions of a circle, specifically struggling to recognize that one full turn (360 degrees) can be divided into fractional parts, with three quarters of a turn corresponding to 270 degrees.

Misconception 2469: Believes a negative number can be replaced with its positive equivalent when answering an addition problem from a numberline
Expanded misconception:
Ignores the concept of absolute value and positional changes on the number line, incorrectly assuming that a negative number can be replaced with its positive equivalent when adding numbers, thus failing to understand direction and the concept of moving backwards or forwards on the number line.

Misconception 2470: Believes the mode is the most common frequency rather than the highest frequency
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize the mode as the value that appears most frequently in a dataset, instead incorrectly identifying the mode as the value with the highest frequency in general, possibly due to misunderstanding the concept or incorrectly recalling its definition.

Misconception 2471: Believes the range is largest add smallest
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to properly apply the concept of range, believing it is calculated by adding the largest value to the smallest value rather than finding the difference between the largest and smallest values."

Misconception 2472: Does not know how to find the next term in a sequence
Expanded misconception:
Does not know or applies incorrect rules to find the next term in a sequence, such as misunderstanding pattern type (arithmetic, geometric, or recursive), failing to identify pattern repetition or increments, or incorrectly applying sequence formulas.

Misconception 2473: Has cubed the base number and the power
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly applies exponentiation rules, has wrongly cubed both the base number and the exponent, resulting in an answer that is neither raised to the correct power nor multiplied by itself the correct number of times.

Misconception 2474: Confuses the square root of the radius with the radius squared in the equation of a circle
Expanded misconception:
Confuses between radical and power operations, specifically, misunderstands or incorrectly applies formulas involving square roots (e.g., distance or radius) and squared terms (e.g., area or radius squared), in equations, such as those related to pythagorean theorem, quadratic forms, circle geometry, or other mathematical contexts where these operations are used interchangeably or incorrectly.

Misconception 2475: When finding the nth term rule for a quadratic sequence, believes they only need to use the first term to calculate the coefficient of n
