Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the entire sequence and uses incomplete information, specifically when finding the nth term rule for a quadratic sequence, believes they only need to use the first term (or an insufficient number of terms) to calculate the coefficient of n and/or the constant term, and does not adequately account for the relationships between consecutive terms or consider multiple terms to identify the underlying pattern.

Misconception 2476: Does not write the place holder zero when doing column multiplication
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly apply place value rules in multiplication, specifically neglecting to include placeholder zeros in appropriate positions when multiplying digits in a multi-digit by multi-digit calculation.

Misconception 2477: Thinks there are 30 seconds in a minute
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands time units and conversions, specifically believes there are not 60 seconds in a minute, but instead thinks there is an incorrect or unspecified number of seconds (e.g., 30) in a minute leading to errors in calculations involving time unit conversions.

Misconception 2478: Thinks the plan view is from the side of the shape
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets or confuses the orientation of a 2D or 3D shape when presented with multiple views, specifically thinking that a plan or top view represents a side or perspective view, and vice versa.

Misconception 2479: When factorising into double brackets, believes the product of the constants in the brackets is of the opposite sign to the constant in the expanded equation.
Expanded misconception:
When factorizing quadratic expressions into double brackets (of the form a(x+b)(x+c) = ?), believes that the product of the constants in the brackets is always of the opposite sign to the constant term in the expanded equation, leading to incorrect assumptions about the signs of the terms in the expanded form.

Misconception 2480: Thinks powers with different bases cannot be equal
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that exponential expressions with different bases can yield the same value, often neglecting to consider the unique combinations of bases and exponents that can produce equivalent results.

Misconception 2481: When multiplying decimals, divides by the wrong power of 10 when reinserting the decimal
Expanded misconception:
When multiplying decimals, fails to correctly reinsert the decimal point by dividing by an incorrect power of 10 or omitting the division altogether, often incorrectly assuming that multiplying by a factor of 100 is equivalent to multiplying by 100 or misunderstanding the concept of multiplying powers of 10 and counting places.

Misconception 2482: Has written something to the power of 1 as 0.1
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand exponentiation rules, specifically that any number to the power of 1 is equal to itself, and mistakenly simplifies or alters the base number when raised to the power of 1.

Misconception 2483: Believes that a negative sign in the base of a power means you need to find the reciprocal 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly interpret negative exponents; believes that a negative sign in the base of a power means that you need to find the reciprocal, rather than understanding that a negative exponent represents taking the reciprocal of the base raised to the positive exponent.

Misconception 2484: When converting from an improper fraction to a mixed number, just writes the tens digit as the number of wholes
Expanded misconception:
When converting from an improper fraction to a mixed number, incorrectly assumes the tens digit or the whole number part of the quotient of the fraction must be the number of wholes, without considering or accounting for additional whole parts that may arise from further division of the remainder.

Misconception 2485: Thinks that times just before midnight are "am" times
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand or apply 12-hour clock conventions, specifically that times from midnight to 11:59 are "am" and times from 12:00 to just before midnight are "pm" (or incorrectly assumes that times close to midnight are part of the previous day's "am" period).

Misconception 2486: Identifies the wrong two angles as base angles of an isosceles
Expanded misconception:
Identifies the wrong two angles as base angles of an isosceles triangle, often mistakenly selecting either the vertex angle and one of the base angles, two non-adjacent angles, or angles that are not part of the same triangle.

Misconception 2487: Changes the given operation when substituting
Expanded misconception:
Changes the given operation when substituting numbers or values into an equation or expression, such as mistakenly substituting addition for multiplication, and/or division for subtraction, resulting in an incorrect calculation or solution.

Misconception 2488: Answers order of operations questions with brackets as if the brackets are not there
Expanded misconception:
Answers order of operations questions without properly considering the hierarchy of operations within brackets or parentheses, often ignoring the brackets or treating them as if they do not affect the order in which calculations should be performed.

Misconception 2489: Thinks cube numbers are triangular numbers
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between two or more types of number sequences (e.g., cube numbers and triangular numbers, or square numbers and square roots), often applying formulas or patterns of one sequence to another.

Misconception 2490: Assumes Pythagoras' theorem doesn't work if they can spot a pattern between the side lengths
Expanded misconception:
Ignores generalizability of Pythagoras' theorem and believes it only applies to standard patterns or specific groups of numbers (such as 3-4-5 or 5-12-13 triangles), failing to recognize that the theorem is universally applicable to all right-angled triangles regardless of the relative side lengths.

Misconception 2491: Thinks that in the equation x=a, a is the gradient
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that in a linear equation, the constant term or a standalone variable on one side of the equation represents the gradient or slope, rather than the value of the variable when the other variable equals a specific value, often zero.

Misconception 2492: Confuses rest and movement on a distance time graph
Expanded misconception:
Confuses rest and movement on a distance-time graph, often incorrectly interpreting a horizontal section as a non-zero speed or a non-horizontal section as zero speed, or misidentifying the actual starting or ending points of motion.

Misconception 2493: Does not know the properties of a rhombus
Expanded misconception:
Does not know the properties of a rhombus, such as opposite sides of equal length, opposite angles being equal, or that the diagonals bisect each other.

Misconception 2494: Does not know the meaning of consecutive
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand or misapplies the concept of consecutive items, sequences, or events in mathematical expressions, potentially leading to incorrect calculations or interpretations when integers are being added, subtracted, multiplied, or divided by a certain value in succession.

Misconception 2495: Compares sizes of angles on a grid by estimating rather than using the gradient of the lines
Expanded misconception:
Compares sizes or angles of shapes (on a grid or otherwise) by estimating and relying on visual impressions, rather than using precise methods such as measuring or comparing the gradients of the lines, applying angle properties (e.g., corresponding, alternate interior, or exterior angles), or employing geometrical reasoning.

Misconception 2496: Believes composite function notation fg(x) means to multiply the first terms of f and g together, and the second terms of f and g together
Expanded misconception:
Misapplies function composition notation by interpreting fg or f(g(x)) as an algebraic product of corresponding terms in f and g, rather than as a nested function where the output of g(x) is the input of f, often resulting in an incorrect manipulation of the given functions.

Misconception 2497: Believes f(x - a) translates the function down a units
Expanded misconception:
Misconceives the effect of horizontal shifts in function transformations; believes f(x - a) translates the function down a units, rather than shifting it horizontally to the right by a units.

Misconception 2498: Thinks I can be subtracted from L in Roman numerals
Expanded misconception:
Thinks that the subtraction rule for Roman numerals (where a smaller numeral placed before a larger one indicates subtraction) is always applicable, and incorrectly applies it to cases where 'I' is not being subtracted from 'V' or 'X'.

Misconception 2499: Thinks the interior angles of any polygon add up to 180 degrees
Expanded misconception:
Fails to generalize the relationship between the number of sides and the sum of interior angles of polygons, incorrectly assuming that all polygons have a total interior angle sum of 180 degrees, typical of triangles, due to insufficient understanding of the formula (n-2) x 180.

Misconception 2500: Believes the gradient of a line is given by an intercept
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distinguish between a line's gradient (representing rate of change or slope) and its y-intercept, incorrectly equating the two or using the y-intercept as a measure of the line's steepness.

Misconception 2501: Subtracts rather than multiplies when calculating total frequency
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the fundamental counting principle by incorrectly performing arithmetic operations (e.g., subtracts rather than multiplies) when calculating total frequency, probability, or outcomes in a problem, often due to misunderstanding the concept of independent events or the correct application of multiplication principle in probability and statistics.

Misconception 2502: Believes a reciprocal equation will have a quadratic graph
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that a reciprocal equation in the form y = 1/x will produce a hyperbola when graphed, and instead mistakenly expects it to produce a quadratic graph in the form y = ax^2 + bx + c.

Misconception 2503: Believes that a greater percentage represents a greater quantity
Expanded misconception:
Lacks understanding that a greater percentage does not necessarily represent a greater quantity unless the quantities being compared have the same base or reference value.

Misconception 2504: Does not realise that two letters together, or a number and letter together, are multiplying
Expanded misconception:
Fails to interpret juxtaposition or adjacent placement of variables (letters) and/or coefficients (numbers) as implicit multiplication, including failure to recognize the multiplication operation when a number is adjacent to a variable or when multiple variables are together.

Misconception 2505: Confuses cl and ml
Expanded misconception:
Confuses milliliters (mL) and centiliters (cL) in measurement problems, or mistakenly uses incorrect prefixes or conversions in a problem requiring precise liquid measurement.

Misconception 2506: Believes cumulative frequency is the sum of each class with the previous class, rather than all previous classes
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand that cumulative frequency represents the total frequency of each class and all preceding classes, resulting in an incorrect calculation by only adding the current class to the immediately preceding class instead of the cumulative total.

Misconception 2507: Mistakes unit of speed for unit of time
Expanded misconception:
'Mistakes unit of speed (e.g. km/h) for unit of time (e.g. hours), resulting in incorrect calculations or conversion between the two units, often by assuming one unit is equivalent to or directly comparable with the other.'

Misconception 2508: Thinks x = ? is the y axis
Expanded misconception:
Misconceives the variable's position in an equation, specifically thinking the variable (often x) represents the y-axis when it actually represents the x-axis, leading to incorrect graphing and understanding of variables in equations.

Misconception 2509: Mixes up the sets in a Venn diagram
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands or confuses the relationships and intersections between multiple sets in a Venn diagram, including incorrectly identifying disjoint sets, overestimating or underestimating overlapping areas, and/or making incorrect inferences about set membership.

Misconception 2510: Only reads the percentage symbol as applying to the units digit
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply percentage symbol correctly, only considering it as modifying a single digit or unit, rather than the entire quantity or base, and neglecting the possibility of applying it as the percentage of a fractional part or percent of a group.

Misconception 2511: Does not know the properties of a linear graph
Expanded misconception:
Does not know the properties of a linear graph, specifically the concept that the graph of a linear equation is a straight line, the relationship between the x and y-intercepts, and/or the slope-intercept form of a linear equation, leading to incorrect predictions or calculations involving linear relationships.

Misconception 2512: Believes when dividing numbers with the same base you divide the powers, rather than subtracting
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the quotient rule of exponents, believing that when dividing numbers with the same base, they simply divide the powers, rather than subtracting the exponents, resulting in mathematical errors when simplifying expressions with exponential divisions.

Misconception 2513: When finding the nth term rule for a quadratic sequence, believes that the coefficient of n squared is determined by first differences
Expanded misconception:
When finding the nth term rule for a quadratic sequence, believes that the coefficient of n squared is determined by first differences, rather than the second differences that represent the rate of change of the first differences, often resulting in incorrect quadratic sequence formulas.

Misconception 2514: Does not cube the conversion factor when converting cubed units
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the exponentiation of units during conversions, such as squaring or cubing, when converting between units raised to a power, resulting in incorrect unit conversions and calculations.

Misconception 2515: Rounds too early in calculation 
Expanded misconception:
Rounds intermediate calculation results prematurely, rather than performing exact calculations throughout and rounding only at the final step or in line with conventional calculation protocols, leading to unintended inaccuracies or imprecise answers.

Misconception 2516: Thinks 3-letter angle notation has to use the nearest vertex on each side of the angle
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly assumes that 3-letter angle notation requires the use of the nearest vertex on each side of the angle, neglecting to account for the order and unique identification of vertices when naming an angle.

Misconception 2517: Does not rearrange the Pythagoras' Theorem formula accurately
Expanded misconception:
Fails to correctly apply or rearrange the Pythagoras' Theorem formula (a² + b² = c²) by incorrectly ordering or substituting variables, neglecting to square the terms, or misinterpreting the formula's structure when solving for different variables.

Misconception 2518: May have made a calculation error using the order of operations
Expanded misconception:
May have made a calculation error using the order of operations, such as misapplying the priority of addition and multiplication or mismanaging parentheses, resulting in incorrect handling of expressions with multiple operations.

Misconception 2519: Confuses a term with a variable
Expanded misconception:
Confuses a term with a variable, often demonstrating an inability to distinguish between constants and variables or mistakenly replacing a variable with a numerical value in the context of mathematical operations or equations.

Misconception 2520: Believes the fraction line represents addition
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the division sign (/) as indicating addition rather than division, incorrectly interpreting mathematical expressions containing fractions or division operations.

Misconception 2521: Does not know how to measure reflex angles
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the concept of measuring reflex angles in a circle and incorrectly applies standard angle measurement principles, often resulting in measuring the acute angle formed within the reflex angle instead of the actual reflex angle, possibly due to lack of understanding of how angles greater than 180 degrees are measured.

Misconception 2522: Believes the total of the circle in a Venn should be included when intersections should be omitted
Expanded misconception:
Failed to account for exclusivity of groups in a Venn diagram by considering areas outside intersections and incorrectly combining or adding overlapping regions.

Misconception 2523: Has added on the power of 1/4 rather than raise to the power of 1/4
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the difference between addition of an exponent and the operation of exponentiation, incorrectly adding the exponent to the base rather than raising it to the fractional power, a fundamental concept in algebra involving rational exponents.

Misconception 2524: Has added the powers rather than multiplying them
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to apply the rule of exponents by adding or substituting exponents when multiplying terms with the same base, often adding instead of applying multiplication rules for exponents."

Misconception 2525: When dividing decimals, divides rather than multiplies when reinserting the decimal.
Expanded misconception:
"When dividing decimals, fails to properly handle decimal placement, either by not multiplying the dividend by a corresponding power of 10 to maintain place value or by not adjusting the decimal point in the quotient accordingly, leading to errors in calculation or incorrect placement of the decimal point in the answer."

Misconception 2526: Believes a downward line has a positive gradient
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the relationship between the direction of a line on a graph and the sign of its gradient, mistakenly thinking that lines which slope downwards have a positive gradient.

Misconception 2527: Writes a repeated root once, rather than in a squared bracket
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct notation when simplifying expressions with repeating roots, resulting in missing or incomplete specification of the repeated root, such as unnecessarily including the repeated root multiple times or neglecting to enclose it in a squared bracket.

Misconception 2528: Thinks that the perimeter on a square grid is the number of squares surrounding the rectangle
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the correct arithmetic operation for the perimeter of a shape on a square grid, mistakenly considering it as the number of squares surrounding or enclosing the rectangle, rather than the sum of the lengths of its sides.

Misconception 2529: Does not think a ton is a unit of mass
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize and apply the distinction between units of mass (e.g., ton, kilogram) and units of volume or capacity (e.g., tonne, US ton, fluid ounces), leading to confusion with the unit 'ton' as a measure of volume or weight rather than mass.

Misconception 2530: Subtracts instead of adds when using inverse operations to solve an equation
Expanded misconception:
Subtracts instead of adds when applying inverse operations to solve linear equations, often resulting in errors when negating or removing terms on the same side of the equation, especially when working with constants being added or subtracted.

Misconception 2531: Does not understand that the volume of an enlarged shape is the volume of the original multiplied by the cube of the scale factor of the lengths.
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand that the volume of an enlarged shape is the volume of the original multiplied by the cube of the scale factor of the lengths, failing to account for the nonlinear relationship between linear scale factors and three-dimensional volume changes, leading to incorrect proportional reasoning and calculation of volumes in scaled variations of solids.

Misconception 2532: Believes order of operations does not affect the answer to a calculation
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the importance of following a standard order of operations when performing arithmetic calculations, typically resulting in incorrect answers due to incorrect prioritization or order of mathematical operations such as addition, subtraction, multiplication, and division.

Misconception 2533: Does not realise that two letters together are multiplying
Expanded misconception:
Fails to interpret implicit multiplication between adjacent variables, coefficients and/or numeric values, or mistakenly treats adjacent variables and/or numeric values as separate entities rather than a single, multiplied term.

Misconception 2534: When converting between non standard form and standard form assumes changes to the base will not affect the power
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the relationship between the base and exponent when converting between standard and non-standard (scientific) form, assuming changes to the base will not affect the exponent or vice versa, resulting in incorrect placement of the decimal point or incorrect calculations.

Misconception 2535: Does not add on 180 degrees when using a protractor to measure a reflex angle
Expanded misconception:
Does not account for the full angular measurement when dealing with reflex angles, specifically neglecting to add 180 degrees to the measured angle when using a protractor, thus resulting in an incomplete or inaccurate angular measurement.

Misconception 2536: Misunderstands the capture-recapture method
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply the capture-recapture method correctly, often due to a misunderstanding of the population estimation formula, such as incorrectly using the number of marked and recaptured individuals, failing to account for the proportion of marked individuals in the population, or not making the necessary assumptions for the method to be valid.

Misconception 2537: Does not know the definition of a rational number
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands or lacks knowledge of rational numbers, including their definition, identification, properties, and operations, which may manifest as incorrect handling of fractions, decimals, or ratios in various mathematical contexts.

Misconception 2538: Confuses the value of the variable with cumulative frequency
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the value of the variable with the accumulation of frequency distributions or cumulative frequencies, failing to distinguish between the actual value and the running total or sum of observed or expected frequencies.

Misconception 2539: Believes the inverse of square rooting is halving
Expanded misconception:
Confuses or misunderstands mathematical operations, specifically believing that the inverse operation of square rooting (i.e., an operation that reverses or undoes square rooting) is halving, rather than squaring.

Misconception 2540: Confuses maximum and minimum
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly distinguish between concepts of maximum and minimum, often incorrectly identifying the largest or most extreme value as the minimum or vice versa, especially in problems involving numerical comparisons or optimization.

Misconception 2541: When finding the square root of a decimal, believes the answer should have the same number of decimal places
Expanded misconception:
When finding the square root of a decimal, believes the answer should have the same number of decimal places as the original number, failing to understand the concept of decimal placement and significant figures in the resulting square root.

Misconception 2542: Believes that dividing by a decimal is the same as dividing by its reciprocal
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider the effect of the divisor's decimal place on the quotient and incorrectly applies the concept of equivalent fractions or reciprocals when dividing by a decimal, leading to a misplaced or miscalculated decimal point.

Misconception 2543: Believes the frequency represented by a pie chart sector can be judged visually, without considering the total it represents
Expanded misconception:
Believes the size or area of a graphical representation, including but not limited to pie charts, bar graphs, and histograms, can be directly interpreted as the relative magnitude or frequency of the data being represented, without considering the scaling factor, proportion, or total it represents.

Misconception 2544: Confuses reciprocal and exponential graphs
Expanded misconception:
Confuses reciprocal and exponential graphs, specifically failing to recognize or distinguish between the characteristics of reciprocal functions (e.g. asymptotes, decreasing/increasing patterns, and domain restrictions) and exponential functions (e.g. asymptotes, exponential growth/decay, and no domain restrictions), often resulting in incorrect identification of function type, asymptotes, or behavior as x approaches positive or negative infinity.

Misconception 2545: Does not know that prime numbers have exactly two factors
Expanded misconception:
Misconceives the properties of prime numbers, failing to recognize that prime numbers have exactly two factors, resulting in incorrect identification or application of prime number characteristics in various mathematical contexts.

Misconception 2546: Confuses the hundreds and tens place value columns
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize and consistently apply place value concepts, specifically confusing the hundreds and tens place value columns, often resulting in digit or number sequence errors in mathematical operations or representations.

Misconception 2547: Multiplies instead of divides when division is written as a fraction
Expanded misconception:
Misinterprets division and fraction operations, often treating the division operation as multiplication when the division is represented as a fraction or interpreting the fraction as an indication to multiply by the denominator rather than dividing by it.

Misconception 2548: Does not increase the denominator when an item is added in a probability question
Expanded misconception:
Does not increase or update the denominator when additional items or events are introduced in a probability question, failing to account for changes in the sample space or total number of possible outcomes.

Misconception 2549: Believes only the first term needs to be divided when factorising an expression
Expanded misconception:
Fails to distribute to all terms in an expression when applying operations such as division or multiplication on factorable terms, often incorrectly factoring or simplifying expressions by only applying the operation to the first term.

Misconception 2550: Thinks there are 10m in a km
Expanded misconception:
Fails to accurately recall or apply unit conversion between kilometers and meters, specifically believing there are 10 meters in a kilometer instead of 1,000.

Misconception 2551: Believes range does not need units
Expanded misconception:
Believes range does not need units, failing to recognize that range requires the same units as the data it represents, often leading to incompatibility in units when comparing or combining measures of spread.

Misconception 2552: When adding decimals, thinks the answer has to have the same number of decimals as the total in the question
Expanded misconception:
When adding or subtracting decimals, incorrectly assumes that the answer must have the same number of decimal places as the original numbers in the problem or operation.

Misconception 2553: Gives the second significant figure when asked for the first
Expanded misconception:
Fails to prioritize correct significant figure in calculations, consistently provides a less precise answer by giving the wrong significant figure, often giving the second significant figure when asked for the first significant figure or the most significant figure.

Misconception 2554: Thinks the diagonal of a square is the same length as the side of the square
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that the diagonal of a square is actually √2 times the length of the side of the square, or incorrectly assumes the diagonal is equivalent to, greater than, or less than the side length of the square, possibly due to misunderstanding the geometric properties or the Pythagorean theorem.

Misconception 2555: Does not understand how to find a scale factor
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand or apply the concept of equivalent ratios to find the scale factor, often incorrectly assuming that scale factor is merely a simple ratio or proportion between corresponding measurements, neglecting to consider proportional changes in size.

Misconception 2556: Thinks the subtraction sign means multiply
Expanded misconception:
Thinks the subtraction sign (or any negative sign) means multiply or add a negative number as the second value in the equation, indicating confusion with the effect of the order of operations involving negative numbers.

Misconception 2557: Mixes up cubing and squaring
Expanded misconception:
Confuses different types of exponents, specifically, mistakenly uses the formula of or applies the concept of squaring when the problem requires cubing or vice versa.

Misconception 2558: When writing fraction from diagram, writes total as denominator
Expanded misconception:
When writing a fraction from a diagram, incorrectly uses the total number of parts as the denominator instead of identifying the actual denominator based on the context or additional given information.

Misconception 2559: When dividing numbers with different bases subtracts the powers but divides the base numbers.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply exponent rules when numbers have different bases, incorrectly subtracting powers and dividing base numbers instead of converting to a common base or applying the quotient of powers rule correctly.

Misconception 2560: Confuses the formulas for the perimeter of a sector and the area of a sector 
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the formulas for the perimeter (or arc length) of a sector and the area of a sector, specifically using the formula for the area of a sector (A = θ/360 * πr^2) in place of the formula for the perimeter (or arc length) of a sector, or vice versa.

Misconception 2561: Believes a sample space should show repeated outcomes, not just unique possible outcomes.
Expanded misconception:
Displays a misunderstanding of sample space in probability, believing it should include every possible outcome arranged in a specific sequence or repetition, rather than just listing all unique possible outcomes.

Misconception 2562: Confuses the lower and upper bound
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the lower and upper bound of a range or interval, incorrectly selecting a distractor that is within the bounds but not the correct value, or one that is outside the bounds due to failure to consider the constraints or limitations of the problem, or misinterpreting inclusive and exclusive bounds.

Misconception 2563: Believes to complete a part whole model you select a value already given in the model
Expanded misconception:
Believes that to complete a part-whole model, you must select a value already given in the model, failing to understand that the missing quantity can be derived by comparing or combining the given values, such as addition or subtraction.

Misconception 2564: Gives the intersect value of a Venn diagram when asked to find the value of a set only
Expanded misconception:
Gives the intersect value of a Venn diagram when asked to find the value of a set only, rather than considering the entire set or the region that includes the intersect value.

Misconception 2565: Confuses the hundreds and thousands place value columns
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the hundreds and thousands place value columns, resulting in errors when determining digits' positional value or magnitude, particularly when moving left or right across multiple place value columns.

Misconception 2566: When subtracting a negative number, subtracts the absolute value rather than adding it
Expanded misconception:
When subtracting a negative number, subtracts the absolute value rather than adding it, ignoring the rules of operating with signed numbers and misunderstanding the concept that two negative signs cancel each other out, resulting in an incorrect addition operation.

Misconception 2567: Believes they only need to multiply the first pair of terms when expanding a double bracket.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to fully apply the distributive property, incorrectly assuming that only the first term in each bracket needs to be multiplied, rather than following through with the multiplication of the remaining terms.

Misconception 2568: Believes all the multiples of a number will also be multiples of its multiples
Expanded misconception:
Fails to understand the concept of multiplication and factor relationships, believing that if a number is a multiple of another number, then all its multiples will also be multiples of the original number's multiples, rather than just sharing a common factor.

Misconception 2569: Confuses pentagon and hexagon
Expanded misconception:
Confuses the properties and attributes of polygons, specifically mistaking a pentagon (a five-sided polygon) for a hexagon (a six-sided polygon), or vice versa, indicative of a lack of understanding of geometric shapes and their defining characteristics.

Misconception 2570: Confuses interior angles with exterior angles
Expanded misconception:
"Has difficulty distinguishing between interior and exterior angles, often incorrectly applying interior angle properties or calculations to exterior angles or vice versa, potentially due to misunderstandings about angle classifications, properties, or relationships in geometric shapes."

Misconception 2571: When dividing numbers with different bases, divides the powers and divides the base numbers.
Expanded misconception:
Fails to properly handle exponents and bases when dividing quantities with exponents, incorrectly applying rules by separately dividing numerical coefficients and powers of the base, instead of following the rule for dividing exponential expressions with the same base (a^m/a^n = a^(m-n)).

Misconception 2572: Rotates by the wrong center of rotation (not including the origin)
Expanded misconception:
Rotates by the wrong center of rotation (not including the origin), incorrectly applying rotational geometry principles by fixing the wrong pivot point, point of reference, or axis of rotation when performing transformations, and failing to account for the correct spatial relationships between objects during rotational movements.

Misconception 2573: Believes the solution to mx + c = a is the y intercept of y = mx +c
Expanded misconception:
Believes the solution to a linear equation in slope-intercept form (mx + c = a or y = mx + c) is always the y-intercept, often resulting in overlooking the need to solve for x and instead providing the constant term c as the solution.

Misconception 2574: Believes that when multiplying by a power of 10 you add zeroes after the decimal point
Expanded misconception:
Believes that when multiplying by a power of 10, you add zeroes after the decimal point rather than shifting the decimal point to the correct position, resulting in incorrect placement of the decimal point in the product.

Misconception 2575: Thinks adding same amount to each side of a ratio gives an equivalent ratio
Expanded misconception:
Fails to recognize that when adding or subtracting the same amount to both the numerator and denominator in a ratio or proportion, the resulting ratio is only equivalent if the added or subtracted amount is multiplied or divided by a corresponding scale factor, misunderstanding the concept of equivalent ratios and proportions.

Misconception 2576: Mixes up square numbers and cube numbers
Expanded misconception:
Incorrectly identifies or calculates quantities as square numbers or cube numbers due to confusion between squared exponents (e.g., x²) and cubed exponents (e.g., x³), leading to selection of incorrect answers that reflect this misunderstanding of multiplicative degrees.

Misconception 2577: Does not think a square root can be negative
Expanded misconception:
Fails to consider that every positive number has two square roots, one positive and one negative, and may automatically discard the negative solution or assume that square roots are exclusively positive.

Misconception 2578: Mixes up sides of a ratio, when working with vectors for line segments.
Expanded misconception:
Mixes up sides of a ratio, direction of scaling, or corresponding components of a ratio when working with proportional quantities, scaling factors, fractions, equivalent ratios, or vectors for line segments, resulting in incorrect application of ratio concepts in problem-solving.

Misconception 2579: Forgets to add the numerator when converting from a mixed number to an improper fraction 
Expanded misconception:
Fails to apply rules for converting mixed numbers to improper fractions, specifically neglecting to add the numerator to the product of the denominator and whole number.

Misconception 2580: Believes degrees can be used to measure length
Expanded misconception:
Believes units of angle measurement (degrees) can be used interchangeably with units of length measurement (e.g., inches, meters, feet) without proper conversion.

Misconception 2581: When factorising a quadratic with a coefficient of x squared, believes the signs and/or positions of the constants are interchangeable
Expanded misconception:
Misconception: When factorising a quadratic equation in the form ax² + bx + c, believes that altering the signs, magnitude or order of the constants (b and/or c) while ignoring the relationship with the coefficient of the x² term (a) will still yield correct factorisation, often failing to check for correct multiplication of constants and/or distribution of the coefficient a when factoring.

Misconception 2582: When multiplying numbers with the same base, multiplies the powers 
Expanded misconception:
"When multiplying numbers with the same base, incorrectly multiplies the powers instead of adding them."

Misconception 2583: Does not know what a cube number is
Expanded misconception:
Does not understand the concept of perfect cube and cube numbers, including calculation and representation, and may struggle with identifying or applying formulas or properties related to cube numbers.

Misconception 2584: Believes that any percentage of a larger number will be greater than any percentage of a smaller number
Expanded misconception:
"Fails to consider the actual values when comparing percentages of different numbers, mistakenly assuming that a percentage of a larger number will always be greater than the same or a smaller percentage of a smaller number, neglecting the relative sizes of the percentages themselves."

Misconception 2585: Believes a cubic expression should have three terms
Expanded misconception:
Believes that expressions, such as quadratics or cubics, must adhere to a specific form with a fixed number of terms, and fails to recognize or verify the existence of coefficients, constant terms, or combined like terms that may alter the apparent structure of the expression.

Misconception 2586: Misunderstands order of operations in algebraic expressions
Expanded misconception:
Misunderstands order of operations in algebraic expressions, failing to apply the correct priority and sequence of operations, including parentheses, exponents, multiplication, division, addition, and subtraction, potentially leading to incorrect groupings or calculations, especially when multiple operations or mixed operation types (e.g., addition and subtraction, or multiplication and division) are involved.
